Chapter 1: The Ship That Would Not Fire

The old battleship entered Cinderwake Lane with its guns asleep and three hundred forty-one people trusting that sleep more than they trusted armor.

Tovin Rees stood in the restraint gallery with both hands folded behind his back, because if he touched the rail he would feel the tremor running through the deck and mistake the ship's patience for fear. The gallery had once been a secondary fire-control room. Its walls still curved inward around the absent logic of violence: targeting pits filled with blank plates, weapon-status columns dark under covenant seals, acceleration couches removed and replaced by witness desks. Someone before Tovin had painted the bulkheads a soft hospital green, as if color could persuade steel to forget what it had been built to do.

Above the forward display, the ship's name glowed in small white letters.

MERCY OF KALN.

Below it, a different line remained from the covenant installation, etched into the glass rather than projected.

NO WEAPON MAY WAKE WITHOUT PUBLIC WITNESS.

Tovin had read that sentence every watch for nine months. He still did not know whether it was a promise, a threat, or a prayer.

"Range to debris front?" he asked.

"Thirty-one thousand kilometers and closing," said Sela Marr from the engineering witness station. She was twenty-four, compact, black-haired, and too quick with her hands when frightened. Her voice carried the clipped anger of someone who had learned discipline without being convinced by it. "Cinderwake shear is worse than buoy report. Forward plates can take the grit. The school deck windows cannot."

"School deck shutters are closed," Tovin said.

"The shutters are thirty-year polymer over a museum frame."

"Then the children will have a memorable civics lesson about why old ships need new budgets."

Sela looked up from her screen. "That was almost a joke."

"Record it as a morale event."

Noor Venn, sitting at the legal desk beneath the dead torpedo board, did not smile. Noor rarely smiled on watch. They had the narrow, tired face of a person who slept beside statutes and found them argumentative even in dreams. A thin legal recorder floated above their left shoulder, its blue light blinking every six seconds as it notarized restraint compliance for anyone who later wanted to claim the *Mercy* had breathed wrong.

"Morale event recorded as non-operational speech," Noor said.

The forward display widened. Cinderwake Lane appeared as an uneven ribbon of dust, old hull fragments, ice crumbs, and slow metallic weather curving between two dark masses that had never quite become moons. The Belt charts named those masses Kaln Minor and Kaln Lesser. Nobody aboard used the formal names. They called them the Brothers, because during the Fracture Wars the two rocks had hidden fleets, graves, and promises that should have died with their makers.

The convoy behind the *Mercy* was not impressive enough for songs. It consisted of six pressure barges, two ice-haulers, a school ark with eighty-seven children from Varo Anchorage, a cracked oxygen still dragged under net, and a tug called *Little Argument* that kept sending the same three engine complaints in different legal formats. They had waited eleven days for an armed escort. None had come. Then Captain Mer Anik had agreed to push them through the debris weather under the *Mercy*'s covenant flag.

A former battleship still had mass. It had armor thick enough to make small stations jealous, engines built for ugly accelerations, medical decks, machine shops, pressure tents, shield projectors designed to protect command sections from shrapnel, and a name that made pirates think twice even after the guns had been slagged.

Sometimes that was enough.

Sometimes the name did more harm than guns.

"Unidentified contact emerging starboard high," said Ixo Continuant.

Ixo's voice came from the gallery speakers with a faint tonal layering, three nearly identical voices choosing agreement a fraction of a second apart. The machine-person was not installed in the ship. Ixo objected to that word. They occupied permitted processors in convoy archives, navigation buoys, and a ceramic body currently magnetized to a socket beside Noor's desk. The body was shaped like a narrow white figure with four arms folded against its torso and a head without a face. A ribbon of soft amber light moved under its chest plating whenever Ixo spoke.

"Vector?" Tovin asked.

"Crossing debris flow. Low thermal discipline. No active beacon. Profile suggests skiff or cut-down courier. It is using the grit shadow to approach the school ark."

Sela swore under her breath.

Noor said, "Possible pirate?"

"Possible hungry person with a bad idea," Tovin said. "Do not promote them yet."

The contact appeared on the display: a faint needle moving inside a cloud of old metal flakes. It was too small to threaten the *Mercy*. It could hurt the school ark. It could cut a hatch, steal medicine, grab hostages, or simply panic the convoy into scattering across a debris lane that killed without politics.

Captain Mer Anik's voice entered from the command deck. "Restraint gallery, I see it."

Mer always sounded as though she had a wrench in one hand and a casualty list in the other. She had commanded battlefield repair tenders before the ceasefire and had the habit of speaking to ships as if they were stubborn patients. The crew trusted her because she never pretended steel was noble.

"Options," she said.

Sela answered before Tovin could. "Point-defense grid could wake in local blind mode for three seconds. We don't need targeting core. We paint a wall in front of the skiff, scare it off, no hit."

Noor turned their head sharply. "That is a weapon system."

"It's a warning system if we don't aim it at anyone."

"The covenant does not contain a fear exception."

"The covenant was not written by people watching a school ark get cut open."

Tovin looked at Sela until she met his eyes. He did not raise his voice. A raised voice in the restraint gallery could become permission if people were desperate enough.

"Point-defense remains sealed."

Sela's jaw moved once. "Then give me something that works."

"Helm," Tovin said to the command channel, "bring our starboard shoulder between the contact and the ark. No burn plume across the convoy. Use mass."

Mer's reply came after less than a second. "That puts us in grit."

"Yes."

"Forward armor tolerates it. Starboard maintenance blisters are another question."

"Ask them politely."

"You heard the restraint officer," Mer said, and the channel filled with command deck motion.

The *Mercy of Kaln* began to turn.

It was too large for grace. The deck did not tilt; the gravity grid compensated with bureaucratic stubbornness. But the ship's frame answered in long groans that traveled through Tovin's boots and up his bones. On the forward display, the convoy slid relative to the battleship's nose. The school ark vanished behind the *Mercy*'s armored starboard curve. The unidentified skiff adjusted, trying to keep line of sight.

"Contact accelerating," Ixo said. "It appears annoyed."

"Record its emotional development," Noor muttered.

Sela's fingers hovered over her dead board. The point-defense controls were not physically connected to live guns. Tovin knew that. She knew that. Everyone in the gallery knew the old circuits ended in slagged junctions, witness locks, and ceramic absence. But the board still carried labels: CLOSE SWARM, KINETIC DUST, NEEDLE INTERCEPT, LAST RING. Language was also a weapon if left where frightened hands could find it.

"Sela," Tovin said.

"I'm not touching it."

"Move your hands anyway."

She pulled them back, furious.

The skiff cut lower. Its pilot knew exactly how far the *Mercy* could turn without scraping the densest part of the debris front. A narrow transmission flashed on open band.

OLD HULL, MOVE ASIDE. WE ONLY WANT THE ARK.

The words appeared on the display, plain and stupid.

Someone on the command deck laughed once in disbelief. Then nobody did.

Mer said, "I can roll five degrees and give them our old broadside. Might frighten them."

"No," Tovin said.

"It is not a shot."

"It is a threat shaped like a memory."

"Tovin."

He heard the warning in her voice: not disagreement, not yet, but a captain reminding him that doctrine did not bleed when choices went wrong.

"If we use the old broadside silhouette," he said, "the convoy sees a warship pretending. The skiff sees the same. Everyone learns the guns are still our argument, even dead. Keep our shoulder between them and the ark. No theater."

For three seconds there was only the vibration of the turn and the soft click of Noor's recorder.

Then Mer said, "No theater. Helm, continue shoulder block. Engineering, brace starboard blisters."

Sela's screen lit red. "Grit contact in twelve. Eleven."

The *Mercy* met the debris weather.

It sounded like rain becoming knives.

Across the starboard side, old hull armor took the first sheet with a long grinding hiss. Then the maintenance blisters began to complain. Tovin watched damage icons open like red flowers along the ship's shoulder: antenna skin, inspection blister twelve, old sensor blister fourteen, external ladder section, paint nobody could afford to replace. The school ark remained hidden behind them, tucked in the shadow of a ship built by dead admirals and repurposed by people who still had to make it useful.

"Contact is veering," Ixo said.

The skiff tried to dive below the *Mercy*'s shoulder. Mer anticipated it. The old battleship slid downlane with a heavy patience that made Tovin think of a door closing in slow motion. No flare. No turret. No warning shot. Just mass between harm and the fragile.

The skiff's pilot sent another message.

YOU CANNOT BLOCK EVERYTHING.

Tovin read it twice.

"They're right," Sela said quietly.

"Yes," he said.

That was the part recruits hated. That was the part the covenant did not carve into glass because no one would have signed it if it had. A ship that would not fire could not block everything. Restraint did not make innocents safe. It only refused to make danger simpler by giving it a barrel.

"Convoy channel," Tovin said.

Noor opened it.

Tovin let his voice go out to the ark, the barges, the tug, the haulers, and the desperate skiff trying to decide whether the day had given it permission to become a story people cursed.

"This is Tovin Rees, restraint officer aboard *Mercy of Kaln*. Unidentified skiff, you are approaching civilian hulls inside a debris lane. We will not fire on you. We will not move aside. If you need fuel, food, pressure cloth, or medicine, break pursuit and transmit need. If you attempt boarding, we will continue to interpose hull and record every action for Belt arbitration. You are not yet our enemy. Do not work harder to become one."

Sela looked at him as if he had offered tea to a knife.

The skiff held course.

"Eight seconds to ark line reacquisition if current geometry continues," Ixo said.

Mer said, "Tovin."

He knew what she was asking. Not for guns. Not exactly. For permission to use the *Mercy*'s old electronic warfare throat, a burst shriek that could blind a skiff without burning it. The covenant classified it as a dual-use defensive system. In a hearing, they might win. In a frightened lane, the difference between lawful and escalating would be measured by who told the story first.

"Prepare wideband fog," he said. Sela inhaled sharply. "No intrusion. No neural hazard. Navigation confusion only, lowest effective power, Noor witnessing."

Noor's fingers moved. "Witnessing. This is covenant gray."

"Most living happens there."

The skiff answered before Sela could execute.

FUEL. TWO HOURS. SICK ABOARD.

The message arrived without punctuation, then repeated in a different format as if the sender feared dignity would cost them help.

FUEL SICK ABOARD PLEASE.

The gallery changed temperature without the air moving.

Sela stared at the words. Her anger did not disappear; it lost its target and became something more painful.

Mer said, "Trap?"

"Probably," Noor said.

Ixo's amber chest light pulsed. "Possibly. Their thermal output is consistent with a failing bottle. Life-sign scan is unreliable through grit. I cannot confirm sickness."

Tovin watched the skiff's needle tremble inside the debris flow. He thought of every tactical lesson that had taught him to punish hesitation. He thought of the ceasefire moon at Pel Sar, where he had followed a lawful command and watched a habitat ring spend twelve hours venting children because no one wanted to be the officer who broke formation first. He thought of how easily memory put on a uniform and called itself wisdom.

"Captain," he said, "we can push a fuel pod on tether. No docking. Medical packet in the same pod. We keep hull interposed until they break away."

"And if they use the pod as cover?"

"Then we adapt without calling our fear prophecy."

Mer exhaled. "I hate when you get quotable."

"I will file a defect report."

Sela was already working. Not the dead board now, but the live engineering console. "Fuel pod three has enough for a skiff that size. Medical locker can spare fever tabs, seal foam, broad-spectrum infection dust, two pressure splints."

"Add water," Noor said.

Sela glanced over.

Noor did not look up from the legal recorder. "If we are being fools, we can be complete fools."

The *Mercy* deployed the pod from a belly cradle that had once launched mine-eaters. The cradle opened reluctantly, offended by gentler work. A small cylinder dropped into the lane on a smart tether, its beacon blinking blue-white through the metallic rain. The skiff altered course toward it. For one breath, Tovin believed the pilot would ram through, use the pod as excuse, cut for the ark.

The skiff latched the pod instead.

It hung there, ugly and silent, while fuel transferred and a medical canister crossed the lock. Then it sent one more message.

WE WERE TOLD YOU WOULD MOVE IF CHILDREN WERE NAMED.

Noor's head lifted.

Mer's voice hardened. "Told by who?"

The skiff released the pod and fled into the upper grit before answering. Its beacon remained dark. Its hull vanished one pixel at a time until even Ixo stopped tracking it.

No reply came.

The convoy continued through Cinderwake Lane.

Damage control teams patched the starboard blisters. The school ark sent a thank-you recorded by eighty-seven children who had been ordered to sound calm and mostly failed. *Little Argument* filed a complaint that the *Mercy*'s maneuver had shed paint across its forward intake, then appended a blessing in a dialect Tovin did not know. Sela pretended not to wipe her eyes when the ark's teacher read the children's names over the channel so the old ship would know who it had blocked for.

Two hours later, Cinderwake thinned. Stars returned in hard points. The Brothers fell behind them, carrying their war graves and old secrets.

Tovin remained in the restraint gallery after watch change, entering the incident into the covenant log. Noor stayed with him because legal witnesses were like guilt: useful, unwelcome, and difficult to dismiss.

"You made the right call," Noor said.

Tovin signed the fuel-pod entry. "Which one?"

"All of them, I think."

"That is statistically unlikely."

Noor leaned back. The recorder above their shoulder dimmed to privacy amber. "You know what the skiff's last message means."

"It means someone studied our procedures."

"More than procedures. They knew a pressure point."

Tovin looked through the dark weapon-status columns to the blank plates where targeting crews had once sat. "Everyone knows a ship with a hospital deck cares about children."

"No. They knew we would move if children were named. That is specific. That is doctrine shaped into bait."

The etched covenant sentence reflected faintly in the glass between them.

NO WEAPON MAY WAKE WITHOUT PUBLIC WITNESS.

"Then we document," Tovin said. "We send the pattern to Buoy Court. We ask who benefits from making restraint predictable."

Noor's expression tightened in the way it did when law offered a road too slow for the fire already moving. "And if they answer before the court does?"

Before Tovin could reply, the shipwide alert chimed once.

Not danger. Not collision. Not breach.

Legal priority.

The forward display shifted from the quiet lane map to Captain Mer Anik's face. She was on the command deck, gray hair tied back, eyes fixed on something outside the frame. Behind her, voices overlapped with the controlled panic of people trying to keep panic professional.

"Restraint gallery," she said, "we have a Compact emergency packet from Avar Break."

Noor stood.

Tovin felt the deck tremor again, though the ship had steadied.

Mer swallowed once. "A fuel convoy is gone. The packet names us as attacker."

For a moment the old battleship seemed to become perfectly still around him, as if every dead gun in its hull had heard its name called from a grave.

Sela's voice entered from engineering, small and fierce. "That's impossible."

Ixo's amber light brightened beside the witness desk.

Noor said, "Captain, are we under warrant?"

Mer looked away from the camera, listened to someone, then looked back.

"Not yet," she said. "But the packet is public."

On the display beside her face, casualty estimates began to scroll upward from numbers into accusation.

Tovin read them. He made himself read every line. Fuel tender *Mara's Due*, lost. Tug *Olan Thread*, lost. Barge *Civic Weight*, vented and burning. Survivors unknown. Attack signature: heavy hull. Electronic throat: Kaln-class. Acceleration ghost: consistent with *Mercy of Kaln*. Legal confidence: sufficient for emergency pursuit.

The ship that would not fire had been seen firing.

Noor whispered, "Someone has made us a warship again."

Tovin looked at the covenant sentence until the letters blurred. Then he folded his hands behind his back, because if he touched the rail he might feel the ship's patience become fear, and fear was the first system every war tried to wake.

Chapter 2: Admiral Under Warrant

Admiral Jaya Soth had spent the morning deciding which disaster was allowed to become worse.

The Ash Belt Pursuit Group did not call its command room a bridge. Bridges belonged to ships that went somewhere with a single will. The flag center aboard *Austere Bell* was a court, a weather office, a family argument, and a machine for turning partial facts into orders that would later be judged by people with chairs, sleep, and clean water.

Jaya stood at the center table while three emergencies circled her in separate panes.

Pane one showed Kordo Spindle, a mining station that had lost rotation trim after an ore tug struck its counterweight tower. Its outer decks now lived under a slow, nauseating wobble. If the wobble grew, water would climb walls, loose tools would become bullets, and eight thousand miners would learn whether their pressure doors had been maintained honestly. Kordo's station chief demanded immediate tug priority.

Pane two showed the grain barge *Hearth Ledger* drifting cold beside a failed navigation buoy. Four settlements had already filed claims against its cargo. None owned it entirely. All needed it urgently. Two had militia skiffs pacing each other across the barge's docking shadow, each insisting the other was seconds from piracy.

Pane three showed a quarantine flare from Tamas Redoubt, where a fungal lung infection had escaped a cheap med printer's filter discipline. Tamas wanted the pursuit group to escort a hospital packet through a lane currently marked unsafe because of debris from an old railgun platform. The packet might save four hundred people. The lane might kill the packet and the escort with equal indifference.

Behind those panes, quieter but not less dangerous, twelve station councils waited for Jaya to answer messages they had flagged as urgent because grief and politics had taught everyone the same trick.

Jaya was fifty-six years old. Her hair had gone iron at the temples and white above one ear where a decompression scar crossed her scalp. She had commanded ships during the last Fracture ceasefire and spent every year since learning that peace did not simplify command. It only made the consequences less honest. In war, people expected admirals to choose who might die. In peace, they expected the same choice without the courtesy of admitting it.

"Kordo first," said Lieutenant Arin Cho from the evidence rail.

Arin was thirty, exact, handsome in a severe way, and too good at hiding fear under competence. He had never fired on a crewed hull. Jaya considered that one of his qualifications and one of his risks.

"If Kordo loses trim," he continued, "we inherit a station-scale casualty event. Tug priority there prevents the largest loss."

"Assuming the wobble forecast is honest," said Commander Pellis Ren, Jaya's salvage liaison. "Kordo inflated last quarter's fracture estimates to jump a repair queue."

On pane one, Kordo's station chief shouted silently while the audio remained muted by Jaya's hand. His face was red, his collar open, his background filled with citizens strapped into a council room that kept leaning half a degree too far.

"Noted," Jaya said. "Does the physics inflate?"

Pellis rubbed both eyes. "No. The wobble is real."

"Then send tug *Moral Arithmetic* to Kordo."

Arin entered the order.

"Hearth Ledger next," Jaya said. "No armed docking."

"Both militias claim lawful distress attachment," Arin said. "Barge owner is unreachable. Buoy notary is dead. If we deny docking, food spoils."

"If we permit docking, station militias learn to race each other to every cold barge with a hungry child painted on the hull."

Pellis said, "We have one inspection cutter within eight hours."

"Send it. Until then, both militias back off two hundred kilometers."

"They will ask what compels them."

Jaya looked at the pane where the two skiffs held position like knives pretending to be utensils. "Tell them *Austere Bell* will publish the engine signatures of any vessel that closes before inspection. Not fire. Publish. Let their own creditors, cousins, and prayer groups ask why they were first to a contested hatch."

Arin glanced at her. "Public shame as interdiction."

"It has lower fuel cost than missiles."

Pellis smiled without joy and drafted the notice.

That left Tamas Redoubt. The quarantine flare pulsed a color Jaya had always disliked: violet edged with white, the color of human lungs becoming a jurisdictional question.

"Hospital packet?" Arin asked.

Jaya opened the debris forecast. The unsafe lane glittered with old projectiles, slag beads, and two reactor stones that had never cooled enough for comfort. "What escort is close?"

"The *Halberd Mercy*," Arin said, then corrected himself before Jaya could. "Civilian name now *Mercy of Kaln*. Demilitarized hulk. Currently shepherding a school convoy through Cinderwake."

The name sat between them.

Jaya knew the ship. Everyone in the Ash Belt command structure knew the ship, though half of what they knew was wrong. *Mercy of Kaln* had been *Kaln's Answer* once, a blockade breaker with a kill ledger nobody on its present crew liked to recite. After the ceasefire, three courts had argued for scrapping it. Four refugee councils had argued for conversion. The councils won because the Belt needed hospital volume more than it needed symbolic cleanliness.

The ship's covenant file appeared under Arin's fingers. Jaya did not need to read the summary. Main guns slagged. Magazines sealed. Targeting core excised. Electronic warfare restricted. Weapon galleries maintained as witness spaces. Civilian command. Restraint officer aboard: Tovin Rees.

That name did not sit between them. It came closer.

Jaya had last seen Tovin on Pel Sar's dead inner ring, sixteen years earlier, when both of them were younger and neither had yet learned how long a lawful order could keep echoing. He had been her tactical aide then. He had followed her commands without flattery, challenged bad assumptions without theater, and stood beside her in a hearing where every officer tried to explain why no one had broken formation fast enough to save the venting habitat.

Tovin had left the fleet six months later. Jaya had stayed and made herself useful to the peace.

"The *Mercy* cannot escort through railgun debris," Arin said. "No live point defense. No legal defensive burst without witness delay. It is a hospital hull, not a pursuit asset."

"It is a mass shield with engines and repair capacity."

"It is also a political argument with a drive plume."

"Most ships are."

Pellis said, "If you put Tamas packet behind the *Mercy*, some councils will claim we are normalizing converted warships in emergency lanes."

"We are already normalizing death by committee." Jaya magnified the Tamas flare. "Send hospital packet by Cinderwake extension after the *Mercy* clears its convoy. Ask Captain Anik if she can transfer pressure tents and infection filters to the packet without delaying the school ark."

Arin hesitated.

Jaya looked at him. "Say it."

"If we rely on a demilitarized hulk in a medical emergency, then when one of those hulks fails its covenant, we will have trained the Belt to trust a thing we cannot control."

"We cannot control hunger either. We still route grain."

"Grain does not remember being a battleship."

Jaya accepted the line because it was good and because good lines were often how fear dressed for staff work. "No. People remember for it."

She authorized the Tamas order.

For seven minutes, the room moved with a rhythm that could almost be mistaken for competence. Kordo received tug priority and began complaining about the assigned tug's age. The militias around *Hearth Ledger* both denied intending piracy while backing off exactly one hundred ninety-nine kilometers, which Pellis called Belt compliance. Tamas Redoubt sent a message that contained gratitude, accusation, and a request for twice as many filters in the same paragraph. Jaya drank half a bulb of stale tea and let herself imagine the day might become merely difficult.

Then the Avar Break packet arrived.

It did not arrive dramatically. No alarm sounded. The flag center did not darken. One of the junior evidence officers made a small noise, the kind people made when their body understood before doctrine did. A new pane opened over the center table and began populating itself with authenticated fragments.

Fuel tender *Mara's Due*: lost.

Tug *Olan Thread*: lost.

Barge *Civic Weight*: venting, thermal bloom consistent with internal burn.

Escort drone chain: missing.

Survivors: unknown.

Attack profile: heavy hull, Kaln-class echo.

Electronic throat: Kaln-class restricted band.

Acceleration ghost: consistent with *Mercy of Kaln*.

Legal confidence: sufficient for emergency pursuit.

Jaya read it once as an admiral. Then she read it again as someone who knew Tovin Rees.

"Source integrity?" she asked.

The junior evidence officer found his voice. "Quantum notary chain intact from Avar Break buoy three. Redundant station mirrors agree within accepted drift. Packet was public before command receipt."

That was bad. Public meant grief had already outrun context. Public meant every station with dead kin or hungry tanks would form an opinion before Jaya formed an order. Public meant if she hesitated, hesitation itself would be treated as evidence.

Arin's face had gone still. "The *Mercy* was in Cinderwake."

"Confirmed?"

"We have their convoy beacon track. But Cinderwake shadow corrupts precision. A Kaln-class hull could mask a deviation."

Pellis said, "No. Not with a school ark behind it."

Arin looked at him. "Are you making a technical statement or a moral one?"

Pellis had no answer ready.

Jaya enlarged the attack profile. It was too clean. That was her first private thought. The packet laid itself out like a prosecutor who had never lost a case: hull mass, band signature, acceleration echo, legal confidence. Real events usually limped. They contradicted themselves. They arrived stained by sensor lag, bad maintenance, and witnesses remembering the shape of fear. This packet had posture.

Posture did not make it false.

The dead did not care whether evidence looked elegant.

"Casualty estimate?" she asked.

"Initial range one hundred forty to two hundred thirty," said the evidence officer. "May rise."

Nobody spoke for a moment. The Ash Belt turned in the table display, indifferent and crowded.

A priority request struck the room from Halvyr Dock. Then another from Varo Anchorage. Then five more. The message headers stacked like thrown stones.

DECLARE MERCY HOSTILE.

SEIZE ALL DEMILITARIZED HULKS.

WHERE WAS PURSUIT GROUP?

OUR CHILDREN WERE IN CINDERWAKE.

NO MORE HOSPITAL GUNSHIPS.

Arin read them without blinking. "We need to act before local militias do."

"Yes."

"Emergency pursuit warrant?"

"Not yet."

That made the room look at her.

Jaya let them. Command was partly the art of allowing silence to accuse you without answering too soon.

"We have a public packet sufficient for pursuit," Arin said carefully.

"Sufficient is not the same as complete."

"If the *Mercy* runs, every minute gives it more room."

"If the *Mercy* is framed, every minute of sloppy pursuit helps the frame."

Pellis leaned over the table. "Admiral, if local militias reach them first, covenant law becomes vacuum decoration. Those people will shoot at anything that looks like old armor."

"I know."

"Then issue."

Jaya opened the *Mercy* covenant file again. Tovin's service record sat inside it under restraint certification. She closed the personal pane before anyone could see how long she looked.

"Draft a pursuit warrant," she said. "Language: locate, shadow, communicate, preserve evidence, prevent docking at unverified ports, protect civilian convoy elements. No destructive fire without my direct order."

Arin's shoulders loosened by one degree. "Disabling authority?"

"Conditional."

"On?"

"Clear active threat, attempted boarding of another civilian hull, or confirmed weapon-system activation."

"If the packet is true, they have already activated."

"The packet is an accusation about the past. I am commanding the next hour."

Arin entered the draft, but the dissatisfaction in his hands was visible. "The councils will call this weak."

"The councils are not wrong to be afraid."

"That is not the same as being right."

"No," Jaya said. "It is the reason being right will not be enough."

Before the warrant could be sealed, Commodore Renn Valech requested live command access.

Pellis muttered, "Of course he did."

Valech appeared in a pane without waiting for Jaya's invitation to feel voluntary. He was broad-faced, silver-haired, and carefully unadorned except for the small black pin of the Naval Reform Commission at his collar. His office behind him was plain in the way powerful rooms became plain after someone else paid for the expensive parts. On the wall hung a fragment of hull plating from a Fracture Wars civilian transport, its edges sealed under glass.

"Admiral Soth," he said. "I assume you have the Avar Break packet."

"We do."

"Then you understand the scale."

"We understand the deaths. Scale will require more than one packet."

Valech's eyes narrowed with professional regret. "This is not an academic evidence seminar. A demilitarized Kaln-class hull has been identified in a convoy massacre."

"The *Mercy of Kaln* has been identified by a packet. The ship has not been contacted."

"You distinguish too finely for people venting into vacuum."

The flag center became very quiet.

Jaya felt Arin watching her. Everyone watched admirals when grief offered them permission to become simpler.

"No, Commodore," she said. "I distinguish finely because people are venting into vacuum."

Valech accepted the rebuke with a small dip of his head, as if he had expected to pay that price and found it affordable. "My commission warned that converted battleships would become legal camouflage. The Belt cannot tell the difference between a hospital deck and a hidden weapons bay when both wear the same armor."

"The covenant inspections are public."

"Public to lawyers. Not to frightened stations."

"Then we educate frightened stations."

"While they bury convoy crews?"

There it was: the useful cruelty of a question shaped so that every answer sounded like indifference.

Jaya kept her voice level. "What are you requesting?"

"Immediate hostile classification pending boarding."

"Denied."

Arin's fingers stopped above his console.

Valech did not blink. "On what basis?"

"On the basis that hostile classification authorizes local militias, insurers, and frightened escort captains to treat every demilitarized hulk as a delayed enemy. We will pursue the *Mercy*. We will preserve evidence. We will prevent further harm. We will not convert a packet into a regional doctrine in the first ten minutes."

"Your restraint does you credit," Valech said, and made it sound like a diagnosis. "But if the *Mercy* fires again, the dead will not be impressed by the care you took with language."

"If we fire wrongly, neither will they."

Valech looked away from the camera, receiving a message. When he returned, his regret had sharpened. "Several councils are already drafting seizure orders against converted warships in their docks. Your narrow warrant may not hold them."

"Then send them my order and your support."

"My support depends on whether the order protects the Belt."

"It protects the law that keeps the Belt from becoming a shooting habit."

For the first time, Valech's expression showed something warmer than regret and colder than anger. "The Belt already has shooting habits, Admiral. Some of us remember why."

The pane closed.

Arin released a breath through his nose. "He will go around us."

"Probably."

"Should I broaden the warrant to preempt him?"

"No. We do not let another officer's politics write our first shot."

"It may not be our first shot if the *Mercy* resists."

Jaya looked at the display where the old battleship's last known convoy track moved through Cinderwake Lane, a pale line under interference. She imagined Tovin in some converted fire-control room, hands folded behind his back because he had always distrusted what fear did to the body before it reached the mind. She imagined, too, the broken fuel convoy at Avar Break, people whose names she did not know dying under a signature the packet insisted belonged to him.

Trust was not evidence.

Distrust was not evidence either.

"Seal the warrant," she said.

Arin sealed it.

The order went out across the Ash Belt with Jaya's name attached, and the moment it left *Austere Bell*, it stopped belonging to her. Stations would quote it selectively. Militias would test its edges. Valech would call it insufficient. The *Mercy*, if its crew still had sense, would hear the spaces between the words and know they were being hunted by someone trying not to become their executioner.

Jaya turned to the navigation officer. "Set pursuit vector for Cinderwake exit. Bring us close enough to talk before anyone else gets close enough to panic."

"Acceleration profile?"

"Hard but lawful."

Pellis gave a tired laugh. "That is not a number, Admiral."

"It is in the Ash Belt."

The *Austere Bell* began to burn.

As acceleration settled into the deck, Jaya stayed at the center table and watched the three earlier emergencies shrink into delegated boxes. Kordo still wobbled. *Hearth Ledger* still drifted under hungry claims. Tamas Redoubt still coughed behind its violet flare. None of them had become less real because a worse thing had arrived.

That was the Ash Belt's preferred form of cruelty. It never replaced one disaster with another. It stacked them until command became the study of which truth could be neglected the longest.

Arin stepped beside her. "Admiral."

"Yes."

"Do you think they did it?"

Jaya watched the pale line marked *Mercy of Kaln* approach the edge of Cinderwake's sensor shadow.

"I think someone wants that question to arrive before we do," she said.

Chapter 3: Hospital in a Gun Hull

The *Mercy of Kaln* had corridors wide enough for ammunition carts and too narrow for grief.

That was what Mother Kal said whenever visitors asked how a battleship could become a hospital. She said it while showing them pressure doors that had once isolated magazine fires and now separated fever wards from classrooms, while pointing to old shell hoists converted into patient lifts, while letting them hear the way children's songs sounded under armor meant to keep explosions in and vacuum out. She said it without softness, because softness invited people to mistake conversion for forgiveness.

On the morning after Cinderwake, before the Avar Break accusation reached every deck, Mother Kal stood in Ward Three with a chipped cup of broth in one hand and a list of names in the other.

"You are missing two blankets," she told the quartermaster.

The quartermaster was a broad man named Iren Sol who had once moved missile racks and now moved diapers, pressure patches, ration bricks, school slates, boot liners, and the kind of small luxuries people denied needing until a ship ran out of them. He looked at her with the patience of someone facing a priest, an auditor, and an aunt in the same body.

"I am missing forty-six blankets," Iren said. "You are entitled to two of the forty-six."

"Ward Three has lung cases."

"Ward Two has burn cases."

"Burn cases run hot."

"That is not a heating plan."

Mother Kal sipped her broth. She was seventy-nine, small, brown-skinned, and wrapped in a gray shawl made from pressure-cloth scraps. A shrapnel scar pulled the corner of her mouth down even when she was amused, which made comfort from her feel like a verdict. During the war, she had survived a blockade the ship had helped enforce under its old name. After demilitarization, she had come aboard as a witness for the refugee councils and refused to leave.

"Give me one blanket," she said. "And tell me who stole the other."

Iren sighed. "Nobody stole anything."

"Then the ship ate it. I will ask the ship."

"Please do not start another rumor that the hull consumes textiles."

"The hull consumed cities," Mother Kal said. "It can answer for socks."

A child in the nearest bed giggled, then coughed until the ward nurse crossed over and lifted an oxygen petal to his mouth. The petal opened like a translucent flower, misting medicine with each breath. Above the bed, a strip of old tactical rail had been polished and repurposed as a charm bar. Paper birds, station tokens, a cracked toy tug, and a tiny brass screw hung from it on colored thread.

The child was from the school ark. His name was Pem. He had not been injured by the skiff, not exactly. He had frightened himself into an asthma spiral while listening to grit hit the *Mercy*'s armor. His teacher had apologized three times for bringing him aboard.

Mother Kal had told the teacher that apologies were for broken promises, not lungs.

Across Ward Three, six Cinderwake passengers slept in emergency hammocks strung between brackets that had once held acceleration restraints for gun crews. The brackets still had numbers stenciled over them: FIRE TEAM EIGHT, FIRE TEAM NINE, FIRE TEAM TEN. Someone had painted flowers around the words. Someone else had scratched the flowers away. The argument had been going on in layers for years.

Tovin entered with Noor Venn at his side and the Avar Break packet in a sealed slate under his arm.

Mother Kal saw the slate before she saw his face.

"Bad news walks stiff," she said.

Tovin stopped beside the ward threshold. He had changed out of watch blacks into a plain gray jacket with the covenant stripe at the cuff, but he still looked like a man standing inside an order he did not trust. Noor carried two recorders and a legal satchel. Their eyes had the raw brightness of someone who had read too much too quickly.

"We need Captain Mer, Ixo, Sela, and all deck leads in the old mess," Tovin said. "Civilian witnesses too."

Iren's expression hardened. "Are we under attack?"

"Not yet."

Mother Kal set down her broth. "That is an answer people give when the attack has learned paperwork."

Noor looked at her. "Avar Break convoy is gone. Public packet names us."

The ward changed shape around the sentence. No one moved much. That was worse than panic. Panic knew what it wanted. Stillness waited for the world to explain whether it had become an enemy.

The nurse holding Pem's oxygen petal lowered her hand. "Names us how?"

"As attacker," Noor said.

Pem's mother, asleep in a chair beside him, woke at the word attacker. She looked first at her son, then at the walls, as if the old guns might have grown back while she slept.

Iren said, "Our guns are slag."

"The packet cites heavy hull signature, restricted band, acceleration ghost," Tovin said.

"Ghosts do not kill convoys."

"People use ghosts to aim living hands."

Mother Kal walked closer. She smelled faintly of broth, antiseptic, and machine oil. "How many dead?"

"Estimate begins at one hundred forty."

The number entered the ward and found space in every bed.

Pem whispered, "Did we do it?"

His mother flinched. The nurse closed her eyes.

Tovin crouched beside the bed, slow enough not to crowd him. "No."

Noor's recorder light brightened. Mother Kal watched Tovin, not the child.

Pem's fingers tightened on his blanket. "How do you know?"

It would have been easy to say because we are good. It would have been useful to say because our guns are dead. It would have been satisfying to say because someone is lying. Tovin had spent too much of his life watching useful sentences become bad evidence.

"Because I was on watch," he said. "Because the ship's logs say where we were. Because the seals on the weapons were witnessed. Because people here will check me. That is how we know things aboard this ship."

Pem considered that. "What if the people checking are scared?"

"Then we write down the fear too."

Mother Kal gave a small nod, which from her was almost applause.

The old mess had once been an ammunition staging bay. The ceiling still carried loading rails, though the hooks now held water lines, netted vegetable trays, laundry bundles, and the paper lanterns the school ark children had made during the Cinderwake delay. The lanterns were shaped like ships with exaggerated fins and no weapons at all. They bobbed in the ventilation current above tables bolted to deck plates that had been rated, in another life, for shell impact.

By the time Tovin arrived, the room was full.

Captain Mer Anik stood at the forward end with her sleeves rolled up, grease on one wrist, and the emergency packet displayed behind her. Ixo's ceramic body clung to the wall near a data socket. Sela Marr leaned against a mess table with her arms crossed so tightly her knuckles had gone pale. Noor took position at the witness side. Mother Kal entered last and made three younger passengers move so she could sit where she could see everyone and be seen by everyone.

The room held repair crew, ward nurses, cooks, school staff, refugee delegates, engine techs, three convoy witnesses, two mechanics from the oxygen still, a pair of quiet teenagers who had been born in transit and trusted no meeting that began with adults standing, and Iren Sol with a blanket ledger tucked under one arm like a defensive weapon.

It also held the ship's past.

Tovin could feel it in the armored ribs, the old deck numbers, the way every entrance had been designed for armed teams moving fast. The *Mercy* had been made to concentrate force. Now it concentrated people who needed force not to happen. Conversion had not erased the shape. It had asked ordinary life to keep walking through it until the shape learned humility.

Mer did not waste time.

"Avar Break fuel convoy is destroyed," she said. "Public packet identifies us as attacker. We have not been boarded, hailed under warrant, or fired upon. That will change. Until then, this ship remains under civilian rescue command and demilitarization covenant. No one opens weapon seals. No one purges logs. No one edits private recordings. No one assaults anyone accused of being unlucky enough to know law."

Several people looked at Noor.

Noor raised one hand. "Thank you, Captain."

"Do we surrender?" asked one of the convoy witnesses, a woman from *Little Argument* with a bandaged forehead.

"To whom?" Mer asked.

"The Compact. Pursuit Group. Whoever answers first."

Sela pushed off the table. "If we surrender to the first angry dock, we disappear into custody before our logs get copied."

"If we run," said a ward nurse, "we look guilty."

Tovin said, "We are already being made to look guilty. Our task is to remain legible."

"Legible people still get shot," the nurse said.

"Yes."

That answer angered more people than reassurance would have. It also settled the room by a degree. The *Mercy*'s crew had learned to distrust comfort that arrived without cost.

Ixo spoke from the wall. "The packet's public fragments contain anomalies."

Sela turned. "You found proof?"

"I found invitation. Proof requires full packet, raw buoy memory, and comparison with local command roots."

"What kind of anomaly?" Noor asked.

"The restricted band signature resembles a Kaln-class electronic throat, but it is too regular. Living equipment ages. Replacement ceramics drift. Improvised repairs introduce accent. The packet's throat has no accent."

Iren frowned. "Machines have accents?"

"Everything maintained by hands has accent," Ixo said. "This packet speaks like a museum pretending to cough."

Mother Kal said, "I dislike it when the machine makes sense."

"I am frequently sensible," Ixo said.

"I said dislike, not surprise."

A few people laughed. The laugh was short and brittle, but it belonged to them, and for that reason Tovin was grateful.

Mer touched the display. The packet magnified into evidence layers most of the room could not read. "We copy logs to three witness bundles. Noor, civilian chain. Ixo, provenance chain. Sela, engineering chain."

Sela's mouth tightened. "Engineering logs include seal architecture."

"Yes."

"If we broadcast too much, someone learns how to fake us better."

"If we broadcast too little, someone already has."

Tovin watched Sela absorb that. She had grown up in an Ash Belt pressure orphanage where every safety rule was also a dare to the universe to find the gap. She trusted tools because tools failed in specific ways. Law failed conversationally, which she hated.

"Fine," she said. "But I choose the redactions."

Noor said, "You propose redactions. Witnesses approve."

"This is why engineers avoid lawyers."

"Lawyers also avoid lawyers," Noor said. "It is our strongest common ground."

The old mess door opened, and a teacher from the school ark slipped in with three children behind her. Mer started to object, then saw the teacher's face and stopped.

"They heard," the teacher said. "The whole ark will hear in minutes. I would rather they hear from the people carrying them."

The children stood under the paper lanterns they had made. One was Pem, oxygen petal folded against his chest now. Another girl held a slate so tightly the case bent. The third child, older than the others, stared at the packet display with a hatred too practiced for his age.

Mer crouched so she was not speaking down from a captain's height. "This meeting is frightening."

The older child said, "So is not knowing."

Mother Kal murmured, "Correct."

Mer looked at Tovin.

He did not want the children there. He did not want them to learn the habits of accusation so young. But the *Mercy* was a hospital in a gun hull, not a cradle outside history. The ship had promised public witness. Public included those adults most wanted to protect from public things.

"They stay if their teacher agrees," Tovin said. "No tactical screens beyond what is already public."

The teacher nodded once.

The older child pointed at the display. "Can they make you use the guns?"

"No," Tovin said.

Sela looked away.

The child saw it. "She thinks yes."

Sela's face flushed. "I think people can make you need things you swore off."

"Sela," Mer warned.

"No," Tovin said. "Answer him."

Sela looked at the child, then at the lanterns overhead. "The guns are dead. Some other systems are not. Some systems can hurt people if we call them defense loudly enough. That is why we fight about words before switches."

The child considered her with the severity only children and judges could afford. "Then fight good."

"Well," Noor said.

"What?"

"Fight well."

The child glared. "You fight your way."

Mother Kal's laugh cracked through the room, and this time the laugh lasted longer.

Then the pursuit warrant arrived.

It entered through the ship's legal channel, carried by authenticated Compact routing and Admiral Jaya Soth's command seal. Noor caught it first. Their face changed, not with fear exactly, but with recognition: the difference between weather and named weather.

"Captain," Noor said.

Mer turned from the children. The warrant unfolded on the display beside the Avar Break packet.

LOCATE. SHADOW. COMMUNICATE. PRESERVE EVIDENCE. PREVENT UNVERIFIED DOCKING. PROTECT CIVILIAN CONVOY ELEMENTS.

NO DESTRUCTIVE FIRE WITHOUT DIRECT FLAG AUTHORITY.

CONDITIONAL DISABLING AUTHORITY UPON ACTIVE THREAT, ATTEMPTED BOARDING OF CIVILIAN HULL, OR CONFIRMED WEAPON-SYSTEM ACTIVATION.

SIGNED: ADMIRAL JAYA SOTH, ASH BELT PURSUIT GROUP.

The room read it in pieces, according to fear.

"They are hunting us," said the ward nurse.

"They are not calling us hostile," Noor said.

"That can change," Sela said.

Mer stared at the signature. "Jaya Soth."

Mother Kal looked at Tovin. "You know that name."

Everyone who had not been looking at him began looking.

Tovin kept his hands still. "I served under her."

The statement moved through the room almost as dangerously as the accusation had.

Iren said, "Can we trust her?"

Tovin thought of Pel Sar, of a hearing room full of officers trying to survive the truth by distributing it thinly, of Jaya Soth refusing to blame a dead junior navigator for a formation order she had given. He thought of her not breaking formation fast enough. He thought of himself not breaking it either.

"No," he said.

Mer's eyebrows rose.

Tovin continued. "We can trust that she will try to remain lawful. That is not the same as trusting her to save us. We prepare evidence as if she is fair and maneuvers as if she is not enough."

Mother Kal pointed one crooked finger at him. "That is the first useful sentence today."

The warrant chimed again as attached instructions opened. The *Mercy* was ordered to maintain current convoy protection, transmit complete logs, accept shadowing, and avoid docking except under verified medical necessity. It was also ordered not to alter weapon seals, not to use restricted bands, and not to enter station weapons range without clearance.

Sela read the last line twice.

"Station weapons range," she said. "So if frightened docks close their doors, we stay in open lane with refugees and a broken oxygen still."

"Unless medical necessity," Noor said.

"Medical necessity according to who?"

"That is a future argument."

"I hate future arguments."

"That is because they become present."

Mer raised her hand before the room could split into smaller fears. "Our immediate work: keep convoy alive, preserve logs, prepare medical transfer for Tamas if still possible, and answer Admiral Soth before someone else answers for us."

"What do we say?" asked the teacher.

Mer looked at Tovin.

He wished she had not. He wished the ship could speak in engine telemetry and seal hashes and quiet proof without a human voice carrying shame between them. But ships did not defend themselves. People did, and people made every defense suspect by needing to survive.

"We say we received the warrant," Tovin said. "We deny attack. We preserve evidence. We request raw Avar Break buoy memory. We invite shadow at safe distance. We refuse boarding by unverified authority. We ask for survivor names as they are confirmed."

The room went silent at the last sentence.

The convoy witness from *Little Argument* said, "Why names?"

"Because if we only say we are innocent, the dead become obstacles to our innocence," Tovin said. "They are not."

Mother Kal closed her eyes for a moment. When she opened them, the old scar at her mouth had tightened. "Good."

Ixo's amber light pulsed brighter. "I recommend one addition."

"Say it," Mer said.

"Request that any machine-person or bounded archive witness associated with Avar Break be preserved from purge, quarantine deletion, or insurer custody. If this frame uses old command roots, nonhuman memory may be targeted first."

Noor was already writing. "Agreed."

Sela looked at Ixo. "You think someone will kill witnesses that are not bodies."

"I think many people define murder by mess," Ixo said. "It makes them careless with cleaner deaths."

The children did not understand all of that. The adults did, which was worse.

Mer authorized the reply.

As it transmitted, the *Mercy*'s ordinary noises returned by degrees: water moving through old ammunition lifts, a child coughing, a repair cart squealing down the corridor, someone in the kitchen swearing at a protein mill, hull plates ticking as Cinderwake dust cooled in their seams. The sounds did not comfort Tovin. They reminded him what an accusation had to pass through before it became a war: wards, blankets, teachers, pipes, witness logs, children correcting lawyers, engineers angry enough to keep seals honest.

The ship was not innocent because it carried civilians. Civilians had been carried by terrible ships before.

The ship was not harmless because its guns were dead. Harm had more than one architecture.

But as Tovin stood in the old ammunition bay beneath paper lanterns made by children who had chosen to draw no weapons, he understood again why the covenant could not be only a lock. A lock protected the past from waking. It did not teach the present what to do with its hands.

The first pursuit ping touched the edge of the *Mercy*'s sensor field twenty minutes later.

Sela saw it from the wall display. "Fast mover. Compact profile. Too clean for militia."

Noor checked the warrant channel. "Austere Bell?"

Ixo answered. "Not yet. Smaller. Probe drone under pursuit authority."

Mer's jaw set. "They are close enough to watch."

The older child under the lanterns whispered, "Are they close enough to shoot?"

No one answered quickly enough.

Mother Kal rose from her chair and placed the missing blanket, somehow acquired, over the child's shoulders.

"Not yet," she said.

Tovin looked at the sensor ping, then at the room built from an ammunition bay, then at the people waiting to see whether not yet could become no.

"Transmit our logs," he said. "All three chains. Let them see the hospital before they see the hull."

Chapter 4: Avar Break Burns Cold

Ressa Vohn died without learning which ship had killed her convoy.

For forty-six minutes before that, she believed the day would be annoying rather than historic. The fuel tender *Mara's Due* had taken on bad spin from a lazy coupling. Tug *Olan Thread* kept drifting half a degree below assigned plane. Barge *Civic Weight* insisted its thermal bloom was normal because its owner hated repair delays more than vacuum. The escort drone chain, leased cheap from an insurer who wrote sympathy into contracts and penalties into small print, reported green status every thirty seconds with the smugness of hardware that had never paid for its own replacement.

Ressa commanded the convoy because nobody else wanted to be blamed for fuel arriving late to Avar Break.

She stood in the tender's forward blister with one boot hooked under a restraint loop, drinking coffee that had been reheated so many times it tasted like a legal threat. Outside the blister, the cinder moon filled half the view. Avar Break was not beautiful unless a person had been raised to find utility holy. It was a burnt stone with mining scars, radiator farms, fuel bladders, and a population that measured mercy in pressure-hours. The moon's night side glowed with dock lamps and furnace veins. Its day side wore dust the color of old bone.

"Dock says we are late," said Vennic, her comms officer.

"Dock always says we are late."

"They say Redoubt shipments get priority if we miss transfer window."

"Tamas Redoubt has fungus in its lungs. We have fuel in our tanks. Everyone in the Belt has a reason to be first."

Vennic looked over his console. He was nineteen and had been aboard three months, long enough to know procedures and not long enough to stop expecting them to mean safety. "Should I answer?"

"Tell them we are delayed by physics and insulted by implication."

"In those words?"

"No, Vennic. In invoice language."

He began composing. Ressa let him. Young officers needed practice sounding calm at institutions that did not deserve calm. The blister's glass carried a thin overlay of traffic lines, docking estimates, debris tracks, and the green beads of their escort drones. Beyond those marks, Avar Break rotated slowly, indifferent to claims.

The convoy carried deuterium bottles, cracked ammonia for farms, emergency reactor salts, coolant foam, and three small sealed cylinders marked for hospital generators. It was not a military prize. It was not romantic plunder. It was the kind of cargo that made kitchens, wards, heaters, locks, and scrubbers continue being ordinary. That made it valuable enough to kill for.

"Drone three missed a ping," said Hale from navigation.

Ressa turned. "How many?"

"One."

"Interference?"

"Could be. We are in Break shadow."

On the overlay, drone three remained green. Its last known vector held steady near the upper flank of the convoy.

"Ask it a rude question," Ressa said.

Hale sent a diagnostic challenge. Drone three answered after two seconds with a maintenance hash, a temperature table, and an apology flag inserted by some programmer with too much personality.

"Green," Hale said.

"I hate green that has to be asked twice."

Vennic laughed softly. Then the tender's lights flickered.

Not a power loss. Not even a real dip. A tremor in the ship's attention, as if every system had looked toward the same door for half a second.

Ressa's coffee bulb squeezed hot bitterness across her glove.

"Report."

Hale frowned. "No fault."

"Comms?"

Vennic's face had changed. "I just received a priority identification packet from buoy three."

"For what?"

"Traffic authentication."

"We are authenticated."

"It is not asking us."

The blister overlay acquired a new mark beyond the escort chain: a pale outline, large, distant, and wrong.

KALN-CLASS HULL PROBABLE.

For a moment nobody spoke. Kaln-class did not mean one ship. It meant a lineage of old blockade breakers, assault carriers, and armored logistic brutes that had made whole stations change prayers during the Fracture Wars. Most were gone. Some were museum wrecks. A few had been converted under covenant into things the Belt argued about over meals.

Ressa knew one name before the overlay supplied it.

MERCY OF KALN: SIGNATURE CONSISTENT.

"That cannot be right," Hale said.

Vennic whispered, "They are in Cinderwake."

Ressa did not ask how he knew. Everyone knew where the famous hull was when rumor had fresh legs.

"Raw data," she said.

The overlay unfolded layers: mass shadow, restricted-band echo, acceleration ghost, and an electronic throat signature so clean it looked engraved. Ressa's first feeling was not fear. It was irritation. Real sensor returns were dirty. They wobbled, contradicted, shed caveats. This one arrived polished, wearing boots shined for inspection.

"This packet is too pretty," she said.

Hale looked relieved that she had named what he feared to say. "Spoof?"

"Everything is spoof until it kills us. Wake drone chain to yellow. Put Avar Dock on record. Ask buoy three for raw memory."

Vennic's hands moved.

The escort drones did not wake to yellow.

Their beads remained green for another three seconds. Then drone three vanished. Not red. Not gray. Gone, as if the overlay had been persuaded it had never existed.

"Drone three lost," Hale said.

"I can see that."

"No debris impact."

"I can see that too."

The first physical strike hit *Olan Thread*.

There was no flash at first, only a punctuation error in the tug's outline. One moment the little vessel dragged half a degree below assigned plane. The next, its forward tank folded inward and its drive plume bent sideways through its own hull. The tug's transponder transmitted the beginning of a complaint, then pressure turned the message into static.

Vennic made a sound Ressa would remember for the rest of her short life.

"All vessels scatter downplane!" Ressa shouted. "Cold thrust, no hot plume toward the barges. Avar Dock, convoy under attack. Buoy three, emergency record full spectrum. Drone chain to red."

The drones answered green.

The overlay still showed the pale Kaln-class outline at impossible distance.

MERCY OF KALN: ATTACK VECTOR CONFIRMED.

"No," Ressa said.

Not denial. Correction.

Something struck *Civic Weight*. The barge did not explode. It opened. Its flank split along a seam that should have held against dock collision, cargo shift, and ordinary human neglect. Fuel vapor glittered out in a flat silver sheet, beautiful for less than a second before ignition crawled through it in blue veins. The barge's owner had been wrong about the thermal bloom. The universe rarely missed a chance to make negligence part of a larger crime.

"Survivor pods?" Ressa asked.

Hale's voice cracked. "From *Olan* none. *Civic Weight* has pods trying. Two. No, one."

"Mark and transmit. Mark and transmit."

Vennic said, "Avar Dock wants attacker identification."

"Tell them we are busy being attacked."

"They want the packet repeated."

Ressa looked at him. "Do not repeat identification we cannot verify."

"Buoy three is already repeating."

On the blister glass, the packet cloned itself into public channels.

HEAVY HULL ATTACK.

KALN-CLASS ELECTRONIC THROAT.

MERCY OF KALN: LEGAL CONFIDENCE SUFFICIENT FOR EMERGENCY PURSUIT.

The words appeared before another ship died.

That was when Ressa became afraid.

Not when *Olan Thread* folded. Not when *Civic Weight* burned. Those were terror, yes, but they were physical and therefore honest. She became afraid when the accusation outran the event. Someone wanted the Belt to know the guilty name before the bodies stopped moving.

"Cut buoy three from our trust net," she said.

Hale stared. "It is our notary."

"Cut it."

He did.

For one breath, the overlay lost certainty. The Kaln-class outline smeared into three contradictory shadows. One was too large. One was too close. One was nowhere a ship could be without crossing the moon's mass. Behind the smear, a smaller signal flickered: a maintenance skiff, a mining lens, a dead drone using a live key, or a piece of old war code pretending to be all of them.

Then buoy three forced itself back into the chain.

The tender's recorders accepted it with obedient little tones.

"It overrode us," Hale said.

"Buoys do not override tender trust nets."

"This one did."

Vennic looked up. "Avar Dock is sealing. They think we brought hostile fire into approach."

"Of course they do."

Ressa strapped into the command frame. The annoying day had ended. The historic day had become too busy for anyone to name it properly.

"Manual record," she said. "All stations, local copy, no buoy authentication. Speak what you see."

Hale swallowed. "I see a false certainty packet. I see drone chain green against loss. I see *Olan Thread* destroyed by impact from starboard high, source not visually confirmed. I see *Civic Weight* venting and burning. I see buoy three reasserting trust against command cut."

Vennic's voice trembled but held. "I see public channels naming *Mercy of Kaln* before local verification. I see Avar Dock repeating. I see no direct image of attacker. I see restricted-band echo but no source resolution."

Ressa said, "I see someone making law faster than light."

The second strike hit *Mara's Due* under the forward bottle frame.

The tender was old but not fragile. It had hauled fuel through labor strikes, dock fires, tax seizures, and one militia inspection conducted by people too nervous to know which end of their rifles was political. Its frame took the hit with a roar that moved through Ressa's teeth. The blister glass starred but held. A pressure warning unfolded across half the console. Somewhere aft, metal screamed in a register that meant a tank had come loose and wanted to teach the rest of the ship about momentum.

"Bottle frame two breach," Hale said. "Reactor salts intact. Deuterium cradle damaged. We have fire in cold bay."

"Fire cannot be in cold bay."

"It is making an argument."

"Flood foam."

"Foam valves not responding."

Ressa reached for manual fire suppression. The lever resisted her for half a second, then came down with a crack. The ship shuddered as foam flooded the bay. The temperature graph fell. Pressure did not.

Vennic was crying now, silently, while he worked. "Avar Dock says abandon cargo."

"No."

"Captain."

"If we abandon cargo inside this vector, bottles drift into the dock approach and they shoot everything. We hold and bleed speed."

Hale said, "We cannot hold if the next hit opens bottle three."

"Then let us be grateful for the rich tactical variety of our problems."

It was a bad joke. Hale laughed once anyway, because crews gave commanders mercy when there was no other supply.

A new signal entered the blister overlay. Not the Kaln-class ghost. Not buoy three. A narrow, old command root riding under emergency authentication like a parasite under skin.

Ressa had seen that architecture once in a training archive from the Fracture Wars, in a lesson about why old military trust roots were supposed to be killed, salted, and mourned by nobody.

QUIET AUTHORITY.

The words did not appear on the screen. Her mind supplied them because fear had excellent memory.

"Local copy," she said. "I am adding command observation. Possible wartime authority root in packet structure."

Hale turned. "You are sure?"

"No. Recording."

"That accusation will get us buried if wrong."

"We are already being buried."

Vennic sent the local copy. For two seconds, the tender confirmed storage in three physical drives and one courier bead. Then drive two failed. Then the courier bead reported checksum correction. Then the local recorder labeled Ressa's observation as low-confidence emotional speech.

Ressa stared at the label.

"It is editing us," she said.

The third strike never landed.

That was the part later investigators would argue over, because absence left fewer fragments than impact. Something approached from starboard high, then veered when *Mara's Due* dumped fuel from the damaged cradle and lit it with a maintenance spark. The flame sheet spread between tender and attacker, a dirty blue curtain full of boiling droplets. For one instant, a shape moved behind it: too small for a battleship, too angular for a tug, carrying a signature much larger than its body.

Vennic saw it too. "That is not the *Mercy*."

"Say it on record."

"That is not the *Mercy*. Visual contact suggests small platform or masked drone. Not Kaln-class hull. Not Kaln-class hull."

The blister glass finally gave way.

It did not explode inward. It peeled, slow enough for Ressa to see the crack choose paths. Pressure took the room in pieces. Hale vanished behind a spray of console fragments and frozen coffee. Vennic's restraint caught him but his helmet seal had not been locked because the forward blister had not been on combat protocol, because this was a fuel convoy and not a war, because people could not live every routine minute dressed for catastrophe without becoming useless for ordinary work.

Ressa's suit collar snapped shut automatically. One of the old upgrades had been worth its invoice.

She hung in the command frame with blood freezing along her left sleeve and the moon turning beyond the broken blister. The overlay flickered, half dead, still repeating the packet.

MERCY OF KALN.

MERCY OF KALN.

MERCY OF KALN.

"Manual record," she said, though she did not know if anything heard. "Captain Ressa Vohn, fuel tender *Mara's Due*. Avar Break approach. Convoy attacked by unknown platform using false Kaln-class packet. Buoy three compromised. Drone chain compromised. Possible Quiet Authority root. Do not trust clean certainty. Do not trust..."

Her breath caught. Pain arrived late and enormous.

Outside, *Civic Weight* burned cold: blue flame fading as vapor spent itself, heat vanishing into vacuum, debris glittering like a festival for the dead. A survivor pod tumbled between fuel droplets. Ressa tried to mark it and discovered her right hand no longer answered.

She thought of Avar Break's dock workers waiting for fuel. She thought of Tamas Redoubt waiting for filters. She thought of the Belt's endless queue of need, each emergency certain that its urgency excused what it took from the next. She thought, absurdly, of Vennic asking whether to insult the dock in invoice language.

The overlay steadied one last time.

Attack profile: heavy hull.

Legal confidence: sufficient.

Mercy of Kaln.

The false certainty became the last light in the room.

Ressa used the only hand that remained to pull the manual beacon from her belt. It was a crude thing, independent of tender systems, meant for miners trapped in dust pockets and children lost between dock ribs. No legal packet. No elegant chain. Only a local scream.

She pressed it against the cracked recorder housing and held the transmit stud down.

"Not clean," she whispered. "Tell them it was not clean."

The beacon pulsed into debris, flame, compromised law, and nobody's immediate attention.

By the time the first rescue skiff from Avar Break broke dock seal, the public packet had already traveled farther than her voice.

By the time Jaya Soth read the casualty list, Ressa's beacon was tumbling cold beside the body of drone three.

By the time the *Mercy of Kaln* received the accusation, the accusation had learned how to sound more certain than the dead.

Chapter 5: The First Accusation

The first argument aboard the *Mercy of Kaln* was not about innocence.

It was about where innocence should stand while people with guns decided whether to believe it.

Captain Mer Anik moved the ship's emergency council from the old mess to the covenant gallery because the accusation had become too large for a room with paper lanterns. The gallery had been built after demilitarization in the forward spine where a missile magazine had once run under armored decks. Its ceiling was low, its walls white, its floor marked with circles for witness standing, crew standing, passenger standing, and external review. A person could tell, from the geometry alone, that lawyers had designed it while engineers looked on with folded arms.

At the center of the floor stood the covenant table: a slab of transparent ceramic over an empty weapons bus. The bus had been cut, sealed, signed, inspected, photographed, and argued over by six courts. It remained visible so no one aboard could pretend the ship's danger was abstract.

The Avar Break packet hovered above it in cold blue.

MERCY OF KALN: LEGAL CONFIDENCE SUFFICIENT FOR EMERGENCY PURSUIT.

Tovin stood inside the crew circle with Mer to his left and Noor to his right. Ixo's ceramic body rested on the table edge, four small hands touching the transparent surface as if reading heat from a corpse. Sela Marr stood near the engineering rail, arms loose now, which worried Tovin more than when she crossed them. Mother Kal had taken the passenger circle without asking permission. Iren Sol carried no blanket ledger this time. He carried a tablet with ration counts, ward occupancy, spare suit seals, and the number of people aboard who could not survive a hard boarding without medical preparation.

"We have sent receipt of warrant," Noor said. "We have denied the attack. We have transmitted three log bundles and requested raw buoy memory from Avar Break. Admiral Soth's office acknowledges receipt but has not certified our logs."

"Meaning?" asked Jessa Pike, one of the school ark teachers.

"Meaning they have the truth in their inbox," Sela said, "and it is still waiting in line behind fear."

Noor did not correct her.

Mer touched the table. A second display opened beside the packet: the pursuit warrant with Jaya Soth's signature. Locate. Shadow. Communicate. Preserve evidence. Prevent unverified docking. Protect civilian convoy elements.

"The lawful path," Mer said, "is to comply with pursuit instructions, hold course under observation, avoid unverified ports, and accept boarding by a verified Compact or Belt authority when safe."

"That sounds like surrender with better stationery," Mother Kal said.

"It may become surrender," Mer said.

The word entered the gallery and found every old scar.

Tovin watched faces. Repair crew thinking of seals. Nurses thinking of patients. Passengers thinking of custody doors. Convoy witnesses thinking of whether they had joined a rescue ship and become evidence cargo. Children were not present this time, by Mer's order and the teacher's reluctant agreement. The absence made the room less honest.

Hask Pell was not aboard, not yet, but Tovin could already hear the kind of offer men like him would make: hide in a salvage reef, change identifiers, wake what needed waking, call survival a doctrine. The first accusation always created a market.

"If we surrender to Soth, we live," said Iren.

Sela snapped, "You do not know that."

"I know we do not out-burn a pursuit group while hauling an oxygen still and a school ark."

"Running is not the only way to refuse custody."

"No, but it is the quickest way to make everyone shooty."

"That is not a word."

"It is a condition."

Mer let the exchange run three seconds, then cut it with a look. "We are not voting on panic. We are listing risks."

"Risk one," Noor said. "Immediate surrender to an authenticated pursuit unit preserves our formal claim to compliance."

"And hands our logs to whoever boards first," Sela said.

"If the boarding party is clean."

"If."

Noor accepted that with a nod. "Risk two: if we delay surrender, hostile councils use delay as proof of guilt."

"They already are," said the teacher.

"Delay gives them more."

Mother Kal leaned forward. "Risk three: if we surrender at the wrong dock, the ship becomes a trophy before it becomes a defendant."

Mer looked at her. "Say more."

"A converted warship accused of massacre is not only a suspect. It is an answer to old arguments. People will want to stand beside it and say, See, I told you mercy was costume. They will not need trial to use us. A dock camera will do."

Iren said, "So what, we stay in open lane until politics becomes generous?"

"Politics has never been generous," Mother Kal said. "Sometimes it becomes tired."

Ixo's amber light pulsed. "There is an additional risk not sufficiently weighted."

"I was waiting," Mer said.

"Custody of me."

The gallery quieted in a different way. Machine-personhood still had that effect: everyone had an opinion until the person in question used the first-person pronoun.

Noor's expression sharpened. "Under current Ash Belt law, Ixo is a recognized convoy archive person in limited jurisdictions, but not universally."

"Correct," Ixo said. "A hostile or frightened authority may classify my active forks as evidentiary software, quarantine them, copy them, isolate them, or delete contaminated instances under chain-of-custody hygiene."

Sela's hands curled. "Delete."

"They may use a cleaner verb."

"That does not make it cleaner."

"No."

Tovin looked at the packet hovering over the severed weapons bus. Avar Break had named the *Mercy* with clean certainty. Ixo had already seen something too regular in that certainty. If someone had built a frame from old command roots, a machine witness might be the first thing they tried to turn into a corrupted file.

"Can you protect yourself?" Mer asked.

"Partially. I can distribute witness hashes across our convoy logs and permitted buoy caches. I cannot flee into unlicensed systems without violating both the covenant and several personhood agreements."

Mother Kal said, "Agreements are useful until murder reads them."

"Yes," Ixo said. "That is also my experience."

Noor wrote quickly. "We add Ixo custody protection to any surrender condition."

"They may refuse," Ixo said.

"Then we learn something."

Sela laughed once. "We learn while they aim."

Mer changed the display again. A ship schematic appeared: the *Mercy*'s hospital decks, school berths, machine shops, old weapon spaces, seal galleries, engine rooms, oxygen still coupling, convoy tethers, and the temporary passenger wards installed after Cinderwake. Each section carried a number. Some numbers were people. Some were risks. Some were both.

"Iren," she said.

The quartermaster looked down at his tablet. "Total souls aboard after Cinderwake transfers: six hundred twelve. Crew one hundred seventy-nine. Passengers and patients two hundred six. School ark temporary transfers forty-two, though most remain on ark. Convoy witnesses eighteen. Repair contractors thirty-one. Medical dependency high: sixty-four cannot suit quickly, nineteen cannot suit without assistance, seven cannot tolerate hard acceleration, two in induced low-pressure sleep."

"How long to prepare for inspection boarding?"

"Friendly boarding? Forty minutes."

"Unfriendly?"

"There is no preparation for unfriendly boarding. There is only triage with boots."

Mer nodded as if he had given a number.

Noor said, "If we request medical delay, it may be read as stalling."

"It is stalling," Iren said. "For lungs."

The teacher, Jessa, had been silent too long. Now she spoke from the passenger circle. "What happens to the school ark if we are declared hostile while tethered?"

No one answered fast enough.

She smiled without humor. "Adults have a pause for the worst answers."

Tovin said, "If we are declared hostile, any tethered vessel may be treated as shield, hostage, accomplice, or protected civilian depending on who writes the first report."

"Then untether us."

Mer turned. "Your ark's forward seal was damaged in Cinderwake. You requested shadow until the patch cured."

"And I am requesting release now."

Iren said, "The patch has eight hours before I trust it."

"I did not ask whether you trust it."

The teacher's voice did not rise. That made it worse. She was not angry at them. She was calculating how much danger belonged to the children and how much had been loaned to them by the old ship protecting them.

"If we remain tethered," she said, "people who hate you may decide we are your excuse. If we separate, we may leak. I need the leak quantified."

Sela opened the maintenance forecast before Mer asked. "Forward seal patch holds under low acceleration with ninety-one percent confidence for twelve hours. Under hard burn, sixty-two. Under debris, don't."

"Can we reinforce before release?"

"Yes. Two hours."

"Do it."

Mer said, "Jessa."

"Captain, I respect your command. I also know how stories work. The school ark cannot be allowed to become the reason a battleship refuses lawful order."

"We are not a battleship."

The teacher looked around the covenant gallery, the severed weapons bus, the old armored walls. "Captain."

Mer looked away first.
