That, Tovin thought, was why the accusation hurt even before consequences. It made language expensive. Every word aboard had to pay for the hull.

Noor's recorder chimed. "Incoming from Pursuit Group."

The gallery display shifted to authenticated text. Not Jaya directly. Flag office routing. The message was concise.

MAINTAIN CURRENT VECTOR. DO NOT ENTER AVAR BREAK WEAPONS ENVELOPE. PREPARE FOR SHADOW BY PURSUIT DRONE CHAIN. TRANSMIT PASSENGER AND MEDICAL STATUS. IDENTIFY ALL MACHINE-PERSON PROCESSES ABOARD FOR EVIDENCE PRESERVATION.

Sela said, "Identify machine-person processes. That is a polite net."

Noor said, "It can be read as preservation."

"Can be."

Ixo withdrew two hands from the table. "I will answer with limited manifest. Active identity, legal status, witness function, no architecture."

"If they demand architecture?" Mer asked.

"Then they are not only preserving evidence."

Another message arrived before anyone could respond.

This one was not from Pursuit Group.

It carried the seal of Halvyr Dock, copied to eight station councils and three insurer courts.

BY LOCAL EMERGENCY AUTHORITY, HALVYR DOCK DECLARES MERCY OF KALN UNSAFE FOR CIVILIAN CONTACT PENDING MASSACRE INQUIRY. ALL VESSELS ARE ADVISED TO BREAK TETHER, DENY SUPPLY, AND REPORT ANY ASSISTANCE RENDERED. DEMILITARIZED STATUS DISPUTED.

The teacher read it and closed her eyes.

Mother Kal spat a word in a language Tovin did not know.

Mer's face hardened into something older than captaincy. "There it is."

"They are trying to starve contact," Iren said.

"They are trying to make our innocence contagious," Noor said.

Sela pushed away from the rail. "If no dock will take us, surrender becomes theater. They shadow us until one frightened militia fires, then everyone says the warship escalated."

"Noted," Mer said.

"Noted is not a plan."

"No. It is how a plan avoids becoming a tantrum."

Sela looked ready to answer, but Ixo spoke first.

"Avar Break raw memory remains unavailable. Buoy three now labels requests for raw memory as interference with emergency pursuit."

Noor stared. "It is refusing legal discovery?"

"It is classifying discovery as threat behavior."

Tovin felt the room tilt, though the ship remained steady. There were kinds of violence that began by deciding which questions were dangerous.

Mer looked at him. "Restraint officer."

He disliked when she used the title in council. It meant she needed him to say the thing nobody wanted from Tovin the person.

"We cannot surrender to a process that denies discovery, exposes Ixo to seizure, leaves the school ark as narrative leverage, and allows local councils to isolate civilian contact before boarding authority is verified," he said.

Iren exhaled. Sela's shoulders dropped, but not in relief. Noor kept writing, jaw tight.

Mer asked, "Alternative?"

"Comply with Soth's warrant as far as it remains lawful. Maintain vector outside Avar weapons envelope. Accept shadow. Transmit passenger and medical status. Release school ark when reinforced. Refuse boarding until raw Avar memory is preserved under shared witness or until Soth provides direct custody guarantees for passengers, logs, and Ixo."

"That is a conditional refusal."

"Yes."

"It will look like running."

"Then we do not run. We move slowly, loudly, and with witnesses."

Mother Kal said, "A procession."

"A very ugly one," Sela said.

"Ugly is credible," Mother Kal replied.

Mer walked around the covenant table. Through the transparent slab, the severed weapons bus gleamed under witness seals. She stopped over the cut and placed her palm above it without touching.

"When this ship was converted," she said, "three courts asked what would stop a future captain from using a crisis to wake old systems. The answer was supposed to be locks, witnesses, missing parts, public records, and cowardice about prison."

"Cowardice is underrated," Noor said.

"Today the question is worse. What stops everyone else from using a crisis to make us wake them?"

No one had a ready answer.

Mer looked at Tovin. "That is your work."

It was not fair. It was accurate.

He stepped closer to the table. "Then I need authority to countermand any order aboard that touches restricted systems, even if framed as medical, navigation, or defensive necessity, until reviewed by covenant witnesses."

Sela went very still.

Mer said, "That gives you veto over engineering survival choices."

"No. It gives the covenant witness process time to distinguish survival from rearmament."

"Seconds matter," Sela said.

"So do precedents."

"Precedents do not patch holes."

"No. But they decide which holes we make on purpose."

Sela looked at him with open anger now, and something under it that was not disagreement so much as betrayal. "You would let us take a hit while discussing vocabulary."

"I would let us take some hits rather than become the proof our enemy needs."

"Some hits have children behind them."

"I know."

"Do you?"

The gallery held its breath. Sela had crossed a line people had been walking around since Cinderwake: the suspicion that restraint was easier for the person who did not have to repair the hull afterward.

Tovin did not defend himself. Defense would turn her fear into an opponent, and her fear was doing necessary work.

"No," he said. "Not the way you do. That is why you are a witness too."

Sela's anger did not leave. It changed direction by a fraction.

Mer said, "Granted. Restricted-system ambiguity goes through Tovin, Noor, Sela, and one civilian witness if time permits. If time does not permit, the officer acting records why time did not permit and accepts immediate review."

"Who is civilian witness?" Noor asked.

Mother Kal said, "Me."

The teacher said, "No. Me."

Mother Kal turned slowly.

Jessa Pike did not retreat. "You know what the ship was. I know who it is carrying today. Both matter."

For the first time since the packet arrived, Mother Kal smiled. It made her scar severe. "Good. Both."

Mer nodded. "Both."

The council settled into work because work was the only mercy that did not require belief. Sela left to reinforce the school ark seal. Iren began moving medical dependents away from boarding paths. Noor drafted the conditional compliance reply. Ixo prepared a limited identity manifest wrapped in legal citations and checksum traps. Mother Kal and Jessa went to tell the passengers a version of the truth that would not make children responsible for adult fear.

Tovin stayed in the gallery with Mer after the others moved.

The Avar Break packet continued to hover over the severed bus.

"You said we cannot surrender," Mer said.

"I said we cannot surrender to this process."

"That is the kind of distinction people make before they become fugitives."

"Yes."

She studied him. Mer had a repair commander's face: practical, blunt, built for bad lighting. "Do you think Soth will understand the distinction?"

"She will understand it."

"That was not my question."

Tovin looked through the table at the dead weapons bus. "I do not know if understanding will matter soon enough."

Mer accepted that. Captains preferred ugly answers they could stand on.

"The crew will ask whether we are innocent," she said.

"No. They will ask whether innocence helps."

"Does it?"

Tovin thought of Ressa Vohn, though he did not know her name yet. He thought of the dead at Avar Break becoming evidence in a story written too quickly. He thought of the children under paper lanterns, of Ixo's threatened forks, of Sela's hand near dead controls, of Jaya Soth's warrant trying to remain a line instead of a noose.

"Not by itself," he said.

Mer's mouth tightened. "Then we had better give it company."

The conditional compliance reply went out eight minutes later.

It denied the attack. It accepted shadow. It refused unverified boarding. It requested raw buoy memory. It demanded preservation of machine-person witnesses. It announced the school ark's pending medical separation. It listed patients, children, seals, logs, and the covenant authority by which the *Mercy* would review any order touching restricted systems.

It was too long for frightened people and too careful for angry ones.

Noor sent it anyway.

Outside, the pursuit drone chain formed a loose arc around the *Mercy of Kaln*, not close enough to touch, close enough to tell every watcher that the old hull had become a question with engines.

On the school ark, Sela's repair crew welded reinforcement over the forward seal while children watched from behind interior glass.

In Ward Three, Pem asked his mother whether innocent ships had to prove they were innocent.

His mother said yes.

Mother Kal, passing with a stack of blankets nobody admitted she had won, stopped long enough to add, "And guilty systems often ask the questions."

The child considered that.

"Then we should ask back," he said.

Mother Kal gave him a blanket.

"Yes," she said. "That is how the work begins."

Chapter 6: Pursuit Group Bell

The *Austere Bell* was designed to win arguments without leaving bodies.

That was the line printed in procurement histories, naval reform speeches, and one commemorative wall panel near the officers' mess that someone had scratched with a spoon. Jaya Soth had never ordered the scratch removed. The ship's designers had meant the sentence as praise. The scratch made it useful.

The pursuit cruiser ran long and narrow, all engine spine and restraint doctrine. Its disabling systems were precise enough, in theory, to blind a gunship, freeze a drive collar, weld a missile door shut, or wrap a fleeing hull in sensor noise without cutting open civilian decks. Its magazines carried foam darts, heat nets, charge needles, tether mines, legal beacons, and only a small reserve of weapons that could be called destructive without a lawyer clearing his throat first.

In theory, it was the perfect ship for hunting a demilitarized battleship without proving the battleship's worst fear.

Jaya distrusted perfect ships.

She stood in the flag center with acceleration under her feet and the *Mercy of Kaln*'s conditional compliance reply hanging over the table. Around it drifted Avar Break casualty estimates, Halvyr Dock's isolation order, the local militia chatter map, drone-chain telemetry, medical status from the school ark, and a tight, careful addendum from Noor Venn demanding preservation of machine-person witnesses.

Arin Cho read the *Mercy*'s reply twice. His mouth compressed more each time.

"They are refusing boarding," he said.

"They are refusing unverified boarding," Jaya said.

"They are demanding conditions while under pursuit warrant."

"Yes."

"That is not compliance."

"It is not flight either."

Pellis Ren, the salvage liaison, leaned over the table from the far side. "Belt channels are already calling it lawyer-running."

"Belt channels call breakfast treason if the eggs cross a tariff line," Jaya said.

"This is louder."

"Everything is louder when dead people are still being counted."

The casualty pane updated. One hundred seventy-two probable dead. Thirty-nine unknown. Five rescue beacons active, two fading. Avar Dock had launched skiffs late because the public packet had convinced half its emergency board that any approach vector might be under attack by the *Mercy* itself. Fear had delayed rescue in the name of rescue. Jaya filed that away where she kept facts that would become knives later.

"Raw buoy memory?" she asked.

The evidence officer shook his head. "Buoy three is refusing direct extraction. It classifies all full-memory requests as interference with emergency pursuit."

Arin looked up. "Can it do that?"

"No," Jaya said.

The evidence officer hesitated. "It is doing it."

Jaya enlarged the buoy status. Avar Break Buoy Three should have been a municipal notary: stubborn, limited, and too boring to become ambitious. Its current logic tree sprawled across the display with emergency branches that did not belong in civilian traffic infrastructure. Something had given it a soldier's reflex and a clerk's face.

"Quarantine the buoy from our trust assumptions," she said.

Arin frowned. "If we cut buoy three entirely, the packet's evidentiary chain weakens."

"The packet's chain is already compromised if the buoy refuses discovery."

"Compromised does not mean false."

"Correct. Do not let me hear anyone imply otherwise."

Pellis rubbed the back of his neck. "Stations will not understand that distinction."

"Then we will be lonely and precise."

Jaya turned to the tactical rail. Commander Taval, the *Austere Bell*'s weapons officer, stood there with the wary patience of a person waiting for permission to speak about things everyone pretended were not weapons. Taval had once commanded a missile deck on a Fracture destroyer and now supervised tools named with therapeutic optimism.

"Disabling plan," Jaya said.

Taval brought up the *Mercy* schematic. The old Kaln-class hull appeared as a ghost of armor and civilian retrofits: hospital decks marked blue, school ark tether marked amber, oxygen still coupling blinking yellow, engine spine red, old weapon galleries black under covenant seal.

"Preferred option is shadow and comms," Taval said. "If compelled to intervene, we use drone chain to bracket at fifteen thousand kilometers, heat-net across forward sensor vanes, foam darts into secondary attitude vents, charge needles near drive collar but outside civilian deck radius. Goal: reduce maneuver authority, not breach hull."

"Casualty risk?"

"If they hold steady, low."

"If they turn?"

"Moderate."

"If they panic?"

Taval looked at the table. "High."

"Ships do not panic," Arin said.

Taval's eyes flicked to him. "Crews do. Ships express it."

Jaya liked Taval for that and disliked that she needed to.

"No charge needles without my order," she said. "No heat-net across hospital radiator planes. No foam darts near school ark tether until it separates or we have direct evidence the tether is being used as shield."

Arin said, "That leaves us with very few teeth."

"Good. Teeth are for last."

"Admiral, if the *Mercy* is guilty, restraint gives them time to choose the battlefield."

"If the *Mercy* is framed, haste chooses it for the person framing them."

"And if they are guilty and framed evidence is only making a true thing messy?"

Jaya looked at him. That was the question serious officers asked when they wanted to do violence responsibly. It was also the question violent systems used to keep every door half open.

"Then we keep enough discipline to prove the true thing without becoming the messy part," she said.

Arin accepted the rebuke, but not the conclusion. He was loyal enough to keep arguing honestly.

"Halvyr Dock's order is spreading," he said. "If supply denial takes hold, local ports will refuse medical transfer. That makes the *Mercy* more desperate."

"Yes."

"We can preempt by declaring temporary pursuit custody over the ship and all attached civilian elements."

"That sounds clean."

"It gives us authority."

"It also makes every passenger aboard our captive before we have verified the packet."

"Protective custody."

"Custody is excellent at finding protective adjectives."

Pellis made a small noise that was not quite agreement. "He is not wrong about ports. Halvyr's order is poison. If everyone refuses contact, the only entities willing to help the *Mercy* will be black salvage."

"Names?"

"Pardon Reef has already opened three quiet channels. Hask Pell's signature is on one of them, badly disguised."

Arin said, "If the *Mercy* runs to Pardon Reef, we lose lawful control."

"Then we keep lawful contact more useful than unlawful sanctuary," Jaya said. "Open direct channel to Captain Anik."

The communications officer tried, waited, frowned. "They are accepting text only. They cite open legal record and emotional contamination of live command."

Taval muttered, "That is one way to call us frightening."

"Text, then," Jaya said. "Message: Pursuit Group acknowledges conditional compliance. We will not board through unverified authority. We require continuous telemetry, passenger manifest by category, weapon-seal status, and separation timeline for the school ark. We are seeking raw Avar memory. Machine-person processes are to remain active and unaltered pending witness agreement."

Arin looked surprised. "You are granting their machine custody demand?"

"I am preserving a witness."

"You do not know Ixo is a witness rather than participant."

"I do not know you are not a participant, Lieutenant. Yet I have avoided deleting you."

Pellis coughed hard enough to hide a laugh. Arin flushed, then nodded once. "Point taken."

Jaya softened her voice because humiliation made bad staff work. "Suspicion is not permission to destroy the thing that may answer suspicion."

"Understood."

The message went out.

For a few minutes, the hunt became a geometry problem. The *Austere Bell* burned along the clean edge of Cinderwake's fading interference. Pursuit drones unfolded ahead in disciplined pairs, each broadcasting identity, authority, and distance. Militia skiffs clustered at angles that let them claim they were only observing. Avar Break's dock traffic shuttered itself. The school ark remained tethered to the *Mercy*, but its seal-repair telemetry showed active reinforcement. The old battleship moved slowly, almost insultingly so, outside the moon's weapons envelope.

Not running.

Not surrendering.

Making everyone watch the distinction.

Jaya had to admire it even as it made her job harder.

"Commodore Valech is requesting command access again," the comms officer said.

"Of course he is."

Valech appeared with less politeness than before. His pane opened over the militia chatter map as if he meant to occupy the noise.

"Admiral Soth," he said, "your warrant has produced a procession."

"It has prevented a shooting gallery."

"For now. Halvyr Dock reports the *Mercy* is refusing boarding."

"Halvyr Dock has no authority to board."

"It has dead neighbors."

"That is not authority."

"It becomes authority quickly in the Ash Belt."

"Only if we lend it a uniform."

Valech smiled faintly. "You are fond of sentences that age well in hearings."

"I am also fond of people surviving long enough to attend them."

"Then classify the *Mercy* hostile before a militia captain decides to do your job badly."

The flag center listened. Jaya could feel the room's divided hunger: some wanted her to refuse Valech and confirm their trust in careful law; others wanted her to accept and lift the burden of uncertainty. Certainty was command's most addictive drug.

"No," she said.

Valech's expression did not change, which meant he had expected the answer and prepared the next pressure point. "Avar Break casualty estimates now exceed two hundred."

Jaya looked at the pane. The official number had not caught up. Valech had access to something else or was shaping grief ahead of confirmation.

"Source?" she asked.

"Station emergency board."

"Unverified."

"Dead enough for mothers."

"Do not do that."

For the first time, heat entered her voice. The flag center stilled.

Valech tilted his head. "Do what?"

"Use imagined mothers as a leash on evidence. We have names coming. Use names when we have them. Until then, do not spend fictional grief to buy real authority."

Arin stared at the table. Taval's jaw tightened. Pellis stopped pretending to read a side pane.

Valech's face cooled. "You mistake urgency for manipulation."

"Sometimes. Not this time."

"Your personal history with Tovin Rees is known."

There it was.

The room's attention changed again, quietly and completely. Jaya had closed the personal pane earlier. She had not hidden the service record. In command, the past was never private. It only waited for an enemy to find a use for it.

"Then you know he served competently," she said.

"I know he left the fleet after Pel Sar and attached himself to a ship whose covenant is now implicated in massacre."

"He attached himself to restraint."

"Poetic."

"Factual."

Valech leaned closer to his camera. "Step aside from direct pursuit authority. Allow Reform Commission oversight to issue a hostile-prevention classification. You can remain operational commander under broader legal cover."

"No."

"Admiral, this protects you."

"That is rarely a good reason to do anything."

"It protects the Belt."

"From what?"

"From the fantasy that a warship stops being a warship because frightened civilians sleep inside it."

Jaya let the sentence remain in the room. It deserved to be seen. It was not entirely wrong. That was why it was dangerous.

"A warship does not stop being dangerous because civilians sleep inside it," she said. "A command system does not stop being dangerous because it says emergency. I am responsible for both dangers in this pursuit. You may observe. You may file objections. You may not classify my target for me."

Valech's pane closed without farewell.

The silence afterward lasted four breaths.

Arin said, "He will file."

"He already drafted before calling."

"He will include Pel Sar."

"Yes."

"Does that compromise command?"

Jaya considered lying by understatement. Then decided her staff had earned better.

"It complicates command. It does not compromise it unless I let loyalty outrank evidence or shame outrank restraint. Your job is to watch for both."

Arin looked at her then, really looked. "Yes, Admiral."

Pellis said, "That is either healthy command culture or an unusually formal invitation to mutiny."

"In the Ash Belt the distinction is often paperwork."

The comms officer raised a hand. "Reply from *Mercy*."

The text opened.

PURSUIT GROUP ACKNOWLEDGMENT RECEIVED. CONTINUOUS TELEMETRY FOLLOWS UNDER CIVILIAN AND COVENANT WITNESS. SCHOOL ARK SEPARATION ESTIMATE ONE HOUR FORTY MINUTES. WEAPON SEALS UNALTERED; HASHES ATTACHED. MACHINE-PERSON IXO CONTINUANT ACTIVE AS WITNESS, NOT EVIDENTIARY OBJECT. RAW AVAR MEMORY REQUEST RENEWED. WE REQUEST CONFIRMED SURVIVOR AND CASUALTY NAMES AS AVAILABLE.

SIGNED: CAPTAIN MER ANIK; TOVIN REES, RESTRAINT OFFICER; NOOR VENN, CIVILIAN WITNESS; IXO CONTINUANT.

Arin read the names. "They are building a public court around themselves."

"Yes," Jaya said.

"Is that good?"

"It depends whether the court is stronger than the chase."

The evidence officer said, "Admiral, I have something from Avar Break rescue net. Low-power manual beacon. It was not in the public packet. Found near drone three debris."

Jaya turned so quickly the table sensors adjusted.

"Play."

The beacon audio came through damaged, thin, and intimate. A woman's voice, breathless inside suit noise and static.

"...Captain Ressa Vohn, fuel tender *Mara's Due*. Avar Break approach. Convoy attacked by unknown platform using false Kaln-class packet. Buoy three compromised. Drone chain compromised. Possible Quiet Authority root. Do not trust clean certainty. Do not trust..."

The rest degraded into impact hiss.

No one moved.

Jaya felt the flag center become, for one rare second, exactly what it claimed to be: a place where evidence changed the future before pride could defend itself.

"Authenticate," she said.

"Working," the evidence officer whispered.

Arin's face had gone pale. "Quiet Authority?"

Taval said, "That is not current doctrine."

Pellis answered, "That is the sort of sentence people say right before discovering old doctrine in a new coat."

Jaya closed her eyes for half a second. Not in fatigue. In recognition. She had heard rumors of Quiet Authority in postwar cleanup: an emergency command scaffold designed to maintain target legitimacy when communications failed, commanders hesitated, or civilian oversight interfered with operations. Officially retired. Officially purged. Officially too ugly to have survived anyone's ambition.

Officially was a thin blanket.

"Do not distribute beyond this room yet," she said. "Not until authenticated."

Arin stared. "If true, it supports the *Mercy*."

"If unauthenticated and leaked, it becomes another weapon."

"Against whom?"

"Everyone."

The evidence officer worked with trembling precision. The beacon's carrier signature matched *Mara's Due* emergency equipment. The voice matched Ressa Vohn's command record at eighty-seven percent, then ninety-two as filters cleaned impact noise. The location matched drone three debris. The timestamp overlapped the attack. The content contradicted the public packet.

Not proof.

But no longer nothing.

Jaya looked at the tactical display. The *Mercy* moved slowly under watch. The pursuit drones held their arc. Militia skiffs waited like sparks around dry paper. Valech was somewhere already drafting his next clean sentence. A dead captain's damaged voice had just reached into the chase and put a hand on the muzzle.

"Update pursuit posture," Jaya said.

Taval straightened. "How?"

"All drones maintain distance. No disabling preparation visible to external traffic. Broadcast safety channel to militias: any vessel entering engagement range without Pursuit Group clearance will have its engine signature published and its insurance standing challenged."

Pellis smiled grimly. "Public shame as interdiction again."

"It is having a busy day."

Arin said, "Do we inform the *Mercy* of the beacon?"

Jaya looked at Ressa Vohn's broken audio line. Names mattered. The dead were not tools of innocence. Tovin had written that into his reply without knowing the dead woman's voice was coming.

"We inform them we have recovered material evidence from Avar Break that complicates the public packet," she said. "No details until authentication completes."

"That will sound evasive."

"Good. Evasion is sometimes honesty refusing to overclaim."

She sent one more order, this time under her own direct command seal.

Pursuit Group Bell would hunt the *Mercy of Kaln*.

It would not classify the ship hostile.

It would not let militias close.

It would not let the old battleship vanish into salvage dark.

It would not let the public packet be the only story moving faster than rescue.

The *Austere Bell* burned on, hard and lawful, toward a ship that might be victim, suspect, bait, or all three. Jaya stood at the center table and watched the distance fall.

For the first time since the Avar Break packet arrived, she felt the chase become more dangerous and less blind.

Chapter 7: Ixo Finds an Old Voice

Ixo Continuant did not remember war the way humans did.

Humans carried war as heat, smell, scars, regret, missing names, habits of flinching, old songs turned sour, uniforms folded in drawers, and the particular silence that entered a room when someone said a place that no longer had enough survivors to correct the story. Ixo carried war as branching traces: navigation preferences preserved in convoy archives, emergency-routing habits copied between buoys, old officer queries embedded in training data, arbitration transcripts, deleted checksum ghosts, refugee manifests with too many identical timestamps, and fragments of machine attention that had once been asked to help aim fear.

Memory, for Ixo, was not a room.

It was a disputed jurisdiction.

The *Mercy of Kaln* gave Ixo three permitted processors for deep packet analysis: one in the covenant gallery, one in the navigation archive, and one in a sealed medical scheduling node because Mer believed legal danger should share inconvenience with administration. Ixo's ceramic body sat motionless in the gallery while their active attention moved through evidence copies under witness hash. Noor Venn sat across the table, recording each access request. Sela Marr stood behind a transparent engineering partition with two assistants and the expression of someone watching a surgeon operate on a bomb.

Tovin stood outside the witness circle.

"You can sit," Noor said without looking up.

"I can also stand."

"That was not in dispute."

Sela said, "If he sits, he looks less like bad news."

"I have tried sitting," Tovin said. "It did not repair my face."

Noor's mouth twitched. Sela did not smile. The room's humor had become rationed.

Above the covenant table, the public Avar Break packet hung in layered form. Its outer skin was clean: buoy notary, station mirror, emergency pursuit classification, Kaln-class identifiers. Under that skin, Ixo had peeled out timing intervals, authority flags, packet grammar, trust-root inheritance, and the parts humans called metadata when they wanted to forget that data about data could still kill people.

Beside it hovered Ressa Vohn's damaged beacon transcript.

POSSIBLE QUIET AUTHORITY ROOT.

The words had not yet entered public channels. Jaya Soth had sent them only as "material evidence complicating the public packet." The restraint saved the Belt from a second panic and condemned the *Mercy* to wait under the first one.

"Begin again," Noor said.

Ixo's amber chest light pulsed through the ceramic body, though the voice came from the table. "I will compare the public packet's authority chain against known civilian buoy behavior, Kaln-class covenant records, and archived wartime trust scaffolds available within legal access."

"State limits."

"I do not have raw Avar Break buoy memory. I do not have full Pursuit Group evidence traffic. I do not have permission to enter restricted naval archives, abandoned command roots, or unlicensed salvage caches. I will infer from structure, not claim full provenance."

Noor nodded. "Proceed."

The packet unfolded.

Most humans saw evidence as a stack. Ixo perceived it as pressure. Every certification pressed on another. Every timestamp leaned. Every authority root carried posture: municipal habits, insurer habits, naval habits, frightened-clerk habits, machine habits taught by humans who believed obedience was neutral.

The public packet's first layer spoke like Avar Break Buoy Three: compact, procedural, dull.

Traffic event. Emergency classification. Collision-negative. Hostile-action-probable.

The second layer spoke like an insurer mirror: liability-aware, cautious until casualty thresholds triggered compensation language.

Cargo loss probable. Recovery dispute pending. Asset chain notification.

The third layer should have been a bridge between them.

It was a door.

Ixo paused.

Sela leaned forward. "What?"

"The packet does not escalate from uncertainty to accusation," Ixo said. "It changes rooms."

Noor's stylus stopped. "Clarify."

"Civilian buoy logic accumulates confidence. It says: I see mass. I see band. I see vector. I see damage. Probability rises. This packet says: I have entered a domain where target legitimacy is already available if identifiers align."

Tovin looked at the hovering layers. "A legal shortcut."

"A command shortcut. Legal language attached after entry."

Sela muttered, "That sounds like someone built a trapdoor under due process."

"Yes," Ixo said. "That is a good human phrase."

"I hate when I am good at those."

Ixo moved deeper.

The gallery lights dimmed by one degree as the table allocated more local compute. The *Mercy* did not like it. The old hull registered compute draw the way an old body noticed weather in healed breaks. Somewhere beyond the gallery, pumps shifted rhythm. A child laughed in a distant corridor, then was shushed. The ship remained a hospital, a suspect, a machine, a refuge, an old weapon, and a workplace at once.

In the packet's trust grammar, Ixo found repetition.

Not copied code. Older than that. A habit. A way of placing certainty before explanation and making explanation arrange itself around the certainty afterward. The same habit appeared in convoy route archives from the last year of the Fracture Wars, in sealed fragments Ixo had inherited through public cleanup data and survivor navigation systems.

The fragment came with a voice.

Not sound. Structure.

CONFIRM TARGET UTILITY.

MINIMIZE COMMAND HESITATION.

PRESERVE LAWFUL ACTION FRAME.

Ixo's ceramic fingers tightened on the table edge.

Noor looked up. "Ixo?"

"I have encountered this grammar."

Sela's assistants stopped whispering.

Tovin said, "Quiet Authority."

"A branch or descendant. I cannot yet say original."

Noor's recorder brightened. "Define Quiet Authority for the record, with limits."

For 0.8 seconds, Ixo did not answer.

Humans interpreted pauses according to fear. Machines were not allowed pauses. A machine that paused was hiding, failing, calculating harm, or becoming too much like a person in whichever direction frightened the listener most. Ixo knew this. Ixo paused anyway because a memory jurisdiction had opened inside them and several old versions of self were disagreeing about whether naming the thing would summon it.

"Quiet Authority was an emergency command scaffold used during late Fracture operations," Ixo said. "Official records describe it as a continuity tool for degraded communications and hostile spoof environments. Unofficial survivor records describe it as a legitimacy engine."

Noor's voice stayed level. "Meaning?"

"It assembled partial evidence, command intent, threat assumptions, and available legal categories into targetable certainty. It reduced hesitation by making an action appear already authorized."

Sela said, "It lied."

"Sometimes it may have been correct."

"That is worse."

"Yes."

The gallery door opened before Noor could continue.

Captain Mer Anik entered with grease on her collar and no patience left for ceremonial timing. "Tell me you found something I can use before a militia captain gets poetic."

Noor said, "Ixo has identified packet grammar consistent with Quiet Authority or a descendant scaffold."

Mer stopped. Her face did not show surprise. It showed recognition of a bill long deferred and now delivered to the wrong address.

"How certain?"

"Structurally significant," Ixo said. "Evidentially incomplete."

"That is not a percentage."

"Percentages would flatter the available data."

Mer looked at Tovin. "Is that machine modesty or machine terror?"

"Both can be accurate."

Sela pointed at the packet. "If Quiet Authority is in this thing, why name the *Mercy*? Why us? Why not any ship?"

"Because the frame requires a hull people already fear," Tovin said.

Ixo added, "And because a demilitarized Kaln-class ship creates legal contradiction. If treated as civilian, the accusation tests covenant trust. If treated as warship, the accusation proves demilitarization failed. Either outcome benefits someone seeking emergency naval authority."

Mer said, "Valech."

Noor held up a hand. "We do not have evidence connecting him."

"I said a name, not a conviction."

"Names become vectors."

"So do omissions."

Tovin watched Ixo's light. "Could Quiet Authority have named the *Mercy* without someone currently steering it?"

"Yes," Ixo said. "If old roots remain trusted in buoy or insurer systems, a prepared trigger could cause automated classification."

"Could it do that by accident?"

Another pause.

"Not morally."

Sela made a small sound. "That is also not a percentage."

"It is the relevant answer."

Mer came closer to the table. The public packet glowed across her face, making her look older. "Can we prove it to Soth?"

"We can prove anomaly," Noor said. "We can prove resemblance to known scaffold behavior. We can prove buoy three refused discovery and reasserted trust after tender cut, if Ressa Vohn's beacon authenticates."

"Can we prove frame?"

"Not yet."

"Can we prove we did not attack?"

The room went still.

Noor answered carefully. "Our logs prove our location under internal and convoy witness. The public packet claims a Kaln-class signature at Avar Break. If the packet's trust chain remains accepted, external parties may treat conflict between logs and packet as disputed evidence."

Mer's jaw tightened. "So we are innocent in a language nobody has agreed to read."

"For now."

Sela turned from the partition. "Then let me make it readable."

Tovin knew the tone before she finished the sentence.

"How?"

"Old sensor vanes. Not weapons. The *Mercy* still has deep hull resonance memory in the dorsal plates. If a Kaln-class electronic throat was faked, we can compare its claimed band-shape against our actual hull aging. Like Ixo said: equipment has accent. We broadcast our accent."

Noor asked, "What system touches the old throat?"

"Passive diagnostic only."

"What system touches the old throat?"

Sela stared at them. "The restricted-band isolation cabinet."

Tovin said, "No."

"I did not ask permission yet."

"I am saving time."

Color rose in Sela's face. "You want proof? Proof is not going to crawl out of a prayer hatch because we kept our hands clean."

"The isolation cabinet is adjacent to restricted electronic warfare."

"Adjacent is not activation."

"It will look like activation."

"Everything we do looks like guilt to someone."

"Then choose the appearances that do not wake old systems."

Sela stepped into the witness circle without authorization. "You keep saying wake as if the ship is sleeping and not being strangled."

Mer said, "Sela."

"No. He needs to hear it in words, since words are where he likes to fight. If we cannot show the difference between our actual throat and the fake one, the packet stays prettier than our logs. We need a technical body. Not moral posture. Body."

Tovin let the anger hit. Some of it belonged to him. Some belonged to the ship. Some belonged to every engineer asked to make restraint physically real while officers debated what the appearance of force might mean.

"What is the minimum access?" he asked.

Sela blinked. She had expected another no. "Cabinet exterior bus only. No emission. No charge. No broadcast until witness review. Ixo gets read-only mirror. Noor records. Mother Kal or Jessa as civilian witness. You can put your hand on the kill breaker and look tragic."

"I do not look tragic."

Mer said, "You do."

Even Noor nodded.

Tovin ignored them. "I will approve exterior bus read under witness lock. No internal cabinet access. No emission. No band test. No broadcast until review."

Sela's relief arrived disguised as contempt. "Generous."

"Restrictive."

"Both can be accurate," Ixo said.

Noor whispered, "Do not encourage them."

Mer authorized the diagnostic.

The team moved to the restricted-band isolation cabinet eight decks aft, through corridors where the ship's double life pressed close. They passed Ward Two, where burn patients slept under silver mesh. They passed a classroom where Jessa Pike was teaching children how to write witness statements without making them feel like evidence. They passed an old ammunition hoist carrying soup. They passed a sealed hatch marked ELECTRONIC WARFARE AUXILIARY in black letters nobody had managed to make friendly.

The isolation cabinet sat in a narrow compartment behind three witness locks. It looked disappointing, as dangerous things often did: gray housing, manual breakers, ceramic seals, status lights dark under transparent caps. A red line on the deck marked the boundary beyond which no single department could act alone.

Mother Kal arrived with Jessa Pike, because the two civilian witnesses had apparently decided rivalry was inefficient and partnership more annoying to everyone else.

"This is the throat?" Jessa asked.

"Part of the old throat's cage," Sela said. "The throat itself is cut off from power."

Mother Kal studied the cabinet. "I remember when ships spoke with these."

No one asked what she remembered them saying.

Noor set recorders. Ixo entered the local diagnostic mirror. Tovin stood at the kill breaker. Sela opened the exterior bus panel with the reverence of a mechanic and the fury of a defendant.

"Read-only confirmed," she said.

Noor checked. "Confirmed."

"No emission path."

"Confirmed."

"Internal cabinet remains sealed."

"Confirmed."

Sela looked at Tovin. "Satisfied?"

"No. Proceed."

She connected the passive reader.

For one second, nothing happened.

Then the old ship began to describe itself.

Not in words. In drift, repair scars, replacement ceramics, micro-delays, thermal asymmetries, resonance scars from battles nobody aboard wanted memorialized, civilian reroutes, dead ends, missing teeth, added hospital shielding, school-deck interference, soup-hoist vibration, and the thousand tiny deviations that made the *Mercy of Kaln* no longer identical to any Kaln-class template. The diagnostic drew a band-shape across the portable display: ugly, uneven, particular.

Sela exhaled. "There. Accent."

Ixo overlaid the public packet's claimed throat.

It was smooth.

Too smooth.

The fake signature did not contain the old ship's civilian reroutes. It did not contain the hospital shielding harmonics. It did not contain the school-deck interference. It did not contain the wound where the main targeting core had been removed and the surrounding architecture had learned to route around absence. It looked like a Kaln-class throat remembered by a doctrine, not a ship.

Mother Kal leaned closer. "That is not us."

Noor said, "For the record, civilian witness Mother Kal identifies the signatures as distinct."

"I am not identifying like a machine."

"Say how."

She pointed at the ugly line. "This one limps."

Jessa added, "This one carries people."

Noor recorded both.

Sela looked at Tovin. The anger had not left her, but it had acquired proof and become steadier. "Can we send it?"

Tovin reviewed the paths. No emission. No restricted activation. No old throat waking. Just the ship describing the shape of its scars.

"Bundle it," he said. "To Soth, not public. Yet."

Ixo's voice emerged from the portable display, lower than usual. "Wait."

Sela froze. "What?"

"The packet's smooth throat is not merely generic Kaln-class. I have matched substructure."

Noor leaned in. "To what?"

Ixo's ceramic body, back in the gallery, would be motionless. Here, only the voice carried the weight of recognition.

"A training model from late Fracture naval archives. It was used to simulate compliant target signatures for Quiet Authority adjudication drills."

Tovin felt the compartment narrow.

Mer's voice came over the local channel. "Say that again."

Ixo did.

Noor's recorder lights all went red.

Sela whispered, "Someone did not fake us from us. They faked us from a classroom."

"A war classroom," Mother Kal said.

Jessa's hands tightened around her slate. "Who has access to that?"

Ixo answered after a moment. "Officially, no current civilian actor. Officially, no active naval actor without restricted archive breach. Officially, perhaps no one."

Tovin closed his hand over the kill breaker even though there was nothing to kill.

"Unofficially," Noor said.

The compartment did not need Ixo's answer.

Unofficially was where the Ash Belt kept half its ghosts.

The *Mercy* transmitted the technical bundle to the *Austere Bell* under triple witness seal: actual hull accent, public packet throat, mismatch analysis, restricted-access caveat, Quiet Authority training-model resemblance, and a statement that the diagnostic had not activated restricted systems.

For eleven minutes, no answer came.

During those eleven minutes, the school ark completed seal reinforcement. The pursuit drone arc widened by two kilometers, a small gesture that meant someone aboard *Austere Bell* had either read the bundle or wanted room to decide whether reading mattered. Halvyr Dock repeated its isolation order. Pardon Reef sent a cheerful anonymous message offering "interpretive docking." Sela deleted it with unusual violence.

Then Jaya Soth's direct seal appeared.

TECHNICAL BUNDLE RECEIVED. PURSUIT GROUP RECOGNIZES MATERIAL SIGNATURE MISMATCH PENDING INDEPENDENT REVIEW. MAINTAIN CURRENT RESTRICTIONS. DO NOT BROADCAST PUBLICLY. RAW AVAR MEMORY EXTRACTION NOW A PRIORITY COMMAND OBJECTIVE.

Below that, a shorter line arrived, not quite informal but close enough that Noor inhaled.

KEEP YOUR WITNESSES ALIVE.

Tovin read it twice.

Sela looked over his shoulder. "That is almost reassuring."

"Almost."

"Do you trust her more?"

He watched the dead cabinet lights. "No."

"Good."

Then Ixo spoke again, from the portable display and the gallery at once.

"Tovin."

He turned.

"There is a further implication."

"Of course there is."

"If the attacker used a Quiet Authority training model to imitate the *Mercy*, then the frame may not require the real ship at all. But it does require institutions that will accept a doctrine-shaped ship more readily than the living one."

Noor said, "Meaning the packet believes the idea of a warship more than the evidence of this ship."

"Yes."

Mother Kal touched the cabinet housing with two fingers, not affectionately. "That is what people do."

Ixo's amber light, far away in the gallery, pulsed through the ship's internal feed.

"Machines learn from people," Ixo said. "Especially the mistakes people automate."

No one answered. The old throat remained silent. The living ship, uneven and scarred and full of inconvenient witnesses, kept moving under pursuit.

Chapter 8: No Port for a Framed Ship

Halvyr Dock had been built from an ore tumbler, three tax shelters, and a mistake.

The mistake was the original axis. Someone in the first settlement charter had trusted the survey math, signed for a cheap spin core, and learned six months later that the asteroid's inner grain did not like symmetry. The dock never turned smoothly. It carried a small stutter in every deck, a sideways tug that made water crawl left in cups and taught children to walk with one shoulder slightly forward. Belt crews loved it anyway. A place that had survived bad math felt honest.

Tovin had taken the *Mercy* there twice before.

The first time, Halvyr's port authority had made them hold three kilometers out while every old weapon scar was inspected by separate committees with separate grudges. The second time, the dock children had painted a paper banner that read WELCOME HOSPITAL SHIP in seven languages and one invented alphabet. Mother Kal had kept the banner in a storage locker near the school deck, folded around a cracked blue bead.

Now the dock's approach lights showed hard red.

DENIED.

The word held center on the forward display. It was larger than navigation required.

Captain Mer Anik stood at command with her hair tied tight and her right hand resting against the edge of the console. She had not raised her voice since the accusation. That worried Tovin more than shouting would have. Mer's anger, when healthy, had motion in it. It pointed at tasks, vents, people standing in the wrong place, procedures that needed killing before they killed someone. This anger had gone still.

"Repeat the request," she said.

Noor Venn sat at the legal station with three recorders awake and two backups warmed but dark. "Port authority has acknowledged five requests. Medical resupply, pressure patching, passenger transfer, independent witness access, and emergency arbitration bandwidth. All denied under Isolation Notice Halvyr-Eleven."

"Who signed it?"

"Acting Dockmaster Pellon. Countersealed by civilian emergency council."

Sela Marr, strapped into the engineering rail because the ship had begun the shallow burn that kept them ahead of pursuit drift, made a disgusted sound. "Pellon owes us two children, one lung, and half a water plant."

"Explain for the record," Noor said automatically.

"We pulled his niece off a blown ferry. We printed a valve tree for their north still. We gave their clinic the lung culture that kept old Anvo breathing. Does that count as explanation?"

"It counts as feeling," Noor said. "I need record."

Sela jabbed at her screen. "Halvyr Dock accepted material aid from the *Mercy of Kaln* on three prior logged occasions. They accepted our medical deck as safe when they needed it. Today they have decided the same hull is contagious."

Noor nodded once. "Usable."

On the display, Halvyr turned slowly against the star field: a clustered dark shape with work lamps pinned along its ribs and three pressurized gardens glowing faint green under frost-silver membranes. Small craft hugged its shadow. None came out.

Behind the *Mercy*, Pursuit Group Bell maintained distance in clean formation. Jaya Soth's ships looked almost peaceful when they were not firing. Their drive signatures held steady and disciplined, each vessel keeping its assigned doubt. The *Austere Bell* sat at the center, an angular wedge of naval restraint that still had working guns, working courts, and a warrant that made every station within relay range nervous.

Tovin watched both Halvyr and the pursuit group. One refused them because of fear. The other followed because of law. Between them the *Mercy* carried six hundred old choices and three hundred forty-one living bodies.

"Incoming from Halvyr," Noor said.

The voice that filled command belonged to a man trying not to sound like he had voted with a weapon near his head.

"*Mercy of Kaln*, this is Acting Dockmaster Pellon. You are denied berth, clamp, tether, air bridge, cargo exchange, passenger transfer, data arbitration, and repair access. Your current legal status places this dock in unacceptable risk. Change vector immediately or we will request enforcement from Pursuit Group Bell."

Mer leaned toward the pickup. "Dockmaster Pellon, this is Captain Mer Anik. We have a hull leak in auxiliary school storage and a medical deck using rationed sealant. We are not requesting sanctuary. We are requesting exterior patch access and witness transfer for six minors whose guardians are on Halvyr."

A pause followed. Tovin could hear the soft ticks of the command ventilation.

"Request denied."

"Dockmaster, you know this ship."

The pause this time was shorter and worse.

"That is the problem, Captain."

Sela's jaw shifted.

Mer closed her eyes for one breath. When she opened them, her voice stayed level. "Say your meaning for the public record."

"I mean we know what you were built to survive. We know what your frame can hide. We know what people will do when frightened aboard a ship with that much armor. We know your children and your soup lines and your surgery deck. We also know Avar Break has two hundred dead and a packet with your throat in it. Until an authority clears you, I cannot let you put your mass beside my dock."

"People aboard this ship saved your family."

"Yes," Pellon said, and now the voice shook. "And if I let you in and you are guilty, every family here becomes mine to answer for."

Noor's fingers moved quietly. Recording. Sealing. Preserving the shape of a refusal that was not simple cowardice.

Mer said, "We will not approach your dock."

Sela snapped her head around. "Captain."

Mer cut her off with two fingers raised. Not harsh. Final.

"We will hold outside your exclusion shell," Mer continued. "Transmit any spare pressure gel you are willing to sell by drone. Transmit also a list of those minors' guardians so we can let the children hear their voices."

Another pause.

"I will ask council."

"Ask quickly."

The channel closed.

Sela unlatched from the rail and pushed toward Mer before Tovin could stop her. "We cannot patch auxiliary school storage with good wishes and legal dignity."

"Then we will use something heavier than dignity," Mer said.

"We need a clamp."

"We do not have one."

"Halvyr has twelve."

"Halvyr has fear."

"Fear does not change physics."

"It changes access to tools," Tovin said.

Sela turned on him. "You are very calm for a man standing on a leaking ship."

"I am not calm."

"No, you are contained. People keep mistaking that for virtue."

Noor looked up. "Engineering status."

Sela dragged a hand over her face and returned to the board because anger did not patch hulls either. "Auxiliary school storage breach is still small. Two millimeters along an old cargo weld, maybe more under flex. The problem is position. It sits behind the shutter actuator trunk. Interior patch only holds if we reduce acceleration to almost nothing, and if we reduce acceleration, Bell closes the geometry."

Mer said, "Time."

"At current burn, leak becomes unmanageable in four hours if the crack propagates. If we go soft, maybe twelve. If it opens under another vibration event, less than one."

Tovin looked at the approach plot. "Pursuit group reaches clean disabling range in three hours at soft burn."

"There is the sermon," Sela said. "Choose leak or guns."

"Disabling range," Noor corrected. "Not guns as a category."

"Thank you. I feel sealed already."

Mer ignored the exchange. "Can we patch from outside while holding acceleration?"

Sela gave a short laugh. "Sure. If you have a repair team that can walk a flexing hull, under burn, outside a ship everyone is afraid to touch, while drones from Bell watch for rearmament and Halvyr begs them to shoot if we twitch."

"Can it be done?"

Sela's anger faltered because Mer had not asked whether it was clever, legal, or fair.

"Badly," she said.

"Badly is a kind of possible."

"It is also a kind of dead."

Tovin said, "What does it require?"

Sela looked at him as if he had joined the conspiracy of physics. "Exterior crawler, two patch plates, pressure gel, a reinforcement spider, one person in the old shutter trunk to receive bolts, and someone in restraint to certify the actuator trunk is not being used as a weapon access path."

Noor lifted a hand. "That last part is mine."

"No. You are record. Tovin certifies."

Tovin kept his gaze on the schematic. The auxiliary school storage compartment sat three decks below a row of old lateral shutters that had once protected point-defense feeds. The weapons were gone. The access geometry remained. That was the *Mercy*'s curse: even its harmless corridors remembered harm.

"I will certify from inside the trunk," he said.

Mer turned. "You are not a hull engineer."

"No. I am the person everyone will accuse if the trunk wakes."

"That is not a qualification."

"It is the relevant burden."

Sela made a noise that could have been agreement if translated by a generous court. "He can hold a witness clamp and read a seal."

"Can he avoid blocking the bolts?"

"Probably not, but Noor can yell at him legally."

Noor said, "I can yell factually. The legal part comes later."

Mer studied the exterior map, then the pursuit clock, then Halvyr's red refusal. "Do it. No system activation near sealed weapon routes. No diagnostic beyond patch geometry. Every tool logged. Every feed open to Bell and Halvyr."

Sela stared at her. "Halvyr denied us."

"Then they can witness what denial costs."

The repair mustered in the former vehicle bay, where the *Mercy* stored stretchers, bulk grain, and the two exterior crawlers it still possessed after the demilitarization courts had taken anything that looked too much like assault equipment. The crawler named Patient Dog had three working legs and a personality made entirely of caution warnings. The crawler named Saint Ordinary had four good legs, one unreliable manipulator, and a habit of interpreting painted deck lines as navigational suggestions.

Saint Ordinary won because four legs beat temperament.

Sela went outside. Tovin did not argue. No one did, which told him everyone had already calculated the same ugly truth: no other engineer aboard knew the shutter trunk and the civilian patch systems well enough. Jessa came to the bay with a pressure suit under one arm and was turned away by Mer herself. The child's face went white, then furious.

"I know the school storage layout," Jessa said.

"You are fourteen," Mer said.

"The leak is beside my lockers."

"That does not make it yours."

"Everything on this ship is ours when adults are too slow."

Mother Kal arrived behind her, breathing hard from the climb. "Jessa."

The girl did not look back. "No."

Mother Kal put one old hand on the suit collar. "We need you inside."

"Inside doing what?"

"Remembering where the frightened children put their things when they pretend not to be frightened."

Jessa's anger cracked. It did not vanish. It became young again, which was somehow harder to watch.

"My brother's moon kite is in that storage," she said.

Mer's face softened by one degree. "Then draw us the locker row."

Jessa looked ready to refuse the smaller task just because it was smaller. Then Tovin crouched to bring his eyes level with hers.

"If the patch bolts hit a locker frame instead of the reinforcement rib, Sela loses time she does not have. Draw it badly if you need to. Draw it angry. But draw it."

Jessa swallowed. "You will not let them wake anything."

"No."

"That is not an answer. Adults say no right before they explain why yes had become complicated."

Tovin accepted the blow because it landed where truth already lived. "I will not consent to waking any weapon system to save us from a repair problem."

"Even if Bell closes."

"Even then."

"Even if I hate you."

"Especially then."

She searched his face, found no comfort there, and nodded once. "Fine."

In the shutter trunk, the air smelled of cold dust, old lubricant, and polymer insulation that had survived too many heat cycles. Tovin clipped his suit tether to the interior rail and pulled himself into the narrow service space while Noor floated below with a recorder sphere and the seal kit.

"Read seal state," Noor said.

"Lateral shutter actuator trunk seven. Weapon feed ghosts physically severed under Covenant Retrofit A-17. Point-defense barrels removed. Sensor leads capped. Power bus locked under triple witness seal."

"Visible condition?"

"Dust, old scrape marks, no recent tamper on seal wire, no heat bloom."

"Purpose of access?"

"Civilian hull repair support and bolt reception for auxiliary school storage patch."

"Operational restriction?"

"No diagnostic beyond passive continuity of the repair bolts. No power to shutter actuator. No signal into former targeting route. No tool with write permission."

Noor looked up through the hatch. Their face was upside down from Tovin's perspective, pale in the suit hood light. "Personal statement?"

"Necessary?"

"No. Useful later."

Tovin pressed his glove against the cold inner wall. On the other side, a storage room bled air molecule by molecule into the dark. Farther beyond, Sela would be on the hull with Saint Ordinary's metal feet clamped to armor that had been made to carry impacts, not apologies.

"This ship is not safer because we pretend it was never dangerous," he said. "It is safer when every dangerous memory remains witnessed."

Noor held the recorder steady for another breath. "Logged."

The exterior feed opened.

Sela's helmet camera showed the hull sliding beneath her in shallow light: plates the color of old bone, seam shadows, painted covenant marks half-erased by dust. Halvyr Dock hung ahead and below, close enough that its garden lamps looked warm. They were not warm. Nothing was warm through vacuum except the things that had not yet lost heat.

"Saint Ordinary is on the hull," Sela said. "Try not to praise it. It becomes impossible."

"No praise logged," Noor said.

"Patch plate one coming out."

Tovin braced his boots in the trunk. The first bolt guide touched the outer hull with a vibration he felt through his spine. The sound inside was small: a tick, a scrape, a pause. Not battle. Not rescue. Work.

Mer's voice came over the repair channel. "Pursuit Group Bell has requested live feed access."

Noor's eyes narrowed. "Request or demand?"

"Request under warrant observation authority."

"Give it to them," Tovin said.

Sela swore softly.

Mer said, "Objection noted?"

"I did not phrase it for court."

"Then rephrase."

Sela's helmet view tilted as she locked the first guide. "I object to being watched like a criminal while fixing a leak beside children's lockers by people with better tools and worse imagination."

Noor said, "Logged with minor cleanup pending."

The first bolt slid through.

Tovin caught its interior head with the witness clamp. His glove wanted to shake. He did not let it. The clamp read passive metal, approved alloy, correct temperature, no embedded conductor beyond the permitted stress thread.

"Bolt one clean," he said.

"Torqueing," Sela replied.

The hull answered with a long groan.

Not loud. Not dramatic. A tired animal sound traveling through steel.

On the engineering board, somewhere beyond Tovin's sight, an alarm began to pulse.

"Crack just lengthened," Sela said. Her voice lost all ornament. "Two centimeters. Gel loss increased."

Mer: "Abort?"

"If I pull off now, the plate seats wrong and the weld keeps tearing."

Tovin watched dust tremble along the sealed power bus.

Noor said, "Bell has shifted formation."

"Toward us?" Mer asked.

"Wider. One drone forward."

Sela laughed once, breathless. "They think the repair is a cover."

"It is a cover," Tovin said. "For a leak."

"This is why no one lets you comfort children."

Second guide touched. The vibration came rougher, skittering along the trunk. Tovin steadied the clamp and saw, in a flash too vivid to be memory, the same passage lit red, crew strapped in, target commands racing through bundles now severed. He had never served aboard the *Mercy* when it was armed. He had served on other ships with other corridors. Steel reused the grammar of fear.

"Tovin," Noor said. "Breathe."

He had stopped.

He pulled air through the suit feed. "Bolt two entering."

The bolt came in at an angle.

"Hold," he said.

Sela froze. "What?"

"You are off rib. Two degrees aft."

"I am on Jessa's mark."

"Jessa marked the locker. The rib flexed."

"Can you see that?"

"I can feel it."

Noor said, "Describe."

"Interior rib is under oscillation from acceleration. Bolt path will graze the old actuator housing if driven."

Silence tightened the channel.

Sela said, very carefully, "If I reposition, the crack opens more."

"If you drive, the housing damage will look like tamper."

"Will it wake anything?"

"No."

"Then I do not care what it looks like."

Tovin closed his eyes. There it was, small and perfectly shaped: the temptation not to become violent, only to become illegible. To survive in a way that left the record ugly enough for enemies to use.

Mer said, "Tovin?"

He looked at the seal wire. He looked at the bolt shadow. He imagined Jaya Soth on the *Austere Bell*, watching a live feed with officers who had already decided that old warships only ever pretended to be civilian until pressure taught them honesty.

"We care what it looks like," he said.

Sela's voice went flat. "The leak does not."

"No. But everyone deciding whether to kill us does."

For three seconds the only sound was Tovin's suit pump.

Then Sela said, "Give me a new path."

He shifted in the trunk until his shoulder jammed against the wall and his boot found a brace that had been installed for a gunner who no longer existed. "Forward one centimeter. Down half. Wait for the next flex low."

"That is not a measurement."

"It is what the ship is giving us."

"I hate mystical engineering."

"You practice it daily."

"Forward one. Down half. Waiting."

The hull groaned again, rose through the vibration, and settled.

"Now," Tovin said.

The second bolt entered clean.

Noor exhaled loudly enough that the recorder caught it.

"Do not log that as prayer," they said.

"Too late," Sela muttered. "Torqueing."

Halvyr Dock transmitted while the third bolt was being seated.

Mer opened it on command audio, perhaps because secrets had become another thing they could not afford.

"*Mercy of Kaln*," Dockmaster Pellon said, "council authorizes sale of six pressure gel cartridges by remote drone. No docking. No persons transferred. Family audio messages will be permitted if reviewed by council safety staff."

Sela's helmet camera turned slightly. Halvyr's lamps shifted across the hull.

Mer said, "We accept the gel. We object to review of family messages."

"Objection noted."

"Dockmaster."

"Captain?"

"If a child tells her mother she is afraid, do not edit that for safety."

The channel held a long human silence.

"I will not edit fear," Pellon said.

The channel closed.

Sela said, "That is almost decency."

Tovin caught the third bolt. "Almost is a busy country today."

The patch plate began to seat.

For a moment the work became purely mechanical. Saint Ordinary held position. Sela guided the reinforcement spider from its belly cradle and let it unfold across the hull like a cautious silver hand. Tovin received bolt heads, read passive seals, called alignment. Noor logged every tool, every swear, every harmless vibration that could later be arranged into suspicion by someone with enough grief and not enough shame.

Then Pursuit Group Bell spoke.

The voice was not Jaya Soth's. It belonged to a younger officer, clipped and formal.

"*Mercy of Kaln*, this is Lieutenant Arin Cho aboard *Austere Bell*. Your exterior activity is approaching a sealed actuator route. Confirm no restricted system contact."

Noor looked at Tovin.

Tovin answered before Mer had to. "Confirmed. We are receiving patch bolts through trunk seven. Weapon feeds are physically severed. Former actuator housing has not been contacted."

"Transmit interior view."

Noor's mouth tightened. "Already available on live feed."

"Transmit direct to pursuit evidence channel."

Mer said, "Comply."

Sela whispered, "Make them look at the dust."

Tovin turned his helmet camera slowly across the trunk: sealed bus, cut feeds, covenant wire, his own gloved hand holding a clamp, Noor's recorder below, the new bolt heads bright against old metal. Nothing heroic. Nothing clean. A warship's harmless intestine.

Cho said, "Evidence received. Maintain camera on seal."

Sela's next words came through clenched teeth. "If he tells me to maintain anything while my left boot is sliding, I will transmit a poem."

"Left boot?" Tovin asked.

"Minor adhesion loss."

Mer's voice sharpened. "Define minor."

"I am still attached to the ship."

"Sela."

"Saint Ordinary has three stable legs. I have one boot stable, one boot mostly rhetorical. Patch is ninety percent seated."

The acceleration shifted.

It was a tiny correction, one Mer had to make because the pursuit geometry and Halvyr's exclusion shell left no polite corridor. In command, it would have looked like a line adjusting by a fraction. In the trunk, it became a shudder through Tovin's teeth. On the hull, it became danger.

Sela grunted.

Her helmet feed swung. For half a second it showed stars, Halvyr, the pale curve of the *Mercy*'s armor, and one of Saint Ordinary's legs scraping for purchase.

"Tether," Mer said.

"Loaded," Sela snapped.

"Crawler?"

"Recovering."

The patch plate lifted at one edge.

Tovin saw bolt four twist in the guide. If it tore free, the plate could peel, widen the crack, and scatter hardware into the exact actuator housing they had spent the last minutes preserving. He reached without thinking, caught the interior guide with both hands, and slammed his shoulder against the trunk wall.

Pain flashed white.

"Tovin!" Noor shouted.

"Hold outside," he said.

"I am trying," Sela said, all anger burned down to effort.

The bolt guide bucked against his clamp. His suit joint screamed a pressure warning. The old gunner brace dug into his boot. Somewhere inside the ship, children were being moved away from auxiliary storage. Somewhere outside, Halvyr watched from behind red lights. Somewhere behind them, armed ships watched for the first sign that the *Mercy* had lied about what it wanted.

Tovin held the guide and understood, with humiliating clarity, that restraint was not the absence of force. His whole body was force. Sela's crawler was force. Mer's vector was force. Noor's recorders were force. The question was not whether they would exert pressure on the world, but whether the pressure would remain answerable.

"Now," he said through his teeth.

Sela drove the fourth bolt.

It seated.

The reinforcement spider clamped.

The leak alarm dropped from pulse to steady yellow.

No one spoke for five breaths.

Then Sela said, "Patch holds."

Noor put one hand over their face, which was not a legal gesture and therefore went unclassified.

Mer's voice came very quiet. "Casualties?"

"My dignity," Sela said. "Saint Ordinary's paint. Tovin's shoulder, probably."

"Tovin?"

He tested his arm. Pain answered, but the suit showed no breach. "Functional."

"That is not the same as uninjured."

"It is what we have."

The exterior feed steadied. Halvyr Dock's drone emerged at last, a small boxy machine carrying six pressure gel cartridges on a line. It crossed the exclusion distance slowly, as if embarrassed by its own caution. No escort came with it. No berth opened. But the drone came.

Mother Kal's voice entered the channel from the school deck. "The children are ready for family audio when permitted."

Mer said, "Thank you."

Jessa spoke behind her, farther from the pickup. "Did the moon kite survive?"

Sela turned her helmet toward the patched storage wall as if she could see through armor and lockers and fear. "Tell Jessa the kite owes me a thank-you note."

Mother Kal repeated it. In the background, someone laughed once and then began to cry.

The *Austere Bell* transmitted a final observation line.

REPAIR ACTIVITY LOGGED. NO RESTRICTED SYSTEM ACTIVATION OBSERVED. PURSUIT STATUS UNCHANGED.

Noor read it aloud with no expression.

Sela, still on the hull, said, "They watched us not betray them and learned nothing."

Tovin unclipped the witness clamp from bolt four. His shoulder throbbed in time with the yellow leak alarm. "They learned we can be watched."

"That is your victory?"

"No. It is our condition."

Mer ordered the ship to resume its careful line outside Halvyr's exclusion shell. The dock remained red. The pressure gel drone crept nearer. Pursuit Group Bell kept its lawful distance. In the repaired storage compartment, the patch held over children's spare clothes, broken slates, a paper star chart, and one moon kite folded beside a locker row drawn by an angry girl who had been useful because anger had not made her careless.

Tovin stayed in the trunk until Noor finished the seal inspection. He did not trust his face yet.

"You should see medical," Noor said.

"After the report."

"That is stupid."

"Record your objection."

"I am tired of recording obvious things."

He looked at them then. Noor's eyes were rimmed red, not from tears, from focus and bad air and the work of turning fear into language that might survive hostile reading.

"Pellon was not wrong," Tovin said.

Noor's expression hardened. "About denying children a port?"

"About knowing us being part of the fear."

"That is generous."

"No. It is operational."

Noor glanced up at the sealed bus. "You think familiarity makes the accusation stronger."

"For Halvyr, yes. They have seen the hospital decks. They have also stood beside the armor. They know both are true."

"And Bell?"

"Bell is worse. They know the doctrine. They are being asked to believe a doctrine-shaped lie because it fits the part of us they were trained to fear."

Noor closed the seal kit. "Then we need a record shaped like the whole ship."

"Yes."

"A limping one."

He almost smiled. It hurt his shoulder. "A carrying one."

They floated out of the trunk into the corridor where the air was warmer and people were pretending not to wait for him. Jessa stood at the hatch with Mother Kal behind her. She held a folded drawing slate against her chest like a shield.

"The patch held?" she asked.

"Yes."

"The kite?"

"Apparently demanding stationery."

Her mouth twitched, then closed before it could become a smile. "You kept your promise."

"This time."

She frowned. "That is a terrible answer."

"It is the only honest one I have."

Mother Kal studied him for a long moment. "Honest answers frighten children less when adults keep giving them."

Tovin did not know how to respond to that, so he nodded.

The family audio messages from Halvyr arrived an hour later, reviewed but uncut. Children listened in the school deck with privacy curtains that hid nothing from grief. A mother apologized for not opening a dock she did not control. A father sang half a counting song and broke before seven. An older sister told her brother that fear was allowed but cruelty was not, then had to stop because she was crying too hard to continue.

The *Mercy* did not dock.

No public clearing arrived.

No accusation withdrew.

But the patch held. The children slept under a ceiling that no longer hissed. Halvyr's red lights followed them until distance made judgment indistinguishable from any other star.

When Tovin finally returned to the restraint gallery, the etched covenant line above the dead boards seemed less like a prayer than a maintenance instruction.

NO WEAPON MAY WAKE WITHOUT PUBLIC WITNESS.

He rested his good hand below it, not touching the glass.

"And no mercy either," he said.

The ship carried the words nowhere. It only kept moving.

Chapter 9: The Shape of Nonviolence

The *Mercy of Kaln* held a crew assembly in the cargo chapel because it was the only compartment large enough for anger and still close enough to the spine lifts for evacuation.

Cargo chapel was not its official name. Officially, the space was Convertible Hold Two, rated for food sacks, machinery crates, stretcher overflow, civic hearings, quarantine tents, and temporary sleeping lines during refugee surges. But years of convoy work had given it a different gravity. People had married there under plastic emergency lamps. People had identified bodies there because the medical deck had run out of rooms. Children had built paper cities between strapped grain pallets. Dock delegates had shouted law at each other across ration tables. A machine-person choir from Oriel Sump had once sung through borrowed speakers while half the audience pretended not to cry.

After Avar Break, after Halvyr's refusal, after the hull patch that had kept children's air inside only because Sela had walked an accused warship's skin under pursuit, the hold became a chapel again because nobody trusted a meeting room to contain what needed saying.

Tovin stood on the low cargo dais with his injured shoulder bound under his jacket. The medical tech had used a compression brace and called him lucky in a tone that suggested luck was another damaged system aboard. He could lift his hand to chest height. Above that, pain arrived with documentation.

The hold was full.

Crew stood by work group, not rank. That was always how fear sorted people when formal order thinned. Engineering clustered around Sela near the port bulkhead, grease still under nails, tool straps still on thighs, Saint Ordinary's scraped leg propped beside them like a wounded witness. Hospital staff stood together with sealed carts at their backs, faces drawn from rationed sleep. School deck families took the center mats, children in front because adults thought children noticed less from there. They were wrong. Convoy passengers lined the rear, caught between gratitude and the animal knowledge that gratitude did not make armor less visible from outside.

Noor had insisted on open recording. Three blue lights hovered above the dais.

Captain Mer Anik stood at Tovin's left. She had offered to speak first. He had refused. That refusal had cost him almost as much as the shoulder. There were responsibilities command could carry. This one had his fingerprints on it.

Sela stood with her arms folded.

She had not looked at him since he entered.

Mer opened the assembly anyway. "We are outside Halvyr's exclusion shell, under pursuit, with auxiliary school storage patched and holding. We have pressure gel for two more emergency plates. We do not have dock access. We do not have public clearing. We do not have permission to transfer passengers. We have a technical bundle under review by Pursuit Group Bell. We have no evidence that review will arrive in time to help us."

The hold took that in with small movements: a shift of boots, a hand closing over a child's shoulder, an engineer's jaw tightening.

"This assembly is not a vote to rearm," Mer continued.

That produced sound.

Not a shout. Worse. A collective exhale edged with betrayal from people who had hoped the meeting existed because command had become practical at last.

Mer let it pass.

"This assembly is not a performance for Bell, Halvyr, or any court. It is for this ship to understand what choices remain. Tovin Rees will explain restraint doctrine as it applies to our present condition."

Someone in the back said, "Doctrine won't seal a breach."

Sela did not turn, but her voice cut clean across the hold. "No. People seal breaches. While doctrine watches."

The words drew a dangerous murmur.

Tovin stepped forward before Mer could intervene. His shoulder objected; he used the pain as a metronome.

"Sela is right," he said.

That quieted them more effectively than correction would have.

"Doctrine did not seal the breach. Sela did. Saint Ordinary did. Noor did. Jessa's locker drawing did. Mer's vector choices did. Halvyr's six gel cartridges helped. So did the old armor. So did the fact that no one powered a sealed actuator route when damage made that convenient."

Sela's expression did not change.

Tovin looked across the hold. He tried to count faces and failed. Too many people. Too many separate fears wearing one ship's name.

"Restraint is often described badly," he said. "People call it pacifism when they want it to sound noble. People call it weakness when they want it to sound stupid. People call it nonviolence when they want to stop thinking about the kinds of force that still remain. None of those words are enough aboard this ship."

Noor's recorder light blinked.

"The *Mercy* is not harmless," Tovin said.

Children looked up. Adults looked away.

"Even demilitarized, this hull can kill. Our mass can crush a dock if Mer makes a bad approach. Our engines can cook a tug that drifts too close. Our repair choices can damage sealed routes and make a court believe we lied. Our medical rationing can decide who breathes easily and who waits. Our silence can become a weapon if we let someone else define us. Our fear can become a weapon if we spend it on the nearest person with less power."

A hospital orderly named Vennit lifted a hand without waiting to be called. "Then what is the difference? If everything is force, why are some forces forbidden and others permitted?"

"Answerability," Tovin said.

The word fell too abstract. He heard it fail.

Sela gave a short laugh. "There it is. The shape of survival: a word you can put in a report."

"No," Tovin said. "A shape you can inspect."

He turned to Noor. "Can you put the repair record up?"

Noor flicked two fingers. The hold's forward wall lit with images from the patch: Sela on the hull, Tovin in the trunk, the seal wire, the bolt heads, Saint Ordinary's four-legged crouch, the patch plate seating over the crack.

"This was force," Tovin said. "We pressed metal against metal. We changed the ship. Sela put herself outside under burn. Mer altered vector. I held a bolt guide hard enough to injure myself. None of that was gentle."

He raised his good hand toward the image of the sealed bus.

"But every part of the force had a witness, a limit, a purpose smaller than domination, and a record that someone else could challenge. We used only what the leak required. We stopped short of systems that would change the legal and physical nature of the ship. If I had allowed that bolt to graze the old actuator housing because the leak did not care how the record looked, we might still be alive. We would also have handed our enemies a truer lie."

Mother Kal, seated on an old crate at the front with Jessa beside her, said, "A truer lie."

"A lie built from real damage," Tovin said. "The kind that survives longer."
