One of the convoy passengers stood. He was a broad man with belt miner's shoulders and a face made hollow by the last two days. Tovin remembered his name after a moment: Daro Kelm, oxygen still mechanic from the cracked barge *Sincere Weather*.

"My sister died at Avar Break," Daro said.

The hold shifted around him. Space opened. Not much. Enough for grief to stand without bumping elbows.

"She was on the fuel string," Daro continued. "I came aboard this ship because my barge lost scrubbers before the packet named you. Your doctor fixed my boy's lungs yesterday. Your engineer saved the school deck today. I know all that. But if the people who killed my sister are using your name, and you refuse to become dangerous enough to survive long enough to prove it, what do I call that?"

No one answered for Tovin. He was grateful and wished someone would.

"You call it a failure if we die," he said.

Mer looked at him sharply.

Tovin did not soften it.

"Restraint does not guarantee survival. It is not magic. It is not moral insurance. It does not make grief fair. If we die because we would not rearm, the dead will not rise to applaud our consistency."

Daro's mouth tightened. "Then why ask us to accept it?"

"Because rearming does not guarantee survival either. It changes what kind of danger we become before the end."

"I am less worried about what we become than who catches us."

"So am I."

Sela uncrossed her arms. "Then say the useful part. If Bell closes, if a drone misreads us, if Valech's people send another packet, if Halvyr's council tells every dock we are plague, how does this restraint get us to proof? Not to a cleaner conscience. Proof."

That was the question. It had always been the question under every argument aboard.

Tovin nodded to Noor.

The forward wall changed from repair images to a route plot. The *Mercy* sat as a blue shape outside Halvyr's exclusion shell. Pursuit Group Bell appeared in white. Ahead, the chart broke into debris lanes, small stations, abandoned buoys, insurance markers, militia claims, legal dead zones, and one thin route through Cinderwake Lane toward Gethsemane Buoy Court.

"We cannot outrun Bell in open vector," Tovin said. "We cannot dock at frightened ports. We cannot broadcast the technical bundle publicly without giving whoever framed us time to poison its chain or trigger a second incident against a station that helped us."

Murmurs rose at that. Tovin waited.

"We can survive routes that armed pursuit ships dislike," he continued. "The *Mercy* has armor and mass. We can move through debris weather at speeds smaller ships avoid. We can protect civilian craft if we encounter them. We can create records in public infrastructure that is harder to edit than a private transmission. Gethsemane Buoy Court is not a court in the old planetary sense. It is a moving arbitration stack distributed across salvage buoys, old cargo memories, station relays, and witness knots. It cannot clear us alone. But it can preserve evidence in enough places that a single command channel cannot erase it."

Sela studied the plot despite herself.

"Cinderwake Lane first," she said.

"Yes."

"That lane is worse since the shear report."

"Yes."

"And we just patched a school-storage leak."

"Yes."

"And Bell will think we are using debris to hide rearmament."

"Yes."

"Your plan is to prove we are not pirates by flying like desperate pirates into a trash storm."

"My plan is to use what this ship still has that is not a gun."

"Armor."

"Armor."

"Mass."

"Mass."

"Damage tolerance."

"Carefully."

"And patience."

"If we can afford it."

Sela looked at the people around her: engineers who trusted her more than they trusted covenant sentences, passengers who wanted protection but did not want to be transformed into accomplices, children who had learned too many adult words this week.

"This is a slow suicide," she said.

The hold went still.

Tovin accepted the words because refusing them would insult the people who had thought them privately.

"It can become one," he said.

"No. It is one. You are choosing a set of limits under conditions where the enemy has not agreed to limits. That is a method of dying politely."

"Sometimes."

"Do not give me sometimes."

"All right. Sometimes restraint is cowardice disguised as principle. Sometimes it is command avoiding guilt by letting physics do the killing. Sometimes it is a way for people with clean rooms to tell people in danger that their suffering looks admirable from a distance."

Sela's anger flickered, thrown off rhythm by agreement.

Tovin continued. "That is why restraint cannot be a mood. It has to be designed. Measured. Revised. Argued with by the people it endangers."

He pointed at the route plot.

"This is not a vow to do nothing. It is a constraint map. We list what we will not do because it would make us the thing the frame requires. Then we list what we will do, hard and immediately, to keep people alive and reach proof."

Noor raised a hand. "For record clarity, that list exists in draft."

Several people laughed weakly, not because it was funny but because Noor's faith in drafts had survived worse conditions than some gods.

Mer nodded. "Read it."

Noor brought up a new board.

PERMITTED UNDER RESTRAINT SURVIVAL DOCTRINE - EMERGENCY DRAFT

1. Maneuver through debris lanes within civilian hull tolerance.
2. Use armor and shield remnants for passive protection only.
3. Deploy repair crawlers and medical drones under open witness.
4. Interpose hull mass to shield civilian craft if doing so does not create intentional collision risk.
5. Jam no lawful pursuit signal, but annotate and publicly mirror all received orders.
6. Refuse custody orders that would disappear witnesses outside recorded process.
7. Preserve evidence through distributed civilian legal infrastructure.
8. Accept pursuit observation when it does not endanger passengers or sealed systems.
9. Do not activate, simulate, reconstruct, route around, test, or symbolically threaten any weapon system.
10. Record dissent.

The tenth line did more work than Tovin had expected. Heads lifted.

Sela read it twice. "Record dissent."

"Yes," Tovin said.

"So later a court can admire how fair you were while ignoring us?"

"So later a court cannot pretend the ship agreed with one voice. So command cannot pretend fear was absent. So if restraint fails, the record shows who warned us and why."

Daro Kelm said, "And if rearmament becomes the only way?"

Noor answered before Tovin could. "Then whoever claims that will have to say which weapon, what target, what authority, what witness, what civilian risk, what exit condition, and why every lesser force has failed."

Sela gave Noor a hard look. "You have a form."

"I have several."

"Of course you do."

Tovin said, "Aboard the *Mercy*, there is no such thing as 'just wake the guns.' There are only specific actions with specific consequences. If anyone reaches for that choice, we slow it down enough to see it."

"Slowing gets us killed," an engineer named Harl said from behind Sela.

"Speed has killed plenty of people too," Mother Kal said.

Harl flushed. "I did not mean-"

"You meant you are afraid. Say that instead."

He looked at the floor. "I am afraid."

The admission moved through the hold with more force than a shout. It made permission.

"I am afraid too," said one of the school aides.

"I'm angry," Daro said.

"I want to get off this ship," said a passenger near the rear.

"I want them to try boarding," muttered an engineering tech.

Mer's head turned. The tech paled but did not retract it.

Sela stepped away from the bulkhead and climbed onto a low crate so more people could see her. Her voice, when it came, was rougher than before.

"I put my boots on that hull today. I watched a dock that owes us lives keep its red lights on. I heard Bell ask for camera angles while my tether loaded. I do not trust courts to catch up before vacuum does. I do not trust admirals. I do not trust pretty rules written after wars by people who wanted to sleep." She looked at Tovin. "I do not trust him enough to let him be the only conscience on this ship."

"Good," Tovin said.

"I am not finished."

"I assumed."

That drew a strained laugh from some corner of hospital staff.

Sela pointed at the list. "But I trust tools I can inspect. If restraint is a tool, then engineering inspects it. Not spiritually. Actually. We get abort criteria. We get damage thresholds. We get authority to call out when the constraint map becomes a murder map."

Mer said, "Agreed."

Tovin felt the shape of the room alter. Not lighter. More load-bearing.

Sela kept going. "And no more treating sealed systems like sacred ghosts only he can interpret. If a trunk, bus, cabinet, or old route matters to survival or accusation, engineering sees diagrams. Legal sees seals. Ixo sees lineage. Mother Kal sees whatever the ship did before we painted it green. We all stop acting like ignorance is safety."

That one hurt more than Tovin expected.

Because she was right.

He had kept certain histories narrow out of fear that knowledge could become temptation. He still believed knowledge could become temptation. The problem was that ignorance had become its own weapon, aimed inward.

"Agreed," he said.

Mer looked at him, then at Noor. "Draft amendment."

Noor's fingers were already moving.

Jessa raised her hand.

The adults' attention turned with the awkward care of people who knew children had been listening the whole time and were ashamed to remember it so late.

"Do children get dissent?" she asked.

Noor blinked. "Yes."

"Not through parents."

"Yes."

"Not as a school exercise adults summarize."

Noor looked at Mer.

Mer said, "Children old enough to ask for record can give record with Mother Kal or another chosen adult present. No one edits content except for private medical information the child asks to keep out."

Jessa considered this like a contract negotiator. "Can we say adults are being stupid?"

"If you can support the claim," Noor said.

Mother Kal made a small sound that was almost approval.

Jessa nodded and sat down.

The assembly might have ended there if the ship had been kind.

The ship was not kind. It was old, pursued, and honest in the brutal way of mechanisms.

The alert came from navigation first: three soft tones, a pause, then Mer's command slate flashing amber.

She read it and the hold watched her face close.

"Pursuit Group Bell is altering formation," she said.

Noor pulled the feed onto the forward wall. The route plot updated. One of Bell's drones moved ahead of the formation, not toward the *Mercy* directly but toward the mouth of Cinderwake Lane.

Tovin's stomach tightened.

Sela climbed down from the crate. "Blocking?"

"Marking," Noor said. "It is dropping lawful hazard pings along the lane entrance."

"Meaning?"

Tovin read the pattern. It was clever. Lawful. Ugly.

"If we enter Cinderwake now, the record can say we ignored fresh hazard markers from pursuit authority," he said.

Mer's voice was flat. "And if we do not enter?"

Noor traced the geometry. "Bell reaches revised disabling envelope in two hours forty."

The hold's new steadiness cracked.

Harl said, "They heard us."

"They had the route plot?" Daro asked.

Noor checked the channels. "No. But they have our vector, Halvyr's exclusion field, and a competent navigator."

Sela looked at Tovin. Not triumphant. Not kind. Demanding.

"Constraint map," she said.

He looked at the board, at the amber drone, at the hazard pings laying law like caltrops in front of the only route that matched their strengths.

"We do not jam the pings," he said.

Sela's mouth tightened.

"We do not spoof the drone. We do not threaten it. We mirror the pings publicly with our own annotation: hazard acknowledged, pursuit placement recorded, medical and civilian status attached, reason for intended transit stated."

"That is paperwork," Harl said.

"No," Noor said, suddenly sharp. "That is making their trap visible."

Tovin nodded. "Then we enter Cinderwake at reduced leading edge, armor-first, with school deck moved inward, hospital carts locked, and exterior patch under constant stress scan. We invite Bell to provide alternate safe custody terms for all passengers and witnesses before we cross the first marker."

Mer understood immediately. "They cannot."

"Not without solving the problem they are using to corner us."

Daro's eyes narrowed. "And if they offer?"

"Then we examine it," Tovin said.

"You would surrender?"

"If they offer recorded, distributed, enforceable custody that protects passengers, evidence, Ixo's standing, and the ship's logs from disappearance, yes."

The hold reacted loudly to that.

Sela raised both hands. "Listen to the whole impossible sentence before you panic."

"Thank you," Tovin said.

"It was not a compliment."

"Still."

Mer stepped forward. Command returned to her like a tool picked up from a table. "Assembly continues in work groups. Engineering drafts damage thresholds for Cinderwake transit. Legal drafts public annotation and custody challenge. Medical and school deck prepare inward shelter. Passenger delegates select two observers for command feed access. Dissent stations open in cargo chapel under Noor's recorders. We move in forty minutes unless a better lawful survival path appears."

No one cheered. That would have been obscene.

But people moved.

That was enough.

The hold broke into purposeful disorder. Sela's engineers dragged a portable table under the route display and began arguing in numbers. Noor pulled two assistants from passenger delegates and started building language dense enough to irritate anyone trying to simplify it later. Mother Kal organized the school deck with a voice that made panic feel impolite. Daro Kelm stood for a long moment as if unsure whether grief allowed him to be useful, then went to the oxygen carts and began checking seals with the competence of a man who knew machines did not care why hands shook.

Tovin remained on the dais until Sela came to him.

For a moment they watched the room work around them.

"I still think this can kill us," she said.

"I know."

"Do you?"

"Yes."

"Not as an abstract burden. Not as a beautiful sentence. Do you know that I may have to tell Jessa her moon kite survived Halvyr and died in Cinderwake because we kept the guns asleep?"

He looked at her then. "Yes."

"And?"

"And if we wake them, I may have to tell her the kite survived because we made every frightened station right about us."

Sela's eyes were bright with exhaustion and fury. "I hate that those are real choices."

"So do I."

"That does not help."

"No."

She looked back at the engineers. "Send me the sealed route diagrams."

"I will."

"All of them."

He hesitated.

She saw it and almost smiled, but there was no humor in it. "There. That little pause. That is why I do not trust you enough."

He accepted the hit. "All of them."

"Today."

"Today."

She nodded once and returned to her people.

Noor approached from the other side with a slate already crowded by clauses. "The public annotation will be ugly."

"Good."

"I mean legally ugly. It accuses pursuit authority of creating necessity while still acknowledging their hazard markers as valid."

"Can it stand?"

"On one leg, in a crosswind, if nobody kicks it."

"That may be our house style."

Noor glanced toward Sela. "She is right about ignorance."

"I know."

"You have a habit of making yourself the lock."

Tovin watched the route wall. The drone's hazard pings blinked one by one across Cinderwake's mouth, patient and lawful and placed by people who might believe they were preventing a disaster rather than arranging one.

"Locks are useful," he said.

"Until everyone else is trapped on the wrong side."

He did not answer.

The custody challenge went out twenty-six minutes later.

It was transmitted to Pursuit Group Bell, Halvyr Dock, three nearby salvage relays, two independent convoy unions, Gethsemane Buoy Court's nearest dormant stack, and every civilian receiver within weak-band range. It acknowledged the hazard pings. It attached the *Mercy*'s civilian manifest, medical status, patch record, sealed-system live feed, technical mismatch bundle summary, and a request for safe recorded custody terms that would preserve passengers, logs, machine-person testimony, and evidence chain integrity.

It ended with Noor's least favorite sentence and Tovin's most necessary one.

IF NO SUCH TERMS ARE PROVIDED BEFORE TRANSIT WINDOW CLOSE, MERCY OF KALN WILL ENTER CINDERWAKE LANE UNDER WITNESSED RESTRAINT SURVIVAL DOCTRINE, ACCEPTING PUBLIC REVIEW OF ALL NONRESTRICTED ACTIONS AND REFUSING ALL UNWITNESSED ESCALATION.

For eleven minutes, no one answered.

Then the *Austere Bell* replied.

HAZARD PINGS ARE LAWFUL. ENTRY INTO MARKED LANE IS NOT ADVISED. PURSUIT GROUP CANNOT ACCEPT PASSENGER TRANSFER AT CURRENT RISK. PURSUIT GROUP CANNOT GUARANTEE MACHINE WITNESS CUSTODY OUTSIDE ACTIVE WARRANT TERMS. PURSUIT GROUP REPEATS ORDER: MAINTAIN CURRENT VECTOR AND PREPARE FOR DISABLING INTERCEPT.

Noor read the response twice.

"They refused the impossible sentence," Sela said.

"They documented the refusal," Noor replied.

Mer gave the order to enter Cinderwake Lane.

The old battleship turned slowly, not like a predator, not like a martyr, but like a large damaged thing choosing the only corridor where its remaining strengths did not have to become guns. Across the ship, children moved inward. Carts locked. Engineers crawled through diagrams Tovin should have shared earlier. Dissent records opened in cargo chapel and began receiving the fear of people who had decided movement was better than waiting but wanted history to know they had not mistaken terror for consent.

Tovin returned to the restraint gallery.

The forward display showed Cinderwake Lane ahead, a ribbon of metallic weather and old war fragments catching thin light. Hazard pings blinked at its mouth. Bell's drone watched from the edge. Halvyr's red dock faded behind.

Sela's voice came over engineering. "Patch stress within tolerance. Armor leading edge ready. Sealed route diagrams received."

A pause.

"I am going to be angry about their condition later."

"Understood," Tovin said.

Noor settled into the legal station. "Public mirror is live."

Mer stood at command with both hands on the rail. "Tovin?"

He looked up at the covenant line.

NO WEAPON MAY WAKE WITHOUT PUBLIC WITNESS.

He thought of Sela's accusation, Daro's grief, Jessa's demand for children's dissent, Noor's warning about locks, Halvyr's red refusal, Bell's lawful trap, and the old ship's many ways of exerting force without admitting it.

"Restraint gallery confirms," he said. "No weapon wake. All permitted force under witness."

Mer nodded.

"Cinderwake entry in ten," navigation called.

The ship crossed the first hazard ping with its guns asleep and its records open.

Nobody aboard mistook that for peace.

Chapter 10: Valech Speaks for the Dead

Commodore Renn Valech kept the names of the Avar Break dead in the wrong order on purpose.

The official casualty file sorted by vessel, duty, legal relationship to the convoy string, and body confirmation status. That was how the Ash Belt counted disaster when it expected lawyers. Valech's version began with the youngest confirmed dead, then the oldest, then the people whose remains were too heat-scattered for confident numbering, then the missing, then the names of ships because the Belt mourned ships in a manner committees found embarrassing and politically useful.

He had learned years ago that grief entered public blood more quickly when order felt human rather than administrative.

The broadcast chamber aboard the command cruiser *Civic Spear* had been converted from an admiral's dining room after the Fracture armistice. Its long table remained bolted under the deck plates. Its viewport had been sealed behind matte acoustic panels. The walls carried no insignia except the convoy authority seal and a narrow memorial ribbon from the old wars: black, gray, black. Valech had objected to removing the ribbon when the communications staff suggested it made the room look severe.

"Severity is not a flaw," he had told them.

Now he stood beneath that ribbon while three cameras and forty-seven relay feeds calibrated his face.

He was sixty-one standard, broad through the shoulders, close-shaved, with a voice that made younger officers stand straighter even before rank entered the room. His uniform was convoy gray rather than fleet black, a compromise color meant to reassure stations that command served commerce and rescue, not war. The compromise had always irritated him. The Belt survived by pretending gray was not just black with dust on it.

An aide named Pellis stood beside the camera stack with the casualty slate hugged to her chest. She had been a legal historian before emergency service made everyone useful in worse ways. Her eyes were swollen. That helped the room. Valech did not like that he noticed.

"Commodore," she said, "the unions are asking whether you will name the *Mercy* directly."

"I will name the dead directly."

"That is not an answer."

"It is the one they deserve first."

Pellis looked down at the slate. "The third list still contains unconfirmed minors."

"Mark them as missing in the audio."

"Their families asked us not to use them."

Valech turned from the camera lights.

Pellis did not step back. He respected that in the abstract and found it inconvenient in the moment.

"Which families?" he asked.

"Two from *Avar Thistle*. One from *Bright Load*. One guardianship group from the fuel tender. They do not want names used in a political statement before body confirmation."

"This is not a political statement."

Pellis held his gaze.

Valech exhaled through his nose. "Remove the unconfirmed minors. Keep the count."

She nodded once, not victory, not relief. "Thank you."

"Do not thank me for obeying the dead badly."

"No, sir."

The chief signal officer raised two fingers. "Live in ninety."

Valech faced the black lens.

In another part of the Belt, the *Mercy of Kaln* was entering Cinderwake Lane under public annotation. Pursuit Group Bell had failed to box it cleanly outside Halvyr, which meant Admiral Soth had chosen observation where a bolder commander might have chosen disabling fire. The casualty feeds were already turning confused: dock councils asking whether refusal of sanctuary counted as complicity; convoy unions arguing about whether hazard pings had become a trap; salvage channels replaying the *Mercy*'s repair footage because people liked watching accused things perform innocence under stress.

That was dangerous.

Not because Valech feared innocence. He told himself that twice and did not examine why repetition had become necessary.

It was dangerous because grief dispersed when given too many questions. Avar Break needed shape. The Belt needed command before ten thousand small jurisdictions turned one massacre into a symposium.

"Live in ten," said the signal officer.

Valech lowered his eyes to the first name.

He did not need the slate. He had memorized them.

The red light opened.

"My name is Renn Valech. I speak tonight as Convoy Commodore of the Ash Belt Emergency Routes, under authority of the Interstation Safety Compact and the post-Fracture convoy covenants. I speak also as a man who has spent forty years reading casualty lists and has never once seen a list become easier because we read it politely."

He waited a measured breath.

"At Avar Break, two hundred and thirteen people are confirmed dead. Forty-one remain missing. Twelve bodies are currently identified by ship effect rather than name. Six vessels are destroyed beyond registry continuity. The dead were fuel tenders, pilots, cooks, scrubber hands, convoy accountants, pressure welders, children traveling under guardianship, two medics, one union courier, and a dock singer who had no assigned duty except keeping frightened crews from hearing their own engines too loudly."

The words left him with practiced weight.

"I will read some names."

He read thirty.

Not all. All would take too long for public anger and too little for private grief. Thirty was enough to make the dead particular and the missing unbearable. He included old Anset who had served in three convoy famines, a child named Lio who had been moved from one doomed barge to another because a pressure bunk had looked safer there, the dock singer, two welders, and Captain Nima Osett of *Avar Thistle*, whose last transmitted order had been for the rear barges to cut loose and scatter.

When he finished, he looked up without wiping his eyes because he had not allowed tears. Tears on command were useful once. Twice became theater.

"These people did not die in a natural shear. They did not die because the Belt is hard, although the Belt is hard. They did not die because convoy crews accept risk, although they do. They died in an attack that exploited the gaps between our authorities: civil docks afraid to arm, armed commands afraid to overstep, demilitarized hulls trusted as symbols while still carrying the mass and architecture of war, and evidence systems old enough to be manipulated by anyone who understands our shame."

He did not say *Mercy of Kaln* yet.

Let them lean toward it.

"The surviving packet from Avar Break names a ship. That evidence is under review. The accused ship contests it. Technical disputes are ongoing. I will not pre-judge a court."

That line was for Soth, and for anyone later counting fairness.

"But I will judge a system that leaves convoy dead waiting for perfect certainty while more ships enter danger under flags nobody can enforce. I will judge a system where a station can refuse a wounded vessel, a pursuit group can be forced to watch hazards multiply, and civilian families are asked to distinguish between protection and threat while debris is already crossing their windows."

His right hand rested on the casualty slate.

"The Ash Belt requires armed convoy command."

There it was.

He let silence make room for objections before he crushed them with care.

"Not warlord command. Not private militia tolls. Not salvage kings deciding who breathes. Armed convoy command under public charter, with standing authority to escort, interdict, board, disable, and when no lesser force will preserve civilian life, destroy. Command that does not have to beg every frightened dock for permission to protect the next fuel string. Command that does not outsource survival to old warships wearing hospital paint. Command accountable after action, yes. But command capable during action."

The words were clean. He had sharpened them for years.

"Some will say this is escalation. They are correct. Civilization is also escalation against vacuum. Law is escalation against revenge. A pressure seal is escalation against death. The question is not whether we escalate. The question is whether we do so in public, under charter, with names attached, or whether we continue to let fear escalate privately in every corridor until the next Avar Break teaches us the same lesson with new bodies."

Pellis stood outside camera range, very still.

Valech shifted his attention slightly toward feed two, the union relay.

"To the families of the dead: I cannot promise speed will honor grief. I cannot promise command will undo loss. I can promise that I will not let your dead be used as an excuse for paralysis disguised as caution."

Now.

"To the crew of the *Mercy of Kaln*: if you are innocent, submit to controlled custody and allow lawful command to preserve your passengers, logs, and machine witness under emergency seal. If you are guilty, every kilometer you travel under a civilian flag deepens your crime. If you are being used by forces older and uglier than your current crew, then stop letting those forces hide behind your covenant."

He leaned one fraction closer.

"To Admiral Jaya Soth: the Belt sees your restraint. The Belt also sees the dead. Pursuit authority exists to prevent the next casualty list, not merely to annotate it."

The signal officer's eyes flicked down. That line would travel fast.

Valech returned to center.

"I am forwarding an emergency charter petition to the Compact councils within the hour. It will request temporary armed convoy command for the Avar corridor, Cinderwake approaches, and adjacent refugee routes until the evidence chain is resolved and replacement protections are in place. I ask dock councils, convoy unions, survivor families, and station governors to sign."

He lifted the slate.

"If you oppose it, read the names first."

He read the final seven names slowly, each chosen for a different political wound: an Earth-born ration clerk, a Mars-trained pilot, a Belt child, an Orison machine-maintenance novice, two old Fracture veterans who had died on opposite sides before sharing a convoy bunk, and Nima Osett.

"For the dead," he said. "For the next living convoy."

The red light closed.

No one in the broadcast chamber moved.

Then the room became work.

Feeds split. Questions arrived. Union channels demanded the full charter text. Dock councils asked whether temporary armed command would override local exclusion authority. A survivor group from Avar Break asked for the complete names of missing persons and then, in the same packet, signed the petition before reading the attachment. Three militia captains offered ships within five minutes. Two of them were criminals under other names. One was worse: an honest man with a clean ship and no patience for courts.

Pellis lowered the casualty slate. "You put Soth in a corner."

Valech accepted a water bulb from another aide. "Soth is an admiral. Corners are part of architecture."

"You publicly framed restraint as delay."

"I publicly framed dead civilians as dead civilians."

"You did both."

He drank. The water tasted faintly of tank metal. Everything aboard ships did, eventually.

"The petition text," he said.

Pellis did not move. "The families whose minors you removed will hear the count."

"Yes."

"They will know."

"Yes."

"You decided the count mattered more than their timing."

Valech looked at her. "I decided the next convoy mattered too."

"That is the sentence everyone uses when the current grief is inconvenient."

The chief signal officer pretended to be busy with an already stable feed.

Valech could have dismissed Pellis. He did not. Useful dissent, if kept close, prevented messier surprises.

"Draft a private apology under my seal," he said.

"Sincere?"

"Do not become childish."

"That was a real question."

He put the empty water bulb down. "Yes. Sincere. Also not retracting the count."

Pellis nodded, accepting the limits of victory again. "The charter text is ready for your review."

"Send it to my wall."

The chamber's side display filled with language that had been waiting for a disaster large enough to bear it.

TEMPORARY ARMED CONVOY COMMAND PETITION

Valech read the first paragraphs with the odd sensation of encountering his own old thoughts wearing fresh blood. Unified escort authority. Emergency boarding power. Restricted disabling fire. Civilian witness officers attached where possible. Machine testimony provision deferred to later committee review. Salvage militias subject to command integration. Demilitarized combat hulls placed under armed-command inspection or exclusion. Evidence disputes subordinated to route protection during active threat.

There.

"Remove 'where possible' from civilian witness officers," he said.

Pellis looked surprised.

"Replace with 'except under immediate kinetic threat, with written justification afterward.'"

She made the edit.

"Machine testimony provision," he continued. "Change 'deferred to later committee review' to 'admissible for operational risk assessment, legal standing reserved.'"

"That will anger the human-only councils."

"They were born angry."

"It will also help the *Mercy*'s machine witness."

Valech's jaw tightened. "It will help command not look archaic."

Pellis made the edit.

He studied the clause about demilitarized combat hulls. This was the blade. Too blunt and everyone would see revenge. Too soft and the charter would fail its purpose.

"Change 'inspection or exclusion' to 'inspection, escort integration, or exclusion by risk finding.'"

"Soth will read that as a path for custody without destroying them."

"Good."

"Is that what you want?"

Valech did not answer immediately.

What he wanted was a Belt where old war roots stayed buried, where ship covenants did not give civilians a false sense that violence could be domesticated by paint and recorders, where command could act before committees taught themselves courage in retrospect. What he had was a framed or guilty ship entering Cinderwake, an admiral with a conscience inconveniently public, and a hidden architecture of evidence that had begun to smell like the Fracture authority systems no one admitted had survived.

"I want leverage," he said.

Pellis saved the charter. "That, I believe."

On the *Austere Bell*, Admiral Jaya Soth watched Valech's broadcast twice.

She watched it first as command required: live, standing, with Lieutenant Arin Cho and the pursuit legal officer present, while the *Mercy* crossed the first hazard ping and entered the leading dust of Cinderwake Lane. She watched it again alone in her ready compartment, seated with her jacket open at the throat and a reheated tea bulb going cold beside her hand.

The second viewing was worse.

During the first, she could measure. Tone, legal claims, public pressure, signal spread, likely effect on dock councils, likely effect on Bell's crews, risk of militia volunteers entering the geometry, risk of the *Mercy* using the broadcast to claim persecution. During the second, the names had time to become people.

She paused on Nima Osett.

Jaya had met Osett once during a fuel famine near Hasker's Spin. Osett had been short, blunt, and completely uninterested in naval ceremony. She had called Jaya's escort plan "beautiful enough to get someone killed" and then been right about the thermal drift. Jaya had changed the plan. Three hundred people had eaten because a convoy captain had insulted an admiral with good data.

Now Osett was a name in Valech's mouth.

Jaya resumed the broadcast.

TO ADMIRAL JAYA SOTH: THE BELT SEES YOUR RESTRAINT. THE BELT ALSO SEES THE DEAD.

She stopped it there.

"Cheap," she said to the empty compartment.

It was cheap because it worked.

Her door chimed.

"Enter."

Lieutenant Cho came in with a slate against his side and the controlled expression of a subordinate carrying news already sharpened by other rooms.

"Commodore Valech has issued an operational advisory tied to the charter petition," Cho said.

"Of course he has."

"It has signatures from seven dock councils, two convoy unions, and the Avar survivor families' provisional committee."

"How provisional?"

"Formed nineteen minutes ago."

Jaya leaned back. "Grief is efficient when signal officers help."

Cho did not smile. He rarely did. He was young enough to believe grimness was a form of honesty and smart enough that it sometimes was.

"The advisory recommends immediate prevention of the *Mercy*'s transit through Cinderwake Lane by disabling intercept before debris density compromises pursuit fire control."

"Recommends."

"Yes, Admiral."

"Not orders."

"Not yet."

"Read the actual command channel."

Cho looked down. "Convoy Commodore requests Pursuit Group Bell assess immediate disabling option under Compact emergency preservation doctrine, in light of public armed-command petition and ongoing risk to civilian route confidence."

"Route confidence," Jaya repeated.

"Yes."

"How many dead does route confidence weigh?"

Cho's eyes flicked up.

"That was not rhetorical," she said.

"I do not know, Admiral."

"Good. Anyone who answers quickly should be removed from command."

She stood and brought up the live pursuit plot. The *Mercy* had entered Cinderwake's outer weather. Dust and slow fragments painted the sensors with granular noise. The old battleship moved carefully, leading with armor, not hiding exactly but making itself difficult to strike without debris interference. Public mirror signals streamed from it in ugly legal braid: hazard acknowledged, civilian manifest, restraint doctrine, custody challenge, sealed system feed, patch status, dissent record index.

Jaya hated the braid. She also admired it.

"Fire-control solution?" she asked.

Cho straightened. "Clean disabling shot available for nineteen minutes if we accept moderate risk of fragment deflection."

"Define moderate."

"Sixteen percent chance of secondary hull strike outside intended engine target zone. Casualty probability variable."

"Civilian areas?"

"Unknown. The *Mercy*'s internal layout is modified beyond old Kaln templates. We have partial from their public feeds."

"So we do not know."

"No, Admiral."

"What does Valech's advisory say about sixteen percent?"

Cho's mouth tightened. "It calls the risk acceptable against projected route destabilization."

Jaya looked at him. "Do you agree?"

He took too long. That was more honest than speed.

"I think if we let them pass every marker, other demilitarized hulls will treat pursuit orders as negotiable," Cho said. "I think if they are guilty and reach buoy infrastructure, they may corrupt the record or trigger more attacks. I think if they are innocent, the real attacker benefits from every hour we spend looking cautious."

"That is not an answer to sixteen percent."

"No."

"Then answer that."

He swallowed. "I would take the shot if you ordered it."

"That is still not the same."

"I know."

The compartment hummed around them.

Jaya remembered Valech as a younger commodore after the Fracture, standing in a hangar full of confiscated weapons and saying, privately, that peace made cowards of people who wanted command without blood. She had argued with him for two hours. He had bought her bad coffee afterward. They had been almost friends before policy calcified.

"Send to Valech," she said. "Pursuit Group Bell acknowledges advisory. Disabling action remains under Admiral Soth's tactical authority. Current fire-control risk to civilian hull sections is not acceptable while non-kinetic containment remains available. Bell will maintain pursuit, observe Cinderwake transit, and update if threat posture changes."

Cho entered it. "He will not like that."

"He has hobbies."

"Admiral."

"Yes?"

"Crew feeds are carrying the broadcast. Some families aboard our ships have Avar ties."

"I know."

"Morale will not improve if we appear passive."

Jaya turned from the plot. "Find me an active verb that does not include shooting a hospital deck by mistake."

Cho had no answer.

"We are not passive because we refuse Valech's preferred timing," she said. "We are pursuing. We are watching. We are preserving a fire-control solution. We are collecting evidence. We are keeping militia volunteers out of the lane if they answer that petition with engines instead of signatures. We are doing several things at once, which is why command is harder than grief."

His face reddened slightly.

She softened by a fraction. "That last sentence is not for you."

"No, Admiral."

"But remember it."

After he left, Jaya replayed the first two minutes of Valech's broadcast again, then shut it off before the names.

"I see the dead," she said.

The compartment did not ask whether seeing was enough.

On the *Mercy*, Valech's broadcast arrived delayed, fragmented, and impossible to ignore.

Noor tried to quarantine it as external morale hazard. That lasted eight minutes. Then someone in hydroponic storage opened the public relay. Someone else mirrored it to the passenger channel with commentary. A child heard the phrase "hospital paint" and asked whether that meant their ward was fake. Three engineering techs watched Valech name the dead and said nothing afterward for long enough that Sela sent them to check the same valve tree twice because repeated work was safer than idle shame.

By the time the broadcast reached the restraint gallery, it had already passed through the ship as rumor, accusation, memorial, political weapon, and simple human pain.

Mer played the official feed anyway.

Tovin stood beside Noor's station. Ixo's amber presence occupied a side display. Sela joined from engineering by audio only, which meant she was either working hard or refusing to let Tovin see her face. Mother Kal requested access as school-deck witness and got it without argument.

Valech read the names.

The gallery listened.

There were moments when Tovin could almost hate Valech cleanly. This was not one of them. The names made cleanliness impossible. Avar Break had not become less dead because the packet was forged. If anything, the frame had stolen from the dead a second time by making their last public use uncertain.

Daro Kelm's sister was not among the thirty. Tovin wondered whether that helped or hurt. He suspected neither word applied.

Valech spoke of gaps between authorities. He spoke of armed convoy command. He spoke of old warships wearing hospital paint.

At that, Mother Kal inhaled.

Noor paused the feed. "Do you need a moment?"

"No," she said. "Play it."

They played it.

TO THE CREW OF THE MERCY OF KALN...

Valech's face, severe under memorial ribbon, filled the gallery wall.

IF YOU ARE INNOCENT, SUBMIT TO CONTROLLED CUSTODY AND ALLOW LAWFUL COMMAND TO PRESERVE YOUR PASSENGERS, LOGS, AND MACHINE WITNESS UNDER EMERGENCY SEAL.

Sela's audio clicked open. "Convenient. He heard Noor's impossible sentence and removed the impossible parts."

Noor said, "He removed the enforceable parts."

Valech continued.

IF YOU ARE GUILTY, EVERY KILOMETER YOU TRAVEL UNDER A CIVILIAN FLAG DEEPENS YOUR CRIME.

Jessa's voice came unexpectedly from Mother Kal's channel. "Can he say that?"

Noor looked pained. "Yes."

"Can we answer?"

"Also yes."

IF YOU ARE BEING USED BY FORCES OLDER AND UGLIER THAN YOUR CURRENT CREW, THEN STOP LETTING THOSE FORCES HIDE BEHIND YOUR COVENANT.

Tovin felt the sentence enter the room and find old weaknesses.

Ixo spoke softly. "That is not entirely false."

Mer stopped the playback.

Everyone looked toward the side display.

Ixo's amber light pulsed once. "The frame uses the covenant. It uses public hesitation around the ship's former class, machine testimony, demilitarized law, and pursuit procedure. Our refusal to submit under unsafe terms is necessary. It is also useful to the adversary."

"Thank you for making the floor vanish," Sela said.

"I prefer accuracy."

"I have noticed."

Mother Kal's voice, thin with age and anger, came through the speaker. "Hospital paint."

Mer said, "Mother Kal."

"No. Let me be old in public for a moment." She cleared her throat. "I painted those walls. Not alone. Children helped. So did two men who had fired real guns from that gallery before any of you arrived. We painted because the old color made people wake from surgery thinking they were prisoners. We painted because pretending is sometimes the first scaffold for becoming. If he calls that false, he does not understand repair."

Noor's recorder lights were red. "Logged."

"Good."

Sela's channel stayed quiet.

Tovin said, "Valech understands repair. He distrusts who controls it."

Mother Kal answered with a sharpness that made her sound thirty years younger. "Do not translate him into wisdom too quickly."

He bowed his head. "Fair."

Mer resumed the feed.

Valech addressed Jaya. The Belt sees your restraint. The Belt also sees the dead.

When the broadcast ended, no one spoke until the live plot chimed. Cinderwake dust thickened ahead. Bell's formation held behind, still outside the best debris lanes but close enough that its drone marked their every meter.

Noor pulled public channels onto a secondary board.

The charter petition was already spreading.

Signatures multiplied as little white marks across the Belt map. Halvyr had not signed yet. Pardon Reef signed under three different shells, two of which immediately accused each other of fraud. Varo Anchorage signed. The Avar survivor committee signed. Several dock militias appended conditional support if armed convoy command included their vessels, which told Noor to start a separate list titled PEOPLE WHO SHOULD NOT BE HANDED A CHARTER.

Sela came onto video at last. Her face was streaked with dust from an open access crawl. "Engineering is getting messages from family channels. Half of them say surrender. Half say run. One uncle says if I wake the guns he knows a priest who will testify it was pre-forgiven."

"That is not how law works," Noor said.

"I am pretty sure it is not how priests work either."

Mer folded her arms. "Effect on crew?"

"Bad."

"Specific."

"Three techs asked whether armed convoy command would take custody of passengers and let crew stay aboard. One asked whether waking shield remnants counts as rearmament if Valech gets his charter. Harl wants to know if dissent records can include support for Valech's petition."

"They can," Noor said.

Tovin looked at the map of signatures. "We need to answer publicly."

Mer's eyes moved to him. "How?"

He did not know yet. The obvious answer was defensive and therefore weak. We are not fake hospital paint. We did not kill them. We cannot submit because custody terms are unsafe. All true. All shaped like a guilty ship explaining itself while the dead waited for someone to make meaning from them.

Ixo said, "Do not answer Valech first."

Noor turned. "Then who?"

"The dead."

The gallery changed around that sentence. Not dramatically. More like a pressure adjustment, painful because ears had been pretending they were fine.

Ixo continued. "Valech's strength is that he positioned command as the only adequate respect for the dead. If we answer him as an opponent, we accept his frame. If we answer only with innocence, we appear to make Avar Break secondary to our survival. We need a record that acknowledges the dead without permitting them to be conscripted into command overreach."

Sela stared through the screen. "You have been learning politics."

"Politics has been learning me."

Noor sat down slowly. "A memorial response."

"And a custody challenge renewal," Tovin said.

Mer nodded. "Draft."

Noor's hands moved. Words formed, failed, vanished, formed again.

"No," Mother Kal said after the first paragraph.

Noor stopped. "You are not on the drafting channel."

"I am old. Channels are suggestions."

Mer looked briefly upward, perhaps asking some absent authority for patience, then said, "Proceed."

Mother Kal said, "Do not say 'we mourn the dead of Avar Break' first. Everyone says that. It has become a curtain."

"What first?"

"Say their names were read and heard aboard the accused ship."

Noor typed.

Tovin watched the sentence appear:

THE NAMES READ BY COMMODORE VALECH WERE HEARD ABOARD MERCY OF KALN UNDER PUBLIC RECORD.

Jessa's voice came through, quieter. "Say the children heard some before adults stopped the feed."

Mother Kal murmured away from the pickup, then returned. "She says not to pretend children were protected from knowing."

Mer closed her eyes. "Add: Some children aboard heard portions of the broadcast before school-deck filtering could be restored. Their questions are now part of our dissent record."

Noor added it.

Sela said, "Say hospital paint is repair."

Tovin looked at her screen.

She lifted her chin. "What? It is."

Noor typed:

THE HOSPITAL SPACES INSIDE THIS FORMER WARSHIP ARE NOT A DISGUISE. THEY ARE REPAIR, LABOR, AND PUBLIC BURDEN. THEY DO NOT ERASE WHAT THE HULL WAS BUILT TO DO. THEY ARE THE REASON WE REFUSE TO LET IT DO THAT WORK AGAIN WITHOUT WITNESS, LIMIT, AND LAWFUL NECESSITY.

"Too long," Mer said.

"Long is allowed," Noor replied. "Short got us killed at Avar."

No one disputed that.

The response grew. It acknowledged each name Valech had read without claiming equivalence to family grief. It repeated the *Mercy*'s willingness to submit under distributed, enforceable custody terms. It rejected emergency custody that would place passengers, logs, or Ixo under a sealed command channel controlled by the same authority chain that might be compromised. It asked Valech to release the complete provenance of the Avar packet to Gethsemane Buoy Court and independent convoy unions. It affirmed that armed convoy command might be necessary in some form but argued that command built on unreviewed evidence would inherit the frame rather than defeat it.

Then came the difficult line.

Noor wrote it, deleted it, and looked at Tovin.

"Say it," he said.

They wrote:

IF THE MERCY OF KALN IS PROVEN RESPONSIBLE FOR AVAR BREAK, ITS COVENANT CANNOT PROTECT IT FROM JUDGMENT. IF IT IS PROVEN FALSELY ACCUSED, AVAR BREAK STILL REQUIRES JUSTICE FOR THE DEAD, NOT MERELY RELIEF FOR THE ACCUSED.

Mer read the whole response twice.

"Send it," she said.

The message went out through the public mirror while the ship crossed the second hazard ping.

For a few minutes, the public channels did something stranger than rage.

They hesitated.

That hesitation did not absolve anyone. It did not slow Bell. It did not stop Valech's petition. But it broke the first clean wave. Some survivor families cursed the *Mercy* for daring to speak the names. Others cursed Valech for using them. A convoy union reposted the custody terms and asked why Bell could not guarantee machine witness standing. A dock council signed the armed-command petition and attached the *Mercy*'s response as required reading. Pardon Reef offered, for a fee, to host a "balanced grief auction," and was muted by almost everyone.

Then Valech replied.

Not with another broadcast. With a command packet to Bell, copied publicly after a delay so short it had to be deliberate.

PURSUE. PRESERVE IF POSSIBLE. PREVENT ESCAPE ABOVE ALL. THE DEAD CANNOT CROSS-EXAMINE A SHIP THAT OUTRUNS THE LAW.

Noor read it in a whisper.

Sela said, "That is an order wearing a memorial ribbon."

Mer's face hardened. "Bell?"

The live plot updated before Noor could answer.

Pursuit Group Bell tightened formation.

Not to fire. Not yet.

To follow them into Cinderwake Lane.

Jaya Soth's direct seal arrived thirty seconds later.

MERCY OF KALN, THIS IS AUSTERE BELL. PURSUIT CONTINUES THROUGH MARKED HAZARD ROUTE. MAINTAIN PUBLIC FEEDS. ANY SEALED SYSTEM ANOMALY WILL BE TREATED AS ACTIVE THREAT. ANY CIVILIAN VESSEL ENCOUNTERED WILL BE ORDERED CLEAR. DO NOT MISTAKE OBSERVATION FOR CLEARANCE.

Below the formal block, in the narrow band she had used once before, came a shorter line.

DO NOT MAKE ME WRONG IN FRONT OF THE DEAD.

Tovin read it, then looked at Mer.

"That line is not public," Noor said.

Mer's mouth tightened. "It will remain sealed unless needed."

Sela leaned toward her camera. "Needed for what?"

"For proving she still knows there is a difference between the dead and the people using them."

Tovin watched Bell's white shapes enter Cinderwake behind them. Valech's petition signatures continued multiplying on the side board, each one a small legal engine. Ahead, debris thickened. Behind, grief acquired guns by the hour.

Mother Kal spoke from the school deck, very softly.

"When adults say 'for the dead,' children should ask who gets to answer."

Noor recorded it.

The *Mercy* moved deeper into the lane. Its hospital paint remained paint. Its armor remained armor. Its innocence remained unproven. Its guilt remained useful to men with charters ready. Around it, the Belt argued with names in its mouth and weapons warming at the edge of law.

Tovin stood under the dead boards and understood that Valech had not merely turned grief against them.

He had made grief into weather.

Chapter 11: Cinderwake Lane

Cinderwake Lane had not been a lane until people began dying in it regularly enough to name the safer mistakes.

The charts showed a pale blue corridor threading between Kaln Minor's broken shepherd field and the long debris scarf left by the battles of Forty-Two Chord. The color lied. Nothing in Cinderwake was pale or blue except old interface tradition. The real lane was black, rust, silver, glass, ice, and invisible velocity. It was composed of things that had once been ships, mines, habitats, cargo frames, missile casings, coolant bells, court buoys, water tanks, heat vanes, family shrines, illegal sensor nests, one shattered observatory mirror, and enough ordinary dust to sand a careless hull down to history.

The Belt called it weather because weather was easier than inheritance.

The *Mercy of Kaln* entered at a crawl by warship standards and a reckless sprint by school-ark standards.

Tovin stood in the restraint gallery with the forward display divided into four layers: navigation plot, debris-density forecast, sealed-system witness feed, and public mirror status. The covenant line above him reflected faintly in the glass. Every few seconds a speck crossed the outer sensor shell and vanished into a calculation that might decide whether three hundred people lived.

Captain Mer Anik had moved command into lane posture. No unnecessary speech. No standing behind chairs. Every person clipped in or braced. Every loose slate, cup, tool, strap, dignity, and superstition secured. The ship's internal lights had gone to amber, not emergency red, because red made passengers hear alarms before alarms existed.

"Leading field at twelve percent," navigation said. "Particle count rising."

Sela's voice came from engineering, flat and busy. "Armor edge temperature nominal. Patch stress green-yellow. Saint Ordinary is secured and sulking."

Noor, at the legal station, said, "Public mirror has four-second delay from lane interference. Still within declared range. Dissent record remains open."

Ixo's amber form occupied a secondary display. "Bell's drone has crossed the first hazard marker behind us."

Mer did not look away from the plot. "Distance?"

"Thirty-one thousand kilometers and closing by forty per minute."

"They can afford faster."

"They can afford appearing capable of faster," Ixo said. "Whether they should is a separate matter."

Noor murmured, "The motto of every lawsuit."

Tovin flexed his injured shoulder inside the brace. Pain moved like a slow door. Medical had given him a stronger block; he had refused it because he did not trust himself numb in the restraint gallery. That had been vanity or prudence. He would find out if the ship shook hard enough.

The first strike came as a sound too small for its consequence.

Tick.

Everyone in the gallery heard it because the ship had gone quiet around Cinderwake, engines smoothing into a disciplined burn, ventilation softened, crew holding breath they needed.

The display marked the impact on the forward port armor: one ice-grain cluster, velocity ugly, mass negligible.

"Armor held," Sela said. "Outer paint is offended."

"Log," Mer said.

"Already."

Tick.

Tick-tick.

The *Mercy* began to pass through the leading veil.

On hospital deck, carts had been locked against inner bulkheads. In the school deck, children sat in pressure hoods with their backs to storage bins and their knees touching because Mother Kal had discovered that fear spread less efficiently when people could feel the living warmth of someone beside them. In hydroponics, water bulbs were netted, pollinator drones sealed, and the herbs clipped because loose basil could clog a vent as stupidly as shrapnel. In cargo chapel, Noor's dissent stations recorded a steady trickle of statements: I think command is wrong. I think Valech is right. I think surrender is death. I think moving is death. I do not consent but I am staying. I consent because there is no other road. My son is asleep and I am lying to him with my face.

Noor kept the index open beside legal telemetry.

Tovin tried not to watch it. He watched it anyway.

"Debris braid ahead," navigation called. "Old hull fragments, slow tumble, uncertain density behind."

The forward plot magnified. A loose skein of metal moved across their projected path, each fragment small compared with the *Mercy*, large compared with a person, and indifferent to both categories.

Mer said, "Options."

Navigation answered. "We can burn above and risk cross-shear. Drop below and lose line-of-sight to public mirror relay. Hold and take it on armor with attitude correction."

Sela said, "Above heats the patch."

Noor said, "Below breaks mirror continuity and Bell will enjoy the poetry."

Ixo said, "Holding increases cumulative strike count but preserves witness and patch tolerance."

Mer looked toward Tovin.

He knew why. Not permission. Shape.

"Mass without threat," he said. "Take it on armor if Sela can hold patch."

"Patch can hold," Sela said. "I reserve the right to hate the sentence."

"Granted," Mer said. "Hold and correct."

The ship turned by fractions.

Old warship armor did not make the impacts dramatic. That was its usefulness and its moral problem. The fragments hit as taps, scrapes, brief hard notes ringing through deep structure. A hospital ark would have vented. A tug would have spun. The *Mercy* accepted the blows and made them small for the people inside.

Tick.

Scrape.

Tick-tick-tick.

Tovin watched stress blooms flower and fade across the armor map. The ship's mass, once designed to bring guns through a battlespace, now did the quieter work of being harder to kill than its passengers.

"This is what Valech means," Noor said softly.

Tovin turned.

Noor did not look up from the recorders. "He means no civilian route should depend on a converted battleship happening to be kind."

"He is not wrong."

"I know. I hate how often villains and useful people share premises."

"Valech is not a villain."

Noor's eyes flicked up now. "Say that again after he gets his charter."

Another scrape ran along the hull, longer than the others. The armor map showed yellow across a forward plate.

Sela swore. "That was not ice."

"Identify," Mer said.

"Ceramic shard. Old heat shield, maybe. Plate scored but intact."

"Patch?"

"Still green-yellow."

Ixo said, "Bell has altered pursuit path. They are climbing above our vector."

The plot updated. Pursuit Group Bell's lead drone and two light escorts rose relative to the *Mercy*, seeking clearer sensor geometry above the debris braid.

Navigation said, "They will have cleaner line for disabling solution in seventeen minutes if the braid thins."

Mer's jaw tightened. "Can we thicken the braid?"

Sela's channel went very quiet.

Tovin looked at the sealed-system display. "Define."

"Natural maneuver," Mer said. "No active debris manipulation."

Navigation answered cautiously. "If we alter attitude five degrees and present broader armor to the slow tumble, our wake will scatter smaller particles behind us. It may complicate Bell's sensor solution. It will also increase strike count and patch vibration."

Noor said, "If done for defensive concealment, Bell can frame it as evasion under hazard."

"If done for hull protection?" Mer asked.

Noor frowned. "Possible. Weak."

Sela said, "It is not for hull protection."

The bluntness helped.

Mer did not snap at her. "No."

The gallery waited.

Tovin felt the old instinct rise: justify, categorize, find the language that made survival fit the rule. He hated how natural it felt.

"We do not use debris as a weapon or screen," he said.

Mer nodded once. "Navigation, maintain declared attitude. If armor protection requires change, state it before execution."

"Aye."

Sela exhaled over open channel. "Good."

"Do not sound surprised," Mer said.

"I am surprised by many good things."

The next five minutes stretched into a discipline of refusing small advantages.

Bell climbed. The *Mercy* held. Public mirror lagged, caught, lagged again. Hazard markers blinked behind them like a dotted accusation. The debris braid thinned but did not clear. Twice navigation suggested minor attitude changes and twice Sela confirmed they were armor-justified, not pursuit-shaped. Noor recorded both with the satisfaction of someone building a narrow bridge over a pit.

Then the civilian craft appeared.

It emerged from sensor clutter ahead and low: a small maintenance skiff, transponder weak, drive cycling in ugly bursts, hull signature consistent with a dock rat or unauthorized salvage runner. It should not have been in Cinderwake during a marked hazard interval. It should not have been anywhere near the *Mercy*'s projected path. It existed anyway, which was the Belt's favorite form of argument.

"Contact," navigation said. "Eight thousand kilometers. Crossing inward. Transponder reads *Moth's Due*. Two life signs probable. Drive unstable."

Noor pulled registry. "Licensed to Pardon Reef. Salvage skiff. Multiple fines. One pending theft arbitration. No violent flags."

Sela said, "Because the universe is tasteful."

Mer was already adjusting the plot. "Collision risk."

"If both hold current vectors, near miss at two hundred kilometers. If their drive pulses again at current error, impact risk rises."

"Hail."

Noor opened channel. "*Moth's Due*, this is *Mercy of Kaln* under public hazard transit. You are inside marked Cinderwake hazard interval. Confirm control."

Static. Then a voice, young and terrified. "Don't shoot. Don't shoot, don't shoot, we lost aft trim, we were just cutting a marker claw, we didn't know-"

Sela said, "Marker theft. Charming."

Mer cut in. "*Moth's Due*, no one is shooting. Shut down aft trim and vent starboard reserve for tumble arrest."

"We can't. Valve's jammed. My brother's in the crawl."

Bell's transmission arrived half a second later, formal and hard.

MERCY OF KALN, DO NOT APPROACH CIVILIAN CONTACT. PURSUIT GROUP WILL DIRECT RESCUE AFTER SECURING ACCUSED VESSEL.

Sela barked a laugh. "After?"

Noor said, "They cannot reach the skiff before its next tumble window."

Navigation confirmed. "We are closest."

Mer looked at Tovin again, and this time it was not just shape. It was burden.

If they altered path toward the skiff, Bell could read it as hostage-taking, shield-taking, evasion, or the old warship using civilians as geometry. If they did nothing and the skiff died, restraint became an alibi for cowardice.

Tovin said, "Interpose mass only if we do not create intentional collision risk."

"We wrote that," Noor said.

"Now we find out whether it meant anything."

Mer's voice became command steel. "Navigation, plot intercept that places our forward armor between skiff and debris stream, not between skiff and Bell. Minimal vector deviation. Public channel open. Engineering, patch tolerance."

Sela answered at once. "Patch goes yellow-red if we yaw too fast. Give me a slow shoulder, not a lunge."

"You have it."

Noor transmitted. "Pursuit Group Bell, *Mercy* is initiating witnessed civilian shield maneuver under declared restraint survival doctrine. No sealed system activation. Public feed remains live. You are invited to provide alternate rescue if faster."

Bell replied with a warning tone before words.

MERCY, ALTERATION MAY BE INTERPRETED AS EVASIVE ACTION.

Noor did not wait for Mer. "Then interpret accurately."

Tovin stared at them.

Noor's cheeks colored. "That was not the final version."

Mer said, "Send the final version next time."

The *Mercy* turned.

Not much. Enough.

The old hull took the new angle with deep reluctance. Stress crawled through the armor map. The patch over auxiliary school storage rose to red-yellow and held. Children would feel the change as a long lean in their bones. On the forward plot, the skiff's tiny erratic path jittered toward a stream of fist-sized metal fragments that would not notice two life signs.

"*Moth's Due*," Mer said, "cut main drive."

"We'll spin."

"You are spinning already. Cut main drive."

"My brother-"

Sela broke in. "Tell your brother to stop being brave near the jammed valve and clip his tether to something welded."

"Who is this?"

"The person with a better view of your bad choices. Cut main."

The skiff's drive winked out.

It tumbled.

The *Mercy* moved between the skiff and the debris stream.

For eight seconds, the forward display became an argument between scale and tenderness. The skiff was a scrap with a pressure cabin. The *Mercy* was a former battleship with armor laid in ugly bands and scars older than most laws. The debris stream struck the *Mercy* across its leading shoulder, a clatter of impacts harder than the earlier veil.

Tick became hammer.

Hammer became rain.

The gallery shook.

Noor's recorder sphere bumped its tether and kept recording. Tovin grabbed the rail with his good hand. Pain flared from his bad shoulder anyway. Mer did not move except for her eyes. On the engineering feed, Sela shouted numbers over alarms.

"Forward shoulder plate red. Patch red-yellow. No breach. Armor flake on outer layer. Saint Ordinary remains secured because I threatened it."

Navigation called, "Skiff cleared first stream."

"Second stream?" Mer asked.

"Thirty seconds."

Bell's lead drone closed from above, its icon sharp and white.

Ixo said, "Bell has a clean line on our engine quarter."

Noor whispered, "They would not."

No one answered because everyone knew "would not" was weather too.

Jaya Soth's voice came live, not packeted.

"*Mercy of Kaln*, hold current vector. Pursuit Group Bell recognizes civilian shield maneuver. Any further deviation will be treated as evasive unless directly tied to contact protection."

Mer opened her channel. "Austere Bell, civilian skiff remains in debris risk. We will complete shield maneuver and then resume declared lane vector."

"You have twenty seconds."

"The second stream arrives in twenty-seven."

Silence.

Then Jaya said, "You have thirty."

Tovin released a breath he had not approved.

Sela said, "Do not thank her. She can hear smug."

The second stream struck lower.

This one carried glass dust and a spinning rod of some old antenna. The dust painted the armor map in speckled yellow. The rod hit with a crack that rang through the gallery like a struck bell.

"Damage," Mer said.

Sela's answer came too late.

"Sela."

"Outer sensor blister gone. Not critical. Patch holds. Forward port thermal scar will need work. I dislike the sound the ship just made."

"Which sound?" Noor asked.

"The one where old metal remembers being old."

The skiff tumbled into the lee of the *Mercy*'s mass, shielded for the moment. Its signal came through ragged.

"We are alive. We are alive. We cut main. My brother says the valve hates him personally."

Mer said, "Stay cold. Broadcast your exact tumble rate. Pursuit Group will direct pickup."

The young voice changed. Fear remained, but now carried suspicion. "You're leaving?"

Tovin closed his eyes.

Mer said, "We are under warrant and cannot take you aboard without increasing danger to you and our passengers. Bell has acknowledged your contact."

"But you came."

"Yes."

"Are you pirates?"

No one in the gallery moved.

Mer looked at Tovin. He shook his head once. Not because the question did not deserve answer. Because any answer from them would sound like asking a rescued person to testify before fear had stopped shaking.

Mer said, "Stay alive. Answer the rescue channel."

The skiff's feed clicked away.

Bell assigned a drone to the skiff. The drone's path was clean, efficient, and careful in the way institutions became after someone else took the first risk. Noor recorded that too, because bitterness did not make facts less useful.

The *Mercy* returned to declared lane vector, one armor shoulder hotter, one sensor blind, one rescued criminal skiff in its wake, and no better legal status than before.

Public channels noticed anyway.

For three minutes the armed-command petition slowed.

Not stopped. Slowed.

A convoy union reposted the shield maneuver with the caption MASS WITHOUT GUNS. A dock council asked whether armed convoy command would have authorized an accused ship to perform civilian rescue under pursuit. Halvyr Dock sent no statement but quietly released the rest of the family audio messages without review delay. Pardon Reef claimed *Moth's Due* had been on a charitable mission, then deleted the claim when four people replied with unpaid marker-claw invoices.

Valech's office issued one line:

EVEN GUILTY SHIPS MAY PERFORM USEFUL ACTS. USEFUL ACTS DO NOT ANSWER CHARGES.

Noor read it aloud.

"He is not wrong," Tovin said.

Sela groaned over the channel. "I am installing a fine for that sentence."

Mer leaned over navigation. "Lane status."

"We have passed the first braid. Main density ahead in twelve minutes. Bell remains above and aft. Drone assigned to skiff. Public mirror delay six seconds but stable."

"Damage status."

Sela answered. "Forward port sensor blister gone. I can reroute through two civilian nav eyes, but they are not built for this debris density. Armor shoulder scarred. Patch is green-yellow again, which is the only good color I believe in now. No sealed system contact. No weapon ghosts whispering. Tovin can unclench."

Tovin had not realized his good hand was still locked on the rail.

He released it.

Ixo said, "The shield maneuver altered public inference."

Noor snorted. "For three minutes."

"Three minutes is a nonzero political unit."

"That is bleak."

"Yes."

Mer said, "Use them."

Noor looked up. "How?"

"Package the maneuver record with the restraint list. Send to Gethsemane Buoy Court stack and convoy unions. Not as exoneration. As precedent."

Tovin nodded. "Show what the ship can do under limits."

Sela said, "Also show what it costs. Include damage."

"Yes," Mer said.

"All of it. The lost sensor, patch stress, shoulder scar, repair estimate, my objection to doing it again unless physics buys dinner."

Noor typed. "That last phrase?"

"Put it in an appendix."

The ship entered the second density layer before Noor finished the package.

This time the danger was not fragments large enough to name but dust too fine to respect. It glittered at the edge of sensor resolution, a powdered memory of ceramics and glass moving in uneven sheets. Shields, what remained of them after demilitarization, could not be used actively without entering a legal gray zone too close to defensive systems. The *Mercy* had passive field baffles, armor, sacrificial vanes, and Sela's increasingly personal relationship with thermal alarms.

"Dust sheet in five," navigation said.

"Attitude?" Mer asked.

"Declared vector acceptable. Recommend micro-roll to spread heating."

Noor checked the doctrine list. "Armor protection justified."

Tovin said, "Witnessed."

Mer gave the order.

The *Mercy* rolled by degrees.

Dust touched the hull.

The sound did not come as ticks now. It came as a hiss through structure, a vast sandpaper whisper. The forward display fuzzed. Public mirror lag spiked to twelve seconds, then twenty, then fell back to nine. The armor heat map warmed from blue to green to yellow along the leading curve.

In the school deck, Mother Kal started a counting exercise. Noor put the audio on low in the gallery without asking. Children's voices counted impacts they could not hear, numbers becoming a rope across fear.

One.

Two.

Three.

Sela said, "Thermal rise faster than forecast."

Mer: "Options."

"Increase roll rate and stress the patch, or reduce speed and let Bell close."

Bell's icons remained above and aft, cleaner than the *Mercy* but not untouched. One escort had dropped back after its own thermal warning. The *Austere Bell* continued, stubborn and bright.

Tovin watched the heat map. Every option had a witness. None had mercy.

"How much speed reduction before Bell reaches disabling geometry?" Mer asked.

Navigation: "If we reduce by three percent for ninety seconds, Bell improves solution but remains outside clean shot through dust. Five percent gives them a marginal window. Ten gives them clean."

Sela said, "I need three."

Mer looked to Tovin.

He said, "Three percent is armor preservation, not surrender."

Noor said, "Record will support."

"Do it," Mer said.

The ship slowed.

It felt like nothing. It meant everything.

Bell gained. The dust hiss softened by a fraction. Patch stress held. Armor temperature leveled just under red. The children's counting reached thirty-seven and then dissolved into laughter when someone skipped forty by accident and an argument began about whether skipped numbers still counted if adults were too frightened to correct them.

Mer allowed the three percent for eighty-nine seconds.

"Resume," she said.

Engines deepened.

The *Mercy* pulled away from Bell's clean window just as the dust sheet thinned.

Noor released a breath. "That was close."

Sela said, "Close is what we call success when everyone involved needs therapy."

Ixo's light pulsed. "Bell has just received Valech's amended advisory."

Mer's face did not change. "Content."

"Not public yet. Intercepted from command metadata and relay header only. Priority: emergency charter alignment. Attachment includes phrase 'demilitarized hulls under risk finding.'"

Tovin felt Noor look at him.

"He is building a legal cage around ships like us," Noor said.

"Around ships like us, militias unlike us, and convoy routes that are actually dying," Tovin replied.

"You are doing it again."

"What?"

"Being fair to the person tightening the cage."

He accepted that. "Fairness is not endorsement."

"It can become delay."

Before he could answer, the forward display cleared enough to show the next hazard.

Navigation said, "Large object ahead. Tumbling slowly. Registry uncertain."

The object emerged from dust like a dark thought.

It was a section of an old habitat ring, two hundred meters long, bent along its spine, windows blown blind, one side furred with impact debris. It rotated lazily across the lane, trailing small fragments. Its surface carried faded paint: not letters, not anymore, just a stripe of green and white that had once meant someone's home believed in gardens.

"Predicted path?" Mer asked.

"Crossing our vector in four minutes. We can pass ahead with increased burn, pass behind with speed reduction, or hold and let it cross while Bell closes."

Sela said, "Increased burn heats the patch and forward shoulder. Passing behind costs time. Holding is Bell's dream."

Noor said, "Can we contact it?"

"No active beacon."

Ixo said, "I detect cold memory echoes. Old court salvage tags. No life signs."

The habitat fragment turned, and for a moment its broken windows reflected the *Mercy* back at itself in pieces.

Tovin knew what the old tactical self would do. Burn ahead. Take the risk. Keep initiative. Treat the dead object as obstacle, not relic.

He also knew what excessive caution would do. Hold too long, hand Bell a solution, call it respect for ghosts.

"Behind," he said before Mer asked. "We lose time but keep heat and record clean."

Sela said, "Behind is safest for the patch."

Navigation added, "Bell gains but still no clean line if we resume after crossing."

Mer nodded. "Pass behind."

The *Mercy* slowed again, not as much this time, and let the dead habitat cross ahead.

As it passed, fragments brushed the forward sensor field. The ship's cameras caught a few clear images: a corridor open to vacuum, a child's mural flash-frozen under glass dust, a hydroponic tray still bolted to a wall that no longer had air, a small memorial plaque too blurred to read.

Noor marked the images for salvage court.

"Why?" Sela asked, softer than usual.

"Because it existed," Noor said.

No one mocked that.

Bell gained thirteen hundred kilometers.

The habitat fragment crossed fully. The *Mercy* resumed speed.

Jaya Soth transmitted a narrow packet:

YOUR VECTOR CHOICE REDUCED COLLISION RISK. ACKNOWLEDGED.

Noor read it. "That is almost praise."

Sela said, "Do not feed it after midnight."

Tovin looked at the old habitat shrinking behind them. "She is building her own record."

"Against Valech?" Mer asked.

"Against whoever later says every choice was obvious."

Mer's expression tightened. "Good."

The third hazard was human.

It arrived not as contact but as message, bounced through a dirty relay from somewhere in Pardon Reef's influence:

MERCY OF KALN, YOU NEED CLEAN EXIT FROM CINDERWAKE. I CAN SELL ONE. HASK PELL.

Noor stared at it. "Absolutely not."

Sela said, "I do not know. I like bargains from people whose names sound like a cough."

"He is a salvage broker with active fraud flags," Noor said.

"That narrows nothing in the Belt."

Mer said, "Do not answer yet."

Tovin read the message again. Not offer of rescue. Not sympathy. Clean exit. As if Pell knew the lane would tighten ahead, or Bell's next geometry, or both.

Ixo said, "Pell's timing suggests access to hazard data not currently public."

"Or he guessed," Noor said.

"Yes. Some criminals are talented."

Mer pinched the bridge of her nose. "Park it. We survive the next layer first."

Cinderwake did not care that politics had queued up.

The lane narrowed around Kaln Minor's shepherd field. Dust streams bent in ways that made navigation swear politely and Sela swear usefully. Bell followed higher than before, losing one drone to thermal retreat but preserving the main pursuit. Public mirror degraded, recovered, degraded. The *Mercy* accumulated scars that did not breach but would cost weeks and money they did not have. The patch over school storage held. The children counted, slept, woke, argued, counted again.

By the time they cleared the worst of the density layer, everyone in the restraint gallery looked older.

Navigation said, with cautious disbelief, "Main Cinderwake density astern. Residual debris ahead, manageable. We remain within lane but past primary hazard."

No one cheered.

Mer asked the important question. "Bell?"

"Still with us. Distance twenty-two thousand kilometers. Clean disabling geometry unavailable for six minutes due to residual dust. After that, improving."

Noor sent the maneuver package to Gethsemane Buoy Court, convoy unions, Halvyr, Bell, and public mirror. It contained everything: shield maneuver, damage, slowdowns, habitat pass, witness logs, dissent index, Sela's appendix with the dinner sentence removed and then restored after engineering objected collectively.

The response from Gethsemane's dormant stack came back as a simple receipt:

EVIDENCE PACKAGE RECEIVED. DISTRIBUTION PENDING BANDWIDTH.

It was not judgment.

It was not safety.

It was somewhere for the record to exist outside the immediate reach of fear.

Tovin leaned both hands against the dead console and let his head hang for one breath. His shoulder throbbed. His eyes burned. The ship smelled faintly of warmed insulation and too many people exhaling relief before relief had permission.

Sela came onto the gallery screen. Behind her, engineering looked like a room that had been assembled by impact and stubbornness.

"We made it through the main lane," she said.

"Yes," Tovin said.

"With no guns."

"Yes."

"With damage."

"Yes."

"With Bell closer."

"Yes."

"With a criminal offering us a clean exit."

"Apparently."

"With Valech building a cage."

"Yes."

She looked at him for a long moment. "I am not less angry."

"I did not expect you to be."

"But I understand the tool better."

That mattered more than forgiveness. Forgiveness would have been premature and possibly insulting.

Tovin nodded. "So do I."

Mer straightened from command. "Next problem."

Noor made a weary sound. "Can we schedule problems?"

"Pell's message," Mer said. "Ixo, provenance. Noor, legal risk. Sela, assume any clean exit costs something physical we will hate. Tovin, restraint implications. Navigation, find me the exit he thinks he can sell before he sells it to someone worse."

The gallery became work again.
