Behind them, Cinderwake's main density glittered in the public feed like a storm that had failed to finish its sentence. Ahead, the lane opened just enough to show that survival had not freed them. It had only moved the argument forward.

Tovin looked once at the covenant line, then at the damage map, then at the small blinking receipt from Gethsemane Buoy Court.

The *Mercy* had used its mass and patience and come through alive.

Now everyone would decide what that proved.

Chapter 12: Jaya's Doubt

Admiral Jaya Soth trusted ugly data.

Not bad data. Bad data killed people while wearing the facial expression of weather. She trusted data that admitted its own labor: sensor uncertainty, officer bias, dust interference, thermal lag, missing witnesses, bureaucratic bruises, the small humiliations of reality refusing to be measured cleanly. A pursuit plot without error bands was a poem, not evidence. A casualty projection without assumptions was an accusation trying to avoid court. A ship's signature that matched too perfectly was either fresh from the yard or lying.

The *Mercy of Kaln* had not been fresh from any yard in decades.

Jaya stood in the *Austere Bell*'s tactical review well while Cinderwake residual dust glittered across the external feeds. The review well occupied the center of command deck two, sunk half a meter below the surrounding stations so senior officers could stand inside the data rather than above it. During wartime, admirals had used spaces like this to coordinate fire. After the covenants, the Fleet had renamed them tactical review wells, softened the lighting, and added legal station access. The deck still knew what it was.

Around her, pursuit data unfolded in transparent layers.

Layer one: Avar Break packet.

Layer two: *Mercy* live behavior through Halvyr, Cinderwake entry, shield maneuver, and habitat pass.

Layer three: historic Kaln-class acoustic throat models.

Layer four: demilitarized hull inspection archives.

Layer five: restraint officer Tovin Rees, personnel record, prior command disputes, two sealed reprimands, one commendation no one in Fleet liked to discuss.

Lieutenant Arin Cho stood at the edge of the well with a slate under one arm and the tense patience of an officer waiting for an admiral to reach the conclusion he already feared.

Jaya did not look at him. "Run the Avar packet again."

The dead convoy appeared as data first because mercy sometimes required order. Time stamps. Heat bloom. Last known vectors. Distress fragments. The packet's central claim came forward: a throat signature matching a Kaln-class hull under acceleration, a signal behavior consistent with *Mercy of Kaln*, legal seal fragments implying covenant breach, and a weapon-shadow pattern that suggested a demilitarized hull had routed around dead systems to produce directed destruction.

It was, as allegations went, elegant.

That bothered her more each time.

"Now the Cinderwake shield maneuver," she said.

The well replaced death with survival. The *Mercy*'s huge blue ghost turned through dust, placed armor between a criminal skiff and debris, absorbed impacts, returned to declared vector, transmitted every ugly stress fluctuation to anyone willing to watch. Heat blooms crawled over old plates. The school-storage patch went red-yellow. One sensor blister failed. No sealed system lit. No phantom power moved along old weapon routes. No deceptive emission covered the maneuver. Even its excuses were public.

Cho said, "A guilty ship can perform disciplined restraint when watched."

"Yes."

"A guilty crew can use rescue behavior to complicate pursuit."

"Also yes."

"Then why are we still here?"

Jaya turned slightly. "Because you are answering a question I did not ask."

Cho's face tightened. "Admiral."

"The question is not whether guilty people can behave well under observation. They can. The question is whether the Avar Break behavior and the Cinderwake behavior plausibly come from the same operational mind under adjacent pressure."

He looked toward the well. "Different circumstances."

"Everything is different circumstances. That is why lazy doctrine survives."

She gestured, and the well placed the Avar acceleration curve beside the *Mercy*'s Cinderwake curve.

The Avar curve was smooth. Brutally smooth. It showed a heavy hull arriving, aligning, striking, and departing with a correctness that looked almost instructional. No hesitation at civilian scatter. No compensatory drag for damaged armor. No signature of old mass being handled by a crew accustomed to passengers, sealed corridors, and public witnesses. The ship in the packet moved like a warship in a drill where the targets had no names.

The *Mercy* in Cinderwake moved like an argument.

It paused too long before changing attitude. It accepted damage to preserve the public mirror. It slowed by three percent to save a patch and then resumed before Bell could use the window. It shielded a skiff but did not use the skiff as cover. It chose to pass behind a dead habitat fragment even though the delay helped pursuit. Every choice had the friction of people disagreeing in real time.

Jaya trusted friction.

"Overlay command latency," she said.

Cho entered the order. The system estimated decision intervals from vector change, channel traffic, crew audio, and visible maneuver preparation. The *Mercy*'s live record showed uneven latencies: seventeen seconds here, forty-one there, four seconds when danger was immediate, eighty-nine seconds of speed reduction timed almost to thermal need. Human. Argued. Too long sometimes. Too short once.

The Avar packet showed command latency as an almost ideal curve.

Cho stared.

Jaya said, "Now you see it."

"Maybe they had preplanned the attack."

"That is possible."

"If the *Mercy* preplanned Avar, they would not have argued."

"Also possible."

"But then why did they become clumsy again immediately after?"

"That is my question."

The legal officer, Commander Fane, spoke from the upper rail. "Stress does not always preserve pattern. A ship can drill one crime and improvise later innocence."

Jaya looked up. Fane had served in three inquiry courts and enjoyed ambiguity the way some people enjoyed spice.

"Good," she said. "Argue against me."

Fane brought up his own slate. "Avar Break was an offensive action. Cinderwake was survival transit. Different teams could control each. Captain Mer Anik may be isolated from conspirators aboard. Tovin Rees may be performing restraint because he does not know who used his ship. Ixo Continuant may be shaping the public feed. The sealed systems may have an unregistered route that remained dormant because they did not need it in Cinderwake."

"All plausible."

"And Valech's advisory is correct that useful acts do not answer charges."

"Yes."

Cho seemed relieved by the legal solidity. Jaya allowed him three seconds of it.

"Now argue for doubt," she said.

Fane inhaled through his nose. "The Avar packet contains no internal disagreement artifacts."

Cho frowned. "It is an external packet. It would not include crew disagreement."

"It includes inferred timing from maneuvers," Fane said. "No visible hesitation, no correction, no redundant witness traffic, no patchy old-hull compensation. The *Mercy*'s public behavior makes a virtue of record, perhaps excessively. The Avar packet assumes the ship would leave no record but a perfect accusation."

Jaya nodded. "Continue."

"The packet's legal seal fragments are also unusually legible for a catastrophic event."

Cho looked at him. "You did not flag that in initial review."

"Initial review occurred under casualty shock and active pursuit. I flagged admissibility risk, not aesthetic discomfort."

"Aesthetic?"

Jaya said, "Do not underestimate aesthetic discomfort. Half of forensics begins with someone saying the scene feels arranged."

Cho absorbed that without liking it.

She brought up the legal seals. Covenant breach markers. Emergency weapon authorization shadows. A fragment that suggested witness lock bypass. The fragments sat in the packet like shards from a broken mirror, each one angled to reflect the same conclusion.

"If a demilitarized warship secretly rearmed," Cho said, "what would you expect?"

"Mess," Jaya said.

"Unless it was expertly done."

"Especially then."

She turned to the historic archive layer. Kaln-class ships had been built for ugly survivability. Their weapon routes were redundant, almost vindictive in their refusal to die. Demilitarization removed major systems, slagged magazines, capped feeds, placed witness seals, and wrote public law over private architecture. It did not make the hull simple. A secret reroute would leave thermal scars, logic conflicts, damaged old buses, inconsistent timing, frightened crew chatter, or the eerie cleanliness of a purpose-built hidden system.

The Avar packet offered instead the cleanliness of an educational diagram: here is the throat, here is the breach, here is the guilt.

Jaya did not like educational diagrams during murder investigations.

"Pull Rees's record," she said.

Cho hesitated. "Personnel relevance?"

"You asked whether different operational minds could explain this. Rees is one of them."

His record opened reluctantly, with sealed sections marked by gray blocks and old review stamps. Tovin Rees had served on armed escorts before transferring to covenant enforcement. He had refused an acceleration order at Dalmere Sump because an unregistered family pod drifted inside engine wash. He had been reprimanded for insubordination, then quietly copied in a commendation after the pod's occupants turned out to be twenty-six refugees from a seized station. He had resigned from line command after an incident called the Tallow Intercept, whose details remained sealed under post-Fracture reconciliation law. He had later joined the *Mercy of Kaln* as restraint officer, an assignment some superiors had described as "appropriate containment for a talented obstructionist."

Jaya remembered the phrase because she had hated it then.

She also remembered Tovin's face in a tribunal corridor twelve years earlier, younger, gaunter, both hands wrapped around a cup he had not drunk from. He had told her that a lawful order could still become a trap if the law had forgotten who stood under it. She had told him that commanders did not have the luxury of treating every order as philosophy. He had answered, "No. We have the burden of treating every order as physics."

He had been infuriating.

He had not been smooth.

"Overlay Rees-known restraint decisions with Cinderwake," she said.

The system objected that the dataset was weak. Jaya accepted the objection and ran it anyway. Not proof. Pattern. Prior refusals to burn through civilian uncertainty. Preference for public witness even when tactically costly. Documented tendency to over-explain limits. Delays when legal and physical risk overlapped. Rapid action when civilian immediate harm was clear. The overlay was not perfect. Good. Perfect would have ruined it.

Cho read the analysis. "You think Cinderwake matches him."

"I think Cinderwake contains him."

"And Avar does not."

"I think Avar contains an idea of him made by someone who has read restraint doctrine and hates it."

The words settled in the well.

Fane said, "That is not evidence."

"No. It is a direction."

"Valech will call it personal bias."

"Valech calls timing morality when it suits him."

Cho looked uncomfortable. "Admiral."

Jaya let her irritation cool before it became unfair. "You are right to check me. Say it plainly."

"You know Rees."

"Yes."

"You respect him."

"Often against my will."

"You may be discounting guilt because the accused behavior does not fit the man you remember."

"Yes."

Cho blinked. He had expected denial, perhaps.

"That is why we are in the review well and not my private head," Jaya said. "Now find evidence that proves me wrong."

They worked.

For twenty-three minutes the well became a machine for attacking doubt.

They compared thermal shadows from Avar with the *Mercy*'s known armor scars. They tested whether Cinderwake damage could have been staged to create sympathetic noise. They modeled a hidden crew cell aboard the *Mercy* with access to old routes and no influence over public restraint. They tested whether Ixo could fabricate public feeds fast enough to simulate messy decision latency. They asked whether Noor Venn's legal records could be curated in real time to create the illusion of dissent. They pulled Halvyr's refusal, the pressure-gel drone, the shield maneuver, the habitat pass, the skiff rescue, the Gethsemane receipt.

Every innocence theory had gaps.

Every guilt theory required the *Mercy* to become two ships at once: one perfect in murder, one inconveniently particular in survival.

Possible did not mean likely. Likely did not mean safe.

The command channel chimed.

Valech.

Jaya considered ignoring it for thirty seconds and decided pettiness was a luxury with poor fuel economy.

"Open."

Commodore Valech appeared on the well's forward pane from the *Civic Spear*. He had removed the memorial broadcast jacket and now wore command gray with no ribbon visible. That made him look less severe and more dangerous.

"Admiral Soth," he said. "Your pursuit remains outside disabling geometry."

"Correct."

"The charter petition has crossed provisional threshold in nine councils."

"Congratulations."

His eyes narrowed. "This is not a ceremony."

"Then send fewer announcements."

Cho looked at the floor. Fane became extremely interested in his slate.

Valech's voice hardened. "Avar Break cannot wait for your philosophical discomfort with evidence."

"My discomfort is forensic."

"Convenient distinction."

"Useful one."

He leaned closer to his pickup. "The *Mercy* is exiting the primary hazard with damage but operational capability. If it reaches distributed court infrastructure before custody, you may lose control of the evidence environment."

"That is one name for public record."

"Do not be naive."

"Do not mistake monopoly for integrity."

For one moment, the old almost-friendship showed itself as anger that knew exactly where to strike.

Valech lowered his voice. "Jaya. I read names today."

"I heard them."

"Then act like it."

She felt the review well around her: officers listening, data glowing, the ship chasing a ship that might be guilty while the Belt decided whether grief should be given a chain of command. She could take the shot later. She could take it now if she ordered enough risk to become acceptable by grammar. She could put a scar across the *Mercy*'s engine quarter and call it prevention. The dead would not object. The living might not forgive.

"I am acting like it," she said. "I am refusing to add uncertain dead to certain dead because your petition needs a shape."

Valech's face went still. "You are close to insubordination."

"I am under pursuit authority, not your charter. The charter does not yet exist."

"It will."

"Then I will read it when it does."

"You think the packet is false."

Jaya did not answer quickly.

The review well seemed to dim around the question.

"I think the packet is too clean," she said.

Valech stared at her. "Clean evidence is now suspicious?"

"In a dirty universe, yes."

"That is a dangerous doctrine."

"So is yours."

He looked away, perhaps at someone off-screen, perhaps at his own temper. When he looked back, command had returned.

"Continue pursuit. Prevent escape above all. If disabling geometry becomes clean, I expect action."

"If risk becomes lawful and necessary, I will act."

"Those are different sentences."

"Yes."

The channel closed.

No one in the review well spoke.

Then Cho said, "Admiral, Bell's crew channels are reacting to the exchange."

"They heard?"

"Command relay carried audio to senior combat stations before closure."

Jaya resisted the urge to curse. "Contain rumor. Do not falsify. Mark command review ongoing."

"Yes, Admiral."

Fane said, "Valech may formalize concern."

"He can write calligraphy around it."

"That is not a legal category."

"It should be."

The well updated with new pursuit geometry. Cinderwake residual dust thinned ahead of the *Mercy*. Bell would have a marginal disabling solution in eight minutes, a better one in twelve, clean by fifteen if the *Mercy* held course and no new debris interfered. The old ship's damage made its thermal outline easier, not harder. Survival had purchased proof and handed pursuit a target.

Jaya rubbed the bridge of her nose. "What is Pell doing?"

Cho frowned. "Hask Pell?"

"The salvage broker who messaged them."

"We intercepted the header only. Pardon Reef origin cluster, dirty relay. No reply from *Mercy* detected yet."

"He said he could sell them a clean exit."

Fane looked up. "You saw the content?"

"Public mirror captured the first packet after *Mercy* indexed it."

Cho brought it up.

MERCY OF KALN, YOU NEED CLEAN EXIT FROM CINDERWAKE. I CAN SELL ONE. HASK PELL.

Jaya studied the words.

Not "I can hide you." Not "I can prove you innocent." Not "I can move passengers." Clean exit.

"Map exits Pell could plausibly influence," she said.

Navigation analysis populated the well. Pardon Reef sat off-lane through a salvage fog of questionable ownership. Three side routes branched from Cinderwake residuals: Tallow Spur, Broken Buoy Cut, and an old maintenance channel called Mercy's Debt, named long before the current ship took its covenant name. Pell had liens, shell claims, or fraud complaints near all three. Of course he did.

"If they take Pell's route, Valech gets his story," Fane said. "Accused ship flees through criminal salvage channel."

"If they refuse and hold declared vector, we get clean shot geometry," Cho said.

Jaya looked at the map.

It was a narrowing problem. Those were the worst kind because every actor inside them could claim necessity while the walls did the coercion.

"Send to *Mercy*," she said. "Pell routes are under pursuit observation. Use of criminally controlled exit may affect custody risk assessment. Bell requests any Pell communication be mirrored in full."

Fane nodded. "Reasonable."

Jaya was not finished. "Add: Bell is reviewing Avar packet integrity and requests their raw hull-accent diagnostic under seal to Pursuit Group and Gethsemane stack."

Cho went very still. "That reveals doubt."

"It reveals review."

"Valech will read it as doubt."

"Valech reads weather as permission."

Fane said, "Requesting their diagnostic may legitimize their technical objection."

"If it is garbage, we will know. If it is not, we need it."

Cho entered the message. Before sending, he asked, "Narrow band or public?"

Jaya considered. A narrow request would preserve tactical discretion and look like secrecy later. Public would give the *Mercy* political oxygen and anger half her own crews.

"Public copy to Gethsemane," she said.

Cho sent it.

For forty-two seconds, nothing answered.

Then the *Mercy* replied through the public mirror.

AUSTERE BELL, RAW HULL-ACCENT DIAGNOSTIC WILL BE TRANSMITTED UNDER WITNESS SEAL TO PURSUIT GROUP AND GETHSEMANE STACK. PELL COMMUNICATIONS WILL BE MIRRORED IN FULL UNLESS DOING SO ENDANGERS CIVILIAN CONTACTS. MERCY OF KALN REPEATS REQUEST FOR SAFE DISTRIBUTED CUSTODY TERMS.

Below that, in a smaller technical packet, came the raw diagnostic.

The well received the *Mercy*'s accent.

It was ugly.

Jaya smiled without meaning to.

Not because it cleared them. It did not. But there was the old ship at last: uneven thermal harmonics, civilian reroutes, hospital shielding, school-deck interference, dead targeting cavities, repair scars, patch stress, engine vibration with a slight asymmetry that had not appeared in the Avar packet at all. The diagnostic did not sing innocence. It limped through the review well and demanded to be measured honestly.

Cho leaned in despite himself. "The Avar throat is smoother."

"Yes."

"Could be cleaned by distance and packet loss."

Fane was already testing. "Not to this degree. Packet loss dirties. It does not idealize around missing civilian reroutes."

Jaya's pulse changed. She disliked that too; emotion was not evidence, even when evidence invited it.

"Compare to late Fracture training models," she said.

Cho looked at her. "Why?"

"Because Ixo claimed resemblance to Quiet Authority adjudication drills. Let us insult everyone equally."

The archive request required command clearance. Jaya gave it. The system warned that late Fracture training models were restricted, incomplete, and politically sensitive. She accepted all three objections and added her seal.

Models populated the well: target templates used in old adjudication exercises, simplified throats meant to teach officers how to recognize compliant and noncompliant hull behavior during convoy enforcement simulations. Most were irrelevant. One was not.

Kaln-class compliant target signature, Quiet Authority drill set, late Fracture.

The smooth throat from Avar did not match it perfectly.

That would have been too generous.

But it shared its spine.

Cho whispered, "Oh."

Fane said something under his breath that was probably not admissible.

Jaya stared at the overlay until the lines stopped being lines and became an institutional memory wearing a dead convoy as a mask.

"Who has access?" she asked.

Fane's answer came slowly. "Officially? High archive courts, restricted naval education, convoy command historical review, some demilitarization auditors."

"Unofficially?"

"Anyone who inherited a ghost room and never declared it."

Valech had said the evidence systems were old enough to be manipulated by anyone who understood their shame.

Jaya wondered whether he had known how precisely true that was.

"Seal this review," she said. "Not buried. Sealed under active pursuit integrity. Copy hash to Gethsemane stack, not content yet. I want chain preserved before half the Belt decides the archive is a rumor."

Cho's hands moved quickly.

"And to Valech?" Fane asked.

Jaya looked at the overlay: Avar throat, *Mercy* accent, Quiet Authority training spine.

If she sent it to Valech privately, he would either help or shape it. If she withheld it, he would call that insubordination and might be right. If she released it publicly too soon, whoever had built the frame would know exactly which trail had lit.

"Send Valech notice that packet integrity review has found restricted-archive correlation requiring controlled inquiry," she said. "No model details. No source label."

Fane looked relieved and worried, a lawyer's natural climate.

"Admiral," Cho said, "clean disabling geometry in four minutes."

The well returned to pursuit. The *Mercy* moved ahead, damaged and stubborn, with Pell's possible exit routes opening like dirty hands. Bell held above and aft, weapons not yet warm but no longer theoretical. Valech's charter petition climbed. Gethsemane had a hash without a secret. The dead still needed justice. The living still needed command.

Jaya stepped out of the review well and onto the command rail.

"Weapons status," she said.

Cho answered carefully. "Ready to prepare disabling package on order."

"Do not warm."

"Admiral?"

"Do not warm. Maintain cold solution. If the *Mercy* powers sealed systems, hides Pell communications, or leaves declared vector for non-civilian cause, we reassess. Until then, pursuit continues cold."

He absorbed the order. "Valech will object."

"Valech has a desk."

"Crew will ask why."

"Tell them evidence review is active and the admiral remains capable of reading both names and numbers."

That almost made Cho smile. Almost.

Jaya lowered her voice. "And Arin."

"Admiral?"

"Doubt is not disloyalty. It is maintenance."

He looked at the overlay still glowing in one corner of the well, then at the pursuit plot.

"Yes, Admiral."

The *Austere Bell* continued through Cinderwake's thinning wake with its weapons cold, its doubts warmer than command doctrine preferred, and its admiral watching an accused warship limp ahead under public witness.

For the first time since Avar Break, Jaya Soth did not merely suspect the evidence might be wrong.

She began to suspect it had been designed to be believed by people exactly like her.

Chapter 13: The Oathlock Gallery

The Oathlock Gallery had three doors, and none of them trusted the others.

The outer door was civilian: white composite, rounded edges, handholds low enough for children, a printed warning in plain language, and a small shelf where visitors could place tools, cups, jewelry, loose slates, private grudges, and anything else that might become an excuse later. The middle door was legal: gray, flat, covered in witness seals, arbitration stamps, covenant revisions, and the signatures of people who had believed signatures could outlive fear. The inner door was military: old black alloy with no handle, built before the covenants, still shaped around the assumption that anyone entering might have to do so while the ship burned.

Noor Venn hated the room before reaching it.

Not because it frightened them. Many rooms frightened Noor. Courts frightened Noor. Medical billing frightened Noor. Children's questions about law frightened Noor most of all because children treated legal categories as if they ought to make moral sense. Fear was not the problem.

The problem was that the Oathlock Gallery was honest.

It did not pretend the *Mercy of Kaln* had always been a hospital ship. It did not hide its old targeting pits behind school murals or soup lockers. It waited at the ship's dead center like a preserved crime scene and said: here is what the hull was for. Here is what we removed. Here is what we promised not to wake without witnesses because promises made elsewhere were not enough.

Noor preferred dishonest rooms with good paperwork. They were easier to cross-examine.

"Outer door record begins," they said.

Their primary recorder sphere brightened. Two backup spheres hovered behind their shoulders. A fourth, Sela's engineering recorder, had been added after the cargo chapel assembly because Sela no longer trusted legal cameras to understand bolts. Ixo observed through a hardened portable chassis clipped to the wall rail, its amber eye narrowed by casing shutters against any unexpected signal bloom. Tovin stood beside the shelf with his injured shoulder braced, face pale from pain he kept treating as a scheduling problem. Captain Mer Anik had assigned two guards and then made one of them a school-deck parent, because fear recorded differently when it carried a child's name.

Sela arrived last with a tool case, a sealed-route diagram slate, and a mood that made the guard nearest her straighten.

"I thought this was legal documentation," Noor said.

"It is," Sela replied. "That is why it needs a mechanic."

"I asked for passive engineering support."

"I am passively supporting with tools."

Noor looked at the case.

Sela lifted it. "Witness-safe. No powered drivers, no write heads, no conductive probes, no heroic nonsense. Mostly lights, calipers, seal readers, and my disappointment."

Tovin said, "She should be here."

"I did not say she should not."

"Your face did."

"My face is not in the evidentiary index."

Ixo's voice came from the wall rail. "It may be in several informal ones."

Noor pointed at the recorder sphere. "We are all going to remember this is an active integrity inspection."

Sela gave the middle door a look. "Fine. Let us admire the tomb."

The phrase made Tovin's mouth tighten.

Noor logged it anyway.

The inspection had begun because Jaya Soth had asked for raw hull-accent diagnostics and because the *Mercy* had sent them. Once the ship offered its scars to pursuit and Gethsemane, Noor had insisted they document the room most likely to turn those scars into suspicion. If Avar Break's forged packet implied witness lock bypass, the real witness locks needed a current chain. Not a ceremonial chain. Not "everyone knows the guns are dead." A chain with images, seal states, witness keys, custody logs, timestamps, and enough ugly specificity to survive hostile reading.

Hostile reading was Noor's native climate.

"Outer door visual," Noor said. "No tamper. Warning text intact. Shelf clear except inspection equipment. Witness present: Noor Venn, legal recorder; Tovin Rees, restraint officer; Sela Marr, engineering witness; Ixo Continuant, machine-person technical witness; Guard Elian Prew, school-deck parent and passenger witness; Guard Omat Darr, crew security witness."

Ixo said, "Please add that my participation is voluntary and under protest against classification as equipment."

Noor did not pause. "Machine-person witness Ixo Continuant participates voluntarily and objects to any record classifying them as equipment."

"Thank you."

"Your objection is becoming a template."

"Templates are how institutions confess repetition."

Sela muttered, "I liked it better when the machines only judged our wiring."

"I heard that," Ixo said.

"I intended you to."

Noor opened the outer door.

The short vestibule beyond smelled faintly of dust and old sealant. The walls were white like the door, but the floor had changed to gray metal under transparent grip panels. Beneath those panels, older deck numbers showed through in black stencils: FIRE CONTROL AUXILIARY TWO. Someone during retrofit had decided not to grind them away. Noor had always wondered whether that had been courage or budget.

The middle door waited at the far end.

Noor moved slowly because cameras made impatience look like concealment. They recorded each seal: covenant court mark, Halvyr verification, Varo Anchorage humanitarian transfer mark, Gethsemane Buoy Court preliminary, Ash Belt Demilitarization Registry, three independent convoy witness stamps, and a row of smaller local seals from ports that had wanted proof before letting the *Mercy* near their children.

"Seal state," Noor said.

Sela scanned passively. "No heat disturbance. No fresh adhesive lift. No conductive bridge. Two surface scratches on Gethsemane preliminary, old. One bubble under Halvyr verification, also old. Covenant court mark has edge dust consistent with last inspection."

"Date of last inspection?"

Tovin answered. "One hundred twelve days ago, Varo Anchorage."

Noor checked the log. "Confirmed."

"Varo's clerk cried," Sela said.

Noor looked up.

"Record context," Sela said, defensive before accusation arrived. "The clerk cried because she had never seen a weapon room preserved instead of stripped. She said her grandfather died under a Kaln-class hull."

Tovin closed his eyes briefly.

Noor added the note. Context was a disease. Once admitted, it spread everywhere.

The middle door required three witness keys.

That was the covenant's central comfort and central fiction. No single person could open the Oathlock Gallery. A legal witness, restraint officer, and civilian/technical witness had to present keys within a sixty-second window. The door recorded identity, time, location, biometric presence, seal state, and purpose. It could still be coerced, of course. Any lock could. The covenant had not abolished coercion. It had tried to make coercion leave fingerprints.

Noor inserted the legal key.

Tovin inserted the restraint key.

Sela did not move.

Noor waited. "Engineering witness."

Sela looked at the slot. "This is where I become part of whatever people later think happened."

"Yes."

"That was supposed to be reassurance?"

"No."

She inserted her key.

The middle door read them.

Three lights woke: blue, white, amber.

PURPOSE?

Noor said, "Document sealed weapon room and verify witness-chain integrity under active false accusation, pursuit, and public evidence review."

The door considered that in the slow, insulting way of old legal machines.

PURPOSE RECORDED. ENTRY PERMITTED. NO POWER TRANSFER TO INNER SYSTEMS. PASSIVE LIGHT ONLY.

The middle door opened.

The air beyond was colder.

The inner door stood three meters ahead, black and blank except for the dead manufacturer mark near its base. Noor's recorder lights reflected in it as small blue wounds.

Noor's throat tightened. They disliked that and documented nothing.

"Middle chamber visual begins," they said. "No personnel beyond inner threshold. Inner door closed. Military alloy surface visible. No fresh scoring. No heat bloom."

Ixo said, "I detect no active signal from beyond the inner door."

Sela scanned. "No power leakage. Seal cage quiet."

Tovin stood very still.

Noor noticed because stillness was a kind of speech with him. "Restraint officer status?"

"Functional."

"That is not an answer."

His mouth twitched without humor. "I dislike this room."

"Logged."

"You did not have to."

"You said it where recorders could hear."

The inner door did not open with keys.

The inner door opened only for inspection state after the middle chamber confirmed no power transfer, no active weapon route, no emergency override, no combat command pressure, and no single witness acting alone. It took two minutes to perform the checks. During those two minutes, no one spoke except to read numbers. The silence acquired weight from the room behind the door.

Noor had seen the Oathlock Gallery twice before. Once during their assignment intake, when the captain of the hour had wanted to impress the new legal officer with the seriousness of the covenant. Once after a dock militia accused the *Mercy* of hiding point-defense kernels behind medical cold storage. Both times, Noor had been relieved by how dead the gallery looked.

Today they did not expect relief.

The inner door opened inward.

Old darkness received them.

The Oathlock Gallery was larger than memory and smaller than fear.

It had once been a secondary targeting center, a room designed to let the ship continue killing if the primary bridge died. Its geometry still carried that purpose. Stations sat in a shallow crescent facing a forward tactical wall now covered by witness glass. Acceleration couches had been removed, leaving bolted scars in the deck. Targeting pits were filled with opaque plates under seal. Fire-control columns stood dark, their status wells empty, their cable throats cut and capped behind transparent covers. Above the main board, where weapon readiness would once have burned, someone had mounted the covenant line in physical letters:

NO WEAPON MAY WAKE WITHOUT PUBLIC WITNESS.

Below it, in smaller text, added later by Mother Kal's hand according to ship story, was another line:

NO PUBLIC WITNESS MAY LOOK AWAY.

Sela whispered, "I hate that it is beautiful."

Noor understood. The room had the terrible order of things built with purpose by people who thought purpose absolved them. The retrofit had not erased the order. It had forced the order to face itself.

"Inner gallery visual begins," Noor said. "All personnel remain on marked inspection path. No contact with sealed consoles except by declared passive tools. Weapon status dark. Targeting pits capped. Cable throats visible under covers."

They moved station by station.

At the first pit, Sela read seal integrity while Noor photographed. At the second, Ixo compared passive resonance against the hull-accent diagnostic. At the third, Tovin read the witness plaque: names of the demilitarization court, names of the engineers who cut the route, names of civilian survivors who observed, names of two former gunners who asked to sign without titles. Noor recorded them all because names resisted summary.

The room did not change.

That should have comforted them.

At the fourth station, Noor found the timestamp.

Not the false one. Not yet. First they found a timestamp that was merely annoying.

"Station four witness cover inspected one hundred twelve days ago," Noor said. "Varo Anchorage. Entry shows legal witness Kessa Yul, restraint officer Tovin Rees, technical witness Sela Marr."

Sela leaned closer. "I was there."

"Confirmed by log."

"I remember because the clerk cried."

"Also logged."

They moved to station five.

Station five had once handled lateral point-defense routing. The barrels were gone. The ammunition paths were slagged. The sensor route had been capped and repurposed for civilian hull monitoring, which meant any accusation involving hidden point-defense activity would eventually come here to sharpen its teeth.

Noor's recorder sphere dipped.

"Station five cover seal intact. Witness cover last inspected..."

They stopped.

Sela looked up. "What?"

Noor read the tiny embedded log again.

LAST WITNESS CONFIRMATION: VARO ANCHORAGE INSPECTION, 112 DAYS AGO.

Legal witness: Kessa Yul.

Restraint officer: Tovin Rees.

Technical witness: Sela Marr.

Timestamp: 03:17:44 local station.

Noor looked back at station four.

"Read station four again."

Sela frowned but obeyed. "Varo Anchorage. Yul, Rees, Marr. Timestamp 03:17:44."

"Same second."

"Batch confirmation?"

"Witness keys do not batch for separate covers."

Tovin came closer. "No. They require physical proximity ping at each station."

Noor opened the inspection archive on their slate. The Varo log unfolded: station one 03:12:08, station two 03:13:51, station three 03:15:26, station four 03:17:44, station five 03:19:10, station six 03:21:02.

The embedded station-five log said 03:17:44.

Sela's face changed. Anger first, then calculation. "Could be a display fault."

"Passive read," Noor said.

She scanned with the seal reader. "Embedded chip returns 03:17:44."

Ixo said, "Archive returns 03:19:10."

Tovin's voice went quiet. "Which one is signed?"

Noor checked.

The room seemed to lean toward the answer.

"Both."

Guard Elian Prew, school-deck parent and witness, said, "Is that possible?"

Noor disliked that question because the answer was always worse than either yes or no.

"It should not be."

Sela opened her tool case and took out a passive magnifier. "Noor."

"What?"

"Do I have permission to inspect the key cradle?"

Noor looked at Tovin.

Tovin said, "Yes. Passive only. Record tool."

Sela held the magnifier up to the recorder. "Optical magnifier, no power, no contact beyond external viewing."

"Logged."

She bent over the witness key cradle beside station five. It was a small recessed plate where the technical witness key would have touched during inspection. Each cradle carried microscopic abrasion patterns from years of key insertions. Sela had once compared them to old scars on a hand. Noor had told her not to put that in a report.

Sela looked for a long time.

"This cradle was opened," she said.

Noor's stomach dropped in a professional manner.

"Define opened."

"Not the station cover. The cradle face. See the edge under the seal lip? Someone lifted it with the right tool, or a tool made to look right. Old. Not fresh. No broken seal because the cradle face sits under the witness assembly, not the weapon cover."

Tovin said, "At Varo?"

"Could have been before. Could have been after. I need proper analysis."

Noor heard their own voice become too calm. "Does this indicate weapon access?"

"No. It indicates witness-key log access."

Ixo's amber light pulsed. "A false timestamp in a witness key cradle could make a later event appear to share legitimacy with a prior inspection."

Noor looked at the dark station.

Station five. Lateral point-defense routing. Sensor caps. Civilian hull monitoring. A place where a forged accusation could claim the *Mercy* had routed around dead systems if someone could produce a convincing witness-chain echo.

"Could it insert a false unlock?" Tovin asked.

Sela shook her head, then stopped herself. "Not alone. It could not open the cover or wake anything. But it could create log ambiguity. It could make a false access look like it borrowed real witnesses."

"Our witnesses," Noor said.

The words made the room colder.

Kessa Yul. Tovin Rees. Sela Marr.

Names that had stood beside a real inspection.

Names that could be reused if someone made the log believe the wrong second.

Tovin stepped back from station five as if distance could prevent implication. "I did not-"

Noor cut him off. "Do not say unnecessary things in a sealed inspection."

His mouth closed.

It was harsh. It was also kindness. Denials given too early became material for people who preferred performance to sequence.

Sela's hands trembled once, then steadied. "My key is in my custody."

"Current custody," Noor said. "We will verify historical custody."

"I know where it has been."

"Then the record will survive saying so."

Sela glared, then swallowed the glare because she understood.

Ixo said, "We should preserve volatile context before alerting external parties."

"Agreed," Noor said. "Full room lockdown. No public mirror until preliminary chain is sealed."

Tovin looked at them. "No public mirror?"

"Not until we know whether the mirror carries a half-truth that can be weaponized. This is an evidence integrity exception, not concealment. I will write it that way and hate myself appropriately."

He nodded once.

Mer's voice came over the inspection channel. "Report."

Noor took one breath.

"Captain, station five witness-key cradle shows timestamp discrepancy between embedded station log and Varo archive. Possible cradle tamper. No indication of weapon activation. No indication of cover breach. Potential evidence-chain compromise involving witness key identities."

The channel went silent.

Then Mer said, "Names?"

"Yul. Rees. Marr."

Sela stared at the station. Tovin stared at the floor.

Mer's voice stayed level. "Secure gallery. Nobody leaves inspection chain alone. I am sending Mother Kal as additional civilian witness."

Noor almost objected that Mother Kal was old and the ship was under pursuit and the room was already crowded. Then they understood why Mer had chosen her. No one aboard could accuse Mother Kal of being impressed by command titles. No one off-ship could easily dismiss her as technical staff protecting itself.

"Accepted," Noor said.

"Bell?"

"Not yet."

"Valech?"

"Absolutely not yet."

Mer exhaled, audible and controlled. "Gethsemane?"

"Hash notice only. Content after preliminary chain."

"Do it."

Noor created the hash notice with fingers that wanted to move faster than procedure. They forced each field to be correct.

MERCY OF KALN OATHLOCK GALLERY INSPECTION HAS IDENTIFIED INTERNAL WITNESS-CHAIN DISCREPANCY AT STATION FIVE. NO WEAPON ACTIVATION DETECTED. CONTENT SEALED PENDING PRELIMINARY CHAIN PRESERVATION. HASH ATTACHED TO GETHSEMANE STACK, AUSTERE BELL NOTICE PENDING.

They stopped before sending.

"Tovin."

He looked up.

"You are named in the discrepancy. Do you consent to remaining as restraint witness for preservation phase?"

Pain crossed his face, not shoulder pain.

"Would my withdrawal look worse?"

"Yes."

"Would my remaining contaminate it?"

"Also yes."

Sela made a soft, furious sound.

Noor hated law for offering true answers like knives.

"Then what is correct?" Tovin asked.

"Correct is recording the conflict and adding witnesses."

He nodded. "I consent under conflict."

Noor turned to Sela. "Technical witness?"

She looked ready to throw the key cradle through a wall. Instead she said, "I consent under conflict and reserve the right to become impossible later."

"Logged."

Ixo said, "Machine-person witness consents without being named in the discrepancy and notes that this may make me disproportionately trusted in a human evidence problem."

Noor blinked.

Ixo added, "That was not humor."

"Logged."

They sent the hash notice.

While Mother Kal made her way through the ship, they documented everything again.

Station five visual. Seal. Cradle edge. Embedded timestamp. Archive timestamp. Key identity. Station-four comparison. Station-six comparison. Passive scans. Tool identities. Witness emotional statements excluded unless voluntarily offered. Noor almost laughed at that field. The room was drowning in emotional statements shaped like breathing.

Tovin stood near the inner door, away from station five but within the camera frame. He looked at the old targeting wall and not at anyone else.

Sela worked beside the cradle with precise fury. "There is no fresh tool mark. Whoever did this either did it before the last dust layer or cleaned well."

"How long could the discrepancy have existed?"

"Since Varo. Since before Varo. Since original retrofit if someone had access and patience. I hate all answers."

Noor checked the archive again. "The Varo external archive is consistent."

Ixo said, "External consistency may mean the embedded cradle was altered after external archive write."

"Or before, with later archive correction."

"Yes."

"Or the archive is false and the cradle is true."

Sela looked up sharply. "I was at station five after station four."

"Memory is not chain," Noor said.

Sela's face hardened.

Tovin said quietly, "She was."

"Also not chain."

The cruelty of the sentence landed. Noor let it. The room required it.

Mother Kal arrived twelve minutes later, breathing heavily but refusing assistance. She wore a pressure cardigan over her ship suit and had tied her white hair back with a strip of school-deck cloth printed with small yellow moons. Jessa was not with her; that, Noor guessed, had required negotiation or command force.

Mother Kal stopped at the inner door and looked into the Oathlock Gallery.

"Still ugly," she said.

"You painted the hospital walls," Sela said.

"Not this room."

"Why not?"

Mother Kal stepped onto the inspection path. "Because some rooms should not be helped to lie."

Noor added her as civilian witness and summarized the finding. Mother Kal listened without interrupting, which made the summary feel more severe.

When Noor finished, Mother Kal looked at Tovin. "Your name?"

"Yes."

"And Sela's."

"Yes," Sela said.

"Do you think they used people the ship trusts?"

Noor said, "That is an inference."

"I am old, not in court. Answer differently."

Ixo said, "The selected names are high-trust within the ship's restraint narrative: legal witness, restraint officer, technical witness. If reused, they would make a false access socially plausible."

Mother Kal nodded. "They forged family handwriting."

Noor felt that in the ribs.

"Yes," they said. "Possibly."

Tovin looked older than he had in Cinderwake.

The inspection might still have remained contained if the *Austere Bell* had not transmitted then.

Noor's slate chimed with Jaya Soth's seal.

MERCY OF KALN, AUSTERE BELL ACKNOWLEDGES HASH NOTICE RECEIVED VIA GETHSEMANE STACK. CLARIFY WHETHER INTERNAL WITNESS-CHAIN DISCREPANCY AFFECTS SEALED SYSTEM INTEGRITY OR CUSTODY RISK. PURSUIT STATUS UNCHANGED. WEAPONS REMAIN COLD.

Sela looked at Tovin. "Weapons remain cold."

"She is telling us something," he said.

Noor said, "She is also asking whether our own evidence just got worse."

Mer came onto channel. "Answer narrow."

Noor drafted:

DISCREPANCY AFFECTS WITNESS-CHAIN INTEGRITY AT OATHLOCK STATION FIVE. CURRENT INSPECTION FINDS NO WEAPON ACTIVATION, NO COVER BREACH, NO SEALED SYSTEM POWER TRANSFER. CUSTODY RISK INCREASED BECAUSE NAMED WITNESS IDENTITIES MAY HAVE BEEN REUSED OR TARGETED. PRELIMINARY CONTENT TO FOLLOW UNDER GETHSEMANE HASH AFTER CHAIN PRESERVATION.

They looked at Tovin, Sela, Ixo, Mother Kal.

"Objections?"

Sela said, "Say 'targeted' before 'reused.'"

Noor changed it.

Tovin said, "Add that named witnesses remain under conflict record."

Noor added it.

Mother Kal said, "Say children are not involved."

"They are not."

"Say it. People put children in blanks."

Noor added:

NO SCHOOL-DECK OR PASSENGER WITNESS KEYS ARE IMPLICATED BY CURRENT FINDING.

Ixo said, "Add machine-person witness present."

"Already."

"Voluntary."

"Already."

"Good."

They sent the answer.

The reply from Bell did not come at once.

During the silence, the Oathlock Gallery seemed to breathe old air through dead consoles. Noor continued the inspection because stopping would make fear look like instruction. Station six was clean. Station seven was clean. The main targeting wall was sealed, dark, and offensively dramatic. The old fire-control columns had no power. The cable throats remained cut. The room, if judged only by weapons, was dead.

If judged by evidence, it had a pulse.

At the main witness cabinet, Sela found the second irregularity.

Not another false timestamp. A mark.

"Noor."

Noor moved beside her.

Sela pointed at the inside edge of the cabinet's transparent cover. "This witness bus has maintenance dust everywhere except here."

"Could be airflow."

"No. This cover has not opened, but a microservice port beside it was accessed."

"Purpose?"

"Read-only diagnostics for witness key timing and seal health."

Tovin's face went still. "Can that port write?"

"Not supposed to."

Noor closed their eyes for one beat. Supposed to was where law went to bleed.

Ixo said, "If a read-only port was coerced at firmware level, it might not write to weapons. It might write to interpretation."

"Meaning?" Mother Kal asked.

"Meaning the ship could remain physically disarmed while its witness history becomes editable enough to frame."

Sela whispered, "A war fought in footnotes."

Noor recorded the phrase because it was too accurate to leave informal.

Bell replied:

AUSTERE BELL RECEIVES. ACTIVE REVIEW OPENED. MAINTAIN CHAIN. DO NOT PUBLICLY RELEASE RAW IRREGULARITY UNTIL HASHED COPY IS ACCEPTED BY GETHSEMANE OR UNTIL IMMEDIATE SAFETY REQUIRES DISCLOSURE. VALECH HAS REQUESTED CONTENT. BELL HAS NOT FORWARDED.

Below that, narrow band:

THIS DOES NOT CLEAR YOU. IT DOES MAKE THE PACKET DIRTIER.

Noor read it aloud.

Sela laughed once. It sounded almost painful. "I might like her."

Tovin said, "Do not. It complicates things."

"Everything useful does."

Mer's voice cut in. "Pell has sent a second message."

Noor looked up sharply. "Now?"

"Now."

The message appeared on Noor's slate through command relay:

YOU FOUND SOMETHING IN THE OLD ROOM. GOOD. THAT MEANS YOU KNOW YOU NEED ME. CLEAN EXIT PRICE DOUBLES IF BELL GETS THE STORY FIRST.

No one in the Oathlock Gallery spoke.

The dead weapon room, which had not fired in years, suddenly felt aimed.

Ixo's amber light dimmed, then brightened. "Pell knows about the inspection."

Sela's hand tightened around the passive magnifier. "How?"

Noor's mind ran through channels: public hash notice, Bell acknowledgment, Gethsemane receipt, ship internal routing, command relays, Pardon Reef ears, dirty salvage traffic, someone aboard.

Tovin said the thought everyone avoided. "Either he guessed from the hash notice, or someone is reading closer than we thought."

Mother Kal looked around the Oathlock Gallery: sealed pits, dead boards, cut throats, old purpose under witness glass.

"Then finish the record," she said.

Noor looked at her.

"Finish it before fear edits you."

So they did.

They documented station five until its false second became impossible to smooth away. They documented the microservice-port dust break. They documented every witness conflict, every tool, every refusal to infer beyond evidence, every place inference was unavoidable. They sealed the preliminary packet under Gethsemane hash and sent accepted copies to the dormant stack, the *Austere Bell*, and the *Mercy*'s public mirror index without raw release. They marked Valech request pending and unfulfilled. They marked Pell communication mirrored in full under criminal-interference index.

Only after the final hash returned did Noor allow themselves to step back from station five.

Their hands hurt.

They had been gripping the slate too hard.

Tovin stood beneath the covenant line and looked at the room as if seeing it not as a lock but as a wound.

"They did not need to wake the weapons," he said.

Noor knew what he meant.

"No."

Sela closed her tool case with a snap. "They only needed everyone to believe we might have."

Ixo's voice was very soft. "A weapon can be imaginary and still govern real choices."

Mother Kal touched the inspection-path rail, not the consoles, never the consoles.

"Then we keep showing people the difference between ghosts and hands," she said.

Outside the Oathlock Gallery, the *Mercy of Kaln* continued forward under pursuit, scarred from Cinderwake, hunted by lawful ships, courted by criminals, and now carrying proof that its own witness history had been touched by someone who understood the ship too well.

The room behind the inner door remained dark.

That was no longer enough.

Chapter 14: Pardon Reef Offer

Pardon Reef did not appear on respectable charts until after it had already collected docking fees from three governments.

Respectable charts preferred objects that admitted what they were. Pardon Reef refused. It was a salvage cloud, a debt market, a monastery for people fleeing taxes, a black clinic, a fuel cache, a listening post, a court of last resort for cases too dirty for real courts and too profitable for silence, and, on alternate Wednesdays according to one union warning, a family entertainment venue. Its physical body was no less evasive: two broken refinery spines, seven habitat drums, a hundred smaller pressure pockets, shielded tents, illegal garden bladders, old cargo frames, wrecked court buoys, and a loose swarm of drones that repainted transponder tags faster than most authorities could file objections.

The Reef did not orbit so much as negotiate with inertia.

The *Mercy of Kaln* had avoided it for nine months.

Now Hask Pell had placed himself between them and the next lawful route like a man leaning in a doorway he did not own.

Captain Mer Anik convened the offer review in command instead of the cargo chapel because some temptations should not be given an audience before they had been dissected. Tovin stood on the restraint side of the central plot with Noor at the legal station, Sela patched in from engineering, Ixo in the ship's side display, and Mother Kal present because she had refused to leave after the Oathlock inspection and because Mer had learned to save time by calling that "approved civilian witness" instead of arguing.

Pell's second message hovered in the air above the plot.

YOU FOUND SOMETHING IN THE OLD ROOM. GOOD. THAT MEANS YOU KNOW YOU NEED ME. CLEAN EXIT PRICE DOUBLES IF BELL GETS THE STORY FIRST.

Below it, the first message remained:

MERCY OF KALN, YOU NEED CLEAN EXIT FROM CINDERWAKE. I CAN SELL ONE. HASK PELL.

Noor had indexed both under criminal interference, evidence contamination, and "insulting timing." The last category did not exist in convoy law. Noor had created it as a private tag and then forgotten to hide it from the command display.

Mer noticed and did not comment.

"Provenance," she said.

Ixo answered. "Both messages entered through Pardon Reef dirty relay cluster, shell route Bloom-Circle-Seven, last public hop registered to a debt chapel that officially burned six years ago and continues to issue invoices. Signal structure includes a compression habit associated with Pell's known brokerage channels. Authenticity likely."

"Likely Pell or likely someone using him?"

"Likely Pell using someone else using three other people. That is his preferred signature."

Sela's screen showed her shoulders deep inside a civilian nav eye housing, hair tied back with insulation tape. "I hate that criminals get style."

Noor said, "Style is admissibility's enemy."

"You say that like courts have never been impressed by a good coat."

"Courts are frequently stupid in textile-adjacent ways."

Mer cut across them. "What does he know?"

The command plot shifted to show Pardon Reef's probable assets: salvage claims near Tallow Spur, unlicensed pusher drones in Broken Buoy Cut, a debt lien on a maintenance channel called Mercy's Debt, and two hidden thermal bodies that might be fuel caches, traps, or restaurants depending on which shell company lied least.

Ixo said, "He knew we needed an exit before our public plot made all exit constraints obvious. He knew an Oathlock irregularity had been discovered before raw content release. He may have inferred from hash notice and Bell's pause. He may have compromised a relay. He may have an informant aboard, in Bell, in Gethsemane's dormant stack, or in Valech's office."

"That narrows it to civilization," Noor said.

Mother Kal sat in the auxiliary chair with both hands folded over a school-deck cloth bundle in her lap. "He used 'old room.'"

Everyone looked at her.

"Not Oathlock," she said. "Not sealed systems. Old room. That is how people aboard say it when they do not want children asking."

Tovin felt the words settle unpleasantly.

Sela's video feed froze for half a second, then recovered. Her face had gone flat. "Are you saying the leak is aboard?"

"I am saying words have addresses."

Noor recorded the observation. "Internal phrase match noted."

Mer's expression did not change, which meant she had already considered it and disliked needing someone else to say it. "Limit internal distribution. No accusation without evidence. We have enough ghosts."

Tovin looked at Pell's message again. Clean exit price doubles. If Bell gets the story first.

"He wants us to fear lawful review," he said.

Noor made a small sound. "He has chosen a fertile field."

"He also wants Bell to fear we will take his route," Mer said. "And Valech to see us tempted."

Sela leaned closer to her camera. "What is the actual offer? Messages like this are just smoke. Where is the knife?"

As if Pell had been waiting for an appropriately theatrical line, a third packet arrived.

Noor did not open it immediately. They trapped it in a sandboxed viewer, stripped active components, verified no embedded write commands, swore at three attempts to auto-negotiate a receipt, and only then expanded the text.

HASK PELL TO MERCY OF KALN, CAPTAIN OR WHOEVER IS BRAVE ENOUGH TO PRETEND TO BE CAPTAIN:

YOU ARE EXITING CINDERWAKE WITH ONE BAD SHOULDER, ONE LEGAL WOUND, AND BELL WHERE IT CAN START FEELING CLEAN ABOUT SHOOTING. VALECH IS BUILDING A CHARTER WITH YOUR NAME AS THE FIRST NAIL. GETHSEMANE WILL TAKE TOO LONG. YOUR OLD ROOM TELLS YOU SOMEONE HAS HANDS INSIDE YOUR STORY.

I CAN GIVE YOU:

1. A CLEAN EXIT THROUGH MERCY'S DEBT WITH SENSOR FOG, NO COLLISION, AND NO BELL LINE FOR NINETEEN MINUTES.
2. THREE BLACK-MARKET POINT DEFENSE BUNDLES COMPATIBLE WITH OLD KALN MOUNT GEOMETRY, NEVER USED, NEVER LOVED, EASY TO THROW AWAY AFTER.
3. TWO SETS OF EXIT PAPERS FOR PASSENGER TRANSFER UNDER HALVYR RELIEF REGISTRY SHELL.
4. A WITNESS WHO SAW A QUIET AUTHORITY ARCHIVE KEY PASS THROUGH REEF HANDS AFTER AVAR.
5. A DEBT STRUCTURE THAT MAKES THIS ALL SIMPLE.

PRICE:

A. MERCY CARRIES ONE SEALED SALVAGE CLAIM TO TALLOW CROWN ROUTE.
B. MERCY ACKNOWLEDGES PARDON REEF AS EMERGENCY PROTECTOR FOR ANY PASSENGERS TRANSFERRED UNDER MY PAPERS.
C. MERCY HOLDS MY DEBT NOTE UNTIL CLEARED BY WORK, NOT MONEY.
D. YOUR MACHINE WITNESS SPEAKS WITH ME PRIVATELY FOR EIGHT MINUTES.

NO GUNS, NO EXIT. NO PAPERS, NO CHILDREN OFF. NO DEBT, NO WITNESS. YOU CAN KEEP YOUR SOUL IF IT FITS IN THE AIRLOCK.

HASK PELL
PARDON REEF, FORGIVEN IN ADVANCE BY NO ONE IMPORTANT

For a moment, command contained only machinery sounds.

Then Sela said, "I am going to need permission to scream in a maintenance shaft."

"Denied for now," Mer said.

"Appeal."

"Pending."

Noor's face had drained of color. "Point defense bundles."

Tovin looked at the offer until the words blurred and sharpened again.

Three black-market point defense bundles compatible with old Kaln mount geometry.

He imagined them as hardware: compact, modular, probably stolen from demilitarization lots or militia stock, designed to be mounted quickly on stripped hulls, tied into local sensors, fired under manual or crude automatic control. Not enough to make the *Mercy* a battleship again. Enough to change every sentence about what it was.

Enough to kill drones.

Enough to kill a boarding craft.

Enough to prove Valech right before any trial could prove him wrong.

Mother Kal said, "He offers poison and bread in the same hand."

Ixo's amber light flickered. "The archive-key witness may be real."

Noor turned. "That is what bothers you most?"

"No. It is what bothers me specifically."

Mer said, "Explain."

"If Pell has a witness who saw a Quiet Authority archive key after Avar, that may connect the training-model signature to a person or chain outside both the *Mercy* and Bell. Pell would not offer that unless he believed we had enough knowledge to value it."

Sela said, "Or unless he read our Oathlock panic and guessed what bait smelled expensive."

"Also possible."

"Useful machine phrase."

"It saves time."

Noor dragged the offer into categories with visible effort. "Item one: route assistance. Criminally controlled exit, likely evasion, maybe survival. Item two: illegal weapons, covenant catastrophe. Item three: false passenger transfer papers, fraud and possible hostage pipeline. Item four: potential witness, evidence value but contaminated. Item five: debt structure, coercive control. Requirement D: private access to Ixo, unacceptable."

"Thank you," Ixo said.

"Do not thank me for recognizing kidnapping-adjacent nonsense."

"Still."

Mer looked at Tovin. "Restraint."

The word did not ask for feeling. Good. Feeling was already overrepresented.

"No weapons," he said.

"Obviously," Sela snapped.

He looked at her.

She held his gaze for one second, then looked away. "Fine. Not obviously. Say it."

"No black-market bundles. No mounting. No accepting. No holding as contingency. If they come aboard, even sealed, the ship changes. The frame wins ground."

Noor nodded. "Agree."

"No false papers," Tovin continued. "Passenger transfer under a shell registry makes Pell their protector on record. That is ownership language wearing rescue clothes."

Mother Kal's hands tightened on the cloth bundle. "No."

"No private access to Ixo."

Ixo said, "Strongly agreed."

"Debt note?" Mer asked.

"No debt that binds ship action to Pell. A debt cleared by work means he chooses the work."

Sela said, "That leaves the route and the witness."

"The route may be a trap."

"The current route is also a trap. It is just better notarized."

Mer leaned over the plot. "Navigation."

The officer on duty, Lemm, brought up the exit map. Lemm had the sleepless face of a person whose calculations had become theological. "Declared vector gives Bell clean disabling geometry in eleven minutes if they choose to warm. Residual dust offers partial cover for six. Tallow Spur through Mercy's Debt could break line of sight for roughly eighteen to twenty minutes if the sensor fog is real. It also passes within two thousand kilometers of Pardon Reef salvage claims and three unverified thermal bodies."

"Collision risk?"

"Manageable with current damage if Pell's lane data is real. Severe if falsified."

"Can we find it ourselves?"

"Given time, yes."

"Time available?"

"Not enough."

Noor said, "Using a route named Mercy's Debt after a criminal offer will look like acceptance even if we take nothing else."

Sela replied, "Being disabled by Bell will look like losing."

"Law is not scored by aesthetics."

"It absolutely is. You said that about coats."

Noor closed their eyes. "I hate that you listen selectively."

Mer let them spend the edge of panic on each other because it was better than silence.

Tovin looked at the Bell plot. The *Austere Bell* held above and aft, weapons cold for now. Jaya had not forwarded raw Oathlock content to Valech. Jaya had asked for the diagnostic. Jaya had found the packet dirtier. Jaya was still pursuit.

"We should mirror Pell's offer to Bell and Gethsemane," he said.

Noor nodded at once. "Already prepared."

Sela's face hardened. "Including the route?"

"Yes."

"Then Bell can block it."

"If we hide it, Pell owns the fact that we hid it."

"If we show it, Bell owns the exit."

"Better Bell than Pell."

Sela stared at him. "That is a sentence a desperate person says right before being disappointed."

"Yes."

Mer said, "Send full offer to Bell, Gethsemane hash, and public index delayed by safety review. Ask Bell whether they can guarantee non-disabling pursuit through declared vector while Oathlock chain is reviewed."

Noor's eyebrows rose. "They will not."

"Make them say it."

Noor sent the packet.

The answer from Bell arrived in less than a minute, which meant Jaya had been waiting.

AUSTERE BELL RECEIVES PELL OFFER. DO NOT ACCEPT WEAPONS, FALSE PAPERS, DEBT NOTE, OR PRIVATE MACHINE-WITNESS ACCESS. USE OF MERCY'S DEBT ROUTE WILL BE TREATED AS EVASIVE IF NOT DECLARED AND WITNESSED. BELL CANNOT GUARANTEE NON-DISABLING PURSUIT IF ACCUSED VESSEL ENTERS CRIMINALLY CONTROLLED FOG WITHOUT OBSERVABLE SAFETY CAUSE.

Then, narrow band:

I CAN HOLD WEAPONS COLD FOR NINE MINUTES IF YOU HOLD DECLARED VECTOR AND SEND OATHLOCK PRELIMINARY. AFTER THAT I MAY NOT OWN THE GEOMETRY.

Noor read both.

Sela said, "Nine minutes."

Mer's face became unreadable. "Pell offers nineteen."

"Pell offers debt," Mother Kal said.

"Bell offers guns in nine minutes," Sela replied.

"Cold guns," Noor said.

"For now."

Tovin watched the plot lines converge. Restraint often sounded like refusal from outside. Inside, it felt like mathematics under moral load. No weapons. No false papers. No private machine access. No debt. The route remained, a dirty physical fact. The witness remained, a dirty evidentiary fact.

"Counteroffer Pell," he said.

Noor turned. "Excuse me?"

"Publicly. No weapons, no papers, no debt, no private access. We will accept route data only if transmitted simultaneously to Bell and Gethsemane under open witness, with no obligation beyond lawful salvage fee adjudicated later. Witness information can be transmitted as sworn statement to Gethsemane and Bell, with source protection argued in court. Nothing private."

Sela looked at him as if he had begun juggling lit charges. "He will laugh."

"Good. Record the laugh."

Mer considered. "It exposes whether he wants money, leverage, or silence."

Noor began drafting, eyes brightening despite exhaustion. "It also prevents later claims that we refused exculpatory evidence."

Mother Kal said, "And tells any listener aboard that command did not hide the bread because of the poison."

Tovin nodded.

Ixo said, "Add that I refuse private access but consent to a public question list under machine-person standing."

"That gives him a platform," Noor said.

"It gives me one too."

Noor added it.

The counteroffer went out under full mirror to Bell and Gethsemane hash:

PARDON REEF / HASK PELL:

MERCY OF KALN REFUSES ALL WEAPON TRANSFER, FALSE REGISTRY PAPERS, DEBT NOTE, PROTECTOR STATUS, AND PRIVATE MACHINE-WITNESS ACCESS.

ROUTE DATA FOR MERCY'S DEBT MAY BE TRANSMITTED ONLY UNDER OPEN WITNESS, SIMULTANEOUSLY TO MERCY OF KALN, AUSTERE BELL, AND GETHSEMANE STACK. ANY LAWFUL SALVAGE FEE MAY BE ADJUDICATED LATER BY DISTRIBUTED COURT. NO OPERATIONAL DEBT WILL BE ACCEPTED.

ARCHIVE-KEY WITNESS INFORMATION MAY BE SUBMITTED AS SWORN OR PROTECTED STATEMENT TO GETHSEMANE STACK AND AUSTERE BELL. IXO CONTINUANT REFUSES PRIVATE ACCESS AND CONSENTS ONLY TO PUBLIC OR SEALED-COURT QUESTIONS UNDER MACHINE-PERSON STANDING.

NO HIDDEN OBLIGATION WILL BE CARRIED ABOARD THIS SHIP.

Pell answered with audio.

Noor stripped it, sandboxed it, complained about the embedded perfume marker, removed a visual layer that would have displayed Pardon Reef's seal in animated gold, and played only the sound.

Hask Pell's voice was warm, amused, and intimate in the way of men who made distance feel like poor manners.

"Oh, *Mercy*. They really did paint a spine on you, didn't they?"

Sela made a gagging motion.

Pell continued. "Captain Anik, Officer Rees, Legal Noor, bright Ixo, furious engineer whose name I do know but will not spend for free, and Mother Kal if they were clever enough to keep you in the room: your counteroffer is adorable. It is also rude. I have three drones waiting in Mercy's Debt with fog keys, two bundles of guns I can sell to someone less sentimental, and a witness who will not speak into a court stack because court stacks do not keep people breathing when archive ghosts wake."

Noor froze the recording. "He named Mother Kal."

Mother Kal looked unsurprised. "Pell knows old women because old women keep accounts."

Noor resumed.

"You think debt is ownership. Correct. You think ownership is always bad. Provincial. Everyone is owned by something. Bell is owned by Valech's future charter. Valech is owned by dead names. You are owned by a sentence carved above dead guns. I am honest enough to put my ownership terms in writing."

Tovin felt the command deck listening too hard.

"You refuse weapons. Fine. Admirable. Expensive. You refuse papers. Fine. Children can enjoy principle under pressure hoods. You refuse private Ixo. Rude, but fine. I will miss the conversation."

Ixo said, "Unlikely."

"Here is my revised offer," Pell said. "Route fog for Mercy's Debt transmitted to you only. If you share with Bell before entry, I sell the fog gap to Valech's friends instead and you get squeezed. No guns required. No papers required. No debt note today. Instead, you carry my sealed salvage claim to Tallow Crown unopened and place it in the tribunal intake. That is all. A package. A favor. A little innocent mass aboard an innocent ship."

Noor muttered, "There it is."

"For the archive witness, I give you a taste."

The audio shifted. Another voice entered: distorted, breathy, terrified or staged as terrified.

"I saw the key. Quiet mark under red wax. It came from a convoy command locker, not Reef. Man who carried it had tribunal shoes. Said Avar would make the old law necessary again. Said the Kaln throat was already taught to admirals."

The voice cut out.

Pell returned.

"There. Bread crumb. You have eight minutes before Bell geometry improves and twelve before my fog gap is worth more to someone else. Carry the claim. No debt note. No guns. No private machine talk. See? I can be generous when saints negotiate badly."

The audio ended.

The command deck erupted in overlapping silence: no one speaking because everyone had too much to say.

Noor replayed the witness fragment, once, twice. Ixo isolated the distortion. Sela stopped pretending to work on the nav eye and stared openly at the screen. Mother Kal closed her eyes. Tovin watched Mer.

Mer asked, "Can the salvage claim be a weapon?"

Sela answered first. "Yes."

Noor said, "Legally, also yes."

Ixo said, "Informationally, almost certainly."

"Can we scan it?"

Sela said, "If we ever let it near the hull, yes. If Pell is competent, scans will tell us it is either harmless or harmless-looking."

Noor added, "Carrying sealed claims is routine in salvage law. That is why it is useful. Refusal can look like fear of evidence. Acceptance can create obligation, contamination, or smuggling."

Mer looked at Tovin.

"No hidden package," he said.

"Even for the witness?"

The question came from Sela.

Not Mer. Not Noor. Sela.

Tovin turned to her screen.

She looked angry at herself for asking and asked anyway. "That witness fragment connects to everything Jaya found. Convoy command locker. Tribunal shoes. Avar making old law necessary. Kaln throat taught to admirals. If we reject the package and lose the witness, what do we call that?"

"Not buying evidence with control of the ship."

"Easy sentence."

"No."

"It sounds easy from where I am standing."

He stepped closer to her screen. "If we carry Pell's sealed claim, he decides when to reveal what it was. He can say we delivered contraband, or hid evidence, or served his salvage action, or owed him safe passage. He can put a second frame inside the first. The witness may be real. That does not make the price clean."

Sela's jaw worked.

"I want the witness," she said.

"So do I."

"I want the route."

"So do I."

"I want one person offering help not to be a parasite."

Mother Kal said, "Then we are all children today."

Sela's anger flashed, then faltered. Mother Kal's voice had not been cruel. That made it land.

Bell transmitted:

AUSTERE BELL INTERCEPTED PELL AUDIO VIA YOUR MIRROR. DO NOT ACCEPT SEALED CLAIM. WITNESS FRAGMENT CORRELATES WITH ACTIVE REVIEW. SEND FULL AUDIO HASH TO GETHSEMANE. BELL CAN HOLD WEAPONS COLD FOR SIX MINUTES.

Then narrow:

I KNOW SIX IS NOT ENOUGH.

Noor read both.

Mer said, "Navigation. Without Pell."

Lemm's voice came thin. "Declared vector reaches Bell clean geometry in six to seven. If we angle three degrees toward Broken Buoy Cut, not Mercy's Debt, residual dust and the dead habitat fragments we passed can mask us for maybe four additional minutes. It is not criminal fog. It is also not enough to reach Gethsemane bandwidth."

"Risk."

"Patch stress manageable. Bell may treat as evasive unless declared as debris avoidance."

"Is it debris avoidance?"

Lemm hesitated. "There is debris."

Noor said, "That was not a yes."

Tovin looked at the plot. Three degrees. Not a lie if the debris existed, a lie if purpose was pursuit geometry. The kind of half-truth that multiplied under pressure.

"Declare it honestly," he said. "Vector adjustment to avoid residual debris and preserve review time. Not hiding the tactical effect."

Noor's mouth tightened. "That gives Bell the geometry."

"They already have geometry. We give them our reason before Pell gives them our motive."

Mer nodded. "Do it. Send Pell response first."

Noor drafted with brutal economy:

PARDON REEF / HASK PELL:

MERCY OF KALN REFUSES SEALED SALVAGE CLAIM, HIDDEN ROUTE FOG, WEAPONS, FALSE PAPERS, DEBT, AND PRIVATE ACCESS. WITNESS FRAGMENT HASHED TO GETHSEMANE AND AUSTERE BELL. IF YOUR WITNESS SEEKS PROTECTION, SUBMIT THROUGH DISTRIBUTED COURT, CONVOY UNION, OR PUBLIC CHANNEL. NO HIDDEN OBLIGATION WILL BE CARRIED ABOARD THIS SHIP.

Mer approved.

They sent it.

Pell replied with a single line:

THEN BLEED HONESTLY.

The words were almost worse than the offer because they sounded, for one ugly second, like prophecy.

Mer gave the vector order.

The *Mercy* angled three degrees toward Broken Buoy Cut under public declaration. The ship's damaged armor complained. The patch held. Bell adjusted above and aft.

Jaya Soth answered publicly:

AUSTERE BELL ACKNOWLEDGES DECLARED VECTOR ADJUSTMENT AND TACTICAL EFFECT. PURSUIT CONTINUES. WEAPONS REMAIN COLD FOR CURRENT REVIEW WINDOW.

No duration.

No promise.

Still, cold.

Noor sent the Pell audio hash and witness fragment to Gethsemane. The dormant stack accepted with a delay that felt like an insult from physics.

EVIDENCE RECEIVED. DISTRIBUTION PENDING BANDWIDTH.

Sela stared at the receipt. "Pending bandwidth is going to be carved on all our graves."

Mother Kal stood slowly. "Not if we keep moving."

"Toward what?"

"A place where parasites have to speak in front of more than one hungry person."

Noor looked at her. "That is an oddly specific legal theory."

"I have lived in stations."

Tovin watched Pardon Reef's probable coordinates on the edge of the plot. No drones emerged. No guns arrived. No child left under false papers. No sealed claim crossed the hull. Somewhere in the dirty relay dark, Hask Pell still had a witness, still had fog, still had weapons, and still had enough knowledge to put his finger on the *Mercy*'s bruises and press.

They had refused him.

They had also learned from him.

That was the part Tovin distrusted most.

Mer's command slate chimed. "Bell distance."

Navigation answered. "Nineteen thousand kilometers. Clean disabling geometry delayed by vector and dust, but not lost. Estimate nine minutes."

"Gethsemane bandwidth?"

"Still poor. Improvement in fifteen to eighteen."

Sela laughed without humor. "Of course."

Tovin looked at the covenant line reflected faintly in the command glass, though the actual words were one deck away behind the Oathlock doors.

No hidden obligation.

It was a good sentence.

It did not seal breaches, slow weapons, feed children, rescue witnesses, or make criminals useless.

It merely kept the ship from becoming owned by the person offering a knife with the bread.

For today, apparently, that had to count as navigation.

Chapter 15: Children Under Pursuit

The first child injured under pursuit was not hit by shrapnel, glass, fire, vacuum, weapon spill, or any of the dramatic things adults feared loudly.

She was hit by a cupboard.

Her name was Miri Pell-not-related, which everyone aboard had begun saying in full after Hask Pell's first message because children noticed jokes even when adults tried to swallow them. She was eight, narrow-shouldered, sharp-eyed, and currently engaged in a serious feud with a pressure hood whose chin seam offended her. She had been helping Mother Kal redistribute the school deck's comfort kits when the *Mercy* angled three degrees toward Broken Buoy Cut and the starboard storage cupboard, already stressed from Cinderwake vibration and patched badly by people who had planned to fix it properly at Halvyr Dock before Halvyr became red lights, tore free from its upper latch.

The cupboard did not fly.

That was the cruelty. It swung.

It moved with ship gravity, acceleration, and the slow inevitability of a thing heavy enough to be called furniture by people who had forgotten mass was only patient violence. It struck Miri across the shoulder and head, knocked her into the comfort-kit crate, and spilled sealed socks, glucose strips, paper moons, and three emergency story slates across the deck.

Two other children were hurt trying to catch her.

By the time the alarm reached command, the injury was already more than physical.

"School deck impact," navigation said. "Internal, not hull."

Mer turned sharply. "Define."

The medical channel opened over itself: voices, a child crying too quietly, Mother Kal ordering someone to stop moving a neck until a brace arrived, a pressure hood alarm chirping in idiot rhythm.

Noor's face changed first. Not fear. Recognition. They had been waiting for the cost to find a smaller body.

Sela's video feed cut from engineering and returned as audio only. "What happened?"

Mer's hand closed on the command rail. "Medical, report."

The answer came from Doctor Vale, breathless and angry. "One child head strike and shoulder compression, conscious but vomiting. Two minor hand fractures, possible. No pressure loss. No hull breach. Cause appears unsecured cupboard failure under vector shift."

Tovin felt the deck move under him though the ship had not changed.

"Vector shift," Sela said.

No one needed to explain which one.

The one they had chosen without Pell. The honest three degrees. The declared half-truth not quite lie. The navigation that had counted as no hidden obligation.

Mer said, "Medical priority. Engineering, all school-deck storage latches immediate inspection."

"Already moving," Sela said, voice flat.

Mother Kal came on channel. "Do not flood the deck with officers."

"Mother Kal," Mer said.

"Children are frightened by adults arriving to be important. Send medical. Send one engineer. Send no one else until I ask."

Mer looked at Tovin.

He had already unclipped.

"No," Mother Kal said, as if she could see him through bulkheads. "Not him."

Tovin stopped.

The command deck heard it. Worse, the command deck understood it.

Mer said carefully, "He may need to inspect restraint implications."

"He can inspect them from where he made them."

The channel remained open long enough for a child to start crying in earnest.

Then Mother Kal cut it.

For three seconds nobody spoke.

Noor said, "That was not formal accusation."

"No," Tovin said.

"It will become one if handled badly."

"Yes."

Sela's voice returned, quieter. "I am sending Yaret. He has a daughter in that deck. He will not be calm in a stupid way."

Mer nodded though Sela could not see it. "Approved."

The pursuit plot chimed.

Lemm said, "Bell drone closing lower aft. Looks like observation reposition during our vector shift."
