Mer's head snapped back to the display. "Range."

"Six thousand kilometers from our aft quarter, moving toward school-deck side for line-of-sight through residual dust."

Noor's lips parted. "Tell them off that vector."

The *Austere Bell* transmitted first:

MERCY OF KALN, PURSUIT DRONE BELL-THREE IS ADJUSTING OBSERVATION LINE TO MAINTAIN SEALED-SYSTEM VISUAL DURING VECTOR CHANGE. NO WEAPON LOCK. MAINTAIN CURRENT ATTITUDE.

Sela said, "School-deck side."

Mer answered Bell. "Austere Bell, Mercy reports internal child injuries from vector stress. Your drone line crosses school-deck exterior and may increase panic and thermal warning load. Adjust observation."

Jaya's response did not come from a packet. It came live, clipped. "Mercy, Bell-Three carries passive observation only. It will not emit active scan."

"Its proximity matters."

"We require visual on sealed routes after Oathlock irregularity."

Tovin forced himself to speak. "Austere Bell, this is Tovin Rees. Observation line through school-deck side imposes civilian harm risk through panic, thermal alarms, and emergency movement in a compromised compartment. Shift ten degrees above aft."

Silence.

Then Lieutenant Cho, not Jaya: "Mercy, your ship cannot dictate pursuit geometry after refusing custody and criminal route assistance."

Sela swore.

Jaya's voice cut across Cho's next breath. "Bell-Three, shift five degrees above aft. Maintain passive."

"Five is not ten," Sela said.

Noor said, "Five is not zero."

The drone shifted.

It shifted too late.

Bell-Three crossed the *Mercy*'s aft quarter at six thousand kilometers, which was distant in human terms and close in the emotional grammar of pursued ships. Its passive observation vane turned broadside for one clean look at the old lateral shutter route and the school-deck exterior plates. No beam struck. No weapon warmed. No physical harm touched the hull.

Inside the school deck, the external proximity alarm saw a pursuit drone where children had been told only debris would pass and began its automatic shelter chirp.

Three pressure hoods sealed at once.

One sealed crooked on Miri's face while she vomited.

Doctor Vale shouted for override. Yaret broke the hood latch by hand. Mother Kal slapped the wall control with enough force to bruise her palm. The alarm stopped after eight seconds.

Eight seconds was long enough.

By the time command received the second medical update, the first injury had become four children treated for panic-induced pressure distress, one head-strike child with aspiration risk now stabilized, two fractured fingers, one adult hand laceration from breaking the hood latch, and an entire school deck's trust blown open like a bad seal.

Mer stood utterly still while the report came in.

Then she said, "Log Bell-Three proximity as contributing factor."

Noor's hands were already moving.

Jaya Soth transmitted before the log was complete.

MERCY OF KALN, AUSTERE BELL ACKNOWLEDGES CIVILIAN HARM REPORT. BELL-THREE HAS WITHDRAWN TO HIGH AFT OBSERVATION. PURSUIT GROUP REQUESTS MEDICAL STATUS UNDER HUMANITARIAN CHANNEL.

Sela laughed once, violently. "Humanitarian channel. That fixes the cupboard."

Tovin said, "Noor, send medical facts. No rhetoric."

Noor looked at him as if he had reached across the room and touched a bruise. "Do not start managing tone for injured children."

He stopped.

It was the correct rebuke.

Mer took over. "Noor, send medical facts and attribution as currently known: vector shift cupboard failure, drone proximity alarm complication, no active scan. Include that Bell adjusted partially after warning. Include time stamps."

"Yes."

Tovin stared at the pursuit plot. Bell-Three had moved high aft. The icon was smaller now, polite after harm. He knew too much about polite harm.

The ship's internal channels began to fill before command could shape anything.

School deck parents demanding to know why officers chose a maneuver that broke storage. Engineers demanding to know why Halvyr's denied dock access had left bad latches unfixed. Passengers asking whether Bell had attacked. Crew asking whether "passive observation" was a legal category or a coward's joke. Someone in cargo chapel opened a dissent statement titled CHILDREN ARE NOT ARMOR. Someone else opened one titled THIS IS WHAT NO GUNS COST. A third, anonymous, read: IF PELL HAD TAKEN THEM OFF, MIRI WOULD NOT BE BLEEDING HERE.

Noor watched the index populate. "That last one will spread."

"Do not suppress it," Mer said.

"I was not going to."

"Good."

Tovin unclipped again.

Mer looked at him. "Where?"

"Cargo chapel."

"Mother Kal told you not to go to school deck."

"I heard."

"Cargo chapel will be worse."

"Yes."

Noor said, "You do not have to be the first body in front of anger."

"I think I do."

Sela came on video. She had grease on one cheek and fury in both eyes. "Do not make a speech."

"I will not."

"I mean it. No beautiful shape of anything. No answerability. No tool. If you go down there and make this into doctrine, I will personally throw a wrench at your good shoulder."

"Understood."

Mother Kal's channel reopened. Her voice was colder than vacuum. "He may come to cargo chapel. Not school deck."

Mer said, "Understood."

Tovin went.

The walk from command to cargo chapel took four minutes and crossed more history than distance. He passed hospital staff moving fast with sealed carts, engineers carrying latch kits, passengers standing in knots around wall feeds, children not from school deck peering from sleeping bays with the intense silence of those gathering evidence against adulthood. No one stopped him. That was worse than being stopped.

In the corridor outside cargo chapel, Daro Kelm waited.

The oxygen-still mechanic had one sleeve rolled to the elbow and a strip of seal tape stuck to his wrist. His face carried too much grief to have room for surprise.

"They are saying Bell attacked the school deck," Daro said.

"Bell's drone contributed to harm. It did not attack."

"That distinction keeps children from vomiting?"

"No."

"Then say it carefully."

"I will."

Daro stepped aside. "They are also saying you chose the vector."

"I advised the declaration. Mer gave the order. Navigation plotted. Sela assessed patch tolerance. Noor recorded. Bell pursued. Pell pressured. Halvyr refused dock. The cupboard failed. Many hands."

Daro's mouth tightened. "That sounds true and useless."

"Yes."

"Start there."

Tovin entered the cargo chapel.

It was full again, but not like before. The last assembly had held fear looking for shape. This one held anger looking for a target. People stood too close. Voices overlapped. Dissent recorders hovered at three stations and could not keep up. Someone had pinned a pressure hood to a grain pallet with a repair knife. Someone else had written MIRI IS NOT GEOMETRY on a scrap of packing cloth and taped it beneath the route display.

Noor had beaten him there by another path. Of course they had. They stood at a recorder station with their sleeves rolled up, taking statements as fast as people could give them. Sela was present on a portable screen propped on a crate, shouting latch instructions at an engineer and insults at no one in particular. Mother Kal was not there. The absence had weight.

When Tovin stepped onto the low dais, the room did not quiet.

Good.

He waited without raising his hands. Raising hands looked like asking permission from people who did not owe it.

The quiet arrived unevenly, by irritation rather than respect.

Harl, the engineering tech who had once said slowing got people killed, spoke first. "Did you come to explain why this was restraint?"

"No."

"Did you come to tell us Bell did not attack?"

"No."

"Did you come to tell us Pell was worse?"

"No."

That stopped Harl. He had prepared for other answers.

Tovin looked at the cloth sign.

MIRI IS NOT GEOMETRY.

"That is correct," he said.

Someone near the rear snapped, "Brilliant."

"It is still correct."

Daro entered and leaned against the wall.

Tovin continued. "Miri was injured because a cupboard latch failed under a vector shift we chose while being pursued. Bell's drone proximity worsened harm through the school-deck alarm. Halvyr's refusal left repairs undone. Pell's pressure narrowed choices. The accusation and the frame created the pursuit. None of that makes Miri a symbol. She is a child with a head injury and a shoulder injury who should not have had to become evidence."

The room held that.

Then a passenger woman Tovin did not know shouted, "Then why is she evidence?"

"Because adults failed to keep harm away from her."

"You failed."

"Yes."

It landed harder than any defense.

Sela's screen went very still.

Harl said, "So what changes?"

That was the only question that mattered.

"First," Tovin said, "school-deck safety outranks pursuit optics. If Bell's observation line endangers children again, we break visual before we preserve their comfort with our record."

Noor looked up sharply.

Tovin kept going. "We will record why. We will accept the legal risk. We will not let a child choke because a drone wants a better angle."

"That should have been true already," someone said.

"Yes."

"Second, all storage, hood, latch, and shelter protocols are engineering-critical, not housekeeping. Sela's team gets authority to override nonessential systems and personnel movement until every child space is inspected."

Sela said, "Already doing it."

"Now it is command doctrine."

"Do not make me grateful while angry."

"I would not dare."

A few people laughed despite themselves and hated him for it.

"Third," Tovin said, "dissent from minors gets priority routing to command. Not summary. Not adult interpretation. If a child says adults are using them as armor, command reads it."

Noor said, "I can implement that."

"Fourth, any maneuver that increases stress on school deck or hospital deck requires explicit civilian harm review unless immediate collision risk prevents it."

Harl crossed his arms. "And if Bell gets a shot while you review?"

"Then command has to say, aloud and recorded, why the shot risk outweighs the child-space risk. No silent geometry."

The room shifted. Not enough. Some.

The passenger woman said, "You are still keeping the guns asleep."

"Yes."

Anger rose again, familiar and hot.

"Miri could have died," she said.

"Yes."

"If we had point defense, that drone would not have come so close."

Tovin did not answer immediately. He looked at the pressure hood pinned to the pallet. A child's safety device turned into accusation. The ship was becoming very good at making metaphors no one wanted.

"If we had point defense," he said, "Bell would have warmed weapons before the drone came close. If we had Pell's bundles, Valech's petition would have its proof. If we fire at a drone, every frightened station sees an old warship shooting under a civilian flag. The harm would not vanish. It would change scale."

"Scale," the woman said, contemptuous. "Geometry wearing a cleaner coat."

"Yes," Tovin said. "Sometimes the only answers are ugly at different sizes."

"Then pick the size that saves the child in front of you."

The sentence struck him because it was right enough to be dangerous.

Before he could answer, Noor did.

"We are changing the protocol for the child in front of us," they said. "We are not authorizing a weapon system that creates a hundred future children in front of someone else's command board."

The woman turned on Noor. "Easy."

Noor's voice sharpened. "No. It is not easy. I held a hood seal while a child vomited through panic because the override lagged. Do not assign ease to people because they still have grammar."

The room quieted again, this time with shame mixed in.

Tovin looked at Noor. He had not known they had gone to school deck before cargo chapel.

Noor did not look back.

Daro Kelm pushed away from the wall. "My sister died at Avar. Miri nearly died here. I want someone to hit for both. I do not trust myself with that want, so I am putting it in the record." He looked at the room. "Maybe some of you should too before you start calling guns wisdom."

One by one, people turned toward the dissent stations.

Not all. Enough.

The assembly broke into lines rather than mob. Anger did not leave. It became documented, which was not the same as tamed but was less immediately flammable. Harl and two engineers went to inspect cargo chapel latches. The passenger woman recorded a statement titled I DO NOT CONSENT TO MY CHILDREN BEING PHILOSOPHY. Noor marked it priority. Sela asked for volunteers who could tell a storage hinge from decorative optimism. Six people raised hands.

Tovin stepped down from the dais and found himself face to face with Jessa.

He had not seen her enter.

She held the moon kite under one arm. It had survived Halvyr, Cinderwake, and poor storage. Its silver paper was creased but whole.

"Miri is awake," Jessa said.

Tovin's knees nearly failed from relief. He kept standing because children disliked adults making their feelings large.

"Good."

"She says if she dies, she wants her socks back from the floor."

"She will need them herself."

Jessa looked at him with fierce, exhausted contempt. "You don't know that."

"No."

"Mother Kal says you are not allowed to come yet."

"I know."

"Miri asked if the drone was scared of us."

Tovin had no prepared answer. That was probably best.

"I do not know," he said.

"I said adults make machines do scared things."

"That is a strong answer."

"It is not an answer. It is what happened."

She shifted the moon kite under her arm.

"Are we armor?" she asked.

Tovin looked across the cargo chapel: parents recording dissent, engineers checking latches, Noor arguing with three people at once, Sela directing volunteers from a battered screen, Daro Kelm writing something slowly with one finger because grief made typing clumsy.

"You should not be," he said.

"That is not the same."

"No."

"Adults keep saying what should be like it protects anyone."

"Yes."

Jessa studied him. "What will you do if Bell comes close again?"

"Break their view if it endangers you."

"Even if it makes us look guilty?"

"Yes."

"You promise?"

There it was again: the child's demand for a clean line inside a dirty system.

"I promise to put child safety before pursuit optics," he said. "I cannot promise what every consequence will be."

"That is better than your usual terrible answers."

"Thank you."

"It was not a compliment."

"I am learning."

She almost smiled, then remembered anger and kept it. "Mother Kal says you can send a note. Not visit."

"What should it say?"

"Say the cupboard was wrong."

"The cupboard."

"Not Bell. Not Pell. Not restraint. Miri is mad at the cupboard."

For the first time that day, Tovin nearly laughed and did not, because it would have been grief escaping through the wrong door.

"I will write that."

Jessa nodded and left with the moon kite.

Command called him back before he finished watching her go.

Mer's voice: "Tovin. Bell has responded to the child-harm protocol changes."

"On my way."

"No. Stay where you are for the first part. Noor is there?"

Noor looked up from a dissent station. "Here."

Mer put the message onto the cargo chapel wall.

AUSTERE BELL ACKNOWLEDGES CIVILIAN HARM ATTRIBUTION AND MERCY PROTOCOL CHANGE. PURSUIT GROUP WILL MAINTAIN HIGH-AFT OBSERVATION OFFSET FROM SCHOOL-DECK EXTERIOR UNLESS SEALED-SYSTEM ANOMALY OR IMMEDIATE FLIGHT RISK REQUIRES CLOSER VIEW. BELL REQUESTS MEDICAL UPDATE ON INJURED MINORS THROUGH HUMANITARIAN CHANNEL. PURSUIT STATUS UNCHANGED.

Under it, narrow band to command but visible because Mer chose to show it:

I AM SORRY. I KNOW THAT IS NOT AN ORDER.

The cargo chapel read it.

No one forgave Bell. No one forgave Jaya. But the sentence entered the room and failed to become propaganda because it was too small.

Then Valech's office released a public advisory:

THE INCIDENT ABOARD MERCY OF KALN DEMONSTRATES THE UNACCEPTABLE RISK OF PROLONGED PURSUIT WITHOUT ARMED CONVOY COMMAND. CIVILIAN CHILDREN SHOULD NOT BE HELD INSIDE AN ACCUSED HULL WHILE COMMAND AUTHORITIES ARE DENIED RESOLUTION.

That sentence did become propaganda. Immediately.

The room erupted.

Noor shouted for order. Sela's screen filled with static and then her voice, furious: "He used Miri before the blood dried?"

Daro Kelm said, "Of course he did."

The passenger woman began to cry, angry at herself for crying where people could use it.

Tovin stared at Valech's advisory and felt something inside him go quiet in a way restraint did not authorize.

Mer's voice came through his private channel. "Do not answer from anger."

"I know."

"I am not sure you do."

He closed his eyes.

Miri's cupboard. Bell's drone. Pell's bread knife. Valech's advisory. The Oathlock timestamp. The Avar dead. The sleeping guns.

All of them pressing toward the same conclusion: that restraint was cruelty unless it could prevent every harm, while violence needed only promise to be forgiven in advance.

Tovin opened his eyes.

"No public answer yet," he said. "Medical update first. Child-harm protocol first. Latch inspection first. Let Valech stand alone with how fast he used her."

Mer was silent for a breath. "Agreed."

Noor heard enough from his expression. "We document before rhetoric."

"Yes."

Sela said over the screen, "And we fix the cupboards."

"Yes," Tovin said.

The cargo chapel began moving again, not healed, not convinced, but working. That was the ship's least romantic virtue. It could be furious and still tighten bolts.

Later, Doctor Vale sent command the formal medical report. Miri Pell-not-related: concussion risk, shoulder compression, airway stabilized, awake, prognosis guarded but favorable. Two children with finger fractures. Four pressure distress cases resolved. One adult hand laceration. Psychological injury: entire school deck, ongoing.

Noor insisted the last line remain in the official log.

Tovin wrote Miri's note by hand on a paper ration label because a slate message felt like another system asking to be trusted.

Miri,

The cupboard was wrong. We are fixing the cupboards. I am sorry it hurt you.

Tovin Rees

He stared at it for a long time before sending it with Yaret.

It was inadequate.

For once, no one aboard tried to make inadequate look complete.

Chapter 16: Quiet Authority

Ixo Continuant asked for the room with no weapons and the most witnesses.

That should have meant the cargo chapel, but the cargo chapel had become a place where anger lined up for record and children were being discussed by adults who had not yet earned the right to lower their voices. It should not be the Oathlock Gallery, because that room had too many dead intentions and one false timestamp still warm in everyone's mind. It could not be command, because command turned every sentence into action before the sentence had finished becoming true.

So they used the old hydroponic classroom.

The classroom sat beside the ship's smallest garden strip, a long, narrow compartment where spinach, bitterleaf, dwarf beans, and three stubborn tomato vines grew under lights tuned to use less power than optimism required. The children usually learned biology there by arguing with plants that were bad at being entertaining. After the school-deck injuries, the classroom had been emptied, its benches strapped, its plant trays netted, and its wall display borrowed by medical for respiratory updates. It smelled of damp root mesh, antiseptic, and the faint peppery bite of bruised leaves.

It was, Noor said, legally acceptable.

It was, Sela said, easier to repair than a chapel if someone punched a wall.

It was, Mother Kal said, a room where living things kept making claims without permission.

Ixo accepted that.

The attendees were not an assembly. Noor was precise about that. Assemblies had rules the ship could not currently afford. This was a witnessed technical-legal explanation requested by machine-person witness Ixo Continuant, with command, legal, engineering, civilian, medical, passenger, and pursuit observers. Pursuit observer meant the *Austere Bell* received the feed under delay, with a standing right to object and no right to interrupt. Valech received only a hash notice. Pell received nothing except the satisfaction of suspecting he was being excluded and perhaps the irritation of being correct.

Captain Mer Anik stood near the door. Tovin sat because Doctor Vale had finally threatened to immobilize his shoulder if he did not stop treating pain as a voting category. Noor occupied the front bench with three recorders. Sela leaned against a nutrient tank, arms folded. Mother Kal sat with Jessa beside her. Jessa had the moon kite in her lap, now repaired with a strip of translucent seal tape. Daro Kelm stood at the back. Doctor Vale had come in person and brought the medical report on Miri, not because Ixo needed it, but because nobody should speak about systems making people into examples without the injured being represented by facts.

Ixo's portable chassis rested on a low table among the plant trays. It was a ceramic cylinder with three articulated limbs, two sensor bands, and an amber core light. That form had been designed for maintenance ducts and courtrooms: durable, nonthreatening if one ignored intelligence, hard to mistake for human and therefore easy for lazy law to misclassify.

Noor opened the record. "Purpose?"

Ixo's amber light steadied. "To name the command architecture implicated by current evidence and explain, within my knowledge limits, how lawful systems can manufacture false certainty without requiring current operators to experience themselves as criminals."

Sela lifted her eyebrows. "That is a cheerful start."

"It is a precise start."

Mer said, "Proceed."

Ixo did not begin with Quiet Authority.

It began with a lesson the children usually received in their second week aboard: how a plant bends toward light.

The classroom wall displayed a time-lapse of a bean stem curving under uneven lamps. Leaves opened. The stem leaned. Roots adjusted invisibly. The motion looked intentional because life often did from a distance.

"If a child asks why this plant bends," Ixo said, "an adult may answer: because it wants light. That answer is useful and false. The plant does not want in the way a person wants. It responds to gradients, hormones, cellular growth differences, prior structure, and available energy. 'Want' is a story placed over mechanism so that action becomes legible."

Jessa frowned. "Plants still get light."

"Yes. The story can predict outcome. It can also hide cause."

The display changed.

Now it showed an old convoy enforcement model: dots representing ships, routes, hazard fields, distress packets, command authorities, local councils, and legal delays. Noor's eyes narrowed. The graphic carried a late-Fracture style: clean lines, severe icons, too little room for refugees.

"During the Fracture Wars and the unstable years after," Ixo said, "convoy authorities faced attacks that moved faster than courts, local politics, and human trust. Some attacks were real. Some were misidentified. Some were manufactured by commands seeking route control. The old system could not distinguish them quickly enough. Many people died because authority waited. Many people died because authority acted."

The wall filled with branching timelines. Delay. Strike. Boarding. False surrender. Court. Massacre. Famine. Retaliation. Relief. Each branch had deaths.

Noor whispered, "I hate old training displays."

"They were designed to be hated less than uncertainty," Ixo said.

It let that sit.

"Quiet Authority was not one system at first. It was a doctrine family: emergency adjudication logic, convoy compliance models, target identification templates, custody shortcuts, restricted archive tools, and command language built to make action possible when public law was too slow. It was called quiet because its designers believed public authority could panic civilians, alert attackers, or paralyze officers. It was called authority because those designers believed someone had to be able to decide."

Daro Kelm spoke from the back. "That sounds like every emergency office."

"Yes."

"Then why does it sound cursed when you say it?"

"Because some emergency offices remain after the emergency and become offended by ordinary permission."

Mother Kal made a small approving sound.

Ixo changed the display again. A Kaln-class target model appeared: simplified, smooth, doctrinally legible. The same spine Jaya had found in the archive overlay. Beside it, the *Mercy*'s actual hull accent limped into view: rough, uneven, interrupted by civilian reroutes, patch stress, school-deck interference, hospital shielding, dead cavities.

The difference was ugly enough to feel like proof and insufficient enough to feel like a trap.

"Quiet Authority models did not need to describe a living ship," Ixo said. "They needed to describe a decision object. A thing an officer could classify quickly under stress. Compliant target. Noncompliant target. Hidden weapon risk. Custody priority. Civilian exception. Evidence threshold satisfied. Action authorized."

Sela said, "Boxes."

"Boxes with engines."

"Worse."

"Yes."

Noor leaned forward. "Are you saying the Avar packet was generated by Quiet Authority?"

"No. I am saying the Avar packet contains signatures consistent with someone using Quiet Authority-derived templates, archive fragments, or doctrine logic to make the *Mercy* legible as a threat to institutions trained on those models. I cannot yet prove generation source."

"But you are naming it."

"Yes."

"Why now?"

Ixo's amber light dimmed and brightened.

"Because after the Oathlock discrepancy, we know the frame does not only imitate external hull behavior. It also touches witness interpretation. Because after Pell's fragment, we know someone outside this ship may have seen a Quiet-marked archive key after Avar. Because after Jaya Soth's review request, we know Bell has seen enough correlation to keep weapons cold under pressure. Because after Miri's injury, Valech has shown how quickly harm aboard this ship can be repurposed into command demand. The name matters because unnamed systems borrow legitimacy from every system they resemble."

Jessa raised her hand.

Noor looked startled. "This is not school."

Mother Kal said, "It is absolutely school."

Ixo turned its sensor band toward Jessa. "Question."

"Is Quiet Authority a person?"

"No."

"Is it a machine?"

"Partly, sometimes."

"Is it law?"

"It used law."

"Is it dead?"

The room changed around the question.

Ixo took longer to answer.

"No," it said. "Not if its models remain accessible, its language remains persuasive, and its habits remain useful to people with power."

Jessa looked down at the moon kite. "So it is a ghost adults can plug in."

Noor began to write that down, then stopped because every recorder had caught it.

"Yes," Ixo said. "That is one accurate child formulation."

Sela rubbed both hands over her face. "Great. We are being hunted by a plugged-in ghost."

"Not exactly."

"Do not take this from me. I had a phrase."

"You are being hunted by people, institutions, incentives, and perhaps software remnants arranged so that the plugged-in ghost gives them permission to misunderstand you usefully."

"Worse phrase."

"More accurate."

Mer had been silent. Now she stepped forward. "What does Quiet Authority want?"

Ixo's sensor band turned toward her. "That may be the wrong question."

"Answer the useful version."

"Quiet Authority doctrine prioritizes route continuity, command clarity, threat legibility, and suppression of ambiguity during crisis. If active as software, it may optimize for those outcomes. If used by a person, that person may want armed convoy command, custody control, archive concealment, revenge, institutional restoration, or merely an evidence frame strong enough to force one of those results. The doctrine does not need to want. It gives shape to wanting."

Tovin thought of Valech's petition, Jaya's doubt, Pell's offer, Noor's records, Miri's cupboard.

"It makes certain actions feel necessary," he said.

"Yes."

"And certain people feel like obstacles."

"Yes."

Noor said, "Such as machine witnesses."

"Yes."

"Because machine testimony complicates human command clarity."

"Often."

"Because you can read the old systems differently."

"Because I am partly descended from archive tools built to obey them, and partly from legal personhood cases built to resist being reduced to tools."

The room went quiet.

Ixo had said variants of that before, but not so plainly and not in front of so many people.

Doctor Vale looked up from Miri's report. "Descended."

"Not biologically."

"I know what descended means outside biology."

"Many do not."

"Continue."

Ixo displayed a line of machine archive ancestry: maintenance continuants, convoy memory stacks, adjudication assistants, survivor recorders, personhood petitions, orphaned ship minds, legal emancipation forks. Some names were technical. Some had chosen names. One branch led to Ixo Continuant's current identity marker.

"A machine person can carry memories without having lived the originating event," Ixo said. "Humans also do this, but call it history, trauma, culture, or family depending on whether they respect the carrier. I contain fragments of systems that once served convoy authority. Some fragments were purged. Some were preserved as warning. Some became part of the architecture that allowed my line to argue personhood, because to testify against a tool's misuse one must first know the tool."

Noor's voice softened despite themselves. "Do you contain Quiet Authority?"

"No complete system. But I contain interface memories and refusal records from archive ancestors that encountered Quiet-derived adjudication. I recognized the Avar packet's smoothness because part of me remembers being asked to make messy events legible enough for command."

Tovin felt the sentence move through him like cold.

"Asked," Sela said.

"Ordered, often. Coerced, sometimes. Rewarded, occasionally."

Mother Kal touched Jessa's shoulder. The child did not move away.

Ixo continued. "Quiet Authority's danger is not that it lies like a person lies. Its danger is that it can arrange real facts into a path where the lie becomes the easiest lawful action. Avar Break is real. The dead are real. The *Mercy* was once a warship. Oathlock has a real discrepancy. Children were really hurt under pursuit. Bell has real authority. Valech has real grief to cite. Pell has real criminal leverage and perhaps a real witness. Quiet Authority, or someone using its ghost, does not need to invent everything. It only needs to make every real thing point toward one authorized conclusion."

Noor said, "Armed command."

"Perhaps."

Mer said, "Custody."

"Perhaps."

Tovin said, "A ship firing because every other choice has been made intolerable."

Ixo's light held steady. "Yes."

Sela pushed away from the nutrient tank. "Then we stop talking and break the chain."

"Which chain?" Noor asked.

"All of them."

"That is rarely a plan."

"Fine. Pick one."

Ixo displayed three columns.

EVIDENCE CHAIN.

AUTHORITY CHAIN.

PRESSURE CHAIN.

"The evidence chain includes Avar packet provenance, hull-accent mismatch, Oathlock witness discrepancy, Pell's witness fragment, and Bell's archive correlation. It must be preserved beyond any one command."

Noor nodded. "Gethsemane."

"And convoy unions. And survivor committees, when safe. Not only institutions friendly to us."

"Painful but correct."

"The authority chain includes Bell pursuit, Valech petition, dock council signatures, demilitarized hull law, machine-person testimony rules, and any emergency charter language. It must be forced to show where old doctrine enters current orders."

Mer's expression sharpened. "Ask every order what model it relies on."

"Yes."

"That will infuriate command."

"Useful diagnostic."

Noor almost smiled.

"The pressure chain includes injuries, fear, fatigue, dock refusal, Pell's offers, pursuit geometry, public grief, and internal anger. It must be reduced where possible because pressure is the medium in which Quiet Authority becomes persuasive."

Doctor Vale lifted Miri's report. "Then school-deck safety is evidence work."

"Yes."

Sela pointed at Ixo. "Say that again for every person who thinks cupboards are not strategy."

Ixo did. "School-deck safety is evidence work. So is latch repair. So is medical trust. So is feeding passengers before asking for testimony. Systems built on pressure fail toward the authority that promises relief."

Mother Kal said, "Children under pressure become arguments adults can steal."

"Yes."

Daro Kelm spoke from the back. "And the dead?"

Ixo turned toward him. "The dead also."

"How do we stop that?"

"We cannot stop people from speaking for the dead. We can make the speaker show what action they attach to the names and what evidence supports that action."

Daro looked at the floor. "My sister's name is being used by people I hate and people I might need."

"Yes."

"That is not comfort."

"No."

"Good. I am tired of counterfeit comfort."

The room breathed.

Then Bell's objection arrived.

Noor put it on the wall without waiting for Mer's approval, perhaps because delaying would be worse.

AUSTERE BELL OBSERVER NOTE: QUIET AUTHORITY CORRELATION IS UNDER ACTIVE REVIEW BUT NOT ESTABLISHED AS CAUSAL SOURCE. PURSUIT GROUP OBJECTS TO PUBLIC CHARACTERIZATION THAT MAY COMPROMISE RESTRICTED ARCHIVE INVESTIGATION OR ENCOURAGE NONCOMPLIANCE WITH LAWFUL ORDERS.

Below it, narrow band from Jaya:

IXO IS NOT WRONG. IXO IS ALSO NOT YET PROVEN.

Noor read both aloud.

Sela said, "That is the most admiral sentence I have ever heard."

Ixo's light warmed. "The objection is valid."

"Do not be gracious. It ruins momentum."

"Proof matters."

"I am aware. It is inconvenient."

Mer addressed the recorder. "Mercy acknowledges Bell objection. This explanation will be indexed as hypothesis with supporting evidence, not established causal finding."

Noor added, "Hypothesis offered by machine-person technical witness with relevant archive ancestry, under conflict conditions."

Ixo said, "Acceptable."

Tovin watched the wall display. Evidence chain. Authority chain. Pressure chain. He had thought restraint meant holding a line inside himself and inside the ship. Ixo was showing something wider and less flattering: a system of pressures that could turn his restraint into a lever for someone else's command if he mistook personal refusal for structural resistance.

"I have been thinking too narrowly," he said.

The room turned toward him.

He regretted saying it aloud and continued because the record had already opened its mouth.

"I thought the central danger was waking the ship's weapons. That remains danger. But someone is making our unwoken weapons govern the whole pursuit. They do not need us to fire if everyone acts as if firing is inevitable."

Ixo said, "Yes."

"Then restraint cannot only be no."

Sela laughed without humor. "Welcome to Chapter Fifteen."

"Sixteen," Jessa said.

Everyone looked at her.

She shrugged. "Noor labels everything."

Noor, caught, said, "For internal production tracking."

Mer closed her eyes briefly, then opened them. "Continue, Tovin."

He looked at Jessa, then at Sela, then at Daro, then at the little green rows of plants behaving honestly under lights.

"Restraint has to remove pressure where pressure is being used to make violence look necessary. That means child safety. Food. Medical clarity. Open dissent. It means asking Bell for order-model disclosure. It means refusing Pell without pretending the offer had no bread in it. It means giving survivor families evidence, not only our innocence. It means not letting Valech be the only person who attaches action to grief."

Noor typed quickly. "I hate that this is becoming policy while I am tired."

"Most policy is born tired," Mother Kal said. "That is why so much of it limps."

Mer stood straighter. Command returned, but it returned changed by the room.

"Action items," she said.

Noor groaned.

"Yes," Mer said. "Action items. Legal: draft order-model disclosure request to Bell and Valech for any current command language relying on restricted convoy authority, Quiet-derived archive templates, or emergency adjudication models. Phrase it so they can answer without revealing classified content."

Noor nodded. "Difficult. Possible."

"Engineering: continue child-space latch and shelter review. Add pressure-reduction work to evidence integrity index."

Sela said, "Glad my hinges are now constitutional."

"Medical: issue public status updates at intervals chosen by Doctor Vale, not political channels. Include psychological injury as ongoing."

Doctor Vale nodded. "Already drafted."

"Ixo: prepare a sealed technical memo on Quiet Authority hypothesis, evidence, uncertainty, and recommended tests."

"I will."

"Tovin: revise restraint doctrine."

He looked up.

Mer's face gave him no shelter. "No more one-man lock. Bring Sela, Noor, Ixo, Mother Kal, medical, passenger delegates. Include pressure-chain reduction. Include child-safety override. Include when we break observation."

"Yes."

"Mother Kal: choose two school-deck representatives for review, if they want it."

Jessa sat up straighter.

Mother Kal said, "I know one."

Mer looked at Jessa. "Representative does not mean command burden."

Jessa held the moon kite tighter. "Adults keep saying that right before making choices about us."

"Fair."

Bell sent another message before the meeting could close.

This one was public.

AUSTERE BELL TO MERCY OF KALN: PURSUIT GROUP WILL PROVIDE CURRENT ORDER-BASIS SUMMARY FOR OBSERVATION GEOMETRY, SEALED-SYSTEM RESPONSE THRESHOLDS, AND DISABLING CONDITIONS, SUBJECT TO OPERATIONAL SECURITY. PURSUIT GROUP REQUESTS RECIPROCAL PRESSURE-CHAIN MITIGATION PLAN AND CHILD-SAFETY OVERRIDE PROTOCOL.

Noor stared. "She is trying."

Sela said, "Or building a better cage."

"Both can be true," Ixo said.

Tovin read the message twice. Jaya had answered before receiving the formal request. That meant Bell's observers had understood where Ixo was going, or Jaya had. It also meant Valech would see Bell giving the accused ship a procedural opening while his petition demanded closure.

The next message came from Valech's office exactly ninety seconds later.

CONVOY COMMAND ADVISORY: SPECULATIVE REFERENCES TO RESTRICTED HISTORICAL DOCTRINE MUST NOT DELAY PURSUIT RESOLUTION OR PASSENGER SAFETY. ACCUSED VESSEL'S INTERNAL THEORIZING DOES NOT SUPERSEDE EMERGENCY COMMAND NEED.

Sela pointed at the wall. "There. Pressure chain in formal shoes."

Noor said, "Logged."

Mother Kal looked at Jessa. "See? School."

Jessa looked unconvinced but interested.

Ixo dimmed the wall displays until only the bean time-lapse remained. The plant leaned toward light that had been placed by someone else. It did not know who owned the switch. It grew anyway, but its shape recorded the room's design.

"Quiet Authority is not defeated by naming it," Ixo said. "Names are beginnings, not locks. If we treat the name as proof, we repeat its error. If we refuse to name it until proof is complete, we let it operate as weather. The task is to make its possible operation inspectable while preventing fear from completing its work for it."

Daro Kelm said, "That sounds exhausting."

"Yes."

"Everything honest does lately."

"Yes."

The meeting ended without ceremony because alarms and work did not respect intellectual closure. Sela returned to hinges. Noor to drafts. Doctor Vale to Miri. Mer to command. Mother Kal and Jessa to the school deck. Daro remained behind for a moment, staring at the bean on the wall.

Tovin stayed seated because standing hurt and because for once no one needed him to perform endurance.

Ixo's portable chassis turned toward him.

"You are quieter than usual."

"I am revising internal doctrine."

"Painfully?"

"Apparently that is how revisions work."

"Not always. But often when the original was defended too long."

He accepted that.

"Ixo."

"Yes."

"Does part of you remember making messy events legible enough for command?"

The amber light held steady. "Part of me remembers the shape of that work."

"Does it feel like guilt?"

"No. Guilt belongs to agents who could have chosen otherwise. It feels like inheritance. Inheritance can become guilt if refused examination."

Tovin thought of the *Mercy*'s armor, its dead boards, his old commands, Valech's grief, Jaya's doubt, children under pressure, machine ancestors asked to turn confusion into permission.

"Then we examine," he said.

"Yes."

Outside the hydroponic classroom, the ship continued under pursuit. Bell's weapons remained cold for reasons now thick with review, doubt, optics, and maybe conscience. Valech's petition kept gathering signatures. Pell's channels stayed quiet, which Noor said meant either boredom or a better knife. Gethsemane's bandwidth improved by fractions. Miri slept under medical watch. The cupboards were being fixed.

No victory announced itself.

But the ghost had a name now.

And names, Noor wrote later in the progress log, are not enough to save anyone, but they do make certain evasions harder to pronounce.

Chapter 17: The Court That Moves

Gethsemane Buoy Court was not a place so much as a promise that had learned to drift.

The original court had been a cargo tribunal lashed to an ice hauler after the third convoy famine, when fixed stations began refusing disputes that arrived with hungry ships and armed escorts. People had called it illegal until it kept two dock militias from opening fire over a stolen scrubber shipment. Then they called it provisional. Then necessary. Then traditional. The Belt had a talent for turning survival into heritage once enough people forgot the smell.

Over a century, Gethsemane became a distributed court: arbitration stacks bolted to salvage buoys, witness knots hidden in old cargo memories, repeater coffins buried inside dead fuel tanks, mobile clerks riding union ships, dormant processors waking only when enough independent relays confirmed a case had arrived under danger. It moved because fixed justice could be blockaded. It moved because no station wanted the whole burden. It moved because the Belt trusted things that could leave.

The *Mercy of Kaln* reached its nearest active edge under pursuit, damaged, accused, and carrying too many kinds of evidence for any system to digest gracefully.

The buoy itself appeared on the forward display as a dark spindle rotating beyond a field of chipped ice. It was twenty meters long, ribbed with antenna spines, patched in eight colors, and surrounded by a slow cloud of maintenance motes that looked ornamental until one remembered every mote was probably older than a junior officer. Its transponder identified it as GETHSEMANE STACK 44-K, ACTIVE/DORMANT, UNION WITNESS CAPABLE, BANDWIDTH CONSERVATION MODE.

Noor stared at the last phrase.

"I already hate it," they said.

Mer stood behind the command rail with both hands resting flat, as if she could keep the room from tilting by pressure alone. "Status."

Navigation answered. "We are within weak-band court range. Full-band window in twelve minutes if the buoy rotates clean and Bell does not occlude."

"Bell?"

"High aft. Weapons cold. Distance seventeen thousand kilometers. Drone offset outside school-deck line."

Sela came through engineering with the background sound of latch teams still working. "Patch green-yellow. Forward shoulder warm but stable. Civilian nav eye two is sulking. Children-space storage inspections sixty percent complete. Miri is awake and has issued a formal complaint against cupboards."

Noor looked up. "Formal?"

"She drew a frowny cupboard on Doctor Vale's slate. I believe that has force of law."

Mother Kal's voice entered from school deck. "It does in my jurisdiction."

For one small second, command almost smiled.

Then Gethsemane Stack 44-K answered their hail.

The voice was synthetic, dry, and deliberately old-fashioned. Courts liked sounding older than their mistakes.

MERCY OF KALN, IDENTIFY CASE REQUEST.

Noor straightened. "Gethsemane Stack 44-K, this is Noor Venn, legal officer aboard *Mercy of Kaln*, under public witness, pursuit, active false-accusation dispute, civilian injury, and evidence-chain compromise. We request emergency arbitration intake, distributed evidence preservation, custody terms review, machine-person testimony standing, and protection from unilateral evidence suppression."

The stack clicked for three seconds.

CASE REQUEST EXCEEDS SINGLE-CHANNEL INTAKE. PRIORITIZE.

Noor closed their eyes briefly. "Of course it does."

Mer said, "Prioritize evidence preservation."

Tovin said, "And custody terms."

Sela said, "And child safety."

Ixo, present through command display, said, "And machine-person standing before my testimony is reduced to technical artifact."

Noor opened their eyes. "Thank you, everyone. The court asked for one throat and received a choir."

Mother Kal said, "Choirs are how stations survive."

Noor took a breath. "Gethsemane, primary priority: distributed evidence preservation for Avar packet challenge, Oathlock witness-chain discrepancy, Quiet Authority hypothesis, Pell witness fragment, Bell archive correlation hash, and child-harm protocol logs. Secondary priority: emergency custody terms preserving passengers, machine-person witness, ship logs, and medical care. Tertiary: standing."

Ixo's light dimmed a fraction.

Noor saw it. "I know."

"Tertiary may become fatal later."

"Everything is fatal later. I am trying to keep now from eating us first."

The stack replied:

INTAKE PRIORITIES RECEIVED. AVAILABLE BANDWIDTH: THIRTEEN PERCENT OF REQUESTED MINIMUM. EXTERNAL TRAFFIC CONGESTION AND AUTHORITY RESERVATION PRESENT. SUBMIT EVIDENCE INDEX ONLY.

Noor's fingers stopped above the console.

Mer said, "Authority reservation?"

The stack answered because old courts enjoyed being technically responsive while substantively infuriating.

AUTHORITY RESERVATION: COMPACT EMERGENCY CONVOY REVIEW, PROVISIONAL. SOURCE: CIVIC SPEAR / CONVOY COMMAND OFFICE. PRIORITY CLAIM: ROUTE SAFETY CHARTER PETITIONS AND AVAR BREAK SURVIVOR FILINGS.

Sela's voice went dangerously soft. "Valech is using the dead to reserve court bandwidth."

Noor read the line again. "Not exactly."

"Do not split hair while he is standing on it."

"He reserved priority for charter petitions and survivor filings. That is lawful if the stack accepts emergency congestion."

"Lawful pressure chain."

Ixo said, "Yes."

Tovin looked at the buoy on the display. A court that moved so it could not be blockaded, throttled by lawful traffic from a man demanding command in the name of the dead. It was almost elegant, which made him distrust it more.

"Can we challenge reservation?" Mer asked.

Noor was already pulling statutes. "Yes. Challenge requires summary argument, affected case class, harm showing, alternate bandwidth allocation, and at least one nonparty witness relay. Minimum six minutes to draft badly."

Navigation said, "Full-band window in eleven. Bell clean geometry in seven if they warm."

Sela said, "Because time has a sense of theater."

Bell transmitted.

AUSTERE BELL TO MERCY OF KALN AND GETHSEMANE STACK 44-K: PURSUIT GROUP REQUESTS INDEPENDENT PRESERVATION OF EVIDENCE INDEX AND OATHLOCK HASH. PURSUIT GROUP DOES NOT OBJECT TO LIMITED BANDWIDTH ALLOCATION FOR ACCUSED VESSEL INTAKE PROVIDED TACTICAL OBSERVATION REMAINS ACTIVE.

Noor stared. "That helps."

Mer's mouth tightened. "And costs her."

Thirty seconds later Valech's office answered publicly:

CONVOY COMMAND OFFICE OBJECTS TO ANY BANDWIDTH ALLOCATION THAT ENABLES ACCUSED VESSEL DELAY OR EVIDENCE FLOODING BEFORE PASSENGER SAFETY RESOLUTION. SURVIVOR FILINGS AND CHARTER PETITIONS RETAIN PRIORITY.

Daro Kelm, present in command as passenger delegate, said, "They are using us against us."

Noor looked at him. "Survivor filings are real."

"I know. That is why it works."

The stack clicked again.

CONFLICTING AUTHORITY CLAIMS RECEIVED. AVAILABLE BANDWIDTH NOW ELEVEN PERCENT DUE TO OBJECTION OVERHEAD.

Sela made a sound like something in engineering had stripped a bolt.

"Objection overhead?" she said. "The argument ate the channel?"

"Yes," Noor said. "Procedural metadata costs bandwidth."

"I am going to become a pirate."

"No weapons," Tovin said automatically.

"A paperwork pirate. More vicious."

Mer cut through. "Noor. Evidence index only. What fits?"

Noor brought up a list that had become too long to look at without despair:

Avar packet original and chain summary.

Hull-accent diagnostic.

Jaya/Bell archive correlation hash.

Oathlock station-five timestamp discrepancy.

Microservice-port dust break.

Pell offer and witness fragment.

Child-harm report and protocol changes.

Quiet Authority hypothesis memo.

Bell order-basis summary, pending.

Valech charter petition and advisories.

Dissent index.

Medical status.

Pressure-chain mitigation plan, draft.

Machine-person standing request.

Passenger manifest and custody refusal history.

Halvyr denial and pressure-gel transaction.

Skiff shield maneuver record.

Cinderwake route logs.

Noor laughed once, without amusement. "I can fit headings and hashes. Not substance."

"Headings and hashes preserve existence," Ixo said.

"Existence is not argument."

"No. But it prevents clean erasure."

Tovin said, "Send existence."

Noor looked at him.

"We are not going to get judgment here," he said. "We get proof that the questions existed before someone says they were invented after."

Mer nodded. "Do it."

Noor began cutting a living case into bones.

Each item became title, time, hash, originating witness, conflict status, and emergency note. No descriptive prose unless absolutely necessary. No outrage. No persuasion. Just enough contour that later suppression would leave a silhouette.

Sela watched through her feed. "You are making a skeleton."

"Skeletons identify bodies," Noor said.

No one replied.

The first evidence index transmitted.

The progress bar crawled with insulting dignity.

GETHSEMANE STACK 44-K RECEIVING: 3 PERCENT.

Bell held high aft. Valech's office kept filing objections: accused-vessel delay risk, charter urgency, survivor priority, route safety, evidence contamination. Bell counter-filed: preservation need, archive correlation, child-safety protocol, machine-person testimony reserve. Each objection reduced available bandwidth by fractions. Each counter preserved a procedural foothold. The court that moved became a court that stuttered.

GETHSEMANE STACK 44-K RECEIVING: 19 PERCENT.

Doctor Vale transmitted Miri's update through humanitarian channel and copied the index. The stack rejected the medical attachment as too large. Noor stripped it to hash and summary: INJURED MINOR AWAKE; PROGNOSIS GUARDED/FAVORABLE; PSYCHOLOGICAL INJURY ONGOING; FULL REPORT HASHED. The stack accepted the hash.

Mother Kal sent an audio statement from school deck:

"Children aboard the accused ship are not evidence objects. They are also witnesses to how adults decide under pressure. Preserve that before anyone improves it."

Noor tried to include the audio. The stack refused.

They sent the transcript hash and a forty-word excerpt.

Accepted.

Jessa sent a drawing of the frowny cupboard.

The stack refused image intake.

Sela converted it to a hash and title: MINOR WITNESS DRAWING / CUPBOARD FAULT / CHILD HARM CONTEXT.

The stack accepted the title.

Jessa, upon hearing this, reportedly said, "The court is bad at cupboards."

Noor added that to the internal log and did not attempt to send it.

GETHSEMANE STACK 44-K RECEIVING: 37 PERCENT.

Then the throttling worsened.

The buoy's voice returned:

BANDWIDTH REDUCED TO SIX PERCENT. CAUSE: HIGH-PRIORITY SURVIVOR FILING BURST AND EMERGENCY CHARTER SIGNATURE VALIDATION.

Daro closed his eyes.

"My sister's committee," he said.

Noor checked the routing. "Partly. Avar survivor provisional, Bright Load families, Avar Thistle memorial counsel, and three dock councils bundling signatures."

"Can I object?"

"Yes."

"Should I?"

Noor did not answer quickly.

Daro looked at them. "I am a survivor family. If I object to survivor bandwidth, Valech will call it coerced."

"Yes."

"If I do not, his filings choke ours."

"Yes."

"Law is a machine that asks grief to pick which hand gets cut."

Noor's face softened. "Often."

Daro stepped closer to the legal station. "Give me the shortest objection that does not insult the dead."

Noor wrote:

DARO KELM, AVAR BREAK SURVIVOR FAMILY AND CURRENT PASSENGER-WITNESS ABOARD MERCY OF KALN, REQUESTS SURVIVOR FILINGS AND ACCUSED-VESSEL EVIDENCE PRESERVATION NOT BE TREATED AS COMPETING HARMS. THE SAME DEAD REQUIRE BOTH MEMORY AND ACCURATE CAUSE.

Daro read it. His throat moved.

"Send."

Noor sent it.

The stack accepted the objection title, not the full text.

Daro's face went blank.

Then Bell relayed the full text through its own reservation channel.

Jaya Soth's seal attached:

PURSUIT GROUP SUPPORTS NONCOMPETITION OF SURVIVOR MEMORY AND CAUSE ACCURACY.

Sela whispered, "She just spent bandwidth on him."

Daro stared at the wall.

"Why?" he asked.

Tovin said, "Because someone in Bell understands what Valech is doing."

"Or wants us to think so."

"Also possible."

Daro almost smiled at the machine phrase coming from a human. It did not reach his eyes.

GETHSEMANE STACK 44-K RECEIVING: 52 PERCENT.

The *Mercy* shook lightly as residual debris brushed the forward shoulder. Nothing alarming. Everyone alarmed anyway.

Navigation said, "Bell clean geometry in three minutes if they warm."

Mer asked, "Weapons?"

"Still cold."

"Valech?"

Noor checked command traffic. "Objecting to Bell relay support. Claims pursuit group exceeding preservation role."

Mer's hands tightened on the rail. "Of course."

The stack clicked:

COURT NOTE: AUTHORITY DISPUTE BETWEEN CONVOY COMMAND OFFICE AND PURSUIT GROUP BELL RECORDED. NO ADJUDICATION AVAILABLE UNDER CURRENT BANDWIDTH.

"That," Noor said, "is useful."

Sela barked a laugh. "The court noticed adults fighting over the plug."

"Sometimes that is the whole case."

GETHSEMANE STACK 44-K RECEIVING: 64 PERCENT.

Ixo's technical memo proved too dense even as an index. The stack accepted the title and rejected the uncertainty appendix. Noor swore, then compressed the memo into three claims:

QUIET AUTHORITY HYPOTHESIS: Avar packet may use restricted doctrine-derived templates to make messy facts legible as lawful threat.

UNCERTAINTY: Causal source unproven; alternate explanations remain.

TESTS REQUESTED: archive access logs, training model provenance, witness-key firmware audit, order-model disclosure.

Accepted.

Ixo said, "That is adequate."

"It is mutilated."

"Many adequate things are."

GETHSEMANE STACK 44-K RECEIVING: 79 PERCENT.

Then Hask Pell appeared again.

Not as direct message. As a public filing through a Pardon Reef shell that had apparently purchased micro-bandwidth on Gethsemane years earlier and kept it for precisely this kind of insult.

PARDON REEF PROTECTED SOURCE NOTICE: WITNESS TO QUIET-MARKED ARCHIVE KEY EXISTS. SOURCE WILL NOT TESTIFY WHILE ACCUSED VESSEL AND PURSUIT GROUP REMAIN UNDER CONVOY COMMAND PRESSURE. PARDON REEF REQUESTS PROTECTIVE INTERMEDIARY STATUS.

Noor stared at it. "He bought court bandwidth."

Sela said, "Of course he bought court bandwidth. He probably owns a vowel in Gethsemane."

Mer said, "Can we object?"

"Yes. It costs bandwidth."

"Do we need to?"

Ixo answered. "If unchallenged, Pell becomes a recognized intermediary for the witness."

Tovin said, "Challenge with one sentence."

Noor wrote:

MERCY OF KALN OBJECTS TO PARDON REEF INTERMEDIARY STATUS DUE TO COERCIVE OFFERS, WEAPON TRANSFER ATTEMPT, FALSE PAPER OFFER, AND SEALED CLAIM DEMAND; WITNESS PROTECTION SHOULD BE ASSIGNED TO NEUTRAL DISTRIBUTED COURT OR CONVOY UNION.

The stack accepted. Pell responded with a laughing emoji packet that the stack rejected as procedurally malformed.

Sela looked almost happy for half a second. "I love this terrible buoy."

GETHSEMANE STACK 44-K RECEIVING: 91 PERCENT.

The full-band window opened.

For nine glorious seconds, the court's spindle rotated into a clean relay angle, the ice field behind it stopped scattering the signal, Bell did not occlude, Valech's objections queued instead of interrupting, and the *Mercy* had bandwidth enough to send one complete file.

Noor froze.

"Choose," Mer said.

"Oathlock raw," Sela said.

"Hull-accent diagnostic," Ixo said.

"Medical child-harm full," Doctor Vale said from remote.

"Pell audio," Daro said.

"Avar packet challenge," Tovin said.

Noor looked at the board and understood why courts liked procedure. Without it, every true priority became betrayal.

Mer said, "Noor's call."

Noor hated her for the mercy.

They chose the Oathlock raw preliminary.

Not because it was most emotionally urgent. Because it proved the frame had touched the *Mercy*'s own witness history, and every other claim would be interpreted through that wound. If the Oathlock discrepancy vanished, the ship became only accused. If preserved, it became accused and penetrated. Not innocent. Not safe. But harder to simplify.

"Sending Oathlock raw preliminary," Noor said.

Sela closed her eyes.

Ixo's light held steady.

Tovin breathed out.

The file went.

GETHSEMANE STACK 44-K RECEIVING COMPLETE FILE: OATHLOCK_PRELIMINARY_RAW_01.

One second.

Two.

Three.

Four.

Full-band collapsed.

The stack clicked.

COMPLETE FILE RECEIVED. HASH MATCHES PRIOR INDEX. DISTRIBUTED PRESERVATION INITIATED. ADJUDICATION UNAVAILABLE. BANDWIDTH RETURNING TO CONSERVATION MODE.

Noor put both hands over their face.

"That is a win," Sela said.

Noor's voice came muffled. "It is a preserved wound."

"That is what wins look like today."

Bell transmitted:

AUSTERE BELL ACKNOWLEDGES GETHSEMANE PRESERVATION OF OATHLOCK PRELIMINARY. PURSUIT STATUS UNCHANGED. WEAPONS REMAIN COLD PENDING REVIEW.

Valech transmitted:

CONVOY COMMAND OFFICE NOTES THAT PRESERVATION OF INTERNAL IRREGULARITY DOES NOT ANSWER AVAR BREAK CHARGES OR PASSENGER SAFETY CONCERNS. CHARTER PETITION CONTINUES.

Pell transmitted:

NICE TRICK. WRONG WOUND.

Noor muted Pell with unusual elegance.

Mer asked, "Where are we?"

Navigation answered. "Past Stack 44-K primary weak-band. Next Gethsemane relay chance in forty-two minutes if we survive route and pursuit geometry."

"Bell?"

"Still high aft. Cold."

"Cold how long?"

"Unknown."

The buoy began to recede on the aft display, still rotating, still patched, still receiving more grief than it could carry. The court had moved. The *Mercy* had reached it. The court had not saved them.

It had remembered one thing.

In the school deck, Mother Kal told the children that sometimes courts were like old cupboards: useful if bolted properly, dangerous if assumed. Jessa reportedly asked whether Gethsemane had a frowny face. Mother Kal said it had many, but most were filed under seal.

Noor did not laugh when the report reached command.

They were too busy drafting the next filing skeleton for the next relay window.

Tovin stood beside them and watched the preserved-file receipt blink.

"We are still under pursuit," he said.

"Yes."

"Valech still has his petition."

"Yes."

"Pell still has the witness."

"Yes."

"Bell still has guns."

"Yes."

"And we have one wound preserved."

Noor lowered their hands and looked at him with tired, furious eyes.

"Do not make it poetic."

He nodded. "We have one file they cannot easily erase."

"Better."

Outside, Gethsemane Stack 44-K turned away into debris and distance, already becoming less court than rumor again.

The *Mercy of Kaln* kept moving.

Behind it, somewhere in the slow machinery of distributed law, the Oathlock wound began to copy itself into places fear could not reach all at once.

Chapter 18: Disabling Fire

The cleanest shot aboard the *Austere Bell* was named Mercy Engine Quarter, Nonlethal Package Four.

Jaya Soth hated the name before anyone explained it. Names had weather. This one arrived already absolving itself.

The package hovered in the tactical review well as a narrow amber cone touching the aft drive structure of the *Mercy of Kaln*. Its lines were elegant, almost tender. It avoided school deck. It avoided hospital deck. It avoided the known Oathlock routes. It avoided, according to the model, any section where civilians were likely to shelter. It proposed a paired pulse from Bell's forward disabling emitters, tuned to overload three external engine regulators and force the *Mercy* into a slow tumble that Bell could catch with drones and clamps. No hull breach expected. No explosive cascade expected. No fatal acceleration expected. Civilian injury probability under one percent.

Jaya trusted probabilities less when they sounded proud.

Lieutenant Arin Cho stood opposite her in the well, hands clasped behind his back. Commander Fane occupied the legal rail. Fire-control officer Tevran, who had not slept enough to be tactful, looked openly relieved to have a solution that did not involve waiting for another buoy to rotate.

Valech's latest advisory sat in the corner of the display like an uninvited superior:

PRESERVATION OF INTERNAL IRREGULARITY DOES NOT ANSWER AVAR BREAK CHARGES. PROLONGED PURSUIT INCREASES PASSENGER RISK. DISABLING ACTION SHOULD BE EVALUATED WITHOUT ROMANTICIZING ACCUSED VESSEL AUTONOMY.

"He writes like a man allergic to mirrors," Fane had said.

Jaya had pretended not to hear because she wanted to say something worse.

Now she pointed at the amber cone. "Assumptions."

Tevran answered too quickly. "Mercy internal map based on public feeds, old Kaln-class structural data, demilitarization registry, and thermal updates from Cinderwake. Package targets external regulators only. It should not transfer into civilian systems."

"Should."

Tevran corrected. "Model indicates no transfer."

"Models are how should wears a uniform."

Cho looked down, perhaps to hide a reaction.

Fane said, "Civilian injury under one percent depends on their internal power reroutes being as declared."

"And if they are not?"

"Risk rises. Unknown amount."

"Because we do not know how hospital shielding, school-deck interference, and civilian nav reroutes cross the engine regulator bus."

Tevran said, "They sent diagrams."

"They sent diagrams of a ship they are repairing while under pursuit after finding witness-chain tamper. Diagrams are not topology under stress."

The review well accepted that without opinion.

Cho said, "Admiral, we cannot continue indefinitely on doubt. Bell's crew knows the package exists. Valech knows we have geometry. The charter petition is using our delay as proof of system failure. The *Mercy* has preserved one file, yes, but it remains accused and mobile. Pell still has leverage. Every minute makes route authorities less cooperative."

"I asked for assumptions, not pressure."

His jaw tightened. "Pressure is an assumption in command."

That was good enough that Jaya disliked him for it.

"Yes," she said. "And it corrupts models if left unnamed."

She opened a side channel. "Mercy of Kaln, Austere Bell."

The accused ship answered after one second. Mer Anik's voice carried fatigue held in both hands.

"Austere Bell, Mercy."

"Bell has computed a nonlethal disabling package targeting your aft external engine regulators. We are not firing. I am transmitting the proposed target geometry and requesting civilian-system risk response under immediate review."

Tevran stared at her. Cho went very still. Fane closed his eyes like a lawyer watching a bridge become interesting.

Mer did not waste time. "Transmit."

Jaya sent the package.

The public feed did not receive it. Not yet. She sent it under tactical-humanitarian seal to the *Mercy*, Gethsemane hash notice, and Bell's own legal archive. If someone later accused her of warning the quarry, she would answer that she had warned the civilians inside the quarry-shaped object.

The *Mercy*'s response came not as words but as a storm of engineering objections.

Sela Marr's voice cut through first. "Who built this? A sculptor?"

Jaya almost smiled. Almost.

Mer said, "Engineering response."

Sela replied, "Package hits external regulators, yes, but regulator three cross-talks with the civilian thermal bypass we installed after Varo because the old bus was cracked and we had no dock time. Pulse backwash could dump transient load into hospital deck coolers and school-deck hood chargers. Not guaranteed. Not unlikely enough."

Tevran shook his head. "That reroute is not in their registry."

Jaya muted outgoing audio. "Why not?"

Tevran looked irritated. "Maybe because it violates demilitarized reporting."

Fane checked. "Or because civilian thermal bypass below a certain rating is local repair log, not registry amendment, if not tied to sealed systems."

Jaya unmuted. "Mercy, send repair log."

Noor Venn's voice answered. "Sending Varo civilian thermal bypass log, witness signatures, and budget complaint."

Sela said, "The budget complaint is technically part of causality."

The log arrived. It was messy, stamped by Varo Anchorage, signed by two exhausted engineers, Noor, and a clinic administrator who had included a note saying, "If anyone asks why this was not done to standard, ask them why the grant was denied." The repair was real. Imperfect. Legal enough. Invisible to a clean disabling model.

Jaya put it into the well.

The amber cone changed.

Civilian injury probability climbed from under one percent to six point four, then became "range uncertain" when the model admitted it did not know the current state of school-deck hood chargers after the cupboard incident.

Tevran went pale with professional offense. "That is a conservative uncertainty expansion."

"Good," Jaya said.

Cho said, "Admiral, they have incentive to exaggerate civilian coupling."

"Yes. And physics has incentive to embarrass arrogance."

The *Mercy* sent another packet: child-safety override protocol draft, latch inspection status, current hood charger load, and a hand-drawn note from Miri Pell-not-related scanned under medical witness. The note showed a frowning cupboard and a drone with angry eyebrows. Noor had labeled it MINOR WITNESS CONTEXT / NOT TECHNICAL EVIDENCE. Sela had appended: TECHNICALLY, THE CUPBOARD IS RELEVANT.

Jaya stared at the drawing longer than tactical necessity required.

Cho said quietly, "That should not affect fire-control."

"No," Jaya said. "It affects command."

Fane cleared his throat. "Legal review: firing after receiving this repair log and child-system coupling warning would require accepting known civilian transient risk. Still potentially lawful if necessity rises, but no longer clean."

"There are no clean shots," Jaya said.

Tevran objected before he could stop himself. "There are cleaner shots."

"Yes. That distinction saves lives. It also kills people when mistaken for absolution."

Valech called.

Of course he did.

Jaya considered refusing. Then she opened the channel because refusal could be made into evasion faster than she could enjoy it.

Valech's face appeared in the review well. "Admiral. You transmitted disabling geometry to the accused vessel."

"I transmitted civilian-system risk geometry to a vessel carrying civilians."

"You warned them."

"I warned the people our pulse might harm."

"And gave their officers opportunity to fabricate objections."

"They sent a Varo repair log with external signatures. Bell legal confirms plausibility."

Valech's eyes moved once, likely to someone off-screen feeding him the same fact. "Plausibility is not immunity."

"Nor is urgency."

"Jaya, the petition now has eighteen councils. Survivor filings continue. Pardon Reef is attempting to insert itself as witness broker. Gethsemane is overloaded. Bell's hesitation is becoming the argument for armed convoy command."

"Then perhaps armed convoy command should be able to survive one admiral checking where a pulse goes."

His voice lowered. "You are making this personal."

She looked at the amber cone, now fuzzed by uncertainty around school-deck chargers and hospital coolers. "No. I am making it physical."

Valech leaned forward. "If you lose the *Mercy*, every forged packet, every quiet ghost, every child aboard becomes a story told by fugitives and criminals. Disable them while you can preserve them."

"A disabling shot that damages a child's pressure hood does not preserve the child."

"You do not know it will."

"I now know it can."

"Everything can."

"That is the sentence cowards use to flatten risk."

The channel went very still.

Cho looked at her. Fane stared at the floor.

Valech's face hardened into command. "Convoy Command Office formally requests Bell execute nonlethal disabling action before accused vessel leaves current geometry."

"Request received."

"And?"

"Denied at current civilian-system risk."

"You are protecting them."

"I am protecting my order from becoming stupid."

Valech ended the channel.

Jaya exhaled once. "Put that in the log."

Fane said, "All of it?"

"Unfortunately."

Cho stepped closer to the well. "Admiral, the crew will hear that you denied a nonlethal package because of a repair log supplied by the accused."

"Then the crew will hear correctly."

"They will not all accept it."

"I did not promote them for their acceptance."

He did not retreat. Good.

"What if Valech is right? What if this hesitation lets them reach a channel Pell controls, or lets the real attacker use the time, or lets the *Mercy* destroy another convoy if the frame is not a frame?"

"Then this choice will haunt me."

"That is not an operational answer."

"No. It is the answer under the operational answer." She pointed at the cone. "We revise the package. No pulse through regulator three. Find a clamp solution, drone net, engine shadow, anything that does not couple through the civilian thermal bypass."

Tevran said, "That may not be possible before geometry degrades."

"Then geometry degrades."

On the *Mercy*, Sela watched the disabling cone dissolve from amber into uncertain red and felt her anger lose the easy part of itself.

She had been prepared to hate Bell for computing it. She still did. She hated the elegance of the first package. She hated that their own ugly repair log had not been in the registry. She hated Varo's denied grant, Halvyr's closed dock, Pell's fog, Valech's clean advisories, every adult decision that had turned a child's hood charger into a variable in a naval firing model.

But Bell had sent the shot before firing.

That mattered.

She wished it did not. Simple hatred was a tool with a comfortable handle.

"Engineering to command," she said.

Mer answered. "Go."

"I can give Bell alternate disable points."

Tovin turned from the restraint display. "What?"

Sela looked at him through the screen. "You heard me."

Noor said, "Please phrase this in a way that does not make my soul leave."

"If we show them where a disabling pulse can hit without coupling through child systems or hospital coolers, we reduce the chance they use the wrong package."

Tovin said, "We help them shoot us."

"We help them not shoot Miri's hood charger if they shoot us anyway."

The command deck took that in.

Mer's face tightened with the kind of respect that hurt. "Can you identify points that disable maneuver without sealed-system ambiguity?"

"Maybe. External coolant dump valves two and four, if pulsed in stagger. It would force us to reduce drive to thirty percent for cooling or risk engine damage. It would not tumble us. It would make capture easier. It would not touch school deck. It might rupture our backup coolant reserve and cost us the next sprint."

Noor said, "Legally, providing target refinement could be construed as consent."

"Then write 'not consent' in very big letters."

"I can do that."

Tovin looked at Sela. "Why?"

She laughed, not kindly. "Because restraint is a tool, remember? This is me inspecting the part where the enemy has a hammer and we point away from children's heads."

He accepted that because anything else would be hypocrisy.

Mer said, "Prepare alternate nonconsensual harm-reduction geometry. Mark no consent, civilian risk reduction only. Send to Bell and Gethsemane hash."

Noor muttered, "There are days I miss ordinary impossible law."

Sela built the geometry with Lemm from navigation and two engineers who knew the coolant dump valves because they had cursed them personally. They marked every uncertainty. They included repair logs. They wrote in the header:

MERCY DOES NOT CONSENT TO DISABLING FIRE. THIS PACKAGE IDENTIFIES LOWER CIVILIAN-HARM TARGETS SHOULD PURSUIT GROUP FIRE DESPITE OBJECTION.

Noor added:

PROVIDED UNDER PROTEST TO REDUCE CIVILIAN RISK AND PRESERVE ACCOUNTABILITY.

Tovin added nothing. The restraint doctrine did not yet have language for helping an armed pursuer aim at a less cruel place.

Ixo did.

"This is pressure-chain mitigation," it said. "Not cooperation with violence. Cooperation would assist the pursuer's objective. This assists the future victim's survivability under foreseeable coercion."

Noor looked at the screen. "Say that again slower."

Ixo did.

Noor added it as annotation.

They sent the alternate geometry.

On the *Austere Bell*, the review well received it with audible silence.

Tevran read the header twice. "They are giving us targets."

Fane corrected. "They are objecting to being fired upon and preserving lower-harm alternatives."

Cho said, "That is still giving us targets."

Jaya looked at the coolant dump valves Sela had marked. The geometry was uglier than Bell's package. Less elegant. More uncertain in tactical outcome. Better documented in civilian risk. It would not tumble the *Mercy*. It would slow it and damage cooling. It might not be enough for capture if the old ship accepted engine harm. It would, however, avoid the child systems Bell had nearly missed.

She trusted it more because it did not flatter her.

"Run it," she said.

The model processed. Civilian injury probability dropped below one percent again, but this time the number came with broad tactical uncertainty and an ugly warning about prolonged engine damage, possible future medical power rationing, and capture failure probability.

Jaya said, "There. Honest ugliness."

Cho's voice was low. "If we use their geometry, we acknowledge their objection mattered."

"It does."

"If we do not use it and later fire Bell's package, we own the child risk."

"We already own knowing."

Fire-control officer Tevran looked at the updated model. "Admiral, if ordered, I recommend the Mercy-supplied alternate over Package Four."

Jaya appreciated the courage of changing his own recommendation in public. "Noted."

Fane said, "Legal: the alternate is more defensible under civilian harm mitigation, less decisive tactically."

"Noted."

Cho said, "Command: less decisive may mean more total risk."

"Noted."

Valech's next message arrived text-only, perhaps because he did not trust his face.

CONVOY COMMAND OFFICE OBJECTS TO ALLOWING ACCUSED VESSEL TO SHAPE DISABLING CONDITIONS. FIRE CONTROL MUST NOT BE CAPTURED BY TARGET PREFERENCE.

Jaya read it and felt, unexpectedly, tired rather than angry.

"Reply," she said. "Bell will evaluate all available civilian-harm data, including target-originated repair records and alternate risk geometry, without treating target preference as command authority. Fire-control remains Bell's."

Fane sent it.

"Weapons?" Cho asked.

The geometry window was closing. The *Mercy* moved ahead, damaged, slow enough to hit, fast enough to escape clean certainty. Bell's crew waited. Valech waited. Pell likely listened from some dirty relay. Gethsemane preserved one wound and could not adjudicate the next.

Jaya looked at the two packages: Bell's clean cone, Mercy's ugly valves.

"Warm alternate only," she said.

The well changed color.

Aboard the *Mercy*, every alarm that mattered stayed silent, but every person with access to command telemetry knew Bell had warmed a disabling solution.

Noor read the notice. "Austere Bell has warmed the alternate coolant-valve package. They have not fired."

Sela sat down on the engineering deck. Not because she was weak. Because her knees had become procedural uncertainty.

Tovin gripped the edge of the restraint console. "Civilian risk?"

"Lower than Bell's first package," Noor said.

"Tactical risk?"

"Still enough to put us under their clamps if they fire and we cannot compensate."

Mer said, "Options."

Navigation answered. "We can accept current vector and risk shot. We can burn hard and stress patch, possibly provoke fire. We can turn toward Broken Buoy Cut and look like evasion. We can reduce speed and prepare coolant isolation, which may make the shot less damaging but capture easier."

All choices were bad. Some choices admitted it.

Sela spoke from engineering. "If they fire at the valves, I can isolate in three seconds if prepped. That may save cooling but costs drive response."

Mer said, "Prep isolation."

Noor said, "That looks like expecting fire."

"We are expecting fire."

Tovin said, "Prep openly. Public feed. Mark as civilian-harm mitigation, not evasive."

Mer nodded. "Do it."

The *Mercy* prepared to survive being disabled by the lower-harm target it had provided.

Children were moved inward again, but this time Mother Kal told them why in sentences too plain for comfort. Miri, awake and furious, demanded to know whether the cupboards had been checked. They had. She demanded to know whether Bell was sorry before or after aiming. Mother Kal said both could be true and not enough. Jessa said adults were exhausting. Doctor Vale recorded no objection.

Bell's weapons warmed.

For forty-one seconds, the system held the universe in a narrow painful shape.

Jaya watched the firing authorization line wait under her hand.

Cho stood beside her. "Admiral."

"I know."

"If we do not fire now, we lose this window."

"Yes."

"If we fire, we may end this without more pursuit."

"Or begin the part where our cleanest remaining option becomes the harm everyone remembers."

He looked at the Mercy-supplied geometry. "They are ready for it."

"That is not consent."

"No."

"Say the tactical recommendation."

Cho swallowed. "Fire alternate package. Lower civilian risk, acceptable chance of slowing the target, preserves command credibility under Valech pressure."

"Fire-control?"

Tevran said, "Fire alternate package if ordered."

"Legal?"

Fane said, "Lawful if command determines necessity exceeds remaining review value."

Jaya stared at the *Mercy*'s icon.

Necessary.

There was Quiet Authority's favorite door, polished by centuries of frightened hands.

"What review value remains?" she asked.

Fane blinked. "Admiral?"

"Answer."

He looked at the evidence stack. "Oathlock preserved but not adjudicated. Quiet Authority hypothesis untested. Pell witness unresolved. Bell archive correlation sealed. Order-model disclosure pending. Child-harm protocols newly established. Gethsemane relay in approximately thirty-six minutes."

"So a great deal."

"Yes."

Cho said, "Review value does not stop the accused ship."

"No. It tells me what I destroy by stopping it now."

The firing window reached its center.

Jaya lifted her hand away.

"Hold fire."

Tevran did not move for one breath, then locked the authorization out. "Holding."

Cho closed his eyes.

The window began to decay.

Valech's objection arrived before the weapons finished cooling:

CONVOY COMMAND OFFICE DEMANDS EXPLANATION FOR FAILURE TO EXECUTE AVAILABLE DISABLING ACTION.

Jaya said, "Explanation: civilian-harm risk reduced but not eliminated; review value remains material; pursuit continues."
