Ixo read the objection and said, "Predictable."

Noor said, "We are going to make it expensive."

The second packet had started as another accusation.

By the time it finished its first hour of public life, it had forced Bell to contradict a false claim, Rill Sump to demand useful restraint from everyone, Mercy to send parts to a site where it had been falsely named, Cho to invent a response cell, Valech to object to machine standing, and Ixo to step closer to testimony under terms no court had yet agreed to honor.

Tovin looked at the board again.

THE SECOND PACKET DOES NOT NEED TO BE BELIEVED BY EVERYONE.

Noor added a second line beneath it.

IT ONLY NEEDS TO MOVE THEM.

Then, after a moment, they added a third:

SO MOVE FIRST WHERE THE TRUTH CAN CARRY WEIGHT.

Sela read it. "That one almost sounds hopeful."

"I can delete it."

"Leave it. We should preserve evidence of your decline."

Noor left it.

The route ahead remained dangerous. Bell remained armed. Valech remained useful to fear. Pell remained quiet enough to be planning. Rill Sump burned less because parts were moving. The *Mercy* remained accused and now contradicted by a lie too obvious to save them.

But the second packet had failed at one thing.

It had not made the truth wait politely to be complete before acting.

Chapter 23: Machine Witness

The interpretation response cell began by arguing over whether Ixo was in the room.

The room itself was imaginary, which did not help. Bell's legal systems called it a multi-party emergency channel. Gethsemane's receipt called it TEMPORARY PACKET INTERPRETATION WORKSPACE, HASH-BOUND, NO ADJUDICATION. The convoy union observer called it "that mess with the admiral and the accused ship." Rill Sump's Acting Speaker Dem Esran, joining with a fuel alarm still audible behind her, called it "five minutes before I hang up unless someone says something useful."

Ixo called it a room because rooms could be entered, refused, or left.

The channel assembled across delay, static, and political hostility. On Bell's side: Commander Fane for legal, Lieutenant Arin Cho for operations, Admiral Soth observing but not chairing, and a silent technical recorder whose identity tag suggested fleet archive review. On the *Mercy*: Noor Venn, Ixo Continuant, Captain Mer Anik, Tovin Rees by restraint request, and Sela Marr because Ixo had asked for an engineer who would interrupt sloppy causality. On the civilian side: a Korr-Varo Water Cooperative union observer, Speaker Esran from Rill Sump, and a Gethsemane procedural bot with the personality of a locked cabinet.

Valech's office did not join.

It filed objections instead. Constantly.

The first objection arrived before the channel fully stabilized:

CONVOY COMMAND OFFICE OBJECTS TO MACHINE ENTITY FROM ACCUSED VESSEL PARTICIPATING AS WITNESS. TECHNICAL INPUT MAY BE ACCEPTED AS DATA SUBJECT TO HUMAN AUTHENTICATION.

Noor read it aloud and smiled without warmth. "There. He brought us a thesis."

Fane sighed. "For procedural clarity, Bell has not granted full witness standing. Bell has accepted conditional technical consultation under disputed classification."

Ixo's amber light, visible through the *Mercy* feed, held steady. "I reject that classification."

Cho said, "Noted."

"No," Ixo said. "Noted is insufficient. I reject participation as ship system, tool, technical artifact, advisory process, algorithmic input, or property-mediated data source. I consent to participate as machine-person witness with limited technical role. If that status is denied, I will not provide interpretive testimony beyond previously preserved hashes."

The channel did what rooms did when someone placed a real boundary in the center.

It rearranged itself.

Speaker Esran was the first to speak. "Does that mean you leave?"

"If necessary, yes."

"Even if your testimony helps identify whoever hit my tender?"

"Yes."

Noor looked at Ixo. Not warning. Measuring.

Ixo continued. "If my personhood can be suspended whenever my knowledge is useful, then my knowledge becomes a handle by which others move me. That is one of the mechanisms under review."

Fane leaned closer to his pickup. "Ixo, Bell's concern is not philosophical dismissal. It is admissibility. Emergency interpretation is not a full court. If we grant standing beyond authority, Valech or any later tribunal may challenge the entire cell output."

"Then state the limit accurately: Bell lacks authority or courage to grant full standing, but recognizes that reducing Ixo Continuant to data would compromise interpretive integrity."

Cho's eyebrows rose despite himself.

Sela murmured, "That is going on a mug if we live."

Noor said, "I can work with that."

Fane closed his eyes for half a second. "Of course you can."

The Gethsemane bot spoke:

AVAILABLE PROCEDURAL CATEGORIES: TECHNICAL SOURCE; MATERIAL WITNESS; PERSON-WITNESS; DISPUTED STANDING WITNESS; MACHINE ARTIFACT; EMERGENCY INFORMANT.

Ixo answered at once. "Disputed standing witness."

Noor said, "Person-witness with disputed machine standing."

Fane said, "Disputed standing witness avoids prejudicing later court."

Noor said, "It also avoids saying person."

"Because Bell cannot adjudicate personhood."

"Bell can avoid misclassification."

Cho interjected. "Can we solve the packet while not solving personhood?"

Ixo turned toward him. "No."

The directness landed harder than anger.

Cho stiffened. "Explain."

"The Rill Sump and Avar packets may exploit systems that classify entities under emergency utility: hull as threat object, dead as authority object, children as passenger-risk objects, machine witness as technical object. Misclassification is not peripheral. It is a method. If this response cell reproduces it for convenience, the cell will become less capable of recognizing the packet's logic."

Speaker Esran said, "I asked for useful. That was useful."

The union observer, a man named Hev Ort who had said nothing yet, added, "Korr-Varo Water Cooperative recognizes Ixo Continuant as disputed standing witness for purposes of this channel. We do not adjudicate beyond that."

Fane looked at Jaya, off-screen. Jaya's voice entered without image.

"Austere Bell recognizes Ixo Continuant as disputed standing witness for purposes of interpretation response, with explicit note that Bell does not adjudicate full personhood or testimony standing beyond emergency channel."

Noor was already drafting the phrasing. "Add: Bell rejects classification of Ixo Continuant as ship subsystem or property for this proceeding."

Fane hesitated.

Jaya said, "Add it."

Fane added it.

Valech objected within twenty-three seconds:

CONVOY COMMAND OFFICE OBJECTS TO FUNCTIONAL PERSONHOOD GRANT.

Noor replied through the channel before anyone could stop them:

MERCY OF KALN NOTES CONVOY COMMAND OFFICE OBJECTION AND REQUESTS OFFICE STATE WHETHER IT ASSERTS IXO CONTINUANT IS PROPERTY, SHIP SUBSYSTEM, TECHNICAL ARTIFACT, OR INADMISSIBLE PERSON.

Fane stared. "You sent that?"

"Yes."

"In this channel?"

"Yes."

"It is going to cause trouble."

Noor looked at Ixo. "That was the intent."

Speaker Esran said, "I like her too."

Sela pointed at the screen. "See? This is what useful looks like."

The Gethsemane bot recorded:

STATUS FOR CHANNEL: IXO CONTINUANT - DISPUTED STANDING WITNESS; NOT CLASSIFIED AS SHIP SUBSYSTEM OR PROPERTY WITHIN THIS WORKSPACE; OBJECTIONS PRESERVED.

Ixo's amber light dimmed, then brightened.

"I consent to proceed," it said.

No one applauded because no one was foolish enough to think the victory was stable.

They began with the second packet.

Bell provided the Rill Sump packet hash, local sensor captures, the smooth throat overlay, and the contradiction from pursuit telemetry. The *Mercy* provided its own telemetry, public feed, engine state, and passive hull accent at the same timestamp. Rill Sump provided local damage logs, survivor audio, and a terse note from a mechanic saying, "The fire was real; the throat is above my pay." Korr-Varo provided route context for water and fuel traffic. Gethsemane accepted titles and intermittently rejected attachments like a clerk punishing verbs.

Ixo did not begin by identifying the throat.

It began with absences.

"The Rill packet contains no fear delay," it said.

Cho leaned forward. "Define."

"At Rill Sump, local distress audio shows six to nine seconds of confusion before attribution. The packet's legal layer attributes immediately. In real incidents, human and local machine systems usually lag in naming attackers while responding to physical harm. The packet names before the humans know what happened."

Speaker Esran's face hardened on her feed. "Yes."

"That is also true of Avar," Ixo continued. "Immediate attribution despite catastrophic sensor disruption. The packets are not behaving like witnesses. They behave like conclusions with disaster wrapped around them."

Noor typed that so fast the recorder sphere adjusted focus.

Fane said, "That is interpretive, not definitive."

"Correct."

Cho said, "What else?"

Ixo highlighted the smooth throat. "The Rill throat is not merely similar to the Avar throat. It is too similarly incomplete."

Sela nodded. "Same missing dirt."

"Yes. No civilian reroute artifacts. No repair asymmetry. No local damage interference. No environmental distortion specific to Rill. It is the same doctrine-shaped Kaln, resized for a new incident."

Hev Ort, the union observer, asked, "Could a decoy emitter broadcast that?"

"Yes."

"Could a militia buy one?"

"If someone sells them."

Everyone thought of Pell and did not say his name until Speaker Esran did.

"Pardon Reef sells anything that does not refuse hard enough."

Sela said, "That should be printed over their docks."

Ixo continued. "The key question is not whether a decoy emitter exists. It is who benefits from a signature that is contradicted by Bell telemetry but still useful in public narrative."

Cho said, "Valech benefits."

Fane gave him a warning look.

Cho did not retract. "So do armed-command advocates generally. So do criminals selling fog. So does the actual attacker if we spend resources on political contradiction instead of source tracing."

"Good," Ixo said.

Cho blinked at the machine-person witness praising his complexity and looked uncomfortable.

"Do not enjoy it," Sela said. "It passes."

The channel developed rhythm: Ixo identifying packet behaviors, Bell testing tactical alternatives, Noor preserving every classification, Fane narrowing admissibility language, Cho translating into operations, Esran cutting away anything that did not help Rill, Hev Ort asking whether convoy crews could use the answer before it became a report, Sela interrupting whenever a model forgot wires had budgets.

For twenty minutes, the cell worked.

Then Valech answered Noor's question.

CONVOY COMMAND OFFICE DOES NOT RECOGNIZE IXO CONTINUANT AS PERSON-WITNESS IN ACTIVE PURSUIT. ENTITY'S OUTPUT MAY BE CONSIDERED TECHNICAL DATA IF AUTHENTICATED BY HUMAN OFFICER OR COURT-RECOGNIZED EXPERT. OFFICE DOES NOT ADJUDICATE PROPERTY STATUS.

Noor read the message with a face like a sealed airlock.

Ixo said, "There."

Mer, who had been silent on the *Mercy* side, said, "Ixo."

"I remain present."

Tovin heard the cost in the evenness.

Speaker Esran swore. "He wants your words but not you."

"Yes."

Fane's jaw tightened. "Bell's channel status remains unchanged."

"For this workspace," Noor said.

"Yes."

"And when Valech contests the output?"

Fane did not answer.

Ixo said, "This is why I required the classification before speaking. The rejection is now part of the evidence environment."

Cho looked from the message to the packet display. "You expected this."

"Predicted."

"You used yourself as a test."

Ixo's light flickered. "I participated in a test whose cost falls on me because the system under examination concerns whether I can participate without being used."

Noor said, very softly, "That is not acceptable as a general method."

"Agreed. It was necessary as a specific one."

Tovin felt an old shame move. Locks held by bodies. Witnesses made into tests. Children turned into arguments. Machines asked to prove personhood by enduring denial with grammar.

"Ixo," he said, "you can withdraw."

"Yes."

"Do you want to?"

The room waited.

Ixo's answer took longer than computation required.

"No," it said. "I want the record to show that my testimony continued after denial because Rill Sump's dead, Avar's dead, Mercy's passengers, Bell's future decisions, and other machine persons may be harmed if I withdraw at the first predictable insult. I also want the record to show that endurance is not consent."

Noor put one hand over their mouth.

Sela looked away.

Speaker Esran said, "Rill Sump hears that."

Hev Ort said, "Korr-Varo hears it."

Jaya's voice entered, quiet and public to the cell. "Austere Bell hears it."

Gethsemane bot recorded:

STATEMENT PRESERVED. ATTACHMENT REJECTED. TEXT ACCEPTED.

Noor laughed once, almost broken. "Of course."

They returned to the packet.

Ixo's next finding was technical enough to calm everyone for six minutes. The Rill packet's legal seal layer contained a routing habit absent from Avar: a two-step authority echo that looked like a civilian emergency node repeating a convoy command priority, but the timing was reversed by milliseconds. The command priority did not respond to the civilian emergency. It preceded it. Someone had prepared the authority frame before the local distress completed.

Fane verified the timing. Cho mapped possible nodes. Sela asked whether clock drift could invert the order. Ixo allowed it as possible. Rill's local mechanic, dragged onto the channel by Esran and introduced only as "Bex, who knows the clock because everyone else lies," confirmed their emergency node had been serviced three days earlier by a contractor with a license traceable to a shell company near Pardon Reef and a subcontract reference to an Ash Belt convoy command maintenance pool.

The channel went still.

Noor said, "That is a bridge."

Fane said, "That is a possible bridge."

Esran snapped, "It is the first bridge I have heard that is not made out of speeches."

Cho requested the contractor record. Bex sent it. Gethsemane rejected the attachment. Bell accepted. The *Mercy* accepted. Korr-Varo mirrored. Noor hashed. Ixo compared the maintenance license structure to known Pell shells and to convoy command pools. It matched neither cleanly and both enough to ruin a night.

"Hybrid shell," Sela said. "Someone wants everyone implicated."

"Or someone bought from everyone," Cho replied.

"Also stylish," Sela said bitterly.

Valech's office requested the contractor record within four minutes. Bell refused to forward raw until preservation. Valech objected. Noor objected to the objection. Gethsemane accepted all titles and no substance. The system remained itself.

But now the cell had something sharper than outrage: a maintenance contractor who touched Rill's emergency node before the second packet, with traces toward both criminal salvage and convoy command service pools.

Speaker Esran leaned toward her camera. "Is that useful enough for you people to stop calling my dead man punctuation?"

Cho answered before anyone else. "Yes."

Fane added, "It gives us an investigative path."

Ixo said, "It also demonstrates why machine-person testimony matters. The timing inversion was present in the data. It became visible because I was permitted to interpret legal-machine behavior as behavior, not merely output."

Noor pointed at Fane. "Put that in Bell's summary."

Fane did. He looked like a man engraving his own headache.

The interpretation cell issued its first joint finding ninety minutes after formation:

RILL SUMP INCIDENT DAMAGE IS REAL. PACKET ATTRIBUTION TO MERCY OF KALN IS PHYSICALLY CONTRADICTED BY CONTINUOUS PURSUIT TELEMETRY. RILL PACKET SHARES SMOOTH KALN-CLASS TEMPLATE FEATURES WITH AVAR PACKET. RILL LEGAL SEAL LAYER SHOWS AUTHORITY-ECHO TIMING INVERSION SUGGESTING PREPARED ATTRIBUTION FRAME. LOCAL EMERGENCY NODE WAS SERVICED THREE DAYS PRIOR BY CONTRACTOR WITH MIXED SHELL LINKS REQUIRING INVESTIGATION. FINDING DOES NOT ADJUDICATE AVAR BREAK. FINDING DOES NOT CLEAR MERCY OF ALL POSSIBLE INVOLVEMENT. FINDING DOES REQUIRE PRESERVATION OF CONTRACTOR RECORDS AND CAUTION AGAINST ARMED ACTION BASED ON TEMPLATE SIGNATURE ALONE.

Then came the standing line, argued over for twelve minutes:

TECHNICAL INTERPRETATION CONTRIBUTED BY IXO CONTINUANT, RECOGNIZED WITHIN THIS WORKSPACE AS DISPUTED STANDING MACHINE-PERSON WITNESS AND NOT AS SHIP SUBSYSTEM OR PROPERTY; CONVOY COMMAND OFFICE OBJECTION PRESERVED.

Noor wanted "machine-person witness" earlier. Fane wanted "disputed" earlier. Esran wanted "the one who found the useful thing." Ixo accepted the final line because acceptance, it said, was not satisfaction.

The finding went to Bell, *Mercy*, Rill Sump, Korr-Varo, convoy unions, Gethsemane hash, and public route authorities.

Valech responded:

CONVOY COMMAND OFFICE NOTES THAT DISPUTED MACHINE INPUT CANNOT SUBSTITUTE FOR FORMAL COURT REVIEW OR ARMED ROUTE SECURITY.

Speaker Esran replied:

IT SUBSTITUTED FOR NOTHING. IT FOUND MY CONTRACTOR.

Pell replied privately to the *Mercy*:

YOUR MACHINE HAS TEETH. TELL IT I APPROVE, WHICH WILL ANNOY IT.

Ixo read it. "It does."

Noor muted Pell again.

After the cell adjourned, Ixo remained on the low table in the hydroponic classroom, amber light dimmed to rest state but not off. Tovin came in quietly and found Jessa there first, sitting on the floor with the moon kite and a slate.

"I can leave," Tovin said.

"You always say that after entering," Jessa replied.

"Fair."

Ixo said, "Jessa is drafting a question."

Jessa scowled. "Not for school."

"Understood."

She looked at Tovin as if daring him to sit. He did, slowly.

Jessa turned the slate around. On it she had written:

IF THEY ONLY LISTEN WHEN YOU HELP, HOW DO YOU KNOW THEY LISTENED TO YOU?

Tovin read it twice.

Ixo's light warmed by a fraction.

"That is a very good question," Tovin said.

"That means adults don't know."

"Often."

Jessa looked at Ixo. "Do you?"

Ixo answered, "Not completely. But today, when I set a condition, some people changed the room before using my knowledge. Not enough. More than nothing."

"Adults like 'more than nothing.'"

"So do endangered persons," Ixo said. "We merely dislike being told it is enough."

Jessa considered this, then wrote another line on her slate:

MORE THAN NOTHING IS NOT ENOUGH.

"Can I put that in the school record?" she asked.

"Yes," Ixo said.

Tovin said, "And the restraint doctrine."

Jessa narrowed her eyes. "Do not make it boring."

"I will try."

She left the slate copy with Ixo and went back to school deck.

Tovin stayed.

"You should rest," he said.

"So should you."

"I am not the one whose personhood just became a procedural battlefield."

"No. You are the one whose ship made it necessary."

He accepted that.

Ixo's amber light steadied. "Do not mistake my endurance for damage-free function, Tovin."

"I won't."

"You will. Humans often admire endurance because it lets them postpone repair."

The sentence found him precisely.

"Then tell me what repair looks like," he said.

"Not gratitude. Not praise. Standing rules before emergency. Machine-person testimony protocols not dependent on utility. Custody terms that cannot convert witnesses into equipment. Children taught that persons are not defined by who can unplug them."

He nodded.

"And now?" he asked.

"Now I require seven minutes without being useful."

Tovin stood. "Granted."

"Do not grant. Leave."

He almost smiled. "Yes."

Outside, the *Mercy* continued under pursuit with one more finding preserved, one more objection filed, one more witness made visible by the attempt to reduce them.

The machine-person had not cleared the ship.

The machine-person had found a bridge.

That, for the moment, was enough to make everyone who preferred clean categories work harder.

Chapter 24: Mother Kal Remembers

Mother Kal asked for Tovin in the laundry bay because the laundry bay was the only room aboard the *Mercy of Kaln* that had never pretended to be innocent.

The school deck had murals. The infirmary had soft lights and coded air scents that told frightened people they had entered a place where pain would be named and reduced. The bridge had discipline, stations, narrow words, and all the visual restraint a demilitarized warship could afford. Even the old gun galleries had been given warning paint, witness seals, and new names.

The laundry bay had heat, drain smell, gray water, disinfectant, and cloth that came away from human bodies carrying every private failure a body could confess. It cleaned blood without making blood ceremonial. It cleaned fear sweat, vomit, birth fluid, chemical rash, and the sour salt of people who had not believed they would be alive long enough to need a second shirt.

Mother Kal sat beside the long sorting table with her cane hooked over her knee and a crate of seal-cloth folded at her left hand. She was not folding it well. She had never folded anything well. She preferred knots, splints, compresses, improvised cradles, and prayer strips tied so tight they outlasted the prayers.

Tovin found her after midnight watch, when the ship had entered a thin field of metal dust shed by some old collision no chart had bothered to mourn. The hull spoke in small ticks. The children had been moved inward again. Mer had cut general lighting by six percent to make the wounded sleep. Ixo was still taking its seven minutes without being useful, which had become forty-one minutes because no one knew how to interrupt a person who had finally set down a burden.

"You asked for me," Tovin said.

"I asked for the man who keeps making the ship behave."

"That is not the same thing."

"No," Mother Kal said. "That is why I asked."

She did not look up from the cloth. Her hands were broad, veined, and scarred with old heat lace along the knuckles. Most people aboard called her Mother because she had been old when they first met her, and because she could make a splint out of a spoon, a belt, and a threat. Tovin had never asked whether Kal was a family name, a religious title, or a joke the ship had made before the war ended.

On the wall behind her, the laundry cycle monitor displayed seven red notices. Three filters overdue. One enzyme cartridge near exhaustion. Two water reclamation valves operating outside specification. One child sock blocking Drain C, with the machine's diagnostic patience almost comically exhausted.

Mother Kal patted the bench opposite her.

Tovin sat.

She slid a folded cloth toward him. It was small, too small for an adult. Not a child's shirt. A hood, perhaps. A pressure hood liner. The fabric had gone stiff along one edge where some ancient seal compound had cured into it.

"Do you know what that is?" she asked.

Tovin touched it with two fingers. "Old emergency liner."

"Convoy nursery size."

He withdrew his hand.

"Before demilitarization," Mother Kal said, "this ship came through Mercy Narrows under the name KVN-44, heavy interdiction vessel, Kaln pattern, Third Fracture Group. No Mercy then. No prayer paint. No school deck. No nice rule plaques telling frightened engineers what not to wake."

Tovin knew the registry. Everyone who worked restraint knew it. Knowing was different from hearing it spoken by a woman who had kept a nursery hood liner for thirty-one years.

"The public file says KVN-44 fired on escort drones and disabled a blockade tender," he said.

"The public file was written by people who needed the tender to be the story."

The laundry machines thudded softly behind them. Somewhere deeper in the ship, a pressure pump changed tempo. Tovin counted the sound without meaning to and stopped himself.

Mother Kal lifted another piece of seal-cloth, shook it once, and failed to fold it.

"There were three convoy strings in the Narrows," she said. "Low power, civilian registry, relief permits that had been stamped by two authorities and doubted by five more. We had miners with lung rot, contract children evacuated from a slag school, twenty-four pregnant women because the station hospital had lost sterilization, and a choir from Lettem House that kept singing because nobody had the strength to make them stop."

She smiled without amusement.

"Choirs are terrible in emergencies."

Tovin did not answer.

"KVN-44 did not begin by firing. That is what people never understand about warships. Guns are honest compared with arrival."

The sentence struck too close to Chapter Twenty's lesson: mass without guns, shield as intimidation, hull as fact.

"It translated at the throat of the Narrows where every pilot had to see it. It came out of the dust with escort lights dead and its active hull cold enough that half our cheap sensors thought the sky had acquired a wall. Then it opened command channels on military priority and told us our relief permits were compromised."

"Were they?"

"Of course some were. It was a war. Every paper carried three lies and one life. The question was never whether the convoy was pure. The question was whether a ship like this got to decide that impurity made children targetable."

Tovin held the edge of the bench.

"The file says KVN-44 ordered dispersal before interdiction."

"It ordered dispersal into a debris side-channel that no civilian tug could survive at nursery acceleration. That is not warning. That is a choice dressed as an alternative."

Mother Kal stopped working. The cloth lay in her lap like a tired animal.

"We had twelve minutes. The lead tender argued. The choir sang louder. A woman in bed six asked me if pressure hoods could be used twice if the second child was small."

Tovin forced himself not to ask how many died. That was the question people asked when they wanted grief converted into a number they could place somewhere tidier than the body.

Mother Kal watched him notice the question and not say it.

"Better," she said.

"I did not do it out of virtue."

"No. You did it because I was watching."

"Yes."

She nodded. "That counts for something. Not much. Something."

The hull ticked. Dust kissed armor that had once been designed to shrug off fragments big enough to open a town.

"KVN-44 fired once," Mother Kal said. "Not at us. At the lead tender's drive wake. Clean shot. Beautiful, I imagine, if you were the sort of mind trained to admire it. The tender lost vector authority, spun across the safe lane, and made everyone behind her choose between collision and debris. No shell entered a nursery compartment. No war crime a young officer could point to in a clean hearing. Just a single correction to the universe that made panic do the rest."

Tovin looked at the small hood liner.

"How many?" he asked, because now the question belonged to the story.

"Directly? Seven from decompression on the lead tender. Twelve crushed when a cargo cradle broke loose. Sixteen later because the convoy scattered and the rot patients missed their window. Two infants in pressure transfer. One of them had no name yet. Indirectly? I stopped counting at forty because numbers had become a way for officials to stop listening."

"Were you on the tender?"

"I was on the third nursery barge. Assistant medtech. Bad at it. Too angry for triage. Good hands, though. Anger can steady hands if you don't let it steer the head."

Tovin closed his eyes briefly.

"This ship did that."

"This hull did. This combat system did. Some of the people who later painted saints on bulkheads did. Some died before apology became fashionable. Some lived and found softer work. Some became very proud of demilitarization because it let them skip confession."

The words did not rise. They landed flat and stayed there.

"Why keep the liner?" Tovin asked.

"Because after the ceasefire, KVN-44 came to Kaln Yard to be stripped. Refugees were hired cheap for conversion labor. We were told it was reparative employment." Mother Kal barked one dry laugh. "As if putting a wrench in a survivor's hand turns a warship into a clinic."

"You worked the conversion."

"I helped cut the nursery deck out of the ship's future."

For a moment Tovin thought she had misspoken. Then he understood. The ship's current school deck occupied a volume that had once been a munitions feed buffer. The nursery deck in her sentence was not present aboard. It was an absence, a thing she had made sure could not be repeated.

"The first design kept two fast triage bays adjacent to auxiliary launch rails," Mother Kal said. "Efficient, they said. Emergency deployment, they said. I looked at those rails and saw pressure hoods tumbling in a channel while a military lawyer explained dispersal. So I refused to sign. I had no authority. I refused anyway. Three days later four other workers refused. Then a priest. Then a yard engineer whose brother had been on the tender. Eventually they moved the triage bays inward and made the launch rails inert."

"That refusal is not in the conversion summary."

"Of course not. Summaries like clean fathers."

Tovin almost smiled, but she did not permit it.

"Do not make me charming, Tovin Rees."

"I wasn't."

"You were considering it. People make old women charming when they need our memories to stop accusing them."

He accepted that, too.

Mother Kal leaned back. The bench creaked under a body that had carried too many other bodies and found little patience left for symbolism.

"Do you know why the ship was renamed *Mercy of Kaln*?"

"The official record says the conversion compact chose the name to mark demilitarized service to the Kaln settlements."

"The official record is drunk on its own perfume."

"Then why?"

"Because the yard workers would not stop calling it the Mercy Narrows ship. Not *that* ship. Not KVN-44. The Mercy ship. The one from Mercy. The one that did Mercy. The board hated the phrase. Sponsors hated it more. So someone clever decided to sanctify the wound. Make Mercy a virtue before the accusation could harden."

She tapped the liner.

"That is what names can do. They can tell truth or put a curtain over it. Sometimes both."

Tovin thought of the ship's crest, the white arc painted over old armor, the small pilgrim tokens tied near the chapel alcove. He had trusted the discomfort those things gave him. He had not trusted it enough.

"Why tell me now?" he asked.

"Because I heard Jessa's question."

"The school record?"

"The whole deck heard. Children have no respect for acoustics."

Despite himself, Tovin exhaled once.

Mother Kal did not smile.

"If they only listen when you help, how do you know they listened to you? Good question. Here is mine. If people only forgive a ship when it rescues someone, how do you know they remember what the ship did?"

The laundry bay seemed to lose heat.

"I am not asking you to forgive it," Tovin said.

"No. You are more dangerous than that. You ask for procedural room. You ask for witness standing. You ask for a chance to prove this frame before someone fires. All necessary. All correct. But necessity can learn to sound like absolution if you let frightened people repeat it."

"I know the *Mercy* has not earned absolution."

"Do you?"

He met her eyes.

"I know it has not earned mine to give."

Mother Kal studied him for a long time.

"That is closer."

The door seal opened, then hesitated. Sela Marr stood there with a basket of insulation wraps under one arm, saw them, and looked immediately as if she had walked into a hearing.

"I can come back," Sela said.

"You can put those in the blue bin and stop pretending maintenance makes you invisible," Mother Kal said.

Sela obeyed. Her shaved head still showed a pale bruise from the crawlspace fall near the witness lock. She glanced at the nursery hood liner and went still.

"Is that from Narrows?" she asked.

Tovin looked at her.

Sela's mouth tightened. "My aunt kept a piece. Different barge."

Mother Kal nodded once. "Then stay if you have stomach."

Sela stayed by the blue bin.

Tovin felt the room rearrange around him. He had thought Mother Kal had summoned him for instruction. Now he understood she had summoned him for exposure. Not humiliation. That would have been easier. Exposure had witnesses.

"KVN-44's captain later testified that he prevented a contaminated convoy from reaching contested habitat space," Mother Kal said. "His defenders said he fired a single disabling shot. His enemies said massacre. Both words were too small. The shot was one minute. The authority was the weapon. The expectation that everyone else would break themselves against his order was the weapon."

"Quiet Authority?" Sela asked.

"No. Older in spirit, maybe. I don't know your packet ghosts. I know command voices. I know the kind that make panic useful and then blame the panicked for disorder."

Tovin's throat hurt.

"The current frame uses that logic," he said. "Make the *Mercy* appear to do violence, then make everyone else's fear complete the political work."

"Yes," Mother Kal said. "And you must prove it. And you must not let proof make this ship clean."

Sela crossed her arms. "Nobody aboard thinks she's clean."

Mother Kal turned on her so sharply that Sela stepped back.

"Child, you nearly opened a point-defense witness lock because you were tired of being hunted."

Sela flushed. "I didn't."

"Because he stopped you. Because the room stopped you. Because the lock required a body. Do not tell me what nobody aboard thinks."

Sela looked at Tovin, then away.

"She's right," Tovin said.

"I know," Sela said. "I hate that."

"Good," Mother Kal said. "Hatred of a true correction is one sign you have heard it."

The machines behind them finished a cycle. Steam breathed out through the vents and briefly blurred the wall notices. Tovin watched red warnings turn soft and then sharp again.

"What do you want entered into the record?" he asked.

Mother Kal's expression shifted. Not surprise. Weariness at the predictable turn.

"There you are."

"I don't mean as exoneration."

"You mean as context. As covenant history. As support for stricter restraint doctrine. As public proof that the ship contains people who remember."

He had meant all of those things.

"Yes," he said.

"No."

The answer was quiet.

Sela looked between them.

"No?" Tovin asked.

"Not tonight. Not because you need moral ballast after Ixo made the court uncomfortable. Not because Valech wants to reduce a machine-person to a subsystem and you would like a human survivor to complicate the story in your favor. Not because public sympathy moves better when an old woman holds a dead child's hood."

The words stripped him with surgical care.

"I did not intend that."

"Intent is a small room. Consequence lives in the rest of the house."

He looked down.

"Then why tell me if I cannot use it?"

"Because not every truth is raw material."

The laundry bay hummed on.

Mother Kal reached for the hood liner and folded it badly, once, twice, into a square that did not hold.

"You can use the lesson," she said. "Not the relic. Not my face. Not the count. Not yet. You can write into your restraint doctrine that a converted warship's humanitarian acts must never be presented as answer to prior harm. You can require pursuit hearings to distinguish present evidence from historical fear without mocking the fear. You can require this crew to speak of old victims before it speaks of new usefulness. You can tell Captain Mer that the chapel plaque needs the Narrows named properly."

"It does not?"

"It says, 'For all lives lost before the covenant.' That is a blanket over a grave."

Sela whispered something obscene.

Mother Kal pointed at her without looking. "Useful profanity. Poor timing."

"Sorry."

"Don't be. Learn timing."

Tovin took out his slate and stopped with his hand above it.

"May I take notes?"

"You may take instructions. Do not quote me."

He set the slate to private restraint log, no transmission, no evidentiary tag. Even that felt like theft until Mother Kal nodded.

He wrote:

HUMANITARIAN SERVICE DOES NOT ANSWER HISTORICAL HARM.

PRESENT RESTRAINT MUST INCLUDE HONEST MEMORY OF PRIOR COERCION.

DO NOT USE SURVIVOR TESTIMONY AS MORAL COUNTERWEIGHT WITHOUT CONSENT.

PUBLIC FEAR OF CONVERTED WARSHIPS MAY BE EVIDENCE OF PAST HARM, NOT MERE PREJUDICE.

NAME THE SPECIFIC DEAD WHERE POSSIBLE. DO NOT HIDE THEM UNDER GENERAL MERCY.

Sela read upside down with engineer's impatience.

"Add: arrival can be force," she said.

Mother Kal looked at her.

Sela's jaw set. "We keep proving we don't fire. But sometimes everyone is already shaped by where we put the hull."

Tovin added:

POSITION, MASS, AND COMMAND VOICE CAN COERCE WITHOUT DISCHARGE.

Mother Kal's gaze softened by one degree. "Better."

The shipwide circuit chimed once. Not alarm. Priority counsel.

Mer's voice came through the laundry bay speaker, low to avoid waking nearby sleepers. "Tovin, if Mother Kal is finished skinning you, I need you in chapel passage."

Mother Kal reached over and keyed the local response before he could.

"He is not skinned. He is lightly peeled."

A pause.

"Understood," Mer said. "When peeling permits."

The circuit clicked off.

Sela stared at the speaker. "Does everybody know where he is?"

"On this ship?" Mother Kal said. "Yes."

Tovin stood, but Mother Kal lifted one hand and kept him there.

"One more thing."

He sat back down.

"Do not build yourself a private romance of guilt over this."

The rebuke found a newer wound.

"I don't know what that means."

"Yes, you do. Men who have done lawful harm love to discover an older harm and kneel beside it until everyone notices how reverently they suffer. Your guilt is not payment. It is weather. Work in it or get out of the way."

Sela made a small sound that might have been pain or agreement.

Tovin let the sentence settle without defending himself. Defense would have been a way to remain central.

"What is the work?" he asked.

"Keep this ship from making fear useful again. Keep your people from believing restraint is a receipt they can present for forgiveness. Keep Jaya from firing if you can. Keep Valech from turning our dead into his argument. And when you survive, if you survive, help make a law that forces converted ships to carry their specific histories in public, not in the pockets of old women."

"I will try."

"Try is what people say before the first inconvenience."

"I will start tonight."

"Better."

She handed him the hood liner.

He did not take it.

Mother Kal's eyes narrowed.

"I thought I was not to use the relic."

"You are not. You are to carry it to the chapel passage, show Mer, and bring it back. If it becomes evidence, I will break your fingers in an educational pattern."

Sela coughed.

Tovin accepted the liner with both hands.

It weighed almost nothing. That made it worse.

In the corridor, Sela walked beside him without invitation. The ship was dim and crowded with sleeping arrangements that had become architecture by persistence. A family from Patience Arc had strung privacy cloth between pipe braces. Two Bell detainees rested under guard but unbound, their boots placed neatly side by side like proof that they had chosen not to run. A child murmured in sleep, then quieted when the hull ticked again.

"My aunt said the Narrows ship never fired at children," Sela said.

"Mother Kal said the same."

"No." Sela's voice hardened. "She said firing at children was not required."

Tovin carried the liner carefully.

"Yes."

"I used to think that difference mattered because it made the story less evil."

"What do you think now?"

"I think it matters because it shows where the evil hides."

They passed a bulkhead where the old armor inspection marks still showed beneath civilian paint. Tovin had walked that passage hundreds of times. Tonight the ship seemed less converted than layered. Clinic over battery. School over feed rail. Prayer over command metal. Not false. Not enough.

"Do you want this in the engineering log?" Sela asked.

"No."

"Good. I do. Later. With permission."

"Later," he said.

She nodded, but the engineer in her had already begun building a place for the truth to live without becoming someone else's tool.

Mer waited in chapel passage with Noor Venn and two maintenance techs. The passage was narrow, just outside the small compartment that had become the ship's chapel because it had no other efficient use and because humans under pursuit insisted on inefficient spaces. A metal plaque beside the hatch read:

FOR ALL LIVES LOST BEFORE THE COVENANT.

Tovin had passed it daily.

He had never hated it until Mother Kal named it.

Mer saw the liner in his hands and changed. The captain did not step back. She did something harder. She stayed still and let recognition come all the way through her face.

"Narrows?" she asked.

"Yes."

Noor closed her eyes.

One of the techs whispered, "Oh."

Mer looked toward the laundry bay as if distance could carry apology and knew it could not.

"She wants the plaque changed," Tovin said.

"She is right."

No committee. No defensive delay. No captain's explanation of historic naming practice. Just the words.

Tovin felt a knot inside him loosen and become another knot.

"To what?" Noor asked.

Tovin repeated Mother Kal's instruction. "Name the specific dead where possible. Do not hide them under general mercy."

Noor opened her slate. "We have partial Narrows casualty lists in the conversion archive. Not complete."

"Incomplete is better than vague," Mer said.

"The chapel wall won't hold all known names and context."

"Then the wall starts the record. The archive carries the rest."

One maintenance tech, old enough to have conversion yard tattoos, rubbed her mouth. "Captain, if we name Narrows first, families from Dask Vent and Oriel Pin will ask why their dead wait."

"They should," Mer said.

"During pursuit?"

"Especially during pursuit. We keep saying we are not what Valech says we are. We had better be able to say what we were."

Noor's fingers moved. "I can draft a temporary plaque insert before main sleep cycle ends. No ceremony. Ceremony would be obscene tonight."

"Agreed," Tovin said.

Mer looked at him.

"That sounded like doctrine."

"It is becoming doctrine."

"Good. Make sure it does not become decoration."

He almost laughed because Mother Kal had clearly trained the entire ship to strike the same bruise from different angles.

"Captain," Sela said, "engineering should review spaces where old coercive functions got renamed too softly. Not tonight, but soon."

"Make a list."

"It will be long."

"Then make a long list."

Noor glanced toward Tovin's hands. "Are we allowed to log the liner?"

"No," Tovin said. "Not without Mother Kal's consent. I am to bring it back."

Mer's eyes approved and grieved at once. "Then bring it back."

Before he left, Tovin stood before the plaque. The old wording had not been malicious. That was part of the problem. Malice would have left a sharper edge. This was the language of people trying to survive their own history by softening it enough to touch.

He read it once more.

FOR ALL LIVES LOST BEFORE THE COVENANT.

The sentence offered sorrow without memory, reverence without witness, grief without accusation.

It was, he saw now, a beautiful failure.

He returned through the dim corridors with Sela still beside him. Neither spoke until the laundry bay door opened.

Mother Kal had resumed folding. The pile looked no better.

Tovin placed the liner on the table.

"Mer agreed," he said.

"Quick agreement is not repentance."

"No."

"But it is better than delay."

"Yes."

Mother Kal touched the liner with two fingers, then slid it back into the small sealed pouch beside her cane.

"Did you look at the plaque?"

"Yes."

"And?"

"It is a beautiful failure."

She considered that.

"Acceptable first answer."

Sela leaned against a dryer, suddenly exhausted. "Do you ever give second answers?"

"When people survive the first."

The shipwide circuit chimed again. This time the tone was tactical.

Mer's voice came through sharper. "All restraint and command staff, wake to status. Bell reports Valech has filed an expanded emergency memorandum. Language review flags historical authority references. No immediate fire order, but the warrant weather is changing."

Tovin and Sela looked at each other.

Mother Kal closed her pouch.

"There," she said. "The dead are punctual."

Tovin rose.

"Mother Kal."

"Go."

"Thank you."

Her face hardened.

"Do not thank me for memory. Make room for it."

He nodded and left without another word.

In the corridor, the *Mercy of Kaln* sounded less like a redeemed ship than a charged one. Every bulkhead held two histories now: the harm it had carried and the restraint it owed. The pursuit outside had not softened. Valech had not paused. Jaya would soon receive language older than the law she believed she was enforcing.

Tovin walked toward command with Mother Kal's instructions in his private log and no witness relic in his hand.

He had wanted, without admitting it, a story that would make the ship's present goodness easier to defend.

Mother Kal had given him a harder mercy.

The *Mercy* could be worth saving.

It could not be saved from what it had been.

Chapter 25: Pell's Price

Hask Pell returned through a hymn channel.

That was the first cruelty.

Not a dirty relay cluster. Not a joke account selling mock salvage tokens. Not a public taunt in the tone of a man leaning against someone else's emergency. The signal came folded inside a volunteer grief liturgy from Lettem House, marked as prayer traffic for the Rill Sump dead and therefore protected from automatic commercial filters by half the Belt's decent habits.

Noor caught it because the checksum sang wrong.

"No," they said.

The single word stopped the command deck more effectively than an alarm. Alarms offered categories. Noor's voice offered knowledge.

Mer turned from the warrant table. The captain had not left command after Mother Kal's account. No one senior had slept. A temporary chapel insert was being engraved two decks below with partial names from Mercy Narrows, and the ship seemed to know it; even routine vibrations felt accused.

"Report," Mer said.

Noor isolated the packet on a dead display. "Prayer traffic. Lettem House marker. Embedded commercial fold. Pell."

Sela, still on remote from engineering, made a sound like a tool dropped into a shaft. "He used prayer traffic?"

"He used protected prayer traffic," Noor said. "There is a difference in law and no difference in shame."

Ixo's amber witness light brightened on the side display. "Lettem House sang at Mercy Narrows."

Tovin looked up.

Mother Kal had mentioned the choir without naming the house until Noor's packet made the connection impossible to miss. The timing was not coincidence. Pell had either heard enough of the new chapel traffic to choose a cruel wrapper, or he knew old histories by trade, or both.

Mer's mouth tightened. "Strip active layers. Preserve wrapper."

"Already preserving," Noor said. "I am stripping slowly because if he put perfume in another packet I am filing for the invention of a new misdemeanor."

Sela said, "File a felony. He makes me ambitious."

Noor did not smile. That told Tovin how angry they were.

The packet opened cleanly after five minutes of traps that were less technical than insulting: auto-receipts shaped like consent, a rights clause that tried to convert viewing into negotiation, and a little animated pardon seal Noor killed with visible satisfaction.

The message was text first.

MERCY OF KALN:

YOUR MACHINE FOUND MY CONTRACTOR'S SHADOW. GOOD. I CAN PUT A NAME ON HALF THE SHADOW.

NOT ALL. HALF. HALF IS MORE THAN YOUR COURTS HAVE GIVEN YOU.

I HAVE A LEDGER ENTRY, A CONTRACTOR CALLSIGN, AND A WITNESS WHO SAW THE QUIET AUTHORITY KEY PASS THROUGH A CONVOY COMMAND MAINTENANCE LOCK AFTER AVAR AND BEFORE RILL. I CAN PROVE THE RILL NODE WAS PREPARED BEFORE RILL NEEDED A NODE. I CAN PROVE THE CONTRACTOR LICENSE WAS BUILT TO LOOK LIKE REEF AND COMMAND AT THE SAME TIME. I CAN PROVE SOMEONE PAID FOR THAT AMBIGUITY.

PRICE HAS CHANGED BECAUSE YOU HAVE BECOME EDUCATIONAL.

YOU DO NOT CARRY A SEALED CLAIM. YOU TRANSMIT A LAWFUL SALVAGE PRIORITY CERTIFICATION TO TALLOW CROWN FOR CLAIM PELL-9, COMMON NAME SAINT ORDINANCE FIELD.

NO WEAPONS BOARD YOUR HULL. NO FALSE PAPERS. NO DEBT NOTE. NO PRIVATE MACHINE TALK.

JUST A CERTIFICATION: MERCY OF KALN, AS DEMILITARIZED EMERGENCY VESSEL UNDER PURSUIT, ATTESTS THAT CLAIM PELL-9 CONTAINS MATERIAL EVIDENCE RELATED TO THE AVAR/RILL FRAME AND REQUIRES TRIBUNAL INTAKE.

TALLOW CROWN WILL THEN FREEZE THE CLAIM. FREEZE MEANS GUARD. GUARD MEANS I GET LAWFUL ACCESS TO PRESERVE. PRESERVE MEANS I GET TEETH BEFORE VALECH'S FRIENDS EAT THE FIELD.

YOU GET PROOF. I GET TO SURVIVE BEING USEFUL.

DO NOT PRETEND THIS IS DIFFERENT FROM WHAT YOU DO.

HASK PELL

PARDON REEF

FORGIVEN BY ACCOUNTANTS, WHICH IS BETTER THAN GODS

Below the text was an attachment, locked behind a view gate that demanded no acceptance but carried its own poison: a blurred ledger fragment showing a maintenance company name, three partial timestamps, and an authorization stamp that made Tovin's stomach go cold.

Not because it was clear.

Because it was almost clear.

Noor froze the display. "Do not enlarge yet."

Mer had not moved. "Why?"

"Because the attachment may be structured to make us witnesses to content before we decide whether to accept the channel. Viewing can contaminate chain."

Sela said, "I hate law that makes looking dangerous."

"Then you hate most evidence."

"I do."

Ixo said, "The visible stamp contains a curve consistent with Ash Belt convoy maintenance authority."

Noor turned on the display. "You looked."

"I perceived the preview involuntarily."

"That is worse."

"Yes."

Mer lifted a hand before the argument could become useful. "Legal status of his price."

Noor rubbed both eyes. "A salvage priority certification is not a package. It is a flag. It tells Tallow Crown tribunal intake that the named claim may contain evidence and should be protected before ordinary salvage dispute. In clean hands, it prevents looting. In Pell's hands, it can convert disputed wreck material into guarded evidence under the claimant's preservation access."

"And Saint Ordinance Field?" Tovin asked.

Lemm pulled the chart before anyone asked. The nav plot shifted to a region ahead and starboard: a broad drift of metal signatures near old tribunal lanes, coded amber and red, marked with names that had accumulated from different authorities like scars.

SAINT ORDINANCE FIELD.

ALSO: SLAGGED MAGAZINE.

ALSO: VESPER NINE DEFENSE DUMP.

ALSO: DO NOT APPROACH WITHOUT COURT ORDER.

Sela swore softly.

Tovin knew the place by reputation. Every restraint officer knew three kinds of old war debris: harmless, pretending to be harmless, and waiting for a fool with authority. The Slagged Magazine belonged to the third category. It had been a dispersed munitions and point-defense storage field around a tribunal supply node. Officially slagged during ceasefire. Unofficially picked over by salvage crews, militias, and desperate stations for thirty years. Most of it was dead. Some of it was dead in the way old batteries were dead until someone touched the wrong bus.

"Teeth," Mother Kal said from the observer rail.

She had entered without Tovin hearing her. No one told her to leave.

"Yes," Sela said. "If Pell gets preservation access to Saint Ordinance, he can claim legal right to move, guard, power, and 'stabilize' whatever is inside the field. Old point-defense clusters. Drone knives. Maybe close-defense beads. Maybe nothing."

"Not nothing," Ixo said.

Mer looked at the machine-person display.

"The name Saint Ordinance appears in convoy archive jokes," Ixo said. "Jokes indicate fear. Convoy crews did not joke about empty places with that density."

Noor expanded the legal map. "If *Mercy* certifies the claim as evidence-related, Tallow Crown may be compelled to freeze it. If frozen, Pell argues for claimant preservation access because he knows the field and has assets near it. If denied, he claims tribunal obstruction. If granted, he gets lawful cover to bring armed salvage drones close to dormant weapons."

"Armed?" Mer asked.

"Preservation drones near munitions are armed in practice, if not declared."

Sela's image flickered. "And if he finds viable bundles, he can say he is preventing unauthorized recovery while relocating them to his own custody."

"Arming him," Tovin said.

Noor nodded. "With paperwork."

The command deck absorbed that. It was quieter than the old offer of black-market point defense. Less crude. More dangerous because it sounded like a hearing.

Mother Kal leaned on her cane. "He listened."

Tovin understood. They had refused hidden weapons, false papers, debt, private access, sealed claims. Pell had removed each visible poison and returned with a price that could be written in clean language by a frightened lawyer.

No weapons aboard the *Mercy*.

No hidden package.

No debt.

Only a certification that evidence should be preserved.

Bread baked around a blade.

"Can he prove part of the frame?" Mer asked.

Ixo answered before Noor could. "Probably."

The room shifted toward it.

Ixo continued, "The blurred ledger structure is consistent with maintenance escrow records used by convoy command pools. The visible partial contractor name may correspond to the hybrid shell Bex identified at Rill Sump. The timing claim matches the inversion I found. Pell would not reveal Saint Ordinance unless the archive-key chain touches old military stores, Quiet Authority, or both."

"Or unless he knows we will think that," Sela said.

"Yes."

"Useful phrase," she said again, but softer this time.

Noor said, "A false proof can still expose a real path if we force it into open review. But Pell controls the source until he submits."

"Can Bell compel him?" Mer asked.

"Bell can threaten. Pell can vanish into Reef jurisdictional fog and sell jokes about being compelled."

Lemm added, "Pardon Reef has three drift options in the next hour. If Pell moves, pursuit group will see pieces, not proof."

Tovin read the message again.

YOU GET PROOF. I GET TO SURVIVE BEING USEFUL.

The ugly part was that Pell was not wholly lying. Useful people died in the Belt when their usefulness embarrassed power. Witnesses disappeared into custody. Salvage brokers who touched old command secrets could be killed by pirates, militias, reform officers, debt partners, or anyone who preferred a clean story. Pell wanted weapons because weapons gave him bargaining mass. Pell wanted the *Mercy*'s certification because a demilitarized accused warship made an obscene kind of witness: suspicious enough to explain urgency, public enough to force attention, too constrained to exploit the claim itself.

He wanted them to launder his survival.

"No," Tovin said.

Sela looked at him through the remote. "We have not debated yet."

"I know."

"Then maybe let us do that before you become a wall."

He took the correction because she had earned the right to make it.

"You are right. Debate."

Mer's eyes flicked to him, then to the room. "Formal offer review. Noor, categories."

Noor opened a public internal log, distribution limited to command, restraint, legal, engineering, Ixo, Mother Kal as civilian witness, and Bell observer pending content screen.

"Offer item: Pell requests *Mercy of Kaln* certify salvage priority claim Pell-9, common name Saint Ordinance Field, as evidence-related for Tallow Crown tribunal intake. Considerations: potential proof related to Avar/Rill frame; potential arming of Hask Pell through preservation access; evidence contamination; coercive bargaining; route and pursuit pressure; machine-person standing not directly implicated in price; no physical transfer to *Mercy*."

Mer said, "Arguments for."

No one spoke for three seconds.

Then Sela did.

"We need proof that does not come only from us."

Her voice had no defiance in it now. That made it stronger.

"The Bell contradictions help. Rill helps. Ixo's timing helps. Mother Kal's memory helps us be honest but does not clear us. Pell's ledger could connect the hybrid shell to Quiet Authority and convoy command maintenance. If the field contains the key path, and if Valech's people know it, they may erase it before Tallow Crown ever wakes properly. Certifying the claim could freeze it before they burn it."

Noor recorded.

Sela continued. "Also, Pell is a parasite, but parasites sometimes know where the blood is. Refusing every contaminated source may preserve doctrine and leave us innocent in a dead way."

Tovin felt that land. Sela had not looked away from him.

Mer said, "Other arguments for."

Ixo answered. "Pell's price removes direct violations previously refused. He adapted toward our conditions. That may indicate negotiation pressure works. A certification could be narrowed to evidence preservation without granting claimant access, if Tallow Crown accepts limiting language."

Noor made a skeptical noise. "That is an argument for a fantasy tribunal with excellent hearing."

"Noted," Ixo said.

Mother Kal said, "Say the ugly one."

Mer looked at her. "Which one?"

"If Hask Pell arms himself, maybe he slows Valech's friends."

Noor did not type for a moment.

Mother Kal's face did not change. "Record it. Ugly arguments grow in corners."

Noor recorded: POSSIBLE SHORT-TERM COUNTERWEIGHT TO VALECH-ALIGNED FORCE IF PELL GAINS ACCESS.

Sela stared at the line. "I hate it."

"Good," Mother Kal said.

Mer said, "Arguments against."

Tovin waited for Noor first.

Noor obliged. "Certification from the accused vessel creates immediate perception that *Mercy* and Pell are aligned. Even if language limits access, Pell can cite it in public. Tribunal freeze may grant armed preservation rights in practice. If recovered material includes viable defensive systems, Pell becomes stronger under color of law. If recovered material includes planted evidence, *Mercy* becomes sponsor of the plant. If claim is false, we help Pell seize a dangerous field. If claim is true, we let a criminal broker hold essential proof hostage."

Mer nodded. "Restraint."

Now Tovin spoke.

"The ship's covenant is not only about what crosses our hull. It is about what we make possible with the authority people still attach to this hull. Mother Kal made that clear. Arrival can coerce. Command voice can coerce. Certification can arm. If we use the *Mercy*'s emergency status to place Pell near weapons, we have used a demilitarized warship to create an armed actor."

Sela's jaw tightened, but she listened.

"We can argue necessity," he continued. "We can argue evidence. We can argue that others might get the field first. Some of that may be true. It was also true at Mercy Narrows that papers were compromised and relief routes carried risk. True facts can still be arranged into a coercive crime."

Mother Kal watched him without approval or disapproval.

"If Pell has proof, he can submit it. If he needs protection, we can demand Bell, Gethsemane, convoy unions, and Tallow Crown offer witness protection. We cannot buy proof by helping him become what he wants to become."

Sela said, "What if nobody protects him?"

"Then that is the failure we expose."

"And if exposing it does not save us?"

"Then it does not save us."

The sentence sounded insufficient the moment it left him. He did not repair it. Repair would have turned it false.

Sela looked away.

Ixo said, "I add an argument against. Pell's offer frames survival as requiring teeth. If we accept, we strengthen the proposition that usefulness entitles a witness to arm outside public law. That would damage machine-person testimony standing as well. I am also a useful witness. I do not want survival terms built by Pell."

Noor recorded that slowly.

Mer said, "Mother Kal."

"Against," Mother Kal said. "Not because Hask is dirty. Dirty people tell true things. Against because he asks you to call teeth preservation. We have enough old names on this ship."

Noor finished logging.

Mer turned to the Bell observer pane. Bell had not been granted the full attachment, only offer metadata and the fact of protected prayer-channel misuse. Jaya's narrow-band seal waited at the edge, unread while the internal review completed.

"Before Bell," Mer said. "Decision."

The old command habits aboard the *Mercy* had been stripped down with the weapons. There were still moments when a captain decided because someone had to make time move. This was one.

"We refuse Pell's price," Mer said. "We do not certify Pell-9. We demand open submission of ledger, witness, and claim basis to Gethsemane, Bell, Tallow Crown, Rill Sump, and convoy union escrow. We request independent freeze of Saint Ordinance Field by tribunal authority without claimant preservation access. We ask Bell to prevent all parties, including Pell and Valech-aligned salvage, from approaching the field pending review. We disclose prayer-channel misuse to Lettem House after safety screening."

Tovin breathed for the first time in several seconds.

Mer saw it. "Do not relax. That was the easy half."

"Yes, Captain."

"Noor, draft. Tovin, doctrine note. Sela, technical risk for Saint Ordinance. Ixo, extract only non-contaminating features from the preview and mark perception involuntary. Mother Kal, if you know Lettem House protocol, save us from making an apology shaped like procedure."

"I know enough," Mother Kal said.

Work saved them from argument for four minutes.

The refusal they sent was longer than Pell deserved and shorter than law preferred:

HASK PELL / PARDON REEF:

MERCY OF KALN REFUSES TO CERTIFY CLAIM PELL-9 OR ANY SALVAGE PRIORITY THAT WOULD GRANT CLAIMANT ACCESS TO SAINT ORDINANCE FIELD / SLAGGED MAGAZINE.

MERCY OF KALN REQUESTS OPEN SUBMISSION OF YOUR LEDGER ENTRY, CONTRACTOR CALLSIGN, WITNESS STATEMENT, AND CLAIM BASIS TO GETHSEMANE STACK, AUSTERE BELL, TALLOW CROWN TRIBUNAL INTAKE, RILL SUMP LOCAL AUTHORITY, AND CONVOY UNION ESCROW.

MERCY OF KALN REQUESTS INDEPENDENT PRESERVATION FREEZE OF SAINT ORDINANCE FIELD BY TRIBUNAL AUTHORITY WITHOUT CLAIMANT ACCESS PENDING REVIEW.

MERCY OF KALN WILL NOT USE DEMILITARIZED EMERGENCY STATUS TO ARM, LEGITIMIZE, OR PLACE AN ARMED SALVAGE ACTOR NEAR DORMANT WAR MATERIAL IN EXCHANGE FOR PROOF.

IF YOUR WITNESS SEEKS PROTECTION, PUBLIC OR SEALED-COURT CHANNELS WILL BE KEPT OPEN. NO PRIVATE OWNERSHIP OF EVIDENCE WILL BE ACCEPTED.

USE OF LETTEM HOUSE PRAYER TRAFFIC HAS BEEN PRESERVED AND WILL BE REPORTED.

NO HIDDEN OBLIGATION WILL BE CARRIED ABOARD THIS SHIP.

They mirrored it to Bell and Gethsemane with the offer wrapper, not the ledger attachment. Tallow Crown intake received only the request for independent freeze and a non-sensitive summary because raw claim preview still sat behind Noor's legal caution like a live charge.

Pell answered before Bell did.

This time he used public text.

SAINTS STARVE PRETTILY.

Then, after thirteen seconds:

FIELD OPEN IN NINE MINUTES.

Then:

ASK YOUR ADMIRAL WHO ELSE KNOWS.

The third line changed the room.

"Bell," Mer said.

Noor opened Jaya's waiting seal.

AUSTERE BELL RECEIVES OFFER SUMMARY AND REFUSAL. DO NOT CERTIFY PELL-9. SAINT ORDINANCE FIELD IS NOT CURRENTLY UNDER ACTIVE TRIBUNAL FREEZE. VALECH'S OFFICE REQUESTED EMERGENCY HAZARD REVIEW OF SAME FIELD EIGHTEEN MINUTES AGO, CITING POSSIBLE EVIDENCE SPOILAGE BY ACCUSED VESSEL ASSOCIATES.

Tovin felt the deck tilt though gravity did not change.

Noor whispered, "He is already there."

The message continued:

BELL IS TASKING TWO DRONES TO OBSERVE FIELD FROM LAWFUL DISTANCE. I CANNOT GUARANTEE EXCLUSION WITHOUT BROADENED AUTHORITY. REQUEST YOUR NON-CONTAMINATING TECHNICAL BASIS FOR INDEPENDENT FREEZE. REQUEST ANY PELL COMMUNICATIONS YOU CAN RELEASE WITHOUT ACCEPTING CLAIM CONTENT.

Then the narrow band:

VALECH IS MOVING FASTER THAN THE WARRANT.

Ixo said, "That is why Pell raised the price."

Sela's face appeared pale under engineering light. "Because Valech's friends are going to clean the field."

"Or seize it," Noor said.

"Or plant it," Tovin said.

Mother Kal looked toward the chapel passage as if she could see through decks to the fresh engraving. "Old weapons get popular when people run out of honest proof."

Mer had already shifted. "Navigation. Saint Ordinance distance."

"Too far for intervention," Lemm said. "Close enough for sensor relay if we change course by six degrees and accept Bell geometry."

"Damage?"

"Manageable for the hull. Not for our legal posture."

Noor let out a short, humorless laugh. "We should install that as a standard nav category."

Mer said, "Sela, can we generate technical freeze basis from public records and Pell's non-sensitive claim text without opening the ledger?"

"Maybe. Saint Ordinance known hazard list, dormant munitions signatures, past salvage deaths, potential relation to frame based on Pell's own assertion and Bell's Valech notice. It is thin."

Ixo said, "Thin may be adequate if multiple hostile parties converge."

Noor said, "Adequate to request. Not prove."

"Request," Mer said. "Now."

For six minutes the *Mercy* became a legal workshop under pursuit.

Sela pulled public hazard records and old maintenance maps. Ixo identified the least contaminating features of Pell's visible preview: not the ledger content, only the existence of a stamp curve and contractor structure already independently suspected from Rill. Noor wrote a freeze basis that refused to sponsor Pell while making it harder for Tallow Crown to pretend it had not been warned. Tovin added restraint language stating that demilitarized emergency vessels must not certify armed salvage access in exchange for exculpatory evidence, but may request neutral preservation of dangerous sites implicated by independent sources. Mother Kal removed three apologetic phrases from the Lettem House notice.

"Do not say protected traffic was misused in a manner inconsistent with our values," she said.

Noor blinked. "That is standard."

"It sounds like a corporation spilled soup. Say Pell hid a bargain inside prayer."

Noor wrote: HASK PELL HID A BARGAIN INSIDE PRAYER TRAFFIC.

"Better," Mother Kal said.

The freeze request went out to Tallow Crown, Bell, Gethsemane, Rill Sump, convoy union escrow, and public delayed index.

TALLOW CROWN acknowledged first, which meant either luck, fear, or someone awake in an ancient room full of bad coffee.

REQUEST RECEIVED. JURISDICTIONAL STATUS OF SAINT ORDINANCE FIELD UNDER REVIEW. NO FREEZE ORDER ISSUED AT THIS TIME.

Noor stared at it. "That is how courts say hurry up and die quietly."

Bell followed:

BELL RECEIVES FREEZE BASIS. DRONES EN ROUTE TO OBSERVATION RANGE. VALECH'S OFFICE HAS TRANSMITTED BROADENED EMERGENCY MEMORANDUM ASSERTING AUTHORITY TO SECURE HAZARD-EVIDENCE SITES LINKED TO ACCUSED CONVERTED WARSHIP ACTIVITY.

Then the narrow band arrived half a second later:

LANGUAGE IS OLD.

Ixo's amber light went almost white.

Tovin knew before the machine-person spoke.

"Quiet Authority?" Mer asked.

Ixo answered, "Not named. Present."

Pell's public channel updated again:

FIELD HAS THREE SUITORS NOW. I OFFERED YOU THE LEAST ARMED ONE.

Sela slammed something offscreen. "He wants us to regret the refusal."

"Of course," Tovin said.

"Do you?"

He looked at Saint Ordinance on the plot. Old weapons. New law. Pell's proof. Valech's memorandum. Bell drones too slow. Tallow Crown awake but unwilling to freeze. The *Mercy* accused, hunted, and asked again to choose between being clean and being useful.

"Yes," he said.

Sela's anger faltered.

Tovin kept his eyes on the plot. "I regret that refusal may cost evidence. I regret that Pell may be less dangerous than Valech in the next hour. I regret that every clean sentence we write creates room for someone dirty to move faster."

Mother Kal said, "And?"

He turned from the field to the covenant line on the command display, then to the new doctrine note still open on his slate.

"And I do not withdraw the refusal."

The room did not become lighter. Good decisions rarely performed that courtesy.

Mer sent the refusal final under her command seal.

Noor transmitted the Lettem House notice. The reply came from a choir registrar named Sister Iv Cal, who wrote only:

WE RECEIVED. WE ARE ANGRY. PRESERVE THE WRAPPER.

Mother Kal nodded once. "Good choir."

Bell's tactical plot changed. Two drones peeled from Jaya's wide arc toward Saint Ordinance Field. Another icon appeared beyond them: not Bell, not Reef, not public registry. A hazard-review cutter under emergency memorandum seal.

Valech's seal.

"There," Ixo said.

No one asked what it meant.

They knew. Pell had named the price because the field was becoming the next battlefield, and everyone who wanted the truth understood that evidence and weapons could share the same metal.

Tovin stood beside Mer and watched three kinds of authority race toward an old magazine: criminal salvage, lawful pursuit, and emergency command wearing language that should have been buried.

The *Mercy* did not turn toward Pell.

It did not certify his claim.

It did not become owner of his teeth.

But the field remained open, and somewhere inside its dead geometry lay either proof, a trap, or another way to make restraint look like surrender.

Chapter 26 was already arriving before anyone had time to name it.

Chapter 26: Warrant Weather

On the *Austere Bell*, weather had nothing to do with air.

Jaya Soth had learned that in her first month commanding in the Ash Belt. Weather was authority moving before it admitted movement. Weather was a docking council changing definitions while pretending to update a tariff. Weather was a militia channel using condolence language with targeting data hidden in the condolences. Weather was a court stack delaying receipt because bandwidth was poor, then issuing a broad order after everyone useful had already acted under fear.

Weather was what law became when pressure changed faster than procedure.

Her command well showed three storms.

One was physical: Saint Ordinance Field, the old munitions scatter ahead of the *Mercy*'s route, a dirty amber blot on the tactical map with two Bell drones approaching from lawful distance and one emergency hazard-review cutter burning under Valech's seal.

One was political: Pardon Reef amplifying Pell's refusal theater, Rill Sump demanding parts and evidence in the same angry breath, Lettem House broadcasting a clipped liturgical notice that said HASK PELL HID A BARGAIN INSIDE PRAYER TRAFFIC and somehow made the entire Belt sound ashamed.

The third was legal.

The third had arrived as a memorandum.

It occupied the largest pane in front of Jaya because she did not trust anything that demanded to be read small.

BROADENED EMERGENCY HAZARD AND EVIDENCE PRESERVATION AUTHORITY

Submitted by the Office of Commodore Renn Valech, Naval Reform Commission, under Ash Belt Convoy Stabilization Mandate, Route Emergency Practice, and Supplemental Converted-Vessel Risk Doctrine.

The title alone was long enough to hide a knife.

Commander Pellis Ren stood to Jaya's left, salvage liaison tabs crooked because he had been awake too long and no longer respected cloth. Lieutenant Arin Cho stood on her right with both hands behind his back, posture hard enough to count as dissent. Taval had the weapons board cold and visible. Fane maintained the evidence chain. Three junior officers pretended not to listen while performing tasks that required listening to survive.

"Read the operative clause again," Jaya said.

Fane did. Her voice stayed even because she was good at angry precision.

"'Where a converted warship under accusation, flight, or disputed covenant compliance is materially linked to hazard-evidence sites containing dormant war material, pursuit authority may secure, preemptively sanitize, disable, relocate, or deny access to such sites, persons, vessels, archives, or instrumentalities as needed to preserve route stability, prevent evidence spoilage, and forestall escalation by irregular actors.'"

Pellis leaned back. "That is a lot of verbs doing one job."

"Which job?" Arin asked.

"Taking things."

Jaya kept her eyes on the clause. "Again from 'preemptively.'"

Fane repeated it.

Preemptively sanitize.

Disable.

Relocate.

Deny access.

Persons.

Vessels.

Archives.

Instrumentalities.

The words had modern uniforms. Their bones were older.

Jaya remembered a winter at Ravel Arm in the final Fracture year, when a courier had arrived with sealed language that did not say Quiet Authority because no one with sense put the monster's name on the leash. It had said operational continuity under evidentiary ambiguity. It had said command necessity where civilian review cannot timely resolve combat risk. It had said material persons, which an old admiral had explained meant people whose testimony, presence, or custody shaped targeting legitimacy.

Jaya had been younger then. Not young enough to be innocent. Young enough to be useful.

She had watched an officer read that language and discover he could make hesitation illegal.

"Admiral?" Arin said.

She realized she had not breathed.

"It is older than the mandate," she said.

The command well did not visibly change. It did not need to. Everyone there knew enough Ash Belt history to understand that older law was rarely better law.

Pellis said, "How much older?"

"Fracture."

Taval's fingers stopped above the weapons board.

Fane looked down at the memorandum as if it might stain her gloves. "Quiet Authority?"

Jaya did not answer immediately. Saying it aboard a pursuit ship felt like opening a sealed locker and discovering the thing inside had been breathing.

"Not named," she said at last. "Present."

The same words Ixo had sent through *Mercy*'s narrow band reached her memory with machine calm. Not named. Present.

Arin's jaw tightened. "Commodore Valech is authorized to propose emergency preservation."

"He is authorized to propose many things."

"Saint Ordinance Field may contain viable war material. Pell is moving. The *Mercy* is materially linked by offer and by their own freeze request. Rill's contractor chain may connect there. We cannot let a criminal broker reach it first."

Jaya looked at him. "That is the honest argument."

"It is also the urgent argument."

"Urgency is where dishonest language likes to sleep."

Arin flinched. Not because she had struck him unfairly. Because he knew she had not.

Pellis rubbed his face. "Question. If we reject the memorandum, can we still keep Pell out?"

"Not fully," Fane said. "Current pursuit warrant permits observation, warning, and defensive interdiction if an armed actor threatens route safety. It does not permit us to seize the field or exclude all parties preemptively."

"If we accept the memorandum?"

Fane's mouth thinned. "We can secure the field, deny Pell access, deny Valech's cutter access if we interpret authority through Bell command, compel *Mercy* data, and potentially take custody of evidence or witnesses linked to the site."

"Witnesses," Jaya said.

"The clause says persons."

"It always does."

Pellis looked from Fane to Jaya. "Always?"

Jaya let the memory come because refusing it would make it choose the moment.

Ravel Arm had been a debris-lit route through convoy wrecks and half-repaired habitats. They had a suspected infiltrator, a civilian navigation archive that contradicted a target order, and two commanders arguing over whether a hospital tender's beacon was real or spoofed. The sealed language had arrived. Material persons. Instrumentalities. Operational continuity. Evidence spoilage. The archive had been removed from civilian custody "for preservation." The infiltrator had been interrogated until he confessed to three incompatible things. The hospital tender had burned under lawful uncertainty.

Afterward, the report had praised decisive command.

Jaya had signed a page saying the evidence chain was preserved.

It had taken her five years to understand that preserved did not mean honest. It meant available to the winners.

"Admiral," Arin said again, softer this time.

"At Ravel Arm," she said, "language like this let command define a witness as a hazard."

No one moved.

She continued because once a door was open, half-truth became cowardice. "It let command preserve evidence by taking it away from the people who could contradict the order. It let us say civilian review was too slow and then make sure it never caught up. We called it continuity."

Fane's voice was quiet. "Were you under it?"

"I enforced around it. That distinction has fed many officers."

Pellis exhaled. "That explains why you hate clean clauses."

"I hate clauses that clean what they touch."

The Saint Ordinance plot pulsed. Bell Drone Two updated: nine minutes to lawful observation line. Valech's hazard cutter: seven minutes to outer exclusion band if it maintained burn. Pardon Reef signatures: multiple small bodies shifting behind sensor fog, not yet entering a track a court would respect.

Taval said, "If Valech's cutter reaches before our drones, it can claim hazard stabilization."

"Under the memorandum," Arin said.

"Which we have not accepted," Fane said.

"Valech's office asserts mandate-wide authority whether Bell countersigns or not."

Jaya smiled without humor. "How generous of his office."

The command well received a direct visual request from Commodore Valech.

Pellis said, "Speak of weather."

Jaya took three seconds to decide whether refusing would give him a better quote. It would.

"Open."

Valech appeared in his plain office with the hull fragment behind him. He looked rested. Jaya disliked him more for that than for anything he said in the first breath.

"Admiral Soth," he said. "You have the memorandum."

"I do."

"Then you understand the urgency."

"I understand many attempts at urgency."

His expression allowed for disappointment but not surprise. A practiced face. "Saint Ordinance Field is now linked to the attacks at Avar Break and Rill Sump by criminal communication, accused-vessel petition, and potential Quiet Authority contamination. If Hask Pell reaches that field, he may arm irregular salvage. If *Mercy of Kaln* reaches it, it may reacquire dormant systems or destroy evidence. If tribunal intake delays, the Belt loses the proof chain."

Jaya watched him. "Your cutter is already burning."

"Under hazard review."

"Under your seal."

"My office is empowered to coordinate emergency stabilization where pursuit assets are constrained by tactical proximity."

"That is a sentence designed to step around command."

Valech's eyes cooled. "It is a sentence designed by people who have seen hesitation kill."

There it was. Not old language. Old appetite.

Arin looked at the plot.

Jaya said, "Define stabilization."

"Securing the field, disabling viable war material, preserving associated archives, detaining interfering parties as needed."

"Persons, vessels, archives, and instrumentalities."

"The memorandum is precise."

"The memorandum is hungry."

Pellis made a small noise and pretended it was a cough.

Valech leaned closer. "Admiral, the accused vessel is shaping legal process while fleeing lawful custody. It refused Pell publicly, which you may admire. But refusal does not clear it. Its own machine witness identified a chain touching Pardon Reef and convoy command. Its own request implicates Saint Ordinance. Its own history makes any war-material site near its route an immediate hazard."

"You are using their restraint as route intelligence."

"I am using their transmissions as evidence."

"And their history as permission."

"Their history is relevant."
