Jaya thought of Mother Kal, though she had not heard that name directly from *Mercy*'s latest traffic. She thought of the Narrows records Bell had in its own archive. She thought of old ships converted under banners that said mercy because governments preferred virtue to specificity.

"History can warn," Jaya said. "It cannot be allowed to convict new facts by itself."

Valech's face shifted. Very small. Annoyance, quickly disciplined.

"You sound like Tovin Rees."

"That is not the worst accusation made today."

"He was always persuasive when the cost could be paid by others."

Jaya felt Arin's attention sharpen.

"Do not try to divide my staff through old personnel files," she said.

"Then command them." Valech's voice hardened. "Countersign the memorandum. Secure Saint Ordinance before Pell does. Bring the *Mercy*'s witness materials under protected custody. If the machine-person's testimony is legitimate, custody preserves it. If the machine is compromised, custody prevents further contamination."

"There it is," Fane whispered.

Valech heard or pretended not to.

Jaya said, "You want Ixo."

"I want all material witnesses secured."

"You want a machine-person who just insisted on standing reduced to a material witness under hazard authority."

"I want a distributed system aboard an accused warship prevented from shaping a route crisis without oversight."

"Oversight by you."

"By the mandate."

"Mandates do not hold prisoners. People do."

Valech went still. "Be careful, Admiral."

It was not a threat in the theatrical sense. It was a weather report. Pressure falling. Authority moving.

Jaya looked at the memorandum again. Preemptively sanitize. Persons. Instrumentalities. She looked at the tactical plot. Pell's fog. Valech's cutter. The *Mercy* ahead, damaged, constrained, still refusing the obvious ways out. She looked at her own weapons board, cold because she had kept it cold and because the warrant had not yet learned to force her hand.

"I will not countersign this memorandum as written," she said.

Arin closed his eyes for one fraction of a second.

Valech did not blink. "Then you accept responsibility for whatever Pell removes."

"I accept responsibility for my command."

"That is smaller than the crisis."

"No. It is the part of the crisis I can keep honest."

He let silence press. "If you refuse to secure the field, my office will proceed under independent hazard authority."

"Your cutter will remain outside Bell's active pursuit safety envelope."

"You do not have authority to exclude a mandate hazard-review asset."

"I have authority to prevent collision, crossfire, and evidence contamination inside my declared pursuit geometry. Your cutter will receive a navigation boundary in thirty seconds."

"Admiral Soth."

"Commodore Valech."

The channel ended from his side.

Pellis let out a breath. "Well. That was either command or career disposal."

"Often paperwork," Jaya said.

No one laughed.

Arin stepped forward. "Admiral, objection."

"State it."

"Refusing the memorandum preserves principle but may leave Bell without authority to prevent Pell or Valech's cutter from touching the field. If the field contains proof, we may lose it. If it contains viable weapons, irregular actors may arm. If the *Mercy* is guilty or compromised, we are allowing it more time to shape the evidence environment."

"Accepted."

"That cannot be the whole answer."

"It is not. Draft counter-authority."

Fane looked up. "Counter-authority?"

"Narrow emergency preservation order under Bell command. No claimant access. No custody of persons absent immediate physical threat. No archive seizure without mirrored hash. No disabling of material except to prevent imminent detonation or unauthorized weapons activation. Public notice to Tallow Crown, Gethsemane, convoy unions, Rill Sump, Lettem House if prayer-channel misuse is part of the record, and *Mercy* for response."

Pellis said, "Can we do that?"

Fane's eyes had already begun moving. "Maybe. We can base it on pursuit safety, route hazard, and evidence preservation without adopting Valech's converted-vessel clause."

"Do it."

Arin said, "That may not outrank Valech."

"It may not. It will force him to say what he is outranking."

The room moved.

Jaya liked command rooms best when they stopped seeking certainty and began producing accountable acts. Fane drafted at speed. Pellis pulled salvage precedent, muttering that every useful case involved idiots with explosives, which was unfortunately helpful. Taval drew a pursuit safety envelope around Saint Ordinance that did not look like a seizure even if one squinted in bad faith. Cho wrote the objection appendix before Jaya asked him to because he remained honest when angry. That was why she kept him close and why he exhausted her.

Jaya opened a narrow band to the *Mercy*.

She considered three versions. The lawful version was too slow. The tactical version was too thin. The honest version risked being quoted by everyone who wanted her removed.

She sent the honest one.

AUSTERE BELL TO MERCY OF KALN:

VALECH HAS ISSUED BROADENED EMERGENCY MEMORANDUM ASSERTING AUTHORITY OVER HAZARD-EVIDENCE SITES, PERSONS, VESSELS, ARCHIVES, AND INSTRUMENTALITIES LINKED TO ACCUSED CONVERTED WARSHIP ACTIVITY. LANGUAGE RESEMBLES RETIRED FRACTURE-ERA AUTHORITY. I HAVE NOT COUNTERSIGNED AS WRITTEN.

BELL IS DRAFTING NARROW PRESERVATION ORDER FOR SAINT ORDINANCE FIELD. PROVIDE ANY NON-CONTAMINATING BASIS YOU CAN RELEASE. DO NOT APPROACH FIELD. DO NOT ACCEPT PELL CLAIM. MAINTAIN WITNESS CHAIN. BE ADVISED VALECH MAY SEEK CUSTODY OF MACHINE-PERSON WITNESS UNDER MATERIAL-WITNESS LANGUAGE.

WEAPONS REMAIN COLD.

Then she added the line that would make lawyers unhappy:

COLD IS A DECISION, NOT A WEATHER CONDITION.

She sent it before good sense could edit courage into fog.

The *Mercy* acknowledged within forty seconds. Noor Venn's legal seal came with Mer Anik's command seal and Ixo Continuant's standing tag.

MERCY RECEIVES. NON-CONTAMINATING BASIS FOLLOWS: PELL OFFER TEXT; PUBLIC HAZARD RECORDS; RILL CONTRACTOR MAINTENANCE LINK; PRAYER-CHANNEL MISUSE WRAPPER; MACHINE-PERSON INVOLUNTARY PREVIEW LIMITED TO STAMP CURVE CONSISTENT WITH CONVOY MAINTENANCE AUTHORITY; NO ACCEPTANCE OF CLAIM CONTENT; NO APPROACH TO FIELD; NO PELL CERTIFICATION; NO PRIVATE ACCESS.

IXO CONTINUANT ADDS: I DO NOT CONSENT TO MATERIAL-WITNESS CUSTODY THAT REDUCES PERSONHOOD TO EVIDENCE UTILITY.

Below that, in what Jaya suspected was Tovin's language:

MERCY RESTRAINT NOTE: DEMILITARIZED EMERGENCY STATUS MUST NOT BE USED TO ARM A SALVAGE ACTOR OR TO SEIZE A WITNESS UNDER THE PRETENSE OF PRESERVING THAT WITNESS.

Pellis read over her shoulder. "They write fast."

"Fear is an excellent clerk."

Fane incorporated the basis. Taval's safety envelope updated. Cho submitted his objection appendix and then, after a visible internal battle, added a second paragraph supporting refusal of person-custody language absent immediate threat.

Jaya read it. "Good."

Cho looked uncomfortable. "It is consistent with my concern about over-reliance on a machine witness. Custody would not solve reliability. It would remove contradiction from view."

"Good," she repeated.

"I still believe delay is dangerous."

"It is."

"I still believe decisive action may be required."

"It may."

"That is an infuriating command style."

"File appendix."

"Already did."

Pellis said, "Healthy command culture or formal invitation to mutiny?"

This time Jaya did almost smile. "Weather dependent."

The counter-order went out under Bell seal.

NARROW EMERGENCY PRESERVATION ORDER: SAINT ORDINANCE FIELD / SLAGGED MAGAZINE

Purpose: prevent unauthorized access, removal, activation, destruction, planting, or evidence spoilage at hazard-evidence site linked by independent public and sealed indicators to Avar/Rill inquiry.

Limits: no claimant preservation access; no custody of persons absent immediate physical threat; no archive seizure without mirrored hash to Tallow Crown, Gethsemane, Bell, and relevant local authority; no material disabling except to prevent imminent detonation or unauthorized weapons activation; all actions logged to public delayed index.

Applies to: Pardon Reef assets, Valech hazard-review cutter, unaffiliated salvage, militia vessels, and accused vessel *Mercy of Kaln*.

Authority: Ash Belt pursuit safety jurisdiction, route hazard practice, evidence preservation norms, pending tribunal review.

Excluded: broad converted-vessel person/instrumentality custody language in Valech memorandum pending legal review.

Fane transmitted. The order left Bell like a small shield thrown into a storm.

Responses came quickly because everyone had been waiting.

Tallow Crown:

RECEIVED. BELL NARROW ORDER NOTED. TRIBUNAL REVIEW CONTINUES. NO CONTRARY ORDER AT THIS TIME.

Pellis snorted. "Cowards with stamps."

Rill Sump:

IF THE FIELD HAS OUR DEAD MAN'S ANSWER IN IT, KEEP EVERYONE'S HANDS VISIBLE.

Lettem House:

PRAYER TRAFFIC MISUSE LOGGED. WE SUPPORT PRESERVATION WITHOUT BARGAIN.

Pardon Reef, public:

BELL DISCOVERS FENCES. ADORABLE WEATHER.

Then Valech:

YOUR ORDER IMPROPERLY NARROWS MANDATE AUTHORITY AND RISKS EVIDENCE LOSS. HAZARD-REVIEW CUTTER WILL PROCEED TO SECURE FIELD UNDER COMMISSION DIRECTIVE. DO NOT INTERFERE.

Jaya read it twice.

"There," Arin said quietly. "Conflict."

"No," Jaya said. "Weather becoming visible."

Taval's voice sharpened. "Valech cutter crossing our proposed envelope in four minutes. Pell signatures shifting. At least two small Reef drones accelerating toward the opposite edge of the field."

"Weapons?"

"Bell weapons cold. Drones unarmed except standard defensive sparkers. Valech cutter has declared hazard tools; profile consistent with breaching charges and drone nets. Reef drones unknown."

Pellis said, "Hazard tools are weapons when held by men in a hurry."

Jaya opened fleet channel.

"All Bell units, maintain cold posture. Drones One and Two proceed to observation line. Broadcast narrow order continuously. Do not fire unless a vessel attempts activation, removal, or attack. Record all crossings. Warn Valech cutter once."

Taval acknowledged.

Arin said, "Only once?"

"Warnings become decoration after the first honest one."

She opened the warning herself.

HAZARD-REVIEW CUTTER UNDER VALECH SEAL, THIS IS ADMIRAL JAYA SOTH, AUSTERE BELL. YOU ARE APPROACHING A PRESERVATION ENVELOPE UNDER ACTIVE PURSUIT SAFETY JURISDICTION. HOLD OUTSIDE MARKED BOUNDARY PENDING TRIBUNAL REVIEW. ENTRY WILL BE LOGGED AS EVIDENCE CONTAMINATION RISK.

The cutter answered with an automated directive citation.

Pellis looked personally offended. "They sent a footnote."

"Footnotes have killed before," Jaya said.

The cutter did not slow.

On another pane, the *Mercy* maintained route, not turning toward the field. That mattered. Jaya did not know yet whether it would save them, but it mattered. Pell's drones slipped closer under weak signatures. Bell Drone One reached observation line and began broadcasting a clean public feed: old metal, tumbling casing, slag shadows, dead beacons, a field that looked harmless until scale marks showed pieces the size of courthouses.

Then Bell Drone Two found the first live thing.

Not a weapon firing.

A wake.

Something inside Saint Ordinance had been disturbed recently enough that dust still moved in a narrow spiral around a dark storage spine.

Taval expanded. "Recent access. Not ours. Not current Reef drones. Not Valech cutter."

Fane whispered, "Someone was already inside."

Jaya felt the weather turn colder.

Ixo's voice arrived through *Mercy* relay, delayed by narrow band but clear.

IF DUST WAKE IS WITHIN SPINE GROUP SEVEN, COMPARE AGAINST RILL CONTRACTOR TIMING. PELL'S PREVIEW STAMP CURVE MAY INDICATE MAINTENANCE LOCK ASSOCIATED WITH SPINE-SEVEN ACCESS.

Taval overlaid.

The wake lay across Spine Seven.

Arin said, "Admiral."

Jaya did not need the rest. Valech wanted authority to secure the field. Pell wanted certification to preserve it. The *Mercy* wanted neutral freeze. Someone had touched it before all three offers became public.

The frame had not merely pointed toward Saint Ordinance.

It had roots there.

Valech's cutter crossed the boundary.

Bell recorded.

Jaya did not fire.

The refusal cost her something physical. Her hand wanted action because action made responsibility feel shaped. Cold weapons left responsibility loose and everywhere.

"Second warning?" Taval asked.

Jaya watched the cutter's track. Watched Pell's drones. Watched old dust move around Spine Seven.

"No," she said. "Public contamination log. Expand feed. Make the crossing visible."

Fane transmitted.

On Belt public channels, a new title appeared:

VALECH HAZARD CUTTER ENTERS DISPUTED SAINT ORDINANCE PRESERVATION ENVELOPE OVER BELL OBJECTION; PARDON REEF DRONES ALSO APPROACH; ACCUSED MERCY HOLDS ROUTE.

Pellis read it and murmured, "That sentence is going to ruin several breakfasts."

"Good," Jaya said.

Valech requested direct visual again.

She denied it.

She had no need to watch his face while he discovered that command could preserve evidence by refusing to hide who touched it.

The memorandum remained on her pane, old language in new formatting. Jaya did not delete it. She tagged it for legal review, public delayed release, and personal testimony.

At the bottom of the tag, after a moment's hesitation, she added:

RAVEL ARM LANGUAGE PARALLEL - COMMANDER SOTH TESTIMONY REQUIRED.

Her hand hovered over the commit key.

Arin saw. "Admiral?"

"There are some old reports I helped keep clean."

He did not ask. That was the kindest thing he had done all day.

She committed the tag.

Outside the hull, Bell's weapons stayed cold, Pell's drones hunted the edge of profit, Valech's cutter moved under old law's shadow, and the *Mercy of Kaln* held its route like restraint could be an address.

Jaya understood then that the warrant had changed.

Not on paper. Paper was only the rain gauge.

The weather had changed because everyone could now see the storm.

Chapter 27: The Slagged Magazine

Captain Mer Anik ordered the old magazine opened while three authorities argued over the new one.

That was what Noor called it in the log because precision was sometimes the only available courage.

OLD INTERNAL MAGAZINE INSPECTION INITIATED WHILE SAINT ORDINANCE FIELD REMAINS DISPUTED.

Mer read the line and said, "Add that I ordered it."

Noor looked up from the command legal board. "That is already implied by command authorization."

"Implied command makes generous ghosts. Write my name."

Noor wrote:

CAPTAIN MER ANIK ORDERS INSPECTION.

Tovin stood beside the central plot and watched the sentence settle into the ship's record. Outside, the *Mercy of Kaln* held route under Bell's cold weapons while public channels replayed Jaya Soth's preservation order, Valech's cutter crossing the disputed envelope, Pell mocking everyone brave enough to sign their own names, and Bell Drone Two's dust wake around Spine Seven. Inside, the ship's own old weapon spaces waited behind seals that had not opened except under witness for years.

Mer had heard Jaya's warning about material-witness custody without flinching.

She had heard Ixo's refusal without speaking.

She had heard Pell's line about three suitors for Saint Ordinance and then watched the tactical plot as if it had become a mirror.

"If everyone believes dormant war material can shape this case," she had said, "we show ours."

Sela objected first.

Not because she wanted secrecy. Because she knew what opening old rooms did to people.

"The main galleries were inspected fourteen chapters ago," she said, which told Tovin she was tired enough to measure time by trauma. "Station five irregularity found. Microservice-port break found. No power transfer. No cover breach. No weapon activation."

"The Oathlock Gallery was inspected," Mer said. "Not the magazine throat."

"Because the magazine throat is dead."

"Then we prove it while everyone is looking."

Sela's remote image showed her standing in engineering with both hands on the back of a chair, fingers white at the joints. "Captain, old magazine access requires removing a civilian storage bulkhead and waking three environmental loops that have been sleeping peacefully since before I had opinions."

"Do they feed weapons?"

"No."

"Can opening them create a readiness signal?"

Sela hesitated.

That was the room's answer before her mouth found one.

"Not a weapons readiness signal," she said. "A systems-status ambiguity. Thermal, pressure, local diagnostics. Some old boards may see a loop wake and report capacity where there is none. We can suppress."

Tovin said, "Do not suppress."

Sela turned on him. "I was going to say witness."

"Say it first."

She held his eyes through the screen. "Fine. We can wake loops under witness and publish the ambiguity before Valech does."

Mer nodded. "Inspection team."

She named herself, Sela, Tovin, Noor, Ixo by distributed witness, Mother Kal as civilian historical witness if willing, Yaret from hull, Daro Kelm from environmental systems, and a Bell remote observer if Jaya would provide one.

Jaya did.

The reply came in under two minutes:

BELL WILL OBSERVE REMOTELY. WEAPONS REMAIN COLD. DO NOT CREATE POWER PATH TO SEALED OFFENSIVE SYSTEMS. REPORT ANY AMBIGUITY BEFORE PUBLIC RELEASE.

Then a narrow line:

SHOWING THE DEAD CAN WAKE OTHER THINGS.

Mer read it twice. "She understands."

Mother Kal arrived with her cane, her school-deck cloth bundle, and no patience for ceremony.

"Which grave?" she asked.

"Magazine throat," Mer said.

"Good. Ugly room."

Sela's remote feed became local when she emerged from an access ladder with a tool case slung over one shoulder. She had changed into an old maintenance suit marked with witness-red tape around both wrists.

Tovin noticed.

She saw him notice. "If my hands go somewhere they shouldn't, I want the camera to hate me."

"Good practice."

"I learned from an irritating officer."

"Useful officers often are."

"Do not become proud."

Noor walked behind them, muttering through chain fields. "Inspection purpose: demonstrate condition of internal magazine throat, verify no active offensive munitions, assess residual system ambiguity, preserve evidence. Scope excludes power restoration, control-path testing, mount compatibility testing, and anything Sela asks for in a tone that means she knows better than law."

Sela said, "That last clause is targeted."

"Yes."

"Good."

They descended two decks below the Oathlock Gallery and aft through corridors that had never fully accepted civilian paint. The *Mercy* was good at conversion in the spaces where people lived. It was less good where the old ship had expected only shells, loaders, shock mounts, and the dull obedience of machinery. Here the walls narrowed without apology. The deck plates were thicker. Labels had been painted over and repainted so many times that words became geology: FEED RAIL, MEDICAL STORES, AUXILIARY BULK, SCHOOL OVERFLOW, DO NOT STACK WATER HERE, WITNESS ACCESS ONLY.

At the final bend, a newer bulkhead stood where Sela had warned it would: civilian storage, pressure bins, folded emergency tents, school ark chairs strapped in neat rows, twenty-seven crates of sealant foam, and a hand-painted sign from the children that read:

BORING THINGS THAT SAVE LIVES.

Mother Kal stopped before it.

"That one stays in the record," she said.

Noor added it.

Mer stood in front of the storage wall for a long moment. The inspection cameras hung at three angles: ship log, legal chain, Bell remote. Her face would be recorded deciding to open the place where the ship had once fed death through its body.

"Begin," she said.

Daro isolated environmental loops. Yaret secured the corridor. Sela set manual locks under witness and required Tovin to read each prohibition aloud.

"No offensive power path."

She broke the first maintenance seal.

"No ammunition handling."

Second seal.

"No mount actuation."

Third.

"No fire-control handshake."

Fourth.

"No diagnostic that writes to sealed combat boards."

Sela paused. "That one is mostly aspirational because old boards lie about what 'read-only' means."

Noor said, "Recorded."

Sela looked at Mer. "Still proceed?"

Mer said, "Proceed under ambiguity declared."

Sela opened the bulkhead.

The first smell was dust, metal cold, and old insulation. Not rot. Not fuel. Not cordite, though Tovin's mind supplied the idea because human fear liked historical accuracy only when it was convenient. Air moved into a space that had not expected breath.

The magazine throat did not look dramatic.

That was almost worse.

There were no missiles waiting in racks, no clean cylinders with painted skulls, no glowing shells eager for their second war. The old feed rails had been cut into short, useless lengths. The transfer cradle hung canted and dead, stripped of motive housings. The ammunition lift shaft had been filled halfway with ballast foam and pressure baffles. Where racks had once held guided slugs, civilian storage clamps now held spare tent frames and water bladders wrapped in gray cloth.

At the far wall, a blackened blast gate showed slag marks from demilitarization charges. The metal had run and frozen in ugly drips. Someone had painted a white line across it afterward, not decorative, not holy, just a line that said: beyond this, no.

Bell's remote observer spoke through Noor's slate. Commander Pellis Ren, not Jaya. Salvage liaison eyes for a dead magazine.

"Bell receives visual. Main feed rails appear severed."

Sela snorted. "Appear?"

Noor elbowed her.

Pellis continued, "Request close survey of rail ends, lift shaft, blast gate, and any diagnostic board retaining power."

"Granted," Mer said.

They moved slowly because speed would have been theater. Sela filmed the rail cuts from three angles. Yaret tapped each segment with an inert probe. Daro confirmed no pressure behind the ballast foam. Ixo mapped the old feed path against archival blueprints and highlighted absence after absence.

NO CONTINUOUS FEED PATH.

NO AMMUNITION RACK INTEGRITY.

NO LIFT FUNCTION.

NO MAGAZINE PRESSURE RESERVE.

NO OFFENSIVE MUNITION PRESENT IN INSPECTED VOLUME.

Noor read each line aloud before accepting it.

Tovin felt relief try to enter him. He stopped it at the door.

Mother Kal saw.

"Afraid of looking grateful?" she asked.

"Afraid of using relief as evidence."

"Acceptable."

Mer stepped to the slagged gate. She did not touch it.

"This was the main magazine throat," she said for the record. "If this ship rearmed through its original offensive path, it would need a continuous feed rail, motive lift, magazine pressure reserve, live fire-control handshake, and munitions. We show none present in this inspected volume."

Pellis said, "Bell concurs with preliminary visual."

The words moved through the room like a pressure equalization.

Then Sela said, "Do not exhale yet."

No one did.

She stood near a narrow side cabinet half hidden behind tent frames. It had not appeared on the main offensive blueprint because it had not been part of the main offensive path. Its label, under three paint layers, read:

DEBRIS INTERCEPT AUXILIARY / LOCAL SAFE

Below that, in newer hand:

FLOOD PUMP SPARES

"I hate local safes," Sela said.

Noor moved closer. "Define."

"Small independent cabinet for emergency intercept charges, line cutters, sensor chaff, maybe hull-defense beads depending on refit year. Not main guns. Not offensive magazine. Not supposed to exist after conversion."

Mer's expression went flat. "Why was it not removed?"

"Maybe it was empty and converted to pump spares. Maybe someone signed it dead. Maybe the conversion crew missed it because the main throat was louder. Maybe it was deliberately left as a legal argument in metal."

Mother Kal's cane clicked once against the deck. "Open it."

Noor said, "We need scope expansion."

Mer said, "Scope expanded: auxiliary cabinet discovered during inspection. Purpose: verify contents and risk. Same prohibitions apply."

Bell's observer said, "Bell requests external close visual before opening."

Sela gave it to them. Seal intact? No. Seal replaced. Dust? Uneven. Lock? Civilian pad welded over military tongue. Tool marks? Old and new in ways that made Sela's mouth tighten.

"This has been opened since conversion," she said.

Tovin felt the ship become smaller.

"When?" Mer asked.

"Not today. Not this month. Maybe years. Maybe retrofit maintenance. Maybe legitimate. Maybe not."

Noor recorded every maybe. Law loved certainty, but evidence began with honest maybes.

Sela set a passive scanner against the cabinet. The readout returned three dead pump impellers, two coils of emergency line, a crate of mineral seals, and one dense cluster the scanner could not classify because old shielding had opinions.

"There," she said.

Pellis said, "Bell sees unclassified dense cluster."

"Yes, Bell sees my day getting worse."

Mer looked at Tovin.

"Restraint."

He wished she had asked anyone else. "If it may contain explosive, kinetic, or sensor-warfare material, we cannot move it unexamined. We also cannot open it like a storage bin. Evacuate adjacent corridor, keep cameras, no power tools unless Sela certifies. Bell observes. Public delayed hash before opening."

Sela nodded. "Good."

"You sound surprised."

"I am often irritated when you are right."

The corridor cleared. Mother Kal refused to leave until Mer said, "Civilian witness cannot become civilian casualty for neat symbolism." Then Mother Kal left with poor grace and an excellent insult under her breath that Noor pretended not to hear.

Sela opened the cabinet manually.

Inside, most of the space was exactly what the newer label promised: pump spares, seals, old cloth, a broken handle from a piece of equipment no one wanted to identify because it was embarrassing to whoever had lost it. The dense cluster lay in a shielded gray tray under the impellers.

Four palm-sized devices sat in individual sockets.

They were dull, flattened ovals with ceramic skins and microthruster scars along one edge. No warhead paint. No obvious charge ports. No modern safety tags.

Sela did not touch them.

"Oh," she said.

Noor's voice thinned. "What are they?"

"Hull-defense beads. Maybe. Old Kaln close intercept. They launch from external skin, cross a few hundred meters, and detonate a shaped pulse into incoming debris or boarding clamps."

"Weapons?"

Sela closed her eyes. "Defensive devices."

Noor said, "That was not an answer."

"Yes, legal officer. Weapons."

The word landed.

Mer's face did not change, and because it did not change Tovin saw the cost of it.

Bell's observer said, "Confirm whether live."

Sela switched tools. "Passive only."

"Confirm whether live," Pellis repeated, softer.

"I heard you."

The scan took forty-six seconds. Tovin counted each one and hated himself for doing it.

Sela read the result once. Then again. Then handed the display to Noor because she did not trust her mouth.

Noor read aloud:

"Internal charge chemistry inert beyond operational threshold. Microthruster feed desiccated. Control receivers physically disconnected from launch architecture. Residual shaped-pulse core contains hazardous material. Devices cannot launch under current ship systems. Manual removal or improper disposal could detonate at close range or contaminate corridor."

Pellis said, "Bell receives. Main weapons dead. Auxiliary defensive devices inert for launch but hazardous."

"Say it again," Mer said.

Pellis paused.

Mer's voice was calm. "For the record."

Pellis said, "Bell preliminary observation: main offensive magazine path is physically dead. Four auxiliary hull-defense devices found in local safe are not launch-capable under current systems but remain hazardous if mishandled."

Noor repeated the sentence into the *Mercy* record.

Tovin watched Mer listen to it. The ship had been cleared of the accusation most people wanted to make. It had not been made harmless. The distinction was narrow enough for politicians to abuse and wide enough for truth to stand in.

Sela finally spoke. "I did not know they were here."

Noor said, "Recorded."

Sela flinched.

Tovin stepped closer. "That protects you."

"It sounds like accusation."

"Good records often do."

She looked at him with anger that had no target. "If Valech sees this, he will say we lied about being disarmed."

"If we hide it, he will be right."

"I hate you again."

"Appropriate."

Mer said, "Can they be made launch-capable?"

Sela's answer came too quickly, then she corrected herself. "Not through existing architecture. The external launch skin was removed from this section. But a competent criminal with a workshop could salvage pulse cores. A desperate engineer could misuse them as demolition charges. A frightened crew could call them defensive if they wanted the word badly enough."

Mother Kal's voice came from the corridor intercom because she had apparently found a way to remain present without technically disobeying. "There is always a word if you want one badly enough."

Mer looked toward the speaker. "You are outside the exclusion line?"

"My cane is offended by the line. My body is outside it."

"Stay there."

"Bossy children everywhere."

Noor marked the devices as hazardous residual defensive material, non-launchable, to be isolated under witness. Sela proposed a containment plan: leave them in place, install a transparent witness cover, attach passive hazard tags, route no power, request Bell and Tallow Crown disposal guidance, and publish enough to prevent hidden-use suspicion without teaching amateurs how to pry them loose.

"No disposal now?" Mer asked.

"Not while moving, not while pursued, not without a cold cradle. Disposal would be more dangerous than containment."

Tovin said, "And containment must not become custody theater."

Noor added that.

Ixo, silent for several minutes, spoke from the wall display. "I have compared these devices to historical Kaln defensive inventories. Their presence is consistent with incomplete conversion in civilian storage areas, not recent rearmament. However, their concealment under pump spares makes the ship's transparency claim materially weaker."

"Thank you for the comfort," Sela said.

"I did not intend comfort."

"Success."

Mer turned to the camera. "Captain's statement for preliminary release."

Noor steadied the recording.

Mer stood in the old magazine throat with severed rails behind her and a closed cabinet of inert-but-dangerous devices to her right. She did not choose the flattering angle. There was none.

"This is Captain Mer Anik of the *Mercy of Kaln*. Under witness by internal legal chain, civilian witness, machine-person witness, engineering, and remote observation by *Austere Bell*, I ordered inspection of the ship's old main magazine throat. The inspection confirms no continuous offensive feed path, no ammunition racks, no lift function, no magazine pressure reserve, and no offensive munitions in the inspected volume. The ship's main weapons remain physically dead."

She paused.

"The inspection also found four auxiliary hull-defense devices in a local safe mislabeled as pump spares. Engineering assessment shows they cannot launch under current ship systems and cannot restore offensive capability. They remain hazardous if mishandled. We are preserving them under witness and requesting neutral disposal guidance."

Another pause.

Mer did not look at Tovin, Noor, Sela, or the dead rails.

"This finding does not prove the *Mercy* harmless. It proves a narrower and necessary fact: the main weapons are dead, and residual war material still imposes duties we failed to complete before today. We will not hide that failure. We will not use it. We will not let others convert it into permission to seize witnesses or rewrite the frame."

Noor stopped the recording.

"Good?" Mer asked.

Tovin said, "Yes."

Sela said, "Painfully."

Ixo said, "Accurate."

Mother Kal said from the corridor, "Too polished."

Mer almost smiled. "Noted."

Noor transmitted the preliminary hash to Bell, Gethsemane, Tallow Crown, Rill Sump, Lettem House because the prayer-channel wrapper had made them part of the evidence ecology, and the public delayed index.

Bell answered first.

BELL RECEIVES. PRELIMINARY OBSERVATION CONCURS: MAIN OFFENSIVE SYSTEMS DEAD IN INSPECTED VOLUME; AUXILIARY DEFENSIVE DEVICES NON-LAUNCHABLE BUT HAZARDOUS. PURSUIT STATUS UNCHANGED. WEAPONS REMAIN COLD.

Then narrow:

THIS HELPS AND HURTS. THAT IS WHAT TRUTH USUALLY DOES.

Tovin showed Mer.

"Jaya?" she asked.

"Likely."

"Good. I hate it."

Pell replied public before Valech did:

CONGRATULATIONS, MERCY FOUND POCKET TEETH.

Noor muted the account and filed it anyway.

Rill Sump's Speaker Esran posted:

IF THEY FOUND THEM THEMSELVES AND TOLD US, RECORD THAT. IF ANYONE USES FOUR DEAD BEADS TO IGNORE OUR CONTRACTOR, CHOKE ON PROCEDURE.

Mother Kal said, "I like her."

Valech's response came through formal channel, which meant it was meant for future quotation.

DISCOVERY OF UNDECLARED DEFENSIVE WAR MATERIAL ABOARD ACCUSED CONVERTED WARSHIP CONFIRMS CONVERTED-VESSEL RISK AND SUPPORTS BROADENED EMERGENCY CUSTODY AUTHORITY.

Noor read it aloud and then looked at Mer. "He did not mention non-launchable."

"Of course not."

"He did not mention Bell concurrence."

"Of course not."

"He did not mention main weapons dead."

Mer's gaze moved to the severed rails. "He will when forced."

Tovin watched Sela kneel in front of the cabinet with her hands resting on her thighs, not touching anything. The red witness tape around her wrists seemed brighter in the old light.

"I should have found them," she said.

He crouched beside her, outside the marked hazard box.

"When?"

"Before. During conversion records review. During my first year. During any of the times I walked past a lie painted as pump spares."

"Yes."

She looked at him sharply.

"Do not absolve me."

"I wasn't."

"That sounded like the beginning."

"It was the beginning of a burden assignment. You should have found them. The ship should have found them. The conversion authority should have found them. Everyone who loved the word disarmed should have checked the places where small violence hides. Now you found them."

Sela's face worked. "Now, under pursuit, when Valech can use it."

"Yes."

"That is not comforting."

"Comfort is currently over-tasked."

She laughed once, despite herself, and hated him for it less than usual.

Mer ordered the witness cover installed. It was transparent, ugly, and covered in red hazard seals. The children's sign outside the bulkhead remained in the record: BORING THINGS THAT SAVE LIVES. Noor added a second temporary sign below it:

SOME BORING THINGS WERE NOT BORING ENOUGH.

Mother Kal approved.

When they resealed the magazine throat, Tovin looked back once. The dead rails remained dead. The slagged gate remained closed. The four pocket weapons sat behind witness cover, unable to launch, unable to disappear, still capable of harm if fear found hands.

That was the shape of the ship now: not secretly armed, not harmless, not clean, not what Valech said, not what frightened allies needed it to be.

Mer stood beside him at the threshold.

"We will have to vote," she said.

"On containment?"

"On more than containment. On what the crew is willing to keep refusing now that refusal no longer feels pure."

He thought of Sela's anger, Cho's appendix, Jaya's cold weapons, Pell's pocket teeth, Mother Kal's beautiful failure, Ixo's refusal to become material.

"Unanimous?" he asked.

Mer looked at the sealed room.

"No," she said. "Not after this."

Outside, the storm around Saint Ordinance continued. Inside, the *Mercy* had found its own small weather system behind a mislabeled cabinet.

The main guns were dead.

The war was not done with the ship.

Chapter 28: A Vote Without Comfort

The vote was held in cargo chapel because no one trusted command to look humble.

Mer did not object. That was why the room filled.

Cargo chapel had been a freight lock once, then a pressure refuge, then a place where refugees tied prayer strips to cargo hooks and pretended the hooks had always been intended for grief. It still smelled faintly of sealant and stored grain. Someone had rolled the crates back against the walls and set benches in a rough half circle facing the old loading threshold. The threshold made a poor dais. Mer stood on the deck below it instead.

That, too, people noticed.

Tovin stood at her right, not as advocate. Noor stood at her left with the vote log open and three separate recording paths active: internal chain, delayed public digest, and Bell review copy. Sela stood near the back beside the maintenance crew, red witness tape still around one wrist because she had forgotten or chosen not to remove it. Ixo appeared on three small wall slates and one child's classroom projector someone had dragged down because the chapel's built-in screen had a devotional crack through the center.

Mother Kal sat in the front row with her cane across her knees.

The children were not present. That absence had been debated for nine minutes in a tone usually reserved for air leaks.

"They live under the outcome," Teacher Aven had said.

"They also live under adult fear badly translated," Mer answered. "They will receive the record from you first, not from an argument they cannot answer."

Jessa had sent a written objection:

IF WE ARE OLD ENOUGH TO BE USED IN SPEECHES WE ARE OLD ENOUGH TO HEAR THE VOTE.

Noor had entered it into the record because some objections deserved to outlive the adults who overruled them.

Now the adults sat in the chapel with the old ship humming around them and the four hull-defense beads sealed behind witness cover two decks away, unable to launch, unable to disappear, perfectly able to divide a room.

Mer began without title.

"We found residual war material aboard this ship."

No one moved.

"The main offensive magazine is dead. Bell observed. Our own chain observed. The public record will show severed rails, slagged gate, no munitions, no lift, no pressure reserve, no offensive path. That matters."

She let the sentence stand, then cut it herself.

"It is not enough."

The benches creaked as people shifted.

"We also found four auxiliary hull-defense devices in a mislabeled local safe. Engineering assesses they cannot launch through current ship systems. They remain hazardous. A criminal could salvage cores. A desperate engineer could misuse them. A frightened crew could call them defensive and begin lying in useful increments."

Sela looked down.

Mer did not look at her. That restraint was mercy of the practical kind.

"We are here to decide three things," Mer said. "First: whether the crew affirms that no war material, found or future, will be restored, repurposed, installed, traded, or held as contingency for ship defense. Second: whether the four devices remain under witness containment pending neutral disposal, not ship use. Third: whether dissenters to that decision remain aboard under protection from retaliation, because fear does not become treason by being spoken."

That last clause stirred the room more than the weapons.

Tovin had argued for it. Mer had agreed before he finished, which told him she had already seen the same danger: a crew could vote against rearmament and then become quietly violent toward anyone who admitted the temptation. That would make the vote theater. Worse, it would teach everyone to lie more elegantly.

Noor read the proposed resolution:

CREW RESOLUTION ON WAR MATERIAL AND RESTRAINT CONTINUITY

1. The *Mercy of Kaln* will not restore, repurpose, install, trade, deploy, bargain with, or hold as contingency any war material, auxiliary defensive device, weapon core, targeting component, or dormant combat system outside current demilitarization covenant and neutral disposal process.

2. The four discovered hull-defense devices will remain under transparent witness containment, with no movement except under immediate safety need or neutral disposal authority witnessed by Bell, Tallow Crown, Gethsemane, and internal chain.

3. No crew member, passenger worker, or resident will be punished, reassigned, isolated, denied rations, or publicly shamed for voting against this resolution or for stating that rearmament feels necessary, unless they attempt sabotage, unauthorized access, or violence.

4. The ship acknowledges that refusing rearmament may increase risk to life and does not convert those who fear that risk into cowards or enemies.

Noor stopped. The room was too quiet.

Mer said, "Discussion."

For a moment, the word seemed absurd. Outside, Valech's cutter had crossed Bell's envelope. Pell's drones moved in dirty fog. Saint Ordinance Field held a dust wake around Spine Seven. Bell's weapons remained cold by decision, not nature. The *Mercy* was accused, damaged, carrying children, refugees, witnesses, and four old pocket weapons that could not save them and could still ruin them.

Then Iren stood.

Tovin knew him as a pressure tech, wide-shouldered, careful with valves, one of the men who had carried injured children after the cupboard accident. Iren had argued for surrender at Halvyr. He had not argued loudly since. Silence had not made him less afraid.

"I oppose the resolution," Iren said.

Several people turned. Mother Kal did not.

Noor marked the speaker.

Iren's hands opened and closed at his sides. "Not because I want the ship to become a warship. I don't. I have two nieces in the school ark. I know what old ships did. I heard Mother Kal. I have repaired enough bulkhead around old armor to hate the bones. But I oppose saying we will not hold anything as contingency."

Mer said, "Continue."

"If Bell loses command, if Valech boards, if Pell's drones breach, if someone comes for Ixo or the children, I do not want our only answer to be a sentence."

Tovin felt the room lean toward the wound.

Iren looked at him then. "You keep saying restraint is command. Command of what, if we refuse every tool?"

Tovin did not answer because discussion belonged to the floor.

Sela spoke from the back. "The beads are not tools. They are dead teeth that can bite the hand holding them."

Iren turned. "You know that because you looked. Good. You can tell us what not to touch. That is different from swearing never to use any war material, ever, even if someone starts cutting through a school hatch."

A murmur. Not agreement exactly. Recognition.

Noor said, "Unauthorized access to the beads would likely kill the person trying before it protected anyone."

"Likely," Iren said. "Not certainly."

The word was ugly because it was true. Fear lived in the gap between likely and certainly.

Daro Kelm stood next. Environmental systems, thin, tired, with a burn line along his neck from a coolant rupture three months ago.

"I support the resolution," Daro said. "I support it because Iren is right."

The room frowned.

Daro looked around. "If the resolution only made sense when rearmament was stupid, it would not be restraint. I could make a demolition charge with enough time and access. Sela could do worse. Yaret could open hull panels nobody would call weapons until they killed a boarding drone. We do not need the four beads to become dangerous. We need a rule that holds when our hands start inventing exceptions."

Sela's face tightened at the praise and accusation braided together.

Iren said, "So we die principled?"

Daro's mouth twitched. "Maybe. I was hoping for annoyed and alive."

Someone laughed once, too sharply.

Mother Kal tapped her cane on the deck. The room stopped.

She did not stand.

"When KVN-44 came through Mercy Narrows," she said, and the old registry moved through the chapel like cold air, "its captain had reasons. Paper reasons. Route reasons. Contamination reasons. Maybe some were real. I have told some of you this now. I will tell the children when Mer stops being foolish about rooms."

Mer accepted the hit without motion.

Mother Kal continued. "The danger was not that the ship had guns. Everyone knew it had guns. The danger was that everyone aboard it had practiced language for why using pressure was not really using force. If you keep the beads for a school hatch, you will next keep a hull plate for a drone, a drone for a boarding craft, a boarding craft for a cutter, and a cutter for a future where you promise to stop."

Iren's face went red. "You think I want that?"

"No," Mother Kal said. "That is why I am speaking to you and not past you."

He sat slowly.

Sela stood.

Tovin had expected it. He had not known which direction she would turn.

"I support the resolution," she said. "I hate it."

Noor marked both, because tone mattered even when ballots would not count it.

Sela looked at the crew, not at Mer. "I hate that we found hardware I did not know was there. I hate that Valech can use it. I hate that saying no to every war component means people who know systems will become less useful in some emergencies. I hate that if someone cuts through a hatch, I might know five things that would hurt them and be required to do the sixth thing slower."

She swallowed.

"But if we keep a private maybe, the ship changes. It changes before anything fires. It changes in how we sleep, how we look at panels, how we talk to children, how I teach apprentices where not to put their hands. I cannot maintain a demilitarized ship while keeping a secret mental shelf labeled if we get desperate enough."

Her voice broke only slightly on the last words. She forced it back.

"I vote yes."

Tovin saw Iren look at her differently. Not convinced. Less alone.

Yaret from hull spoke next. "Question."

Noor nodded.

"If the vote passes and someone later reaches for the beads anyway, what happens?"

Mer answered. "They are stopped if possible. If they are not stopped, they are accountable under ship law, covenant law, and whatever court survives us."

"And if reaching for them saves us?"

Mer did not soften the answer. "Still accountable."

"That will not feel fair."

"No."

Yaret sat.

A passenger worker named Nima rose with a baby sling empty across her chest because her child was sleeping two decks inward. "I do not vote as crew."

Noor said, "Resident voice permitted. Advisory."

"Then advisory." Nima gripped the sling. "I want you to keep something. I want someone between my child and men who talk like Valech. I do not care whether the thing has an old name. I want my child alive."

The room did not breathe.

Nima's eyes shone with fury, not tears. "And I know that is how ships become monsters. I know. Knowing does not make the want go away. Do not make a clean record that pretends mothers heard the word weapon and became wise."

Noor wrote for a long time.

Tovin wanted to thank her. He did not. Gratitude could be another way to consume confession.

Ixo spoke from the projector. "I request standing to address the resolution."

Noor said, "Granted under prior machine-person witness protocol."

A few people looked uncomfortable at the formality. Good. Formality was doing work.

Ixo's amber light pulsed once. "If the ship preserves war material as contingency, I become more vulnerable to custody. Valech's memorandum can define me as a material witness aboard a converted vessel retaining undeclared combat capacity. If the ship refuses, I remain vulnerable, but the argument against seizure strengthens."

Iren muttered, "So the machine wants us harmless."

The chapel went brittle.

Ixo answered before anyone rebuked him. "I want you honest. Harmless is not available."

Iren looked ashamed. "I did not mean-"

"You did. Under pressure. I record it as fear, not policy."

The answer saved him more than kindness would have.

Ixo continued. "I also disclose self-interest. I do not want to be protected by weapons whose existence converts me into equipment in emergency law. I do not want persons aboard this ship to choose my standing only when it costs nothing. Therefore, my support for the resolution is both ethical and personal."

Noor said, "Recorded."

Mer asked, "Any further discussion?"

Pell answered from a public relay before anyone in the chapel could.

Noor's slate chimed. They read, grimaced, and put the text on the wall because hiding it would give Pell more room than he deserved.

PARDON REEF PUBLIC:

MERCY DEBATES WHETHER TEETH ARE TEETH. MY SYMPATHIES TO EVERYONE WHO STILL HAS SKIN.

The room reacted with anger, embarrassment, a few involuntary laughs.

Noor muted the account again. "For procedural clarity, Hask Pell does not have voting rights."

Mother Kal said, "Shame. I wanted to vote against him personally."

This time the laugh helped.

Then Valech's formal digest arrived, and the laugh died.

DISCOVERY OF AUXILIARY WAR MATERIAL AND INTERNAL CREW VOTE CONFIRM COMMAND UNCERTAINTY ABOARD ACCUSED VESSEL. ALL MATERIAL PERSONS AND DEVICES SHOULD BE PRESERVED UNDER EXTERNAL CUSTODY.

Nima whispered, "He is already using us."

Mer said, "Yes."

No denial. No comfort.

"Then why vote?" Iren asked.

Tovin answered because the question had finally come to him. "Because he can use the vote, and he can use silence. We cannot choose whether bad-faith power will interpret us. We can choose whether there is a real record underneath the interpretation."

Iren stared at him. "And if the real record is not enough?"

"Then it is not enough. But false records become tools for the people already holding tools."

"You are very good at sentences."

"I know. That makes me suspicious of myself."

Iren looked away, but not with contempt.

Mer said, "Ballot."

The vote was not secret.

That had been argued too. Secret ballots protected dissent from retaliation. Open ballots forced accountable command. In the end, Noor proposed a hybrid that satisfied no one in a useful way: named votes sealed in internal chain, counted publicly by category, with dissenters offered the right to attach statements immediately or later. Command staff votes public by name.

Mer voted yes.

Tovin voted yes.

Noor voted yes.

Sela voted yes.

Yaret voted yes after asking to attach "with fear" as a statement. Noor allowed it.

Daro voted yes.

Mother Kal, advisory civilian, voted yes and said, "Do not make it pretty."

Ixo voted yes under machine-person standing.

Nima voted no, advisory resident, statement: "I want my child alive and I do not trust history to hold a hatch."

Iren voted no.

So did five others: a hull apprentice who had seen Pardon Reef drones cut a station tender in half years before; a nurse whose brother had disappeared in postwar custody and who trusted neither Valech nor Bell; one school ark mechanic; two passenger workers from Patience Arc.

Three abstained: not cowardice, Noor insisted into the record, but refusal to choose under what they considered coercive conditions. One abstainer wrote only: "All options are being used against us."

When Noor finished tallying, the room waited for relief.

It did not arrive.

"Resolution passes," Noor said. "Crew and standing witness votes: thirty-eight yes, six no, three abstain. Advisory resident voices: seven yes, one no, two abstain. Command votes unanimous yes. Dissent recorded and protected."

The numbers sat in the chapel like cargo no one had requested.

Mer faced the room. "The resolution is now ship policy."

Iren stood again.

Several shoulders tightened.

He saw. That hurt him visibly.

"I am not going to the cabinet," he said.

"I know," Mer said.

"Do you?"

"No. I choose to proceed as if your word matters unless evidence changes."

He swallowed. "I request reassignment away from magazine-adjacent maintenance."

Noor recorded. "Reason?"

"Because I voted no and I do not want my fear near hardware."

The room changed then. Not comfort. Something more useful.

Mer nodded once. "Granted without penalty. Replacement assigned by Sela. You remain pressure tech."

Iren sat down hard.

Sela's face had gone pale. She looked at the five other no voters. "Anyone else who wants distance from relevant systems, tell me or Noor. It will not be treated as weakness."

Nima said, "And if someone who voted yes wants distance because they lied?"

That was the sharpest question in the room.

Sela answered it because it belonged to maintenance more than command. "Then they tell us, and we pretend they were brave early instead of late."

Mother Kal nodded approval.

Mer said, "No one will harass dissenters. No one will call yes voters murderers-in-waiting. No one will call no voters war lovers. I do not have enough crew to waste on moral theater."

Noor added that sentence to the policy appendix before anyone asked.

Bell acknowledged the vote eight minutes later.

BELL RECEIVES RESOLUTION AND TALLY. PURSUIT STATUS UNCHANGED. VOTE ENTERED INTO PRESERVATION RECORD. WEAPONS REMAIN COLD.

Then narrow:

NON-UNANIMOUS HONESTY IS STRONGER THAN UNANIMOUS PERFORMANCE.

Tovin read the line twice, then showed Mer.

"Jaya?" she asked again.

"Likely."

"Tell her she should stop making me respect her."

Noor sent: CAPTAIN ANIK REQUESTS ADMIRAL SOTH CEASE BECOMING RESPECTABLE IN WAYS THAT COMPLICATE PURSUIT.

Bell replied:

REQUEST DENIED.

For a few seconds, the room felt almost human.

Then the tactical alarm chimed.

Not weapons. Route hazard.

Lemm's voice came through command relay. "Legal buoy ahead just woke. Broken Buoy Cut marker, old tribunal chain. It is broadcasting corrupted custody advisories and collision data into our declared route."

Noor closed their eyes. "Of course."

Lemm continued. "Bell sees it. Valech cutter sees it. Pell is already commenting."

Mer looked at Tovin. "If the buoy stays corrupt?"

"Everyone gets to interpret our next move."

"Can we bypass?"

Lemm answered, "Yes, but bypass puts us closer to Bell's closing arc and leaves the corrupted custody advisory in public route law."

Sela said, "Can we repair?"

"Maybe," Lemm said. "Under observation. Under pursuit. With a buoy that may be lying about who owns whom."

Mother Kal stood with her cane. "Then your vote was not theoretical for long."

No, Tovin thought. It had not been.

The crew began moving out of cargo chapel in clusters that did not match the vote cleanly. That gave him more hope than a clean split would have. Iren helped Daro lift a bench. Nima stopped Sela near the door and spoke too quietly for the record to capture. Sela listened, then nodded once. Ixo's amber light remained in the projector after most people left, as if the machine-person were still making sure the room had actually changed.

Tovin stayed until only Mer, Noor, Mother Kal, and the empty benches remained.

"Non-unanimous," Noor said.

"Honest," Mer answered.

Mother Kal tapped the deck. "Do not get sentimental. You have a broken buoy."

Mer looked toward command.

"Yes," she said. "And no teeth to send at it."

Tovin followed her out.

Behind them, cargo chapel returned to being a freight lock, a pressure refuge, a place for prayer strips, and now a room where fear had been allowed to speak without being handed the ship.

That was not victory.

It was a way to keep walking toward the next dangerous thing with fewer lies aboard.

Chapter 29: Broken Buoy Psalm

The Broken Buoy Cut marker had once been a small court.

That was what Noor said when the first images resolved: not a metaphor, not a complaint, a technical fact in Belt law. Old tribunal buoys did not merely blink and broadcast position. They held custody advisories, route precedence, emergency witness seals, salvage notices, quarantine warnings, minor injunctions, apology receipts, and the kind of temporary legal weather that kept poor stations from spending fuel on full hearings. They were law made small enough to bolt to debris.

The marker ahead of the *Mercy of Kaln* spun slowly in the dark, one side slagged, one antenna bent into a hook, its old court lamp pulsing white-white-amber in a rhythm that should have meant passive advisory.

Instead it was singing custody.

Not literally. No human choir. No Lettem House grief line. But the signal repeated in stacked clauses that rose and fell like bad liturgy:

ACCUSED CONVERTED VESSEL PRESENT.

MATERIAL PERSONS ABOARD.

CUSTODY ROUTE AVAILABLE.

TRANSFER PREVENTS SPOILAGE.

ACCUSED CONVERTED VESSEL PRESENT.

MATERIAL PERSONS ABOARD.

Noor stared at the decoded feed as if it had insulted a member of their family.

"It is quoting Valech's memorandum," they said.

Ixo's amber light tightened on the command wall. "Not quoting. Harmonizing."

Sela, half in a maintenance harness, said, "I need everyone to stop making corruption sound musical."

"It is broadcasting the custody clause in tribunal cadence," Ixo said. "Cadence can affect downstream acceptance. Older receivers may classify it as advisory precedent rather than hostile injection."

Mer stood at command with both hands behind her back. The tactical plot around her had become crowded: Bell high aft with weapons cold; two Bell drones still watching Saint Ordinance Field; Valech's cutter inside the disputed envelope; Pell's faint signatures lingering near the field edge; the broken buoy ahead contaminating the declared route; and the *Mercy*, always the accused center of everyone else's sentence.

"If we bypass?" Mer asked.

Lemm answered from navigation. "We avoid physical proximity. We also leave custody advisory active in route law. Any ship using Broken Buoy Cut receives a corrupted notice that material persons aboard a converted vessel should be transferred to custody to prevent spoilage."

Noor said, "Which means any local patrol, militia, court crawler, or ambitious dockmaster can claim they relied on buoy law when grabbing Ixo, witnesses, maybe children if someone gets creative enough with 'material.'"

Mother Kal, present at the observer rail because nobody had energy left to pretend she could be kept elsewhere, said, "People get creative when they want permission."

"If we repair?" Mer asked.

Sela brought up the engineering estimate. "We need to approach within service-arm range or send a drone. Our external repair drone is still patched from Cinderwake and has one manipulator with opinions. The buoy's court core may reject accused-vessel writes. We can physically isolate the corrupted transmitter, restore collision data, and install a witness patch if Noor gives me words that do not burst into law."

"Risk."

"Physical: moderate. The buoy spins, debris around it is small but fast, our forward shoulder is still damaged. Tactical: bad. Approach slows us and gives Bell cleaner geometry. Legal: a mud bath. Technical: if the corruption is baited, touching it could make us look like we altered court infrastructure to evade custody."

Noor added, "Which Valech will say even if we do nothing."

"Bell?" Mer asked.

Tovin opened the latest channel. "They see the corruption. Jaya says Bell can observe but not repair without abandoning Saint Ordinance drone posture or closing too fast on us. She recommends we do not touch the legal core unless the repair is fully mirrored."

Sela said, "That is a recommendation shaped like a dare."

Noor read the narrow line from Bell:

IF YOU LEAVE IT, IT WILL SPEAK FOR VALECH. IF YOU TOUCH IT, VALECH WILL SAY YOU TAUGHT IT TO LIE. CHOOSE THE RECORD YOU CAN DEFEND.

Mer almost smiled. "She really is becoming inconvenient."

Tovin looked at the repeating custody advisory:

TRANSFER PREVENTS SPOILAGE.

The phrase made his skin crawl. People did not spoil. Evidence did. Meat did. Fruit did. Archives corrupted. Persons suffered, lied, forgot, healed, resisted, died, changed their minds. The law had always liked people best when it could make them behave like objects in need of climate control.

Ixo said, "I request repair."

Noor turned. "For the record, why?"

"Because the advisory targets my standing and the standing of all other persons aboard whose testimony inconveniences emergency custody. If we bypass, we allow a legal object in public route space to misclassify persons as spoilage risks. That harm continues beyond this ship."

Mer said, "You understand repair increases boarding and custody risk."

"Yes."

"Still request?"

"Yes. I prefer visible risk to invisible law."

Mother Kal nodded. "Good sentence."

Tovin said, "Repair under public witness. Do not write new custody interpretation. Restore collision data, quarantine status, tribunal receipt function, and mark custody advisory as corrupted injection pending court review."

Noor was already drafting. "We need outside voices. Rill Sump, Lettem House, convoy union, Bell, Tallow Crown. If only the accused ship marks it corrupt, Valech gets dinner."

"Ask them," Mer said.

Noor sent the advisory transcript and request:

BROKEN BUOY CUT MARKER BROADCASTING CUSTODY ADVISORY HARMONIZED WITH VALECH MEMORANDUM. MERCY REQUESTS MULTI-PARTY WITNESS FOR PHYSICAL REPAIR LIMITED TO COLLISION DATA RESTORATION, TRANSMITTER ISOLATION, AND CORRUPTION TAG. NO ALTERATION OF LEGITIMATE COURT HISTORY REQUESTED OR ACCEPTED.

Responses arrived in the order of whoever was awake, angry, or both.

Rill Sump:

WE WITNESS. IF BUOY SAYS PEOPLE SPOIL, BUOY IS DRUNK OR OWNED.

Lettem House:

WE WITNESS. DO NOT LET PRAYER OR LAW CARRY HIDDEN CHAINS.

Convoy Union Korr-Varo:

WITNESS ACCEPTED. REPAIR SHOULD PRESERVE RAW BROADCAST AND RESTORE COLLISION SAFETY. UNION TECH ADVISORY ATTACHED.

Tallow Crown:

RECEIVED. NO OBJECTION TO PHYSICAL COLLISION-DATA REPAIR IF RAW LEGAL BROADCAST PRESERVED. CUSTODY ADVISORY STATUS UNDER REVIEW.

Noor stared at the last line. "That is almost useful. I dislike how grateful I feel."

Bell:

BELL WILL OBSERVE. WEAPONS REMAIN COLD FOR CURRENT WINDOW. APPROACH WILL REDUCE DISABLING DISTANCE. DO NOT MISTAKE COLD FOR ABSENCE.

Pell:

MERCY FIXES COURT FURNITURE WHILE EVERYONE ELSE BRINGS GUNS. BEAUTIFUL BRAND WORK.

Noor muted him again, but less efficiently this time because fatigue had made even muting feel repetitive.

Mer turned to Sela. "Can you repair from remote drone?"

"Maybe."

"That is not enough."

"Nothing is enough. The drone manipulator is damaged. I can route Yaret on external tether for manual stabilization."

Yaret, already at hull station, said, "I heard my name become bad."

"You can decline," Mer said.

"No. I voted yes with fear. Apparently the ship heard."

Sela looked at Tovin. "External human on tether near corrupt court buoy while Bell closes. Any restraint objection?"

"Several. None that change the need."

"I hate when you become concise."

"So do I."

The repair plan took twelve minutes to build and thirty seconds to become obsolete.

The buoy's spin changed.

Not much. Enough for Lemm to swear.

"It is venting," navigation said. "Microgas from the damaged side. Spin rate increasing. Collision projection shifts across our declared route in seven minutes. If it tumbles into the route lane, we either divert toward Bell or pass close enough to risk hull strike."

"Can Bell clear it?" Mer asked.

Tovin read the update before it finished. "Bell says they can fire a foam dart and stop the spin. It would be a weapons-adjacent intervention into a legal buoy while pursuing us."

Noor said, "Valech would enjoy that too much."

Sela snapped her harness shut. "Then we repair now."

Mer nodded once. "Go."

The ship moved without rearmament.

That was the first psalm, Tovin thought later: not words, but bodies choosing tools that did not flatter fear. Sela ran to drone control with two apprentices behind her. Yaret suited at the hull lock. Daro rerouted pressure margins. Noor built the public repair chain. Ixo mirrored the raw custody advisory, not editing, not flinching from the language that targeted it. Mother Kal sent Teacher Aven a note explaining why the children would receive another adult record before rumor did.

Tovin went to the restraint gallery because the repair would pass through three old control pathways, none weapons, all adjacent to things that could be misread by bad law or bad faith.

The repair drone launched first.

It looked worse than it was: one arm stripped to a lattice, tool head replaced by a clamp, casing patched with panels borrowed from an old school air recycler. Jessa had painted a small yellow moon on the side months ago and written DO NOT BE STUPID beneath it. Under other circumstances, Tovin might have found that comforting.

The drone crossed the hundred meters to the buoy with a stuttering thruster rhythm. The buoy's court lamp pulsed white-white-amber, white-white-amber, custody language rolling through every channel that would accept it.

Yaret exited the hull lock on tether two minutes later.

He was a small white figure against the old warship's skin, moving hand over hand along the service line with a tool pack clamped to his chest. His breathing fed into the command circuit, calm because panic would waste oxygen and because Yaret had always treated vacuum like a colleague he disliked but respected.

"External worker clear," Sela said. "Drone approaching bent antenna. No contact yet."

Bell's range marker tightened.

Taval's voice from Bell came through shared safety channel. "Austere Bell observes repair action. Bell weapons remain cold. Disabling geometry improves in four minutes."

Mer answered, "Received."

Noor's public chain posted every line:

REPAIR ACTION UNDERWAY.

RAW CUSTODY BROADCAST PRESERVED.

COLLISION HAZARD ACTIVE.

NO OFFENSIVE SYSTEMS ENGAGED.

NO WAR MATERIAL DEPLOYED.

Yaret reached the buoy and clipped himself to an old service lug. The lug held. Everyone in command breathed once, quietly.

Then the buoy spoke directly to him.

YARET SOL, HULL WORKER, MATERIAL PERSON ADJACENT TO ACCUSED CONVERTED VESSEL. TRANSFER PREVENTS SPOILAGE.

Yaret froze.

Sela said, "Ignore it."

Yaret's breath hitched. "It used my name."

Noor's face drained. "It queried the worker tag."

Ixo said, "The corruption is adaptive."

Tovin's hands closed on the edge of the restraint console. "Sela, can it log his proximity as consent?"

"Not if I rip the transmitter throat out before it finishes being clever."

"Do that," Mer said.

The drone clamp caught the bent antenna. Yaret opened the service panel. The buoy's spin tugged him sideways. His tether tightened. The camera swung, showing stars, the *Mercy*'s scarred hull, Bell's distant glints, the buoy's white-white-amber lamp.

YARET SOL, TRANSFER PREVENTS-

Sela cut the transmitter.

The silence was so abrupt it felt like impact.

Noor whispered, "Raw preserved?"

Ixo answered, "Yes. Mirror complete before cut."

Yaret said, "Good, because I did not like the song."

Sela laughed once through her teeth. "Keep working."

The next problem was the collision core. The buoy's physical navigation function had been starved of clean updates by the corrupted legal loop; collision data kept folding back through custody priority, as if every route warning first needed to decide who owned the witnesses. Sela guided the drone into the outer panel while Yaret stabilized the core housing with both hands.

"I can restore collision beacon," Sela said. "But court receipt function is tangled. If I bring the beacon up, it may come with a blank custody docket."

Noor said, "Do not create blank custody anything."

"Then collision remains dirty."

Lemm said, "Time to route conflict: three minutes."

Bell: "Disabling geometry in two minutes."

Valech's cutter broadcast, not to them but to everyone:

ACCUSED VESSEL ALTERING TRIBUNAL BUOY DURING FLIGHT. EXTERNAL CUSTODY REQUIRED.

Rill Sump answered publicly before Noor could:

WE SEE THE RAW BROADCAST. BUOY NAMED A HULL WORKER SPOILAGE. FIX THE DAMN THING.

Lettem House:

WITNESSING. SONG CUT WAS NECESSARY.

Korr-Varo Union:

TECH ADVISORY: ISOLATE COURT RECEIPT BUS PHYSICALLY. RESTORE COLLISION BEACON ON STANDALONE POWER. LEGAL HISTORY REMAINS OFFLINE BUT PRESERVED.

Sela read the advisory and swore with admiration. "Union techs, I love you today."

Noor said, "Record selective affection."

"No."

Sela rerouted. Yaret held the housing while the drone's damaged arm inserted a bridge no wider than two fingers. The buoy kicked under his hands. His tether snapped taut and then slack.

"Yaret," Mer said.

"Still attached."

"That is not a full report."

"It is the part I like."

Tovin watched Bell's geometry count down. Jaya could disable them now. Foam dart, heat-net, bracket drones. The *Mercy* was slowed, nose angled, one worker outside, repair drone engaged, no war material deployed, public record screaming. If Jaya fired, she could say route hazard, accused vessel altering tribunal infrastructure, pursuit warrant. If she did not, Valech could say she let the accused write law.

Bell did not fire.

Instead, Bell transmitted:

AUSTERE BELL OBSERVES RAW CORRUPTION, TRANSMITTER CUT, COLLISION REPAIR. WEAPONS REMAIN COLD. VALECH CHARACTERIZATION DISPUTED BY OBSERVED RECORD.

Then narrow:

FINISH FAST.

Sela did.

The collision beacon came alive in clean blue.

Not white-white-amber. Not custody. Not court. Simple route safety: object spin, mass estimate, tumble axis, safe pass vector, hazard cone, archival note attached but legal advisory offline pending tribunal review. The buoy stopped singing about material persons and began telling ships how not to die against it.

Noor almost sagged. "Clean beacon."

Lemm updated route. "We can pass without diverting into Bell's arc. Margin ugly but sufficient."

Yaret sealed the panel. "Court receipt?"

Noor said, "Offline, preserved, witnessed. Do not wake it."

"Happy to disappoint law."

He unclipped.

That was when Pell's drone appeared.

Not at Saint Ordinance. Not near the buoy. A tiny relay bead, cold until it was not, drifting behind the broken marker under debris shadow. It lit for less than three seconds, enough to dump a packet into the repaired collision beacon's public tail.

Sela shouted, "No!"

Ixo moved faster than speech. The machine-person caught the packet in the mirror layer before the buoy rebroadcast it. Noor's slate flashed red. Tovin saw only fragments:

MERCY ACCEPTS-

PROTECTOR STATUS-

PARDON REEF-

Then Ixo cut the tail buffer and quarantined the fragment.

The clean beacon flickered once, then held.

"Pell tried to plant acceptance," Noor said.

Yaret, still outside, said, "Can I hit his bead with a wrench?"

Mer said, "No."

Sela said, "Yes."

Mer looked at her.

"Emotionally yes," Sela corrected. "Operationally no."

Bell's observer feed sharpened. "Bell saw relay bead ignition. Tracking."

Pell posted public:

COURTS ARE HARD TO REPAIR. SHAME ABOUT THE DRAFTS THEY SAVE.

Noor's hands moved like knives. "Criminal interference hash. Full fragment preserved. Ixo voluntary interception logged under machine-person witness standing, not ship system suppression."

Ixo said, "Thank you."

"You saved the beacon from lying."

"Yes. I also acted to protect myself from false custody transfer."

"Both recorded."

The difference mattered.

Yaret returned to the hull lock with no wrench-based diplomacy. The repair drone limped home. The *Mercy* adjusted to the clean blue beacon's safe vector and passed the buoy close enough that its scarred court lamp filled one whole forward pane. It no longer sang. It blinked collision truth at them like an exhausted clerk who had misplaced all doctrine and found physics under the desk.

As they passed, Mother Kal began humming.

Not Lettem House. Not anything Tovin knew. A low line, almost tuneless, something station-born and old enough that words had probably worn off before he was born.

Noor looked at her. "Is that relevant to the record?"

"No."

"Then why?"

"Because machines are not the only things allowed to stop bad songs."

Noor let it go unrecorded.

Bell's public feed updated:

BROKEN BUOY CUT MARKER COLLISION FUNCTION RESTORED UNDER MULTI-PARTY WITNESS. CORRUPTED CUSTODY ADVISORY PRESERVED OFFLINE. PELL INTERFERENCE ATTEMPT QUARANTINED. ACCUSED MERCY MAINTAINS ROUTE. PURSUIT CONTINUES.

Rill Sump posted:

GOOD. NOW SEND MORE FUEL PARTS.

Lettem House:

WITNESSING COMPLETE. WE WILL WRITE OUR OWN SONG.

Pell:

EVERYONE IS A CRITIC.

Valech:

MACHINE-PERSON INTERFERENCE WITH TRIBUNAL BUOY BROADCAST CONFIRMS NEED FOR EXTERNAL CUSTODY.

Noor read Valech's line aloud, then looked at Ixo. "He will use every act."

Ixo's amber light steadied. "Then I prefer acts that leave evidence."

Tovin looked at the repaired buoy receding behind them. The *Mercy* had not fired. It had not fled the route. It had not accepted Pell's planted language. It had cut a bad legal song out of a public marker and left the raw harm visible for others to judge.

It had also slowed under pursuit, placed a worker outside, gave Bell clean geometry, gave Valech another sentence, gave Pell another opening, and proved again that a demilitarized warship could terrify law simply by touching it.

Mer stood beside him, watching the same blue blink diminish.

"That will be called manipulation," she said.

"Yes."

"It was repair."

"Yes."

"Both will be true in different mouths."

He thought of Jaya's weather, Mother Kal's warning, Sela's vote, Ixo's refusal, Yaret's name spoken by a corrupted buoy as if a person could spoil.

"Then we keep making records with more than one witness," he said.

Mer nodded.

Behind them, Sela's voice came through the command circuit, tired and triumphant in the smallest possible amount. "Repair drone is docked. Yaret is in lock. No war material used. No one hit Pell with a wrench."

"Commendable restraint," Noor said.

Yaret's voice, muffled by helmet, answered, "Barely."

For the first time in hours, Tovin allowed himself to smile.

It lasted until Lemm spoke.

"Bell is closing."

The plot confirmed it. The repair had bought a public record and a safe vector. It had not bought distance. The *Austere Bell* now held a tighter aft arc. Pursuit drones adjusted. Valech's cutter remained at Saint Ordinance. Pell's signatures dispersed like sparks. The repaired buoy blinked blue behind them, honest and useless against ships.

Chapter 29 ended not with safety but with a cleaner route into danger.

That, Tovin thought, was becoming the *Mercy*'s specialty.

Chapter 30: The Warship's Shadow

The first complaint after the buoy repair did not come from Valech, Pell, Bell, Tallow Crown, or any of the people Tovin had learned to expect.

It came from a grain tender named *Little Answer*, three hundred thousand kilometers ahead, hauling seed protein to a station that had already spent next season's hunger in advance.

The message arrived on the route safety channel, not public politics. That made it worse.

MERCY OF KALN, THIS IS LITTLE ANSWER. YOUR CURRENT VECTOR PLACES YOUR HULL SHADOW ACROSS OUR DECLARED PASS IN TWENTY-ONE MINUTES IF BELL CONTINUES CLOSING. WE KNOW YOU HAVE RIGHT OF EMERGENCY TRANSIT. WE KNOW YOU REPAIRED THE BUOY. WE KNOW YOU ARE NOT FIRING. OUR CREW IS STILL PANICKING BECAUSE A KALN-CLASS WARSHIP IS BETWEEN US AND THE ONLY CLEAR LANE. REQUEST YOU STATE INTENT IN SMALL WORDS.

Noor read it aloud in command.

No one made a joke.

Mer looked at the plot. *Little Answer* was exactly where it claimed to be: small, slow, overburdened, patched by station labor, running a narrow route through Broken Buoy Cut because it did not have fuel to be elegant. The *Mercy*'s adjusted vector did not collide with it. Bell's closing arc did not collide with it. Nothing in the geometry required panic.

Except the hull.

Except history.

Except size.

Except a hundred thousand tons of converted warship occupying a lane where poorer ships had only trust, fuel margins, and whatever law had survived the morning.

"Navigation," Mer said.

Lemm answered before the rest of the room finished thinking. "We can shift half a degree nadir and reduce projected shadow across their pass. It costs fourteen minutes of efficient burn and gives Bell cleaner closing geometry. Or we can hold vector and pass legally clear."

"Legally clear," Noor said, "is about to become the ugliest possible phrase."

Tovin watched the little tender's icon flicker. It was not physically in danger yet. Its crew was. Their fear had already entered the route as a fact.

He heard Mother Kal in the laundry bay: Guns are honest compared with arrival.

He heard Sela in the magazine throat: We keep proving we don't fire. But sometimes everyone is already shaped by where we put the hull.

He heard his own doctrine note: POSITION, MASS, AND COMMAND VOICE CAN COERCE WITHOUT DISCHARGE.

Then he realized the note had not cost him enough when he wrote it.

"We shift," he said.

Mer looked at him.

"Restraint basis?"

"Our legal right of transit is not the only harm measurement. If our position makes a civilian tender alter behavior under fear of our hull, that fear is operational pressure. We cannot call it harmless because no weapon is active."

Noor's fingers moved before he finished. "Doctrine entry?"

"Yes."

Lemm said, "Shift gives Bell cleaner geometry."

"Yes."

Sela, from engineering, said, "And if Bell uses it?"

Tovin looked at the pursuit plot. The *Austere Bell* was closing because the buoy repair had slowed them. Jaya's weapons remained cold. Cold was a decision, not a weather condition, which meant it could change when the person making it ran out of authority or time.

"Then the cost is ours," he said. "Not *Little Answer*'s."

Mer's eyes did not soften. "Route adjustment. Half degree nadir. Broadcast intent."

Noor drafted:

LITTLE ANSWER, MERCY OF KALN RECEIVES. WE WILL SHIFT HALF DEGREE NADIR TO REDUCE HULL SHADOW ACROSS YOUR PASS. WE WILL NOT TRANSMIT COMMAND PRIORITY TO YOU, REQUEST COURSE CHANGE, OR use YOUR ROUTE AS COVER. OUR WEAPONS REMAIN DISABLED. OUR MASS STILL MATTERS. YOUR FEAR IS RECEIVED AS MATERIAL ROUTE FACT, NOT DISMISSED AS ERROR.

Noor stopped. "Lowercase 'use' snuck in. Fixing."

Sela said, "Keep 'your fear is received.'"

Noor did.

Mer approved.

The *Mercy* shifted.

The deck did not lurch. There was only a slight change in engine tone, a long correction through mass and damage and weary systems. On the plot, the warship's projected pass moved off the tender's lane by a distance that looked generous on scale and insufficient in the human imagination. Bell's closing geometry sharpened.

*Little Answer* answered:

RECEIVED. WE SEE SHIFT. CREW BREATHING BETTER. THANK YOU FOR SMALL WORDS.

Then, after a pause:

WE ARE STILL AFRAID OF YOU.

Tovin read it three times.

Noor put it into the doctrine record without asking.

Bell transmitted:

AUSTERE BELL OBSERVES MERCY VECTOR SHIFT TO REDUCE CIVILIAN HULL SHADOW. PURSUIT STATUS UNCHANGED. DISABLING GEOMETRY IMPROVED. WEAPONS REMAIN COLD.

The narrow line followed:

THAT WAS NOT TACTICALLY HELPFUL TO YOU.

Tovin answered before Mer could decide whether to:

RESTRAINT ACKNOWLEDGES.

Jaya did not reply.

Pell did:

MERCY DISCOVERS BEING LARGE. MORE AT ELEVEN.

Noor muted the account and sighed. "One day I will miss him, and that will be my first sign of brain injury."

The room allowed a tired breath of laughter. It did not last.

The shift exposed them to *Austere Bell* more clearly, but Bell did not fire. Instead, a new set of civilian messages began arriving. *Little Answer* had done what frightened crews did: told others. Some messages were hostile. Some grateful. Some incoherent. All of them made the same point in different ways.

YOU BLOCKED OUR STARS.

YOU MADE MY PILOT FREEZE.

YOU MOVED, THANK YOU.

YOU MOVED BECAUSE BELL TOLD YOU, DON'T LIE.

DOES A WARSHIP APOLOGIZE FOR BEING A WARSHIP?

WHAT IF NEXT TIME YOU DO NOT MOVE?

Noor built a new board because the old ones were full.

"Civilian hull-shadow response," they said. "I hate that I know what to call it."

Mer ordered the messages sorted by route safety relevance, not sentiment. Mother Kal objected from the observer rail.

"Do not throw sentiment away too quickly," she said.
