He glanced toward the maintenance crawl and looked sick.

"I know."

Mer's voice came over Tovin's command bead. "Status."

Tovin kept his hands open. "Passengers holding Junction C-Blue. No weapons visible. Improvised barricade. Medical distress present. Access to school inward passage and Ixo minor relay crawl obstructed."

Noor, on the channel, said, "Do not call it mutiny yet."

Pel heard through someone else's open slate and laughed once, ugly. "Call it what lets you sleep."

Nima lifted Sori higher. "We want transfer."

Tovin did not step closer. Junctions had memories. Crowds did too.

"To whom?" he asked.

"Bell. Tallow Crown. Mercy's Edge. Anyone with a hospital that is not inside a hunted warship."

Pel said, "Valech offered safe medical transfer."

Sela's voice snapped. "Valech offered to buy humans with Ixo."

Pel turned on her. "And command refused for us."

The corridor tightened.

Tovin felt the truth of the accusation and the danger of letting it become the only truth.

"Command refused to surrender Ixo as condition of biological safety," he said.

"My patient is biological," Pel said.

"Yes."

"She does not care about standing doctrine."

"No."

"Then move."

Tovin looked at Sori. The child made a small sound, almost a whimper, then settled badly. He knew very little medicine and enough fear to know when adults were not exaggerating.

"What do you need medically right now?" he asked.

Pel's eyes narrowed, suspicious of any question that might become delay. "Cold pack cycle, fever antivirals, pediatric lung scan, and a doctor who is not triaging under pursuit with half a pharmacy."

"Can our medical deck provide first three?"

"Yes."

"Then why block the lift?"

Nima's face twisted. "Because if we send her inward, she becomes another reason command says stay aboard. If we hold here, Bell sees."

Noor said quietly in Tovin's ear, "They are creating a visible hostage condition with themselves as hostages."

Tovin wished the sentence were wrong.

Mer's voice followed. "I am coming."

"Captain," Tovin said, "recommend you wait."

The channel went silent for one beat.

"Reason."

"They need command visible but not yet embodied. If you arrive now, refusal has a face close enough to hate."

Nima heard enough from the open slate. "She should come. She made the decision."

Mer said, "I am coming slowly."

That was the best they would get.

Ixo's amber light appeared on a small corridor maintenance panel before anyone asked. Sela stepped toward it instinctively, then stopped because movement toward Ixo near an angry crowd could mean too many things.

Nima saw the light.

"No," she said.

Ixo answered, "I am present."

"You are why she is still here."

"Partly."

Pel's mouth tightened. "Don't do that."

"Do what?" Ixo asked.

"Agree in a way that makes us cruel."

Ixo's light dimmed. "I do not intend that."

"Intent is a small room," Mother Kal said from behind Tovin.

He turned. "You should not be here."

"I have been told that in better corridors."

She came forward with her cane, not past Tovin, not toward the barricade, just enough for Nima to see her. Teacher Aven came with her, pale and furious, holding a bundle of slates under one arm. Behind them, Mer entered the far end of the corridor and stopped at Tovin's earlier line without being told. Noor arrived beside her with a recording slate already active. No guards. No security team. No theatrical proof of trust. Just the people who would be blamed.

Nima saw Mer and tightened around Sori.

"Open the lane," she said.

Mer's voice was low. "For medical treatment inward, yes. For unsanctioned transfer to Bell under Valech's terms, no."

The first line of passengers shifted. A young man Tovin did not know put his hand on an emergency locker as if it were a weapon, then seemed surprised by his own hand and pulled it away.

Sela saw. So did everyone.

Iren stepped between him and the locker.

"Don't," Iren said.

The young man whispered, "They will let the baby die for the machine."

Iren's face changed. "No. They may get us killed for not trading Ixo. That is bad enough. Do not improve the accusation."

Tovin had never liked him more.

Pel said, "We are wasting time."

Mer nodded. "Yes. Medical first. Politics while moving."

"No," Nima said. "If I let her go inward, you will seal us out."

Noor said, "We can write terms."

Pel barked a laugh. "Terms?"

"Yes," Noor said. "Immediate pediatric treatment on medical deck under witness, no separation from guardian unless medically necessary, no use of Sori's treatment as consent to remain aboard, no punishment for this junction seizure if no one damages systems or blocks treatment further, immediate request to Bell and Mercy's Edge for neutral medical consult, and a recorded review of passenger transfer options not conditioned on Ixo surrender."

The corridor listened because practical terms had weight fear could touch.

Nima looked down at Sori. "Transfer options?"

Mer said, "Yes. Not Valech's bargain. Real options if any exist."

"You said Bell custody is dangerous."

"It is."

"You said staying is dangerous."

"It is."

"Then what are you offering?"

Mer's answer came without ornament. "Treatment now. Truth about options. No lie that I can make either safe."

Nima's face crumpled and hardened again. "That is not enough."

"No."

Noor wrote the terms and projected them on the corridor wall. Ixo added a statement without being asked:

IXO CONTINUANT DOES NOT OBJECT TO PASSENGER MEDICAL CONSULT OR TRANSFER REVIEW THAT DOES NOT REQUIRE MACHINE-PERSON DISAPPEARANCE.

Pel stared at it. "That helps."

Nima said, "It does not save her."

"No," Ixo said.

The baby coughed then, a small wet sound that ended the first phase of the mutiny more effectively than command could have. Pel moved. Nima moved with her. The barricade did not come down, but it opened a body's width. Tovin stepped back first, making space. Sela pulled two crates aside without comment. Iren helped her, hands shaking.

They carried Sori through.

The crowd did not disperse.

That mattered.

Pel and Nima went toward medical with Sela escorting, not guarding. Mother Kal followed because nobody wanted to argue about it. Teacher Aven stayed at Junction C-Blue with the remaining passengers, which was either brave or professionally deranged.

Mer remained at the line.

"The junction stays open," she said.

A passenger worker named Havel shook his head. "No. We keep it until transfer review."

Noor said, "Blocking Ixo relay crawl creates witness-integrity risk."

Havel looked toward the maintenance panel where Ixo's amber light remained. "Maybe that is the only leverage passengers have."

The word leverage made the corridor colder.

Tovin said, "If you threaten Ixo relay access, you reproduce Valech's bargain inside the ship."

Havel's face reddened. "Do not compare us to him."

"Then do not use the same hinge."

Havel stepped forward.

Iren put a hand on his chest. Not a shove. A plea with fingers.

"Stop," Iren said. "He is right in the worst way."

Havel looked at Iren with betrayal. "You voted no."

"Yes."

"You wanted contingency."

"Yes."

"Now you defend them?"

Iren's voice broke. "I am defending the line I almost crossed."

Teacher Aven spoke then. "Children are listening."

Everyone froze.

She lifted the slates in her arms. "Not here. Through vents, open channels, adult faces, missing nurses, rumor. They are always listening. Whatever you do next becomes what they think fear permits."

Havel stepped back half a pace.

Noor whispered on command channel, "Teacher just did more than law."

Mer said, "Yes."

Bell transmitted publicly into the arbitration lane:

AUSTERE BELL RECEIVES REPORT OF INTERNAL PASSENGER SEIZURE AT JUNCTION C-BLUE. BELL WILL NOT EXPLOIT MEDICAL DISTRESS FOR BOARDING POSITION DURING ACTIVE TREATMENT WINDOW. REQUEST STATUS OF MINOR PATIENT AND WITNESS-INTEGRITY RISK.

Jaya's narrow line came to Mer:

VALECH WILL USE THIS. I WILL NOT.

Mer read it aloud to the corridor.

Havel looked suspicious. "Why should we believe Bell?"

Tovin said, "You should not believe without record. That is why it is public."

Mercy's Edge wall keeper sent:

STATION MEDICAL CAN CONSULT PEDIATRIC FEVER BY OPEN CHANNEL. NO DOCKING. NO SPEECHES.

Rill Sump:

SEND BABY STATS. ARGUE AFTER.

Lettem House:

WITNESSING JUNCTION TERMS. CHILD FIRST. NO MERCY BY EXCLUSION.

Pell posted:

PASSENGERS DISCOVER CAPTAINS ARE JUST WALLS WITH RANK.

Noor, remarkably, ignored him.

The medical consult began. Sori had a fever, respiratory inflammation, and a treatable infection made worse by stress, recycled air, and the ship's damaged pharmacy. Mercy's Edge advised a dosage adjustment. Rill Sump's mechanic Bex somehow knew a substitute inhalant recipe from mining kids and sent it with three warnings and one insult. Sela confirmed the compound. Pel administered.

For eleven minutes, Junction C-Blue stayed half-held and half-open while a baby breathed under the attention of three stations, one pursuit ship, one accused warship, a machine-person witness, and the worst people in the Belt pretending not to watch.

When Sori's fever began to come down, Nima cried without sound.

Pel reported it to the corridor.

Some people sagged. Some looked angrier because relief removed the cleanest justification for what came next.

Mer said, "Transfer review now."

Noor projected options:

1. Remain aboard under arbitration lane protection and continued medical consult.

2. Request Bell-supervised transfer to Mercy's Edge medical custody, requiring station consent, Bell safety guarantee, and no Valech condition. Current station position: no docking; consult only.

3. Request Bell medical shuttle without custody transfer. Current Bell position pending.

4. Accept Valech humanitarian framework. Command rejects because it requires Ixo disappearance; passengers may state objection but command will not comply.

5. Independent evacuation pod launch. Not safe in current geometry.

Nima returned from medical without Sori, who stayed with Pel and Mother Kal. She looked emptied and sharper.

"You should have shown this before," she said.

Mer accepted the hit. "Yes."

Noor did not soften it. "We did not have all positions before."

"You had enough to say no for us."

Mer said, "Yes."

The corridor waited for a defense.

Mer did not give one.

Havel said, "We want option three."

"Bell medical shuttle without custody," Noor said. "Bell?"

Bell took forty-nine seconds.

BELL CAN PREPARE MEDICAL SHUTTLE FOR SUPPLIES AND REMOTE CARE PACKAGE. BELL CANNOT ACCEPT PASSENGER TRANSFER WITHOUT CUSTODY STATUS CLARIFIED UNDER CURRENT WARRANT AND TRIBUNAL REVIEW.

The corridor erupted.

Nima shouted over it, "So there is no door."

Mer said, "Not one that does not become custody."

"Then Valech was the only door."

Ixo's light dimmed.

Nima saw it and looked away.

Tovin said, "Valech was not a door. He was a sorter."

"Easy for you."

"No."

"You do not have a child."

The sentence landed because it was true and because truth could be unfair without becoming false.

Tovin said, "No. I do not."

Teacher Aven stepped beside Nima. "I do. Thirty-seven at the moment, by professional delusion. I want them off this ship every hour. I also do not want them taught that safety is something powerful people offer when you help them disappear someone."

Nima whispered, "I know."

"Knowing does not make it hurt less."

"No."

The barricade came down after that, not all at once. People moved crates with the reluctance of those surrendering their last proof of agency. Havel cursed while doing it. Iren lifted until his hands shook too badly and then asked Sela to take over. Sela did, without comment.

Noor recorded the event as:

PASSENGER SEIZURE OF JUNCTION C-BLUE ENDED WITHOUT VIOLENCE; MEDICAL DISTRESS TREATED; TRANSFER REVIEW CONDUCTED; DISSENT REMAINS.

Then they looked at Tovin. "Too clean?"

"Add that command failed to present transfer options before distress became leverage."

Noor added it.

Mer saw and nodded once.

Nima did not thank anyone. She returned to medical. Havel refused to leave the corridor until Mer promised there would be a passenger council seat in all further custody discussions. Mer promised. Noor made it binding enough to cause future inconvenience.

Iren stayed after the others left.

"I should be confined," he said.

Sela frowned. "For what?"

"I stood with them."

"You also stopped Havel."

"After standing with them."

Tovin said, "You disclosed. You de-escalated. You remain under watch for relevant systems, not punishment."

Iren laughed bitterly. "That sounds like a sentence built by committee."

"It was built by exhaustion."

Iren looked at Ixo's maintenance panel. "I thought about leverage too."

Ixo answered, "I know."

"Are you angry?"

"Yes."

"At me?"

"Partly."

Iren nodded. "Good."

It was becoming a ritual, Tovin realized: people asking the machine-person not for forgiveness but for anger honest enough to stand beside.

Valech's public statement arrived before Junction C-Blue had fully cleared:

INTERNAL PASSENGER UNREST CONFIRMS CAPTAIN ANIK CANNOT PROTECT BIOLOGICAL CIVILIANS WHILE PRIORITIZING MACHINE TESTIMONY AND FAILED COVENANT SYMBOLISM.

Bell responded before *Mercy* did:

AUSTERE BELL OBSERVED MEDICAL DISTRESS, JUNCTION SEIZURE, AND RESOLUTION WITHOUT VIOLENCE. PASSENGER FEAR IS MATERIAL. VALECH CHARACTERIZATION OMITS OFFER CONDITIONS.

Jaya had spent political capital again.

Noor said, "She is going to run out."

Tovin looked aft on the plot, where Bell remained close and cold and dangerous. "Yes."

The *Mercy* continued down the arbitration lane, still held by witnesses, now carrying a fracture no public statement could mend. Passenger council representation was added to custody review. Medical supplies from Bell were negotiated without passenger transfer. Sori slept with fever reduced. Nima sat beside her and refused visitors. Ixo withdrew from nonessential displays for twenty minutes after requesting not to be useful. This time, no one made it leave the room; they simply stopped asking.

In command, Mer looked older.

"We kept the junction," Tovin said.

"No," she answered. "We kept the ship from learning a worse lesson. The junction is just open."

That was more accurate.

And far less comforting.

Chapter 36: Jaya Boards a Wreck

Jaya Soth left the *Austere Bell* because there were too many clean rooms.

Command wells were made to turn horror into panels. Evidence boards made death sortable. Legal overlays took jagged human loss and gave it fonts, colors, precedence tags, and little confidence bars that made officers feel less like they were guessing in a room full of ghosts. Jaya had needed those tools for thirty years. She trusted them the way she trusted pressure seals: constantly, without romance, and never without checking for hairline cracks.

Now every panel was too clean.

Avar Break. Rill Sump. The forged custody order. Saint Ordinance Spine Seven. Valech's memorandum. Pell's offers. Ixo's archive fork. Each fact had arrived with timing that could be explained. Each explanation had then pointed at another fact that arrived with timing just as convenient. The pattern was no longer a line. It was a polished corridor.

She disliked polished corridors.

"Admiral, you do not need to board personally," Arin Cho said.

They stood in the forward lock bay while the boarding skiff pressurized. Arin wore a field suit because he had insisted on accompanying her and because she had not yet found an ethical way to bolt him to the command deck. Pellis Ren checked a salvage scanner with the air of a man offended by every object in the universe. Fane carried the evidence kit. Two security ratings stood by with cold sidearms that would remain sealed unless vacuum itself developed hostile intent.

"I know," Jaya said.

Arin tried again. "Sending a remote drone preserves command continuity."

"Remote drones see what we ask them to see."

"So do admirals."

She looked at him. "Better."

He blinked.

"That was an actual objection," she said. "Keep it."

Pellis muttered, "Healthy command culture continues to resemble an argument losing paperwork."

The wreck waited nine thousand kilometers off Bell's port-forward pursuit envelope: a convoy tender named *Kestrel Mercy*, which everyone involved had agreed not to mention around the *Mercy of Kaln* unless absolutely necessary because the Belt had become indecently fond of word collisions. It had been found by Bell Drone Two during the Saint Ordinance observation sweep, tumbling in a slow shadow behind shale fragments, transponder dead, hull cut open, life signs absent. Its registry placed it near an old support route between Tallow Crown and Rill Sump. Its last filed cargo: coolant sacks, court ink cartridges, pediatric filters, and three sealed maintenance lockers.

Its last unofficial relevance: one of those lockers bore a contractor stamp close to the hybrid shell Bex had identified.

Valech wanted the wreck secured under his broadened hazard authority.

Pell publicly offered to sell "tasteful mourning rights."

The *Mercy* could not approach without making every bad sentence worse.

So Jaya went.

The skiff crossed under minimal burn. Bell remained in pursuit posture, but Jaya had shifted tactical command to Taval with orders so narrow they could cut skin: no boarding of *Mercy*, no firing absent immediate threat, no enforcement of unverified custody language, no acceptance of Valech hazard orders without her countersign. She knew the limits of those orders. Absence made command brittle. Presence at the wreck made evidence less secondhand. Every choice spent a different kind of authority.

The wreck grew in the skiff's forward pane.

*Kestrel Mercy* had been small, a tender built for useful indignity: carrying parts, persons, documents, and whatever else a station needed but could not afford to request with pride. Now it looked peeled. One side of the pressure hull had opened along a seam too straight for random debris. The cargo spine was broken but not scattered. The cockpit blister had cracked inward. A string of frozen droplets shone near the aft lock like somebody had tried to make stars out of coolant and blood.

No one spoke for the last minute of approach.

Jaya preferred that.

The skiff latched to a bent service lug. Fane established evidence field. Pellis cursed softly at the salvage beacon because it had been disabled with "insulting competence." Arin checked the security feed twice and then, to his credit, did not tell Jaya again that she should have stayed aboard.

They entered through the aft lock.

The wreck smelled even through suit filters: cold metal, burned polymer, old air, and something organic the filters politely flattened into warning chemistry. The tender's emergency lights had died. Their helmet beams found cargo nets, torn insulation, a child's glove stuck to Velcro on the wall, and a line of floating ink beads from a ruptured court cartridge drifting like black eggs.

Fane began the chain. "Boarding party aboard *Kestrel Mercy*. Time mark. Admiral Soth present. Lieutenant Cho present. Commander Ren present. Evidence officer Fane present. Security Ratings Ibb and Solt present. Purpose: casualty confirmation, evidence preservation, contractor-link inspection, hazard assessment."

"Add no assumption of attacker," Jaya said.

Fane added it.

They moved.

The first body was in the corridor, strapped halfway into an emergency web. A woman, mid-forties perhaps, eyes closed by vacuum not peace. Her hands were locked around a manual seal lever. The lever had not completed travel.

Arin stopped.

Jaya let him.

Pellis said quietly, "She tried to close aft after breach."

Fane marked body position, seal status, tissue condition, suit absence. No speeches. The dead deserved more than speeches and less than use.

In the cargo spine, the damage became peculiar.

"This is too polite," Pellis said.

Jaya turned. "Explain."

He pointed his scanner at the ruptured lockers. "A chaotic boarding or debris strike leaves argument everywhere. This hull has argument, yes, but see the maintenance lockers? Three opened. Two ignored. One cut. The cut one is the contractor locker. Clean breach, display outward, stamp exposed. Whoever did this wanted the next investigator to meet the stamp before the bodies."

Arin said, "Or the attacker searched specifically for the contractor locker."

"Then why leave the stamp visible?"

Fane's beam caught the mark: a maintenance escrow stamp, half burned, still legible enough for an evidence camera. The same curve Ixo had described. Convoy maintenance authority. Hybrid shell structure. Quiet Authority's modern shadow wearing procurement clothes.

Jaya crouched near the locker without touching it.

The evidence lay there like a confession waiting for applause.

She disliked applause more than polished corridors.

"Fane," she said, "record: visible contractor stamp appears preserved despite surrounding thermal damage. Mark as suspicious preservation, not merely discovered evidence."

Fane's voice stayed even. "Recorded."

Arin leaned closer. "There is residue inside."

Pellis scanned. "Archive wafer fragments. Mostly slagged. Conveniently, one corner survived with label."

Jaya almost laughed. It would have been obscene.

The label read:

QA CONTINUITY / ROUTE LEGITIMACY TRAINING SET / PARTIAL

Arin swore.

That, too, she let happen.

Pellis's face had gone hard. "Nobody labels forbidden continuity data that clearly unless they intend an idiot to find it or a court to fear it."

"Could be arrogance," Arin said.

"Arrogance usually misfiles. This is theater."

Jaya looked at the wafer fragment. The case around it had burned. The label had not. The locker door had warped. The stamp had not. The tender had died ugly. The proof had survived handsome.

"Too neatly," she said.

Fane recorded.

Arin looked at her. "But it may still be real."

"Yes."

"A planted real thing."

"Possibly."

"Or a staged fake thing built from real labels."

"Possibly."

Pellis said, "Welcome to salvage. Everything is both until it invoices."

They continued forward.

The cockpit blister held two more dead. Pilot and navigator, both strapped, both killed by decompression after impact or breach. The navigation console had been cut open. Not smashed. Cut. Someone had removed the local route buffer and then placed a broken shard of it in the pilot's lap.

Arin saw it first. "Why?"

Jaya said, "So we would ask why."

Fane recorded: ROUTE BUFFER REMOVED; FRAGMENT PLACED IN PILOT LAP; PLACEMENT NONFUNCTIONAL, PRESENTATIONAL.

Pellis scanned the cut. "Tool mark matches compact service cutter, not debris. Could be salvage after death."

"By whom?"

"Someone with access, time, and a poor understanding of subtlety."

Jaya looked through the cracked blister. Outside, Varrow Shale turned slowly. Far beyond it, Bell was a point of lawful force. Farther still, the *Mercy* sat in its arbitration lane under attention that might not be enough to save anyone.

"Find the missing buffer," she said.

They found the absence instead.

In the tender's small court cabinet, where minor custody receipts and tribunal ink cartridges should have been, someone had left a single physical strip of old-fashioned paper sealed in a transparent sleeve. Paper in space was either sentimental, legal, or bait. This one was all three.

Fane photographed before reading.

The strip said:

PRESERVE THE OBJECTION. DISCARD THE OBJECTOR.

No signature.

No timestamp.

No need.

Ixo's archive fork sentence returned to Jaya: They do not need to kill a witness if they can leave behind the version that stops objecting.

Arin whispered, "That is for Ixo."

Jaya said, "Or for me to think of Ixo."

"Does that distinction help?"

"It prevents obedience to the feeling."

Fane's voice was tight for the first time. "Admiral, this message should not exist here if the wreck predates Ixo's disclosure."

Jaya turned sharply. "Time."

Fane checked. "Tender death estimate from thermal and body condition: before the archive fork public summary by at least six hours. Paper strip could have been placed after death. But by whom, and when?"

Pellis looked toward the open hull seam. "Small drone could enter after breach. Reef style. Commission style. Anyone style if they were close."

Arin said, "Pell?"

Pellis smiled without humor. "Pell would add better typography and an invoice."

"Valech?"

Jaya did not answer.

The skiff alarm chimed: incoming directive.

Valech's seal.

Fane read it through external channel. "Commodore Valech orders immediate transfer of all recovered Quiet Authority materials from *Kestrel Mercy* to hazard-review cutter custody under emergency preservation."

Pellis said, "He has excellent timing."

Jaya looked at the visible stamp, the surviving label, the staged buffer shard, the paper strip, the bodies.

"No."

Arin's head turned. "Admiral?"

"No transfer."

"Under what authority?"

"Evidence contamination risk, active pursuit review, staged-scene indicators, and the fact that I am standing in the blood."

Fane did not write the last clause.

Jaya noticed. "Record it."

Fane did.

They packaged the wafer fragment without removing it from context first: full-scene scan, microphotography, residue capture, tool-mark model, thermal pattern, label analysis, stamp relation, body positions, and the paper strip. Every step mirrored to Bell, Tallow Crown, Gethsemane, Mercy's Edge stakeholder chain, Rill Sump, and, after one long breath, the *Mercy of Kaln*.

Arin objected. "Sending to *Mercy* before review may allow accused vessel to shape response."

Jaya said, "They are already part of the chain. Hiding staged evidence from the target of staging helps the stagehand."

"Objection maintained."

"Recorded."

Pellis looked almost approving. "You two should sell tickets."

The *Mercy* response came from Noor first:

MERCY RECEIVES WRECK SCENE SUMMARY. NOTES PHRASE PARALLEL WITH IXO PERSONAL TESTIMONY. REQUEST RAW MEMORY NOT BE USED TO FORCE CORRELATION. REQUEST PROTECTION OF DEAD TENDER CREW FROM SYMBOLIC OVERUSE.

Then Ixo:

I RECOGNIZE THE PHRASE AS CONCEPTUAL PARALLEL, NOT DIRECT QUOTE. DO NOT LET A PLANTED MESSAGE MAKE MY MEMORY SEEM STAGED AFTER THE FACT.

Then Tovin:

THE SCENE MAY BE DESIGNED TO MAKE EVERY TRUE THING LOOK MANUFACTURED.

Jaya read that three times.

"Damn him," she said softly.

Arin looked alarmed.

"For being right," she said.

The wreck creaked around them, metal adjusting to heat from their suits and the faint forces of attachment. Jaya moved back to the corridor where the first dead woman still held the seal lever.

"We are missing motive for the tender," Arin said. "Why destroy it? Why stage it?"

Pellis answered, "To feed us proof at the correct angle."

Fane said, "To link Quiet Authority, contractor chain, Ixo's memory, and Mercy suspicion into one object."

Arin said, "But if it points to Quiet Authority, it hurts Valech."

Jaya looked at the dead woman's hands.

"Unless the point is not to prove Quiet Authority," she said. "It is to make Quiet Authority proof inseparable from contamination. Every time we touch it, someone can say it was planted. Every time we ignore it, someone erases it. Every path becomes suspect."

Arin was silent.

Then: "What do we do?"

It was the first time in days he had asked without implying an answer.

Jaya stood in the wreck of a small ship that had carried filters and court ink and people who did not wake expecting to become a tactical lesson. She thought of Ravel Arm, of moral notes excluded, of evidence preserved badly, of Ixo's apology not yet evaluated, of Mer refusing a door because someone was under it, of a baby fever turning a corridor into a referendum, of Bell's cold weapons and the political heat eating their margins.

"We refuse the neat story," she said.

"Which one?"

"All of them."

Back aboard the skiff, Jaya recorded a field statement before leaving the wreck.

"This is Admiral Jaya Soth aboard the destroyed tender *Kestrel Mercy*. Preliminary inspection confirms possible Quiet Authority-related material and contractor-link evidence. The evidence is suspiciously preserved relative to surrounding damage. Several scene elements appear presentational rather than operational, including visible contractor stamp, surviving labeled wafer fragment, staged navigation buffer shard, and paper message referencing witness preservation logic. This does not exonerate any party. It does not authenticate the material as planted or genuine. It requires preservation under multi-party chain and forbids custody by any single interested authority."

She paused.

"The dead aboard this tender are not punctuation."

Fane stopped recording only after the last word had settled.

On return transit, Valech demanded live command access.

Jaya denied it.

He sent text:

YOUR REFUSAL TO TRANSFER HAZARD MATERIAL WILL BE NOTED.

She answered:

SO WILL YOUR HURRY.

Pellis read it and said, "I will deny enjoying that."

Arin sat across from Jaya in the skiff, helmet in his lap, face gray with more than suit fatigue. "Admiral."

"Yes."

"If the evidence is staged, then the frame is larger than Avar."

"Yes."

"If the evidence is genuine and staged, then whoever did this had access to real Quiet Authority material."

"Yes."

"If the evidence is fake, they understand the pattern well enough to counterfeit it under our noses."

"Yes."

He looked toward the wreck shrinking behind them. "There is no clean branch."

Jaya thought of Ixo's archive fork. "No."

"Then what are we pursuing?"

That question deserved a better room than a skiff and a better day than this one.

"Not certainty," she said. "Conduct."

Arin frowned.

"We pursue in a way that preserves enough conduct for truth to survive arrival."

He looked like he hated the answer.

Good. It was not a comforting answer.

The skiff latched to the *Austere Bell*. Before Jaya removed her helmet, Fane forwarded the full preliminary packet. Bell command received it. Tallow Crown received it. Mercy's Edge received it. Rill Sump received it and immediately posted:

DEAD ARE NOT PUNCTUATION. KEEP THAT LINE.

Lettem House:

WE WILL.

Pell:

Kestrel Mercy? EVEN THE WRECKS HAVE BRAND MANAGEMENT NOW.

Noor from the *Mercy* filed another coercive-framing note, visible to all. Jaya hoped the legal officer lived long enough to bury Pell under a bibliography.

Valech's public response arrived as Jaya stepped back into Bell command:

ADMIRAL SOTH'S FAILURE TO SECURE HAZARD MATERIAL UNDER CENTRAL AUTHORITY RISKS FURTHER EVIDENCE SPOILAGE. CONVERTED VESSEL ADVOCATES WILL EXPLOIT AMBIGUITY.

Jaya stood in her command well again. Too clean. Too bright. Necessary anyway.

"Status of *Mercy*," she said.

Taval answered. "Still in arbitration lane. Internal unrest resolved. Medical supplies prepared. No new deviation. Weapons cold."

"Keep them cold."

Arin looked at her.

"For now," she said, because command required honesty even when hope wanted shorthand.

The wreck packet opened across the central display. The dead tender's cut hull, preserved stamp, too-perfect label, paper message, and seal-lever body arranged themselves into panels.

Jaya forced herself to see the panels without letting them become the room.

Somewhere between the wreck and the accused warship, someone had tried to make truth look like bait.

That meant truth was still dangerous to them.

For the moment, Jaya took that as the only hopeful evidence she had.

Chapter 37: The Old Target Room

Tovin entered the Oathlock Gallery because the wreck made him want a weapon.

He did not tell himself otherwise.

That was the first safeguard.

The desire did not arrive as bloodlust, which would have been easier to condemn. It came as competence. It came as an old clean feeling under the ribs when Jaya's wreck packet resolved into too-neat evidence and Bell's bracket tightened and Valech's hurry showed through every formal phrase. It came as a map of possible actions drawn before conscience could catch up: blind Valech's cutter with a false coolant bloom, snap Bell's drone chain with a pressure burst, burn through the arbitration lane before Mercy's Edge could retract consent, use the four hull-defense cores as static traps, make the *Mercy* huge in every sensor until everyone smaller moved.

No firing. Not necessarily.

Just tactical clarity.

That was the dangerous part. His worst self did not ask to kill. It asked to solve.

So he requested access to the old target room under witness.

Noor said, "Absolutely not," then, "Why?" with no pause between, because they were a good legal officer and a tired human in the same body.

Mer listened to the request from command, face unreadable. The arbitration lane held. Bell remained cold. Medical supplies from Bell had arrived by remote package and been received under more witness than most treaties. Sori slept. Nima still refused visitors. Ixo had withdrawn from nonessential display and was not asked to attend.

"Purpose," Mer said.

Tovin answered in the only terms that would not rot. "The wreck packet made me generate tactical options that violate the covenant without initially feeling like violations. I need to document the reflex before it becomes invisible."

Sela, from engineering, said, "That is the worst good idea I have heard today."

Noor closed their eyes. "I hate that I agree."

Mother Kal, of course, said, "Go ugly or do not go."

They went ugly.

Access team: Tovin Rees, Captain Mer Anik, Noor Venn, Sela Marr, Mother Kal, Yaret from hull, Daro from environmental systems, Bell remote observer by text only, Ixo absent by request with right to review later, passenger council observer Havel because the mutiny had earned inconvenience, and Teacher Aven because children were not present but the ship had finally accepted that education began before explanation.

Tovin objected to Havel at first.

Noor looked at him. "You asked to document invisible reflex. Do not choose only witnesses who already trust your horror."

He accepted that.

The Oathlock Gallery opened with the same sequence as before: witness seals, local cameras, Bell receipt, no power path, no fire-control handshake, no diagnostic writes, no sealed board access. Sela made Tovin read the prohibitions aloud again.

"No offensive power path."

First seal.

"No ammunition handling."

Second.

"No mount actuation."

Third.

"No fire-control handshake."

Fourth.

"No diagnostic that writes to sealed combat boards."

Sela paused with her hand on the last lock. "Add: no tactical simulation using live pursuit geometry without dead-box isolation."

Noor wrote it.

Tovin repeated, "No tactical simulation using live pursuit geometry without dead-box isolation."

"Add: no dead-box output to navigation, engineering, or command without review."

He repeated it.

"Add," Mother Kal said, "no admiring yourself."

The room turned toward her.

She looked back. "You think that is funny because it is not technical. Put it in plain words."

Noor, after a moment, wrote:

NO AESTHETIC OR COMPETENCE-BASED VALIDATION OF HARMFUL TACTICAL OUTPUT.

Mother Kal snorted. "Lawyers ruin everything. Acceptable."

Tovin repeated that too.

The old target room opened.

It was dark, as always. Darkness had become part of its restraint. The main targeting wall remained sealed and dead. The cable throats stayed cut. The stations were covered in witness glass and warning tape. The air smelled like old metal and newer shame. On the far bulkhead, the covenant line waited:

A weapon sleeps only while every hand agrees not to wake it.

Noor set the dead-box slate on a portable stand. It had no link to ship systems, no output path, no ability to send anything but a human-readable transcript through a second review device. Sela had physically removed three ports and looked happier for it.

"Input set," Noor said. "Wreck packet summary, current public pursuit geometry generalized by delay, Valech cutter position generalized, Bell bracket generalized, arbitration lane constraints generalized, ship damage profile generalized. No live tactical feed."

Havel folded his arms. "Generalized means you can pretend it is harmless."

Tovin answered, "Generalized means it cannot steer the ship."

"Can it still tempt you?"

"Yes."

Havel did not look reassured. Good.

Mer said, "Proceed."

Tovin stood before the dead-box and let himself become the thing he feared under witness.

He did not need old consoles. That was the revelation and the shame. The room helped, but the machinery was not the source. His body remembered command geometry. His eyes grouped constraints without permission. Bell above and aft. Valech's cutter near Saint Ordinance. Mercy's Edge moral exclusion. Arbitration lane public tether. Passenger unrest. Ixo custody risk. Wreck evidence staged. Pell opportunistic relays. Damaged forward shoulder. Four inert hull-defense cores. Old magazine throat dead. Broken Buoy repaired. Varrow Shale fan.

The answer formed quickly.

Too quickly.

He spoke it because silence would have been vanity.

"Option one: create a false thermal emergency in medical ring to compel Bell to widen bracket and permit humanitarian drift. Unacceptable because it uses passenger medical fear, repeats Valech's biological sorting logic, and may trigger real evacuation risk."

Noor recorded.

Havel stared.

Tovin continued.

"Option two: vent noncritical coolant through port-forward scar to mimic loss of vector control, drift toward shale edge, force Bell to choose rescue posture over pursuit. Unacceptable because it creates debris risk, deceives Bell and Mercy's Edge, and makes restraint dependent on fraud."

Sela's face had gone flat. She knew exactly how it could be done.

"Option three: use witness-cover hazard around hull-defense beads to request neutral disposal window, then exploit disposal geometry to mask course correction. Unacceptable because it converts residual war material into maneuver cover."

Yaret whispered something profane.

"Option four: allow Havel's passenger council to publicly request transfer again at a timed moment, using Bell's non-exploitation commitment to suspend bracket. Unacceptable because it weaponizes passenger fear and makes dissent a tactical asset."

Havel's arms dropped.

Tovin made himself look at him.

"I thought of it in less than a second."

Havel's face drained. "I did not ask to be useful like that."

"I know."

"Do you?"

"Yes."

The word was not enough. He let it be not enough.

Mother Kal said, "Continue."

Tovin turned back to the dead-box.

"Option five: bait Valech's cutter into overcrossing Bell's preservation boundary by releasing partial wreck-analysis contradiction, then use Bell-Valech conflict to widen route. Unacceptable unless disclosure is independently required, because timing truth for tactical advantage can still contaminate truth."

Noor stopped writing for half a beat, then resumed.

Mer watched him with command attention and something like grief.

"Option six," Tovin said, and felt the old part of him smile.

He hated that most.

"Use the *Mercy*'s hull shadow deliberately. Announce inability to reduce mass pressure near Mercy's Edge unless Bell opens a corridor. This would force local stakeholder and pursuit authority into negotiation under public fear. Unacceptable because it uses the ship's historic coercive effect as leverage after naming that effect as harm."

Mother Kal's cane struck the deck once.

Not approval. Marking a body found.

The room seemed colder.

Sela said, "How many more?"

Tovin looked at the dead-box. "Many."

"How many are good?"

"All of them, tactically."

Noor's voice was quiet. "Say the other half."

"All of them are corrupting."

Havel sat down on the floor without asking. Teacher Aven lowered herself beside him.

Mer said, "What is the lawful option?"

"Continue arbitration lane. Preserve wreck packet under multi-party chain. Request Bell medical supplies without transfer. Maintain no-fire around Mercy's Edge. Prepare for authenticated review. Refuse single-authority custody. Accept that Bell may still disable us."

"Tactical value?"

"Poor."

"Moral value?"

Tovin almost answered too quickly. He stopped.

"Not pure. It still keeps everyone aboard under risk they did not individually choose. It still uses public witnesses as shield against worse authority. It still depends on Jaya spending political capital. It still leaves Ixo useful. It still leaves passengers afraid."

Mer waited.

"But it does not make any of those facts into a hidden weapon."

Noor wrote that down.

The old target room remained dead. Tovin was sweating.

Bell's observer line printed:

BELL RECEIVES DEAD-BOX RESTRAINT REVIEW. ADMIRAL SOTH REQUESTS PERMISSION TO ADD PARALLEL NOTE.

Mer looked at Tovin.

"Granted," he said, though it was not his authority alone.

Jaya's note appeared:

PURSUIT COMMAND HAS MIRROR TEMPTATIONS: USE CIVILIAN FEAR TO COMPEL HEAVE-TO, USE PASSENGER MEDICAL DISTRESS TO JUSTIFY BOARDING, USE MACHINE-WITNESS VULNERABILITY TO SIMPLIFY EVIDENCE, USE STAGED WRECK TO FORCE CENTRAL CUSTODY. BELL RECORDS THESE AS REJECTED OPTIONS AT CURRENT TIME.

Noor read it aloud.

Sela said, "At current time."

Mer said, "Honest."

Havel looked at the Bell text, then at Tovin. "So everyone in command is standing around thinking of ways to use us."

No one answered quickly enough.

Havel laughed once, hollow. "Good to know."

Teacher Aven said, "It is good to know. It is terrible to learn."

"That is teacher nonsense."

"Yes. I am working."

Tovin crouched so he was not looming over Havel. The act itself felt calculated. Everything did now. He did it anyway.

"I cannot promise not to think tactically," Tovin said. "I can promise to make the thoughts answerable before they become plans."

Havel looked at him. "That is what you offer passengers?"

"Yes."

"It is not enough."

"No."

Havel nodded slowly, not forgiving. "Put that in the passenger council terms."

Noor wrote:

PASSENGER COUNCIL TO RECEIVE NOTICE WHEN PASSENGER FEAR, MEDICAL DISTRESS, TRANSFER REQUESTS, OR DISSENT APPEAR IN RESTRAINT REVIEW AS POTENTIAL TACTICAL LEVERAGE.

Sela winced. "That will make every meeting worse."

Noor said, "Then it is probably governance."

Mother Kal walked to the main targeting wall. She did not touch it.

"This room did not become harmless because you made it confess," she said.

"No," Tovin said.

"And you did not become harmless because you confessed inside it."

"No."

"Good. Beware people who leave confession feeling clean."

The dead-box chimed: review complete, no output path, no tactical export. Sela physically removed the memory wafer and placed it in an evidence sleeve labeled:

DEAD-BOX RESTRAINT REVIEW - HARMFUL OPTIONS - NO OPERATIONAL USE

Havel watched the sleeve. "Can that leak?"

Noor said, "Everything can leak."

"Comforting."

"Not my field."

Mer ordered a summary released to the arbitration record, not the operational details. That was a difficult line. Too little, and the confession became private performance. Too much, and the harmful options became a menu. Noor wrote the summary like someone threading wire through a wound:

MERCY OF KALN CONDUCTED WITNESSED RESTRAINT REVIEW IN SEALED TARGETING SPACE AFTER WRECK PACKET TRIGGERED TACTICAL TEMPTATION. REVIEW IDENTIFIED MULTIPLE POTENTIAL COURSES THAT WOULD USE CIVILIAN FEAR, PASSENGER DISTRESS, RESIDUAL WAR MATERIAL, PURSUIT CONFLICT, OR HULL SHADOW AS LEVERAGE. ALL WERE REJECTED AS COVENANT-CORRUPTING. PASSENGER COUNCIL OBSERVER PRESENT. BELL PARALLEL NOTE RECEIVED. NO OPERATIONAL OUTPUT EXPORTED.

Ixo, absent by request, sent one line after reviewing the summary:

THIS IS A USEFUL CONFESSION. USEFUL IS NOT ENOUGH. KEEP IT REPEATABLE.

Tovin sat on one of the old observer steps after the room cleared.

Mer remained.

For a while they listened to the dead room not wake.

"You scared Havel," Mer said.

"Yes."

"You scared Sela."

"Yes."

"You scared yourself."

"Not enough."

She sat beside him, which captains should not do in old target rooms but did anyway when command had become too vertical.

"I read your old service file," she said.

"I assumed."

"Jaya wrote that you had exceptional ability to see nonlethal coercive geometry."

Tovin closed his eyes.

"I was proud of that once."

"You were good at it."

"I am still good at it."

"Yes."

He opened his eyes and looked at the targeting wall. Dead glass. Dead boards. Warning tape. The covenant line that had become insufficient but still necessary.

"That may be the worst thing about me," he said.

Mer shook her head. "No. The worst thing would be believing goodness made the ability disappear."

He almost wanted comfort from that. She did not offer it.

Outside, the arbitration lane held. Bell watched. Valech hurried. Pell joked. The passengers reorganized their fear into council procedure. Ixo rested somewhere in the ship's distributed body, not useful for twenty more minutes by explicit agreement. Jaya carried a wreck back into a clean command room and tried not to let panels make the dead into punctuation.

Tovin stood.

"Next chapter," he said, then stopped because the phrase made no sense aloud and too much sense in the mind.

Mer looked at him.

"What?"

"Nothing. The next problem."

"Which is?"

He looked at the old target wall and felt the answer gathering where all the harmful options had gathered: not as desire this time, but as dread.

"Proof," he said. "The only way to expose the packet may require touching systems that look like rearmament."

Mer's face hardened.

"Then we had better make this confession repeatable."

Chapter 38: Proof That Can Kill

Sela found the proof in a dead thing.

That was how she described it at first, which made Mer ask whether she meant a person, a system, or a metaphor. Sela, who had slept two hours in the last thirty and had come back from the slagged magazine with old explosive residue under her nails, stared at the captain as if categories were luxuries other ships could afford.

"A board," she said. "A dead board. Half dead. Old targeting gallery witness lattice, sealed under the Oathlock Gallery after the conversion. It was not removed because nobody trusted it enough to move it."

Noor looked up from the evidence table. "That is never the opening sentence of a good morning."

"It is not morning."

"Then that helps."

The evidence table in Auxiliary Review Two had become a smaller, colder version of a court. The wreck packet from *Kestrel Mercy* sat in three quarantined copies, none trusted. The forged material-witness order against Ixo sat beside them in a separate containment slate. Jaya's field statement from the wreck was pinned above the table because Noor had decided that the dead should be present in plain language, not reduced to checksum entries. Bell's legal mirror provided a narrow external clock. Tallow Crown's subordinate channel, embarrassed and unusually quiet, watched without comment.

Ixo attended in one body only. They had chosen a maintenance column at the far wall, its service lights dimmed to blue-white, a visible concession to the passengers who still flinched when a room filled with Ixo's distributed voice.

"Define witness lattice," Mer said.

Sela put the old schematic on the wall. It showed a shape that had once been a fire-control room pretending to be an argument. Human stations. Machine assists. Legal transcript taps. Calibration throats. Output locks. A hundred small paths that told a warship what it thought it was allowed to see.

"During the war, the targeting gallery did not only calculate solutions," Sela said. "It recorded how the solution became lawful. Sensor input, command authority, threat classification, predicted collateral, dissent markers, override language. The old admirals wanted a record that could defend them after the shooting."

"Convenient," Noor said.

"Yes. But convenience leaves fossils."

Tovin stood with his arms folded, saying nothing. He had not returned to the Oathlock Gallery after the review. The room had stayed in him anyway.

Sela enlarged a portion of the schematic. "The packet that frames us uses a command-throat signature. It imitates the Mercy's old fire-control grammar well enough to fool shallow review. But the wreck packet also carries timing residue from the thing that generated it. The current ship cannot read that residue because we removed the combat interpreter. The archive fork cannot read it without turning Ixo into the sole expert witness, which Valech will attack. Bell cannot read it because Bell never had our throat geometry."

"And the dead board can," Mer said.

"The witness lattice can. If we power the calibration side only."

Noor leaned back.

"Say the bad part."

Sela did not look away. "The power path is shared with the old targeting gallery wake circuit."

Nobody moved.

In a quiet ship, a breaker closing could sound like a decision. In the *Mercy*, even unclosed breakers had political weight. They had voted against rearmament. They had promised passengers that the ship would not become what Valech said it already was. They had told Bell, Mercy's Edge, Rill, Tallow Crown, and every anxious station listening that old war material would remain dead.

Now Sela was asking them to touch a circuit named in the old manuals as part of targeting readiness.

"Weapon power?" Mer asked.

"No."

"Mount power?"

"No."

"Launch authority?"

"No."

"Could it be represented as targeting activation?"

Sela's mouth tightened. "Yes."

Noor rubbed both hands over his face.

"Could it be represented that way by an honest observer who does not know our internals?"

"Yes."

Tovin finally spoke. "Could Valech represent it that way before the truth finishes crossing the lane?"

"He will," Sela said.

Ixo's maintenance column made a small mechanical sound as one of its lenses focused.

"The proof is technically available," Ixo said. "It is socially lethal."

Mer looked at the blue-white lights. "Lethal to whom?"

"To restraint, if handled privately. To my standing, if I become the interpreter. To your standing, if the ship wakes a targeting circuit under pursuit. To Valech's accusation, if the witness lattice confirms the packet used a command sequence the *Mercy* could not have generated after demilitarization."

"And to people?" Havel asked from the doorway.

No one had invited him to Auxiliary Review Two. That was no longer how the ship worked. Passenger council observers had standing when passenger fear could be used as leverage. Tovin had made sure that sentence became procedure before shame could cool into an anecdote.

Mer nodded him in.

Havel stayed by the door. He had slept badly. Everyone could tell. The old gardener's hands shook when he held the frame, not from age alone.

"And to people?" he repeated.

Sela answered him because it was her circuit. "If the constraints fail, it could wake a partial target environment. Not weapons. But a target environment. The ship would see like it was preparing to fire, even if it could not fire."

Havel looked at Tovin. "That is rearmament in the room where my fear lives."

Tovin accepted the sentence without defense. "It may be."

Mer turned her head.

He had learned, in the old target room, to mistrust fast distinctions when they favored him.

"It may also not be," he said. "That is the problem. A weapon can be restored by intent, by power, by access, by doctrine, or by public fear. We can prevent the first three with hardware. We cannot prevent the fourth by saying a better word."

Noor pointed at him. "That sentence goes in the protocol."

"No," Tovin said. "It goes in the warning."

Mer looked at the schematic for a long time.

"Build me a proof protocol," she said. "Not a permission slip. A thing someone outside this ship can hate honestly and still verify."

They took forty minutes and made the ugliest honest document Noor had ever drafted.

It began with the accusation against themselves.

MERCY OF KALN PROPOSES TO ENERGIZE A SEALED TARGETING GALLERY WITNESS LATTICE FOR THE LIMITED PURPOSE OF TESTING WHETHER THE AVAR BREAK WRECK PACKET CONTAINS COMMAND-THROAT RESIDUE IMPOSSIBLE FOR THIS SHIP TO HAVE GENERATED AFTER DEMILITARIZATION. THIS ACTION WILL LOOK LIKE PARTIAL TARGETING WAKE TO OUTSIDE OBSERVERS. THE RISK IS MATERIAL, POLITICAL, AND MORAL.

Then came the constraints.

No weapon mount power. No magazine bus. No launch path. No active range solution. No navigational cue export. No target designation. No authority transfer from the lattice to command systems. Physical breaker removals filmed before activation. Passenger council observer present. Bell external clock present. Mercy's Edge and Rill invited to witness. Tallow Crown main channel invited, subordinate mirror excluded unless authenticated. Ixo limited to checksum comparison after the lattice produces a human-readable trace. Abort authority held by Captain Mer Anik, Restraint Officer Tovin Rees, Engineer Sela Marr, Passenger Council Observer Havel Iren, and Bell Austere Review, any one sufficient.

Noor added one line after a long silence:

SUCCESS DOES NOT AUTHORIZE FURTHER SYSTEM RESTORATION.

Havel read it twice.

"Put that in larger letters," he said.

Noor did.

Mer sent the protocol before Sela touched the first breaker.

The lane answered like a room full of people reaching for weapons they had promised not to draw.

Mercy's Edge accepted observer status and warned that the station would increase evacuation readiness during activation. Rill sent two auditors and a bitter note that said a weapon's shadow could not be audited after it killed trust. Bell acknowledged the protocol and assigned a red confidence marker until physical locks were visible. Tallow Crown main channel remained slow but present, its old tribunal machine clearing dust from whatever passed for memory.

Valech answered in twenty-one seconds.

MERCY OF KALN ANNOUNCES INTENT TO ACTIVATE TARGETING SYSTEMS WHILE UNDER LAWFUL PURSUIT. ALL CIVIL AUTHORITIES SHOULD TREAT THIS AS CONFIRMATION OF FAILED DEMILITARIZATION. STAND CLEAR OF ARBITRATION LANE.

Jaya answered six seconds after him, not on his channel.

BELL REVIEW HAS NOT CONFIRMED REARMAMENT. ALL UNITS HOLD FIRE ABSENT WEAPON POWER, ACTIVE TARGET DESIGNATION, OR HOSTILE MOVEMENT. RECORD ALL SENSOR READINGS. DO NOT COMPLETE COMMODORE VALECH'S SENTENCE FOR HIM.

Tovin read that twice before letting himself breathe.

Mer did not smile. "She bought us minutes."

"She bought herself an inquiry," Noor said.

"Then use the minutes."

They moved to the Oathlock Gallery with more witnesses than the room had seen since it was sealed.

Sela walked first, carrying a breaker tray in both hands. Behind her came Mer, Tovin, Noor, Havel, Mother Kal, Aven from the passenger council, two crew techs with body cameras, and one medical aide because fear had become an engineering variable. Ixo remained outside the room by agreement. Bell's transcript scrolled on a portable slate held by Noor. Mercy's Edge watched through a delayed clean feed. Rill watched with legal disgust. Jaya watched from a command room full of officers who probably hated that she had not yet fired.

The old target wall waited in darkness.

Sela stopped before the covenant line.

"I am going to remove three physical jumpers," she said for the record. "They connect the witness lattice to target solution export, mount cue export, and command authority return."

She removed them slowly. Each came out with a click that seemed too small for the anger gathered around it.

Noor photographed the empty sockets. Havel stepped close enough to see, then stepped back.

"I confirm visible removal," he said, voice thin but steady.

Bell's slate printed: BELL OBSERVER CONFIRMS VISUAL REMOVAL. CONFIDENCE RED TO AMBER-RED. CONTINUE.

Sela placed the jumpers in a transparent evidence box and handed it to Aven.

"If I ask for these back without a full council note," Sela said, "you may hit me with the box."

Aven stared at her.

Noor said, "That is not a formal power."

"Then make it one later."

Tovin stood beside the old restraint station. He did not touch it. The handrests remembered another shape of him, and he did not owe the room nostalgia.

"Warning for the record," he said. "This activation may produce visible tactical graphics. Their appearance does not mean the ship can fire. Their appearance does mean the ship is asking an old war instrument to testify. We are responsible for that choice."

Havel swallowed.

"Passenger council records objection and consent to witness," he said. "Not consent to rearm."

Noor added it.

Sela opened the calibration sheath. The breaker beneath was not large. It was almost disappointing: a pale ceramic thumb set in a cradle of old metal, tagged with five different warnings from five different eras of regret.

"On my count," Mer said. "Abort words remain open."

No one spoke them.

"Three. Two. One."

Sela closed the breaker.

The Oathlock Gallery woke like something remembering pain in a language it had not used for years.

Light ran across the wall in thin lines. Not red, not weapon red, not the old arterial glare of fire-control readiness, but a dim amber that touched each dead panel and then withdrew from most of them. Three stations tried to boot and failed because their jumpers were gone. One old speaker coughed static until Sela killed its audio path with a hand switch. The floor trembled once as the witness lattice took clock from the ship and then from Bell.

Outside, alarms crossed the arbitration lane.

Noor's slate filled.

VALECH BROADCAST: TARGETING WAKE CONFIRMED.

BELL: NO WEAPON POWER OBSERVED.

MERCY'S EDGE: CIVILIAN SHELTER STATUS INCREASED.

RILL AUDITOR: GRAPHICS VISIBLE. CONTINUE ONLY IF OUTPUT REMAINS SEALED.

JAYA: ALL UNITS HOLD.

The target wall drew Avar Break.

Not the massacre. Not bodies. Not the tender wounds. A geometry only: ships as pale marks, gates as slow wounds in space, debris as gray silence. Then the wreck packet appeared as a second layer, trying to make the *Mercy* occupy a firing posture it had never held.

Sela's voice was barely above a whisper. "Lattice accepted packet."

"Can it compare?" Mer asked.

"It is comparing."

The wall split into three columns.

OLD MERCY THROAT.

CURRENT MERCY SEALED STATE.

PACKET CLAIM.

Lines ran between them, hundreds of small legal nerves. Some matched. Too many matched. The old warship had taught its enemies well by existing long enough to be studied. Tovin felt Havel's breathing change as the first matches appeared.

"Say the bad facts," Havel demanded.

Tovin did.

"The packet knows old Mercy grammar. It knows enough to imitate our command cadence. It knows enough that an outside court could believe it if they only compare surface form."

More lines turned amber.

Then one line turned white.

The wall froze.

Sela stepped forward.

"There."

Noor's voice sharpened. "Explain."

"Command priority echo."

"In words that survive court."

Sela dragged the trace wider. The white line became a ladder of time stamps.

"The packet claims the *Mercy* generated a priority echo before it received the sensor condition that would have allowed that echo. Old fire-control systems could anticipate, but they still had to receive the classification seed. This trace puts command before classification."

Ixo spoke from the corridor, kept outside but listening through the agreed channel.

"That matches Ravel Arm precursor behavior."

Mer lifted a hand. "Limited role."

Ixo stopped.

Sela nodded, grateful and annoyed. "The ship's own old witness lattice can say this without Ixo. The packet uses a command-first structure. The *Mercy* after demilitarization cannot generate it. The *Mercy* before demilitarization would have marked it as emergency override, not standard convoy threat. Whoever made this knew our throat shape but used a different doctrine."

"Quiet Authority," Noor said.

"Do not write conclusion yet," Tovin said. "Write: command-first structure consistent with unauthorized emergency authority template."

Noor wrote exactly that, angry at the caution because it was correct.

The wall moved again.

The white line forked.

A second trace emerged under the packet claim, thinner and harder to read. It was not a command sequence. It was an accounting stub.

PARDON REEF ESCROW INDEX: SPINE-SEVEN / PELL-LOCK / SAINT ORDINANCE PRESERVATION HOLD.

For one second nobody understood it.

Then Noor did.

"Pell," he said.

Havel turned toward him. "What does that mean?"

Noor had the look of a man realizing a witness had been in the room for days wearing a joke as armor.

"It means part of the forged packet touched a salvage escrow ledger before or during packaging. Not our ledger. Pardon Reef's. Pell's operation."

Mer's eyes narrowed. "He made it?"

"No conclusion," Tovin said again, though he wanted one badly. "The packet passed through or borrowed an escrow index tied to Pell's Saint Ordinance preservation claim. Could be contamination. Could be planted. Could be brokered access."

"Could be the price he tried to sell us," Noor said.

Sela checked the lattice load. "We need a live ledger comparison. The trace is too small."

Noor was already opening a channel.

Pell answered with his face too cheerful and his background too dark.

"Captain," he said. "Counselor. If this is about my outstanding moral invoice, I am afraid my office hours are under review."

Noor sent him the trace.

Pell's smile did not vanish. That was worse. It stayed exactly where it was and lost all temperature.

"Where did you get that?" he asked.

"From the warship you thought was too ashamed to look at itself," Noor said.

Pell looked past the pickup, not at anyone in the room.

"You powered the old gallery."

"Under witness," Mer said.

"Then Valech is already writing your obituary."

"And you are about to preserve your ledger," Noor said. "Publicly. Multi-party chain. Now."

Pell's eyes returned to the channel.

"No."

Havel made a small sound.

Noor leaned closer to the slate. "Pell, if that trace is planted, your ledger clears you. If it is contamination, your ledger explains it. If you brokered access, your ledger keeps you alive long enough to testify."

"You assume I own my ledger," Pell said.

The room changed.

Mer's voice dropped. "Who does?"

Pell's cheerful mask cracked just enough to show the man under it counting exits he did not have.

"Everybody who ever paid to survive after the war," he said. "Everybody who learned that law arrives late and bills early. Everybody who wanted a clean certificate for dirty salvage. Everybody with enough uniform left to make a contractor feel patriotic."

"Names," Noor said.

"Names are stored in the ledger."

"Then preserve it."

Pell laughed once. It sounded like a tool breaking.

"You woke a targeting gallery to prove a packet lied. Do you understand what everyone like me just heard? The dead ship can read old money. Valech will come for the ledger. Quiet hands will come faster. If I preserve it, I die with a clean chain of custody. If I burn it, I may die with ambiguity."

Mother Kal, who had said nothing since entering the room, stepped into view of the channel.

"Ambiguity does not bury bodies gentler," she said.

Pell flinched. Not much. Enough.

The lattice alarm chirped.

Sela looked down. "Heat limit in ninety seconds."

Mer made the decision before anyone could argue with physics.

"Export the trace to Bell, Mercy's Edge, Rill, Tallow Crown main, and Jaya. Seal local copy. Power down."

"We do not have the ledger," Noor said.

"We have proof enough to make him burn it," Mer said. "That means we have proof enough that it matters."

Sela began shutdown. The target wall collapsed column by column. Avar Break vanished. The packet claim vanished. The white command-first trace stayed longest, then folded into a checksum Noor watched like a man memorizing a face before burial.

Outside, Valech's broadcast grew louder.

TARGETING SYSTEM ACTIVATION CONFIRMED. MERCY OF KALN HAS VIOLATED DEMILITARIZATION TRUST. ALL CIVIL AUTHORITIES SHOULD WITHDRAW RECOGNITION OF ITS HUMANITARIAN STATUS.

Bell answered with a narrower truth.

BELL OBSERVED NO WEAPON POWER, NO TARGET DESIGNATION, NO LAUNCH PATH, AND NO HOSTILE MOVEMENT. BELL ALSO OBSERVED USE OF A SEALED TARGETING WITNESS LATTICE. CONFIDENCE REMAINS DEGRADED. EVIDENCE TRACE RECEIVED.

That was the best they were going to get.

The old room went dark.

For a moment, nobody spoke. The proof existed now. It had not saved them. It had merely changed the shape of danger from accusation to evidence.

Havel handed the jumper box back to Sela, then did not let go immediately.

"This still felt like rearmament," he said.

Sela met his eyes. "I know."

"It also felt like you were telling the truth."

"I know that too."

He released the box.

Noor's slate pinged.

The message from Pardon Reef contained no greeting, no joke, and no signature.

LEDGER UNDER SANITATION EVENT.

A second line followed.

IF YOU WANT HUMAN MEMORY, STOP ASKING MACHINES TO HOLD WHAT WE WERE PAID TO FORGET.

Noor read it aloud.

Tovin looked at Mer. The old target room had shown them a command that came before its excuse, money attached to a wound, and a witness about to choose fire over clarity.

Proof, he thought, could kill. Not because truth was violent by nature, but because every hidden power built defenses around its own exposure and called the defenses peace.

Mer turned toward the door.

"Noor," she said. "Find me a human witness before Pell finishes burning himself clean."

Chapter 39: Pardon Reef Burns Its Ledger

Pardon Reef did not burn like a station in distress.

It burned like an office destroying itself politely.

The first sign was not flame. There was no atmosphere enough for flame where Pell kept his serious records. The first sign was a cooling spike across three sealed vaults on the reef's inward spine, followed by a sudden bloom of heat in the external radiator vanes. Then came a legal notice, perfectly formatted, distributed to all registered contractors, creditors, insurers, port magistrates, and salvage partners within the reef's commercial radius.

SANITATION EVENT UNDER PRIVATE LEDGER AUTHORITY. NO PUBLIC SAFETY HAZARD DECLARED. NO PERSONAL RECORDS SUBJECT TO CIVIL CLAIM ARE AFFECTED. DELAYED ACCESS MAY APPLY. THANK YOU FOR YOUR PATIENCE.

Noor read it aloud in Command Review and made every sentence sound like evidence tampering.

"He thanked us," Sela said.

"That is how you know it is a crime," Noor replied.

Mer stood at the central display with both hands clasped behind her back. The *Mercy* had powered down the old target room, sealed the trace, and still somehow felt louder than before. Valech's accusation had traveled farther than the proof. Stations that had repeated Bell's caution had done so after repeating Valech's simpler line first: targeting systems activated. Demilitarization trust degraded. Humanitarian status questioned.

Truth moved with conditions. Fear moved clean.

Tovin watched the thermal map of Pardon Reef. The station was not large enough to be beautiful and too old to be efficient. Its hull had been patched with five generations of debt. Salvage arms nested around the core like broken fingers. On the inward spine, three vaults vented heat into black.

"Can we stop it remotely?" Mer asked.

Sela's answer was immediate. "No."

"Can Rill?"

"Rill can file an injunction so fast it makes lawyers feel young. It still needs an access key or local enforcement."

"Mercy's Edge?"

"No reach."

"Bell?"

Noor looked up from his slate. "Bell can declare spoliation of evidence. Bell cannot make Pell's vault obey a moral preference."

"Valech?"

Everyone looked at her.

Mer did not look away. "Answer the question."

Sela said, "Valech has cutters closer than we do."

Noor said, "Valech will seize the vault, call the sanitation proof of pirate conspiracy, and decide which parts of the ledger survive."

Tovin said, "Which may be what Pell expects."

The room sat with that.

Ixo spoke from a wall relay, not physically present by choice. "Pell's message implies machine-held records are vulnerable. It also implies human memory exists outside the ledger."

"He may be lying," Noor said.

"He is certainly shaping us," Ixo said. "Those are not mutually exclusive."

Mer turned to Noor. "Find the human."

"I am trying."

"Try visibly."

Noor understood the order. He opened a general evidence channel, signed with the *Mercy*'s current degraded status and Bell's observation hash, and sent the first message to Pardon Reef, Mercy's Edge, Rill, Tallow Crown main, and Jaya's command channel.

MERCY OF KALN REQUESTS HUMAN TESTIMONY REGARDING PARDON REEF ESCROW INDEX SPINE-SEVEN / PELL-LOCK / SAINT ORDINANCE PRESERVATION HOLD. DIGITAL LEDGER SANITATION EVENT IN PROGRESS. ANY PERSON WITH DIRECT KNOWLEDGE MAY PROVIDE STATEMENT UNDER MULTI-PARTY WITNESS PROTECTION. NO TRANSFER TO MERCY REQUIRED. TESTIMONY MAY BE HELD BY BELL, MERCY'S EDGE, RILL, OR TALLOW CROWN MAIN.

He added, after a moment, one line in plain speech:

IF YOU WERE PAID TO FORGET, YOU MAY STILL CHOOSE TO REMEMBER.

Havel read it from the passenger gallery feed and came down to Command Review without waiting for an invitation.

"That line will scare people," he said.

"Good," Noor said.

"Not everyone responds to fear by becoming brave."

Noor's jaw worked once. He was tired enough to answer badly and experienced enough to stop himself.

"You are right," he said. "Tell me how to say it."

Havel looked surprised. Then wary.

"You are asking me?"

"You are the reason passenger fear is no longer an invisible tool in this ship. Yes."

Havel approached the display slowly. He read the message again.

"The line makes memory sound noble. People who were paid to forget may not feel noble. They may feel bought, trapped, ashamed, or lucky to have survived."

Noor gave him the edit slate.

Havel did not touch it at first.

"I am not a lawyer."

"That may help."

Havel changed the line.

IF YOU WERE PAID, PRESSURED, OR FRIGHTENED INTO SILENCE, YOU CAN GIVE A LIMITED STATEMENT WITHOUT BOARDING MERCY OF KALN.

Noor read it, then nodded.

"Better."

"It is less pretty."

"Evidence usually is."

They sent the revised message.

Pardon Reef answered with static and invoices.

The public channels filled with arguments that had the shape of law and the temperature of panic. Valech declared the sanitation event proof of collusion between a rogue warship and criminal salvage brokers. Pell's commercial rivals declared themselves shocked by private-ledger irregularities they had probably envied for years. Three minor stations announced temporary suspension of *Mercy* refugee transfers pending clarification of humanitarian status. Mercy's Edge refused to suspend medical relay and was immediately accused of sheltering pirates by accounts that appeared too quickly and repeated each other too precisely.

Jaya sent no public statement.

That worried Tovin more than a bad one would have.

In the Oathlock Gallery, Sela's body cameras watched the sealed jumper box. In Engineering, crew whispered around breakers as if words might turn them back on. In the nursery compartment, Nima asked whether the old ship had nearly become a gun again, and no one answered in a way that satisfied anyone. In the chapel, Mother Kal added a temporary card beneath the half-revised memorial plaque:

PROOF MAY COST TRUST. DO NOT SPEND TRUST WITHOUT NAMING THE COST.

Tovin saw it on the internal feed and felt the sentence settle into him with the weight of an order he had not earned.

Noor's first human witness arrived as a refusal.

The woman would not give her name. She would not show her face. She connected through Rill, using a delay mask and a voice flattening filter that made every word sound exhausted by machinery.

"I know Pell-locked ledgers," she said. "I will not testify."

Noor did not lean forward. He had learned not to look hungry when a frightened person entered a record.

"Can you tell us why?"

"Because people who testify against ledgers become ledger entries."

"Who are you afraid of?"

"Everyone who knows what an index is."

"Do you know Spine-Seven?"

Silence.

Tovin watched the audio line tremble with breathing.

"I am not asking for a name," Noor said. "I am asking whether the index in our trace is real."

More silence.

Then: "Spine-Seven was a sink."

Sela, listening from Engineering, whispered a curse into the private crew channel.

Noor kept his voice even. "Define sink."

"A place to put costs no sponsor wanted visible. Hazard premiums. survivor hushes. illegal tow fees. post-battle access. reactor bribes. bodies sometimes."

Havel, present as passenger observer again, closed his eyes.

"Did Pell control it?" Noor asked.

"Pell administered pieces."

"Who controlled it?"

"You want a tidy villain because courts like tidy rooms."

"I want usable truth."

"Then use this: Pell's ledger is not a book. It is a market. If he burns his copy, other copies become more valuable. Anyone offering you a perfect surviving ledger is selling you a leash."

Noor marked the sentence.

"Will you give that under witness?"

"I just did."

"Under an identity seal?"

"No."

"Without identity, they will attack reliability."

"With identity, they will attack my door."

Noor looked at Mer.

Mer said nothing. Command could not make this clean by wanting it clean.

The witness cut the channel.

Rill certified that the connection had come from a registered postwar contractor berth and that the voice mask had not been altered during statement. Bell accepted the statement as anonymous human testimony, low-weight, independently relevant, insufficient for direct attribution.

Noor stared at the certificate.

"Low-weight," he said. "Everybody wants courage to arrive notarized."

Tovin said, "Would you testify in her place?"

Noor gave him a hard look, then let it go because the question deserved better than pride.

"No," he said.

The second witness arrived as a death report that had not yet happened.

Pell transmitted it himself.

For all his theater, he did not look drunk, panicked, or triumphant. He looked washed out by harsh station light. Behind him, the private office that had once framed his face with lacquered panels and salvage trophies had been stripped to bare insulation. Two small cases floated behind his shoulder, tethered to a rail. One was marked with the code from the *Mercy*'s trace. The other bore no mark at all.

"Counselor," Pell said.

"Preserve the ledger," Noor said.

"You never flirt first."

"Preserve it."

"I have a proposition."

"No."

Pell sighed. "You wound the market."

Mer stepped into the pickup. "Pell."

That changed him. His eyes shifted, not away, but deeper into calculation.

"Captain."

"If you are going to ask for protection, ask."

"I am going to ask for usefulness."

"That is worse."

"Usually."

Noor cut in. "Your vaults are sanitizing. Your ledger may be the only live bridge between the forged Avar packet, Saint Ordinance, and whoever is wearing old authority under a new uniform. If you destroy it, you become part of the frame."

Pell smiled faintly.

"Counselor, I became part of other people's crimes long before your ship decided restraint needed paperwork."

"Then testify."

"To what?"

"Spine-Seven."

"A sink."

"Saint Ordinance preservation hold."

"A lock."

"Pell-lock."

"A joke that became a brand."

"Names."

Pell looked at the unmarked case.

"Names are expensive."

"People are dead."

"People being dead is why names are expensive."

Mer said, "If you bargain now, you lose me."

Pell's expression flickered. For the first time, Tovin wondered whether Mer's regard mattered to him. Not love. Not loyalty. Something poorer and therefore harder to dismiss: the desire to be thought salvageable by someone who had refused to buy him.

"I am not bargaining," Pell said. "I am explaining the trap. If I transmit the ledger, Valech can call it manufactured by a criminal under pressure from a rogue warship. If I keep it, Quiet Authority or whatever heir wears that name can come through the old payment channels and erase the people who touched it. If I surrender it to Bell, Bell becomes the battlefield. If I surrender it to Tallow Crown, the old tribunal wakes with a mouth full of money and war. If I burn it, everyone curses me and some people stay alive."

Noor said, "That is the argument of every man who hides evidence."

"Yes," Pell said. "Sometimes guilty men borrow real reasons."

The statement landed harder because it did not defend him.

Tovin moved closer to the pickup.

"What do you want to survive?"

Pell looked at him.

"That is an interesting question from a restraint officer."

"Answer it."

Pell's smile tried to return and failed.

"Three clerks. One pilot. A woman who carried Saint Ordinance manifests after the war because her children needed air ration credit. A cutter crew that moved bodies before the tribunal radius reached them. A boy who is not a boy anymore and should not be killed because he authenticated the wrong archive when he was seventeen."

"Names," Noor said softly.

"No."

Mer's voice was equally soft. "Then how do we protect them?"

Pell touched the marked case behind him. It rotated once, lazy in low station spin.

"I will burn the ledger. I will not burn the receipts."

Noor froze.

"Receipts are still records."

"Receipts are human-scale. They show that payment happened, not the entire market. They can prove the index existed. They can corroborate your trace. They do not give every hunter a map to every frightened door."

"Who holds them?"

Pell looked past the pickup again.

"Some of the people who were paid."

Havel spoke before Noor could.

"They kept proof?"

"Some kept shame," Pell said. "Some kept insurance. Some kept a reason not to die as a liar."

The unmarked case drifted closer to him.

"I have six receipt hashes and three contact paths. I will send them to Mercy's Edge, not to you."

Noor's anger returned. "Why not us?"

"Because your ship just woke a target room and made every coward with a badge feel prophetic. Because anyone who comes directly to you can be dismissed as pirate-adjacent. Because if I am going to do one decent thing by accident, I prefer it survive your reputation."

Mer accepted that without flinching.

"Mercy's Edge," she said.

"And Rill."

"And Bell."

"Bell gets timing proofs, not names."

"Tallow Crown main?"

Pell hesitated.

"Not yet."

"Why?"

"Because old courts remember old bargains."

Ixo's voice entered through the review channel. "That is an inference I can support without personal archive disclosure. Quiet Authority language has appeared in tribunal-adjacent systems before."

Pell's eyes moved toward the speaker.

"The machine has manners."

"The machine has standing," Noor said.

"That too."

The thermal bloom on Pardon Reef's vaults rose another degree.

Sela reported from Engineering. "Vault One is gone as a recoverable structure. Vault Two will cross physical memory failure in four minutes. Vault Three is being cooled from a separate loop."

Noor looked sharply at Pell. "Why save one?"

Pell's face went still.

"Because not all burning is mine."

The room understood that a second force was inside Pardon Reef.

Mer's hand moved to the edge of the display. "Who is on your station?"

"Contract inspectors."

"Whose?"

"They brought Valech's warrant language and no Valech uniforms."

"How many?"

"Enough."

Tovin felt the tactical answer form and rejected it before it finished. Acceleration window. Hull shadow. Threat posture. Rescue Pell as proof. Use the old ship's mass to frighten men who had frightened everyone else. It was a clean, ugly geometry.

He said, "We cannot go in with force."

Mer gave him one glance, approving nothing, hearing everything.

Pell laughed quietly. "I did not ask to be rescued."
