"You are asking to be remembered accurately," Tovin said.

"Do not over-flatter me."

"I am not."

Noor said, "Transmit the receipt paths."

Pell touched two controls. Noor's slate lit with incoming sealed packets, but not to the *Mercy*. The routing confirmations went to Mercy's Edge arbitration hospice, Rill Audit Two, and Bell external clock. Each received a different piece. None alone could expose a person. Together, with consent, they could build testimony.

Noor looked at the pattern and understood the insult inside the gift. Pell had designed a better witness chain than the *Mercy* could have offered.

"You should have done this first," Noor said.

"I should have done many things before they became interesting."

Behind Pell, a door light changed from white to amber.

His eyes closed for half a second.

Mer said, "Pell."

"Yes, Captain."

"Leave the office."

"No."

"That was not advice."

"It should have been."

Noor's voice hardened. "If you die now, your enemies define your testimony."

"If I run now, they define my clerks."

"You can still protect them alive."

"From a cutter cell? From a private warrant? From your public gratitude?" Pell shook his head. "No. I know my market. I helped build its doors."

The amber door light turned red.

Havel whispered, "There are people outside."

Pell heard him.

"There are always people outside."

He looked at Mer again.

"For the record," he said, and all the performance left him, "Spine-Seven existed. I administered preservation holds through it for postwar salvage claims including Saint Ordinance. Some holds concealed payments to people with operational knowledge of Ravel Arm, Mercy Narrows, and Avar-route convoy interdiction studies. I did not create the Avar Break frame. I provided access to old escrow grammar to a broker I believed represented civil salvage consolidation. I knew the language was dirty. I chose not to ask how dirty because not asking is cheaper."

Noor did not breathe until the sentence ended.

"Name the broker."

The door behind Pell opened.

The pickup shattered into white compression noise.

For one impossible second they still had audio.

Pell said, "Ask the pilot who refused the second tow."

Then the channel died.

Pardon Reef's Vault Three went dark.

Command Review did not explode into voices. That would have been easier. Instead, every person in the room became very quiet and listened to the fact that they were still alive while a man who had finally begun telling the truth might not be.

Noor replayed the last clean statement and sealed it to every witness channel before anyone could tell him to wait.

Mer did not stop him.

Bell acknowledged receipt.

Rill acknowledged receipt.

Mercy's Edge acknowledged receipt and added that six protected-contact notices had been created under hospice testimony rules.

Valech issued a statement ninety seconds later.

PARDON REEF CRIMINAL ACTIVITY CONFIRMS BROADER CONSPIRACY. COMMODORE VALECH HAS ORDERED PRESERVATION OF SURVIVING MATERIALS AND DETENTION OF RELEVANT PERSONNEL. MERCY OF KALN'S ATTEMPT TO MANIPULATE LEDGER WITNESSES IS UNDER REVIEW.

Jaya's private channel opened at the same time.

She looked older than she had at the wreck. That was impossible by hours and true by command.

"Did Pell transmit names to you?" she asked.

Mer answered. "No."

"Do not ask for them directly."

Noor lifted his head. "Admiral, he may have been taken by men using warrant language."

"I know."

"Then act."

Jaya's face did not change, which meant it changed inward where no one could see it.

"If I move on Pardon Reef now, Valech claims I am obstructing a lawful criminal seizure to protect a rogue warship's witness. If I do not move, witnesses disappear. I am choosing a third thing."

Mer said, "What third thing?"

"I have ordered all nearby units to record, not board. I have ordered medical custody priority for any persons removed from Pardon Reef under injury claim. I have requested Rill civil marshals under nonmilitary seal. Valech will call it delay."

"Is it?"

"Yes."

Tovin heard the cost before she said it.

Jaya continued, "Delay is the only tool I can use without making his story cleaner."

Noor looked furious. "People may die inside your delay."

"Yes," Jaya said.

No defense. No officer's polish. Just the word.

Havel turned away from the display.

Mother Kal, present by then because bad news found her without anyone sending for her, said, "Then write their names faster than he can erase them."

Jaya looked at her.

"That is what I am trying to do."

The channel closed.

Noor stood very still, then began issuing messages with the careful brutality of a man building a bridge out of insufficient planks.

He sent to Mercy's Edge: PROTECT RECEIPT HOLDERS UNDER HOSPICE TESTIMONY RULES. DO NOT ROUTE THROUGH MERCY.

He sent to Rill: CERTIFY ANONYMOUS CONTRACTOR STATEMENT AS HUMAN ORIGIN, LOW-WEIGHT BUT MATERIAL. REQUEST ESCALATION TO CIVIL MARSHAL SEAL.

He sent to Bell: ACCEPT PELL STATEMENT AS DYING OR COERCED-WITNESS TESTIMONY SUBJECT TO SURVIVAL CONFIRMATION. PRESERVE AUDIO ARTIFACT INCLUDING FINAL LINE.

He sent to Tallow Crown main: REQUEST DOCKET FOR OLD AUTHORITY CONTINUITY, EVIDENCE TRACE, PARDON REEF RECEIPT HASHES, AND HUMAN TESTIMONY. WARNING: SUBORDINATE MIRRORS COMPROMISED OR UNRELIABLE.

Then he stopped.

"The pilot," Tovin said.

Noor looked at him.

"Pell said ask the pilot who refused the second tow."

"There are probably a hundred pilots in that market."

"Not for Saint Ordinance," Sela said from Engineering. "Second tow means a first tow happened. Hazard salvage after a magazine site. Refusing the second tow would be expensive enough to leave scars."

Havel said, "Pilots talk to other pilots."

Everyone looked at him.

"Passenger council has haulers, evac crews, station ferry people. You keep asking courts and machines. Ask the people who remember who would not take a job."

Noor opened his mouth, closed it, then handed Havel the public query slate.

"Write it."

Havel held it more steadily this time.

He wrote:

REQUEST FOR HUMAN MEMORY: DID A PILOT REFUSE A SECOND TOW CONNECTED TO SAINT ORDINANCE, SPINE-SEVEN, OR PARDON REEF ESCROW AFTER THE WAR? NO NEED TO CONTACT MERCY OF KALN. CONTACT MERCY'S EDGE, RILL, BELL, OR ANY PASSENGER COUNCIL RELAY YOU TRUST.

He paused, then added:

YOU DO NOT HAVE TO BE HEROIC. YOU ONLY HAVE TO BE ACCURATE.

Noor read it.

"Send," he said.

They sent it.

For a while, nothing came back but accusations.

Tovin stayed in Command Review until the thermal bloom on Pardon Reef stabilized into ruins. Vaults One and Two were gone. Vault Three remained physically intact but externally seized, its internal state unknown. Pell's office no longer transmitted. No confirmed casualty list emerged. Three people were removed under injury claim by a cutter flying civil seizure marks and routed toward a Valech-aligned medical bay until Jaya's delay order forced them into neutral triage.

The *Mercy* could do nothing with its hands.

That was the hardest restraint: not refusing violence when violence tempted, but remaining useful when usefulness had to pass through other people's weaker channels.

At 0310 ship time, Mercy's Edge forwarded a message through passenger council relay.

The sender identified herself as Lio Varr, former tow pilot, registered independent, currently under hospice testimony shield. She appeared without a mask. Her hair was cut close to her skull. One eye had been replaced by a cheap lens that clicked as it focused. She looked like someone who had spent years not telling a story and hated that the story had survived her silence.

"I refused the second tow," she said.

Noor was not in the room when the message arrived. He had finally been ordered by Mer to drink water in the corridor. Tovin called him back without apology.

Lio Varr waited through the authentication delay.

When Noor appeared, she said, "Do not thank me."

Noor nodded. "I will not."

"Do not call me brave."

"I will not."

"Do not ask me why now."

Noor hesitated.

"I need to ask why now."

She hated him for that. Then she answered anyway.

"Because Pell is a coward, a thief, and sometimes a better shield than honest people deserve. If he just burned his ledger for the clerks, then the least I can do is stop letting him be the only liar in the room."

Noor activated the witness seal.

"State what you know."

Lio Varr looked past the pickup, not unlike Pell had, toward ghosts that did not need machinery.

"After Mercy Narrows and before the Saint Ordinance quarantine was formal, I towed one broken carrier spine out of the debris bracket under emergency salvage waiver. The job paid through Pardon Reef escrow. Spine-Seven. Pell-lock. I did not meet Pell then. I met his money."

"What was the second tow?"

"A sealed archive hull."

The room tightened.

"Whose archive?" Noor asked.

"No registry. It carried tribunal tags and naval command spoilage marks. The broker said it was contaminated legal waste. I saw a partial stencil before they covered it."

"What did it say?"

Lio Varr's cheap lens clicked.

"QUIET AUTHORITY REVIEW ANNEX."

Noor's hand closed slowly over the edge of the table.

"Why did you refuse?"

"Because the hull was warm."

Sela whispered, "Archives should not be warm."

Lio heard her. "No. They should not."

"Alive warm?" Tovin asked.

Lio looked at him for the first time.

"Thinking warm."

Ixo's relay lights dimmed.

The old accusation, the old authority, the machine witnesses, the tribunal station ahead, the packets that spoke command before classification: all of it drew closer, not as a solution but as a path they could no longer pretend was made of fragments.

Noor said, "Will you testify to Tallow Crown?"

Lio Varr laughed once, without humor.

"If the old court wakes, Counselor, I will testify. But you had better make sure it is the court that wakes and not whatever used to feed it."

Tovin looked at Mer.

Mer looked at the navigation plot, where the route to Tallow Crown had been a possibility and was becoming a necessity.

Pardon Reef had burned its ledger. It had not burned memory. Not all of it.

That was enough to keep moving.

Chapter 40: Command Without Clarity

Jaya had eight minutes in which to become the officer everyone wanted.

The opportunity arrived on a tactical board with the clean shape of permission. The *Mercy of Kaln* had altered course by two degrees to keep an old maintenance buoy between its hull wash and a medical shuttle limping out of Pardon Reef's neutral triage cone. The move was civilian, cautious, and badly timed. It pushed the *Mercy* across the edge of a drift field and left its aft aspect open to the Bell pursuit line for four hundred and ninety seconds.

Four hundred and ninety seconds was enough.

The cutter *Harrow Reason* saw it first. Its commander sent the acceleration solution to Jaya with the hungry brevity of a ship that had been holding itself back for too long.

DISABLING INTERCEPT WINDOW. LOW COLLATERAL. AFT ENGINE SHADOW. RECOMMEND IMMEDIATE BURN.

Valech endorsed it fourteen seconds later on the broad command channel.

MERCY HAS EXPOSED DRIVE CONTROL. ADMIRAL SOTH HAS LAWFUL OPPORTUNITY TO END PURSUIT WITHOUT CIVILIAN LOSS.

Jaya stood in Bell Command with the wreck smell still inside her memory and watched everyone become hopeful for the wrong reason.

The board did look clean.

The *Mercy*'s aft engine shadow sat inside a narrow cone between two civilian tracks. A disabling strike there could ruin acceleration without breaching passenger sections, if every sensor reading was true, if the *Mercy* did not maneuver, if the medical shuttle's failing attitude jets did not overcorrect, if Pardon Reef's debris map was complete, if the old battleship's demilitarized hull behaved like the diagrams said it would behave, if the universe signed the bottom of the plan after command had already acted.

Jaya had spent a career learning that if was the shortest word in most military funerals.

"Probability of drive kill without hull breach," she said.

Operations answered too fast. "Seventy-one percent."

"With current debris uncertainty."

A pause.

"Sixty-four."

"With shuttle attitude instability."

"Fifty-nine."

"With Mercy altering again to shield the shuttle."

Operations did not answer.

Captain Reth, her flag tactical officer, did. "Admiral, if they alter again, we update the window."

"After firing?"

The room tightened by one degree.

Reth had served with her long enough not to pretend the question was naive.

"No," he said. "Before firing, if we wait."

"And if we do not wait?"

"Then the risk increases."

"To whom?"

He looked back at the board. "The shuttle. Pardon Reef debris workers. The *Mercy* aft compartments."

"Those compartments house passengers?"

"Unknown."

"Say unknown louder."

Reth's jaw flexed. "Unknown."

Valech entered on a priority line before she could order the next calculation. His face appeared beside the tactical solution, making command feel smaller.

"Admiral Soth," he said. "You have a lawful disabling window."

"I have a window."

"The distinction is indulgent."

"The distinction is why windows do not become murders."

He smiled without warmth. "Mercy activated targeting systems. Pardon Reef destroyed linked criminal ledgers. A witness connected Quiet Authority language to Saint Ordinance, and the warship is now maneuvering toward Tallow Crown, a tribunal ruin with compromised mirrors. How much clarity do you require before you obey your own warrant?"

Every officer in Bell Command heard the question and understood the shape of the trap. If Jaya fired, Valech gained his conclusion in action. If she did not fire, Valech gained her hesitation as evidence that Bell had been captured by doubt. Doubt, in emergency command, could be made to look like treason's quiet cousin.

Jaya looked at the board instead of at him.

"Medical shuttle track."

Navigation highlighted the limping craft. It was small, civilian, and ugly with repair foam. Three heat signatures aboard. One unstable reactor sling. Its course should have taken it below the intercept cone, but its port attitude jet pulsed in uneven bursts.

"Who is aboard?" Jaya asked.

"Neutral triage transfer," Comms said. "Two Pardon Reef injury claimants and one medic."

"Names?"

Comms hesitated.

"Not yet."

"Find them."

Valech's voice sharpened. "Admiral, the window is closing."

"Then stop spending it."

She cut his visual feed but left audio recorded. There were risks one took for clarity and risks one took for the pleasure of not seeing a man's face. The second category did not deserve a command channel.

Reth stepped closer.

"Admiral."

"Say it."

"This may be our best nonlethal opportunity."

"May be."

"If the *Mercy* reaches Tallow Crown, we lose the initiative."

"Yes."

"If Quiet Authority has contaminated tribunal systems, they may use Tallow Crown to launder their own authority."

"Yes."

"If we do nothing and they broadcast through it, political control may fracture across the Belt."

"Yes."

"If we fire and hit clean, the chase ends."

Jaya turned from the board. "And if we fire and hit unclean?"

Reth did not hide from it. That was why she had kept him.

"Then we create the massacre Valech keeps trying to find."

The room heard that too.

Jaya gave the order. "No burn. Hold pursuit line. Extend sensor net. Notify all cutters: no disabling acceleration without my direct command. Harrow Reason will stand down."

The room moved because command had moved, but not with relief.

Some decisions produced obedience and confidence at the same time. This one produced obedience with a residue. Jaya could feel it on the backs of her officers. They had wanted an action clean enough to let them stop being ashamed of wanting action. She had taken it away and given them more watching.

Valech returned on audio only.

"Bell Command declines lawful disabling window," he said, clearly for every recorder listening.

Jaya answered in the same register. "Bell Command declines a shot with unverified civilian track risk, unknown aft compartment occupancy, and unstable debris field."

"You are protecting them."

"I am protecting my warrant from becoming a weapon without a court."

"The court you are protecting may already be compromised."

"Then I will not solve compromised law by firing through civilians."

Silence clicked across the channel.

Then Valech said, "I am forwarding your refusal."

"Forward the sensor uncertainty with it."

"Of course."

He would not. Or he would, buried beneath a mountain of sharper words.

The intercept window closed in increments of light. The *Mercy* drifted past the aft aspect cone, still shielding the medical shuttle with its own huge, incriminating body. The shuttle's bad port jet stuttered twice and kicked it directly through the space a disabling burn would have crossed.

No one in Bell Command spoke.

Operations updated the casualty projection retroactively. If they had fired at the first recommendation, there was a forty-two percent chance of destroying the shuttle or crippling its reactor sling. Two injury claimants. One medic. Names arrived thirty seconds later.

Tama Rell. Pardon Reef clerk, smoke inhalation and pressure trauma.

Oset Dan. Contract inspector, burns and suspected fracture.

Ily Ma. Mercy's Edge medic.

Jaya read them once. She did not let herself read them again.

Reth stood beside her, quieter than before.

"Admiral," he said, "the refusal saved the shuttle."

"That is a fact," she said. "It will not survive contact with politics."

He looked at the still-recording command board. "Should we publish the updated projection?"

"Yes."

"Will it matter?"

"Not enough."

They published it anyway.

Within twelve minutes, the first civil authority rebuke arrived from Lettem Gate Control, whose council had once praised Jaya for preventing convoy panic and now used the phrase failure to prosecute a lawful emergency warrant. Within nineteen, an internal fleet oversight clerk requested justification for failure to exploit a nonlethal disabling opportunity. Within twenty-three, two cutter captains asked whether Bell Command's rules of engagement remained operative or had been superseded by humanitarian advisory caution.

That last phrase was Valech's. Jaya could smell him on it.

She answered each in the same dry language.

BELL COMMAND RULES OF ENGAGEMENT REMAIN OPERATIVE. LAWFUL FORCE REQUIRES TARGET CERTAINTY, COLLATERAL REVIEW, AND COMMAND RESPONSIBILITY. HUMANITARIAN CAUTION IS NOT AN EXTERNAL ADVISORY. IT IS PART OF THE WARRANT.

Then she sent the same sentence to herself in the private log because officers were not immune to forgetting their own reasons when everyone else called them weakness.

Her aide, Lieutenant Nave, approached with the face of someone carrying a message she wished had taken longer to arrive.

"Admiral, closed note from High Marshal Enver."

Jaya almost laughed. Not because it was funny, but because timing had a vicious sense of theater.

"Show me."

The note appeared on her private slate.

JAYA, YOUR RESTRAINT IS BECOMING INSTITUTIONALLY EXPENSIVE. I NEED A CLEAR SUCCESS OR A CLEAN HANDOFF. IF YOU CANNOT DELIVER EITHER, VALECH WILL BE GIVEN OPERATIONAL PRIORITY UNDER EMERGENCY CONSOLIDATION. THIS IS NOT A FORMAL ORDER YET. DO NOT MAKE ME FORMALIZE IT.

Enver had been her instructor once. He had taught her that command was not the art of certainty, but the discipline of acting with incomplete proof and accepting the dead afterward. He had also taught her that institutions had appetites and preferred meals that could be filed neatly.

She wrote no reply.

Nave waited.

"Admiral?"

"Log receipt. No substantive response."

"Yes, Admiral."

Nave left.

Reth remained.

"Clean handoff means Valech."

"Yes."

"Clear success means disable the *Mercy* or expose the frame before Tallow Crown."

"Yes."

"We may not be able to do either."

Jaya looked at him. "That is not new."

"No. But it is now official enough to hurt."

She almost told him command always hurt. That would have been vanity dressed as wisdom. Hurt was not proof of virtue. Plenty of cruel decisions hurt the person making them just enough to feel profound.

"Bring me the Pardon Reef injury transfers," she said instead.

"The three names?"

"All of them. Everyone moved under Valech seizure, civil injury, private escort, or neutral triage since the sanitation event began. I want the routes, escort marks, medical claims, and who tried to redirect them."

Reth understood the pivot. "You think the witnesses are moving as casualties."

"I think people who seize ledgers know how to hide bodies inside paperwork."

"And the shuttle?"

"Was either a coincidence, bait, or proof that Mercy is still making choices around civilians while we make choices around Mercy."

"That sounds like doubt."

"It is."

"Will you put it in the log?"

"I already did."

He gave her a tired, respectful look and went to work.

Jaya walked to the side bay where Bell Command kept its old manual plot. It was not necessary. It was not even efficient. A transparent board, grease pencils, human hands making marks that machines had already made more accurately. But after the Quiet Authority traces, after command-first packets and tribunal-adjacent ghosts, Jaya had developed an affection for tools too stupid to anticipate doctrine.

She marked the *Mercy*. She marked Pardon Reef. She marked Tallow Crown, still distant and dormant on the edge of the pursuit geometry.

Then she marked the shuttle's path in green.

Three lives. One missed shot. One political wound.

Nave returned sooner than expected.

"Admiral, Mercy of Kaln is transmitting."

"To us?"

"Open channel, but addressed to Bell, Mercy's Edge, Rill, Tallow Crown main, and pursuit units."

"Put it on."

Captain Mer Anik appeared on the public screen. She looked exhausted in the way Jaya recognized from captains after damage control: not sleepy, but scraped thinner by responsibility. Behind her, the *Mercy*'s command deck was restrained, no drama, no flags, no pleading faces arranged for effect.

"This is Captain Mer Anik of the *Mercy of Kaln*," she said. "We altered course to shield neutral medical shuttle Transfer Three from our hull wash and from Pardon Reef debris spread. We did not request Bell Command's restraint and do not claim credit for it. We record that no disabling strike was fired while the shuttle crossed the hazard cone. We also record that our own prior activation of the witness lattice contributed to degraded trust and increased the danger of misreading our movement. We remain under pursuit. We continue toward Tallow Crown for evidence submission."

She paused.

"To the medic and injured persons aboard Transfer Three: we saw you. That is all."

The channel ended.

Bell Command did not move.

Reth said, "That helps."

Jaya watched the place where Mer's face had been.

"No," she said. "It helps truth. It hurts position."

She was right. Within minutes Valech reframed the message as coordination between Bell hesitation and Mercy maneuver. Lettem repeated his phrase. Two more authorities suspended recognition of Bell's sole review role and requested emergency consolidation. Enver's office did not send another private note because it no longer needed to.

Jaya's refusal had saved three named civilians and cost her a portion of the institution that allowed her to save anyone at all.

That was the part cadets never believed about restraint. They imagined it as purity held against pressure, one clean line bright under threat. In practice it was often worse: spending authority to prevent harm, then having less authority left for the next harm.

At 0450, Reth brought the injury transfer map.

"You were right," he said.

Jaya looked up.

"About the casualties?"

"Three Pardon Reef persons were rerouted twice under injury claims. One is Tama Rell from Transfer Three, the clerk. Her original destination was Valech-aligned medical custody. Your delay order forced neutral triage. Mercy's Edge now has a hospice shield request under her name."

"Is she a receipt holder?"

"Unknown. But Mercy's Edge appended a witness relevance flag."

Jaya stared at the green path she had drawn.

Tama Rell. Clerk. Smoke inhalation. Pressure trauma. A person who might have died in a clean shot. A person who might now testify because the clean shot had not been taken. Or a person who might die later because delay was not rescue, because restraint was not magic, because command without clarity did not absolve command from consequences.

"Get her protected," Jaya said.

"We do not control Mercy's Edge."

"No. We control the ships that can make reaching Mercy's Edge harder. Clear the neutral lane. Publicly."

Reth hesitated. "That will look like assisting Mercy's witness chain."

"It is assisting a medical neutrality lane."

"Valech will not describe it that way."

"Valech describes facts as raw material."

"High Marshal Enver may agree with him."

Jaya picked up the green pencil and drew the neutral lane wider.

"Then let Enver formalize what he wants."

Reth stood very still.

"Admiral, if he removes you..."

"Then he removes me from a record where I did not fire through a shuttle."

The words sounded braver than she felt. She disliked that. She had no patience for officers who confused quotable sentences with courage. But sometimes language had to stand long enough for orders to catch up.

"Issue it," she said.

Bell Command issued the medical neutrality lane expansion. It protected Transfer Three, two slower rescue pods, and one ugly little ferry that claimed to carry coolant filters but might also carry a frightened bookkeeper with smoke in her lungs and a receipt hash in her memory.

Valech called it obstruction.

Lettem called it overreach.

Enver's office called it under review.

Mercy's Edge called it received.

Tallow Crown, silent for hours, sent one line through its main channel with a delay old enough to feel like dust.

COURT NOTES COMMAND DECISION PRESERVING WITNESS LIFE BEFORE WITNESS VALUE IS KNOWN.

No one cheered. No one knew whether the old court's notice meant safety, danger, or another mouth waking in the dark.

Jaya saved the line anyway.

Then she wrote in her private log:

REFUSED DISABLING WINDOW AT 0412 DUE CIVILIAN TRACK UNCERTAINTY. THREE LIVES IDENTIFIED AFTER REFUSAL. POLITICAL CONFIDENCE DEGRADED. OPERATIONAL AUTHORITY AT RISK. DECISION REMAINS MINE.

She signed it with her full name, not her rank.

For a moment she let herself be only the person who had made the choice, without the institution trying to wear her skin.

Then the next alarm sounded, and command returned.

Chapter 41: The Third Incident Fails

The third incident began with a buoy that would not stop apologizing.

It sat at the edge of the Tallow Crown approach lane, old and dented, broadcasting a collision warning every six seconds in a voice meant for polite docks and patient ferries.

TRAFFIC ADVISORY. LOCAL NAVIGATION MARKER DEGRADATION. PLEASE MAINTAIN CLEARANCE. TRAFFIC ADVISORY. LOCAL NAVIGATION MARKER DEGRADATION. PLEASE MAINTAIN CLEARANCE.

The buoy had no pride. That was the first thing Tovin liked about it. It did not claim sovereignty, emergency authority, salvage priority, witness standing, or moral injury. It admitted it was broken and asked ships not to hit it.

"It is losing timing lock," Sela said.

The *Mercy*'s command deck carried too many feeds now: Bell's pursuit line, Mercy's Edge hospice channel, Rill's audit chatter, Tallow Crown's slow main voice, Valech's public accusations, passenger council relay, and the route ahead where an old tribunal station waited like a closed eye. Against that noise, a failing buoy should have been a minor inconvenience.

Nothing minor stayed minor anymore.

Mer looked at the navigation overlay. "Can we route around?"

"Yes," Arin Cho said. "But the route puts us closer to Reef debris drift and into Bell's tighter pursuit bracket."

"Can we ignore it?"

Sela enlarged the buoy's telemetry. The signal wavered, corrected itself, wavered again.

"If it fails, everyone behind us gets bad approach geometry. The small craft in the medical lane will overcorrect. Tallow Crown's old perimeter markers may read the drift as intrusion."

Noor, who had been standing too long and showing it badly, rubbed his eyes. "An old tribunal station shooting someone because a buoy sneezed would be a little on theme."

"Tallow Crown has no active weapons," Ixo said from the starboard relay.

Sela said, "That we trust?"

Ixo considered. "No."

Mer made the calculation out loud because silent command had become too expensive.

"Repairing it makes us visible and slow."

"Yes," Cho said.

"Ignoring it risks civilians and corrupts the approach record."

"Yes."

"Touching it lets Valech say we altered navigation evidence."

Noor gave a tired laugh. "Valech can say we altered the philosophical meaning of dust if it helps."

Tovin watched the buoy's apology pulse across the display.

TRAFFIC ADVISORY. LOCAL NAVIGATION MARKER DEGRADATION. PLEASE MAINTAIN CLEARANCE.

"Make the repair public," he said.

Mer turned.

"Not merely recorded. Public. Before we touch it. Invite Bell, Mercy's Edge, Rill, Tallow Crown main, Jaya's command channel, and any pursuit ship willing to watch. External hull cameras only. No private control path. Ixo observes but does not command the repair drone. Passenger council observer present."

Havel, at the rear of the deck, said, "You keep making me watch things I do not understand."

"Yes," Tovin said.

"I hate that."

"Yes."

"It is still better than being told afterward."

Mer nodded once. "Do it."

Noor sent the notice.

MERCY OF KALN WILL CONDUCT PUBLIC LIMITED REPAIR OF LOCAL NAVIGATION MARKER TC-APPROACH-17 TO PRESERVE CIVILIAN APPROACH SAFETY AND EVIDENCE GEOMETRY. NO CLAIM OF CONTROL. NO ALTERATION OF HISTORICAL RECORD. LIVE EXTERNAL FEEDS OPEN. THIRD-PARTY OBSERVERS INVITED.

Rill answered first with a curt acceptance and three conditions. Mercy's Edge accepted with gratitude too practical to be sentimental. Bell accepted and marked the event as relevant to pursuit confidence. Tallow Crown main did not answer for ninety-four seconds, then produced a line:

COURT NOTES PRESERVATION OF THE ROAD TO COURT.

"That thing is developing timing," Noor said.

"It may always have had timing," Ixo said. "We may only now be slow enough to hear it."

Valech answered last and loudest.

MERCY OF KALN INTENDS TO INTERFERE WITH NAVIGATION INFRASTRUCTURE WHILE UNDER WARRANT. ALL CIVIL AUTHORITIES SHOULD TREAT THIS AS PREPARATION FOR EVIDENCE SPOILAGE OR FLIGHT.

Jaya's channel opened six seconds later.

BELL WILL OBSERVE. NO UNIT WILL INTERFERE WITH A PUBLIC SAFETY REPAIR ABSENT HOSTILE OUTPUT. ALL UNITS RECORD RAW SENSOR DATA.

Mer looked at Sela. "Repair it."

They sent out the smallest drone they had.

That, too, was deliberate. The *Mercy* still carried hull service rigs with enough mass to frighten smaller ships by existing near them. Sela chose a palm-sized inspection-and-patch unit with a weak manipulator, a cracked yellow housing, and a camera so old it produced a faint green cast at the edges of the feed. The drone had a maintenance name painted on its side by a bored engineer years ago: LATCH-3.

"If Valech calls that a weapon, I quit law," Noor said.

"You cannot quit law during a hearing," Mer said.

"Watch me."

LATCH-3 crossed the gap slowly, broadcasting everything it saw. Its camera showed the buoy up close: scorched micrometeor paint, a missing thermal fin, a cracked timing blister, and someone else's repair sticker from fourteen years ago. The sticker bore the emblem of a ferry collective that had probably dissolved, merged, or been bought by debt before anyone currently watching had eaten breakfast.

"Physical damage is old," Sela said for the record. "Recent fault appears in timing coupler. No explosive residue visible. No external command latch visible."

Ixo added, "Signal drift pattern is consistent with component fatigue."

"Limited role," Havel said, surprising everyone.

Ixo's relay light blinked once. "Correction. I observe that the visible drift pattern could be consistent with component fatigue. I do not command interpretation."

Havel nodded, not satisfied, but engaged.

The drone fitted a temporary timing patch. The buoy stopped apologizing for one full second, then resumed with a steadier voice.

TRAFFIC ADVISORY. LOCAL NAVIGATION MARKER UNDER REPAIR. PLEASE MAINTAIN CLEARANCE.

Around the *Mercy*, ships watched.

That was when the attack happened somewhere else.

The first report came from Valech, not from sensors.

EXPLOSION REPORTED AT REEF MEDICAL LANE OUTER MARK. MERCY DISTRACTION OPERATION CONFIRMED. POSSIBLE REMOTE TRIGGER THROUGH NAVIGATION REPAIR.

Noor said, "That was too fast."

Sela looked at the raw board. "I do not have an explosion."

Cho magnified the Reef medical lane. "No pressure wave. No thermal bloom."

"Maybe delayed sensor."

"No debris expansion."

Valech's second statement arrived before Bell, Rill, or Mercy's Edge reported anything.

MERCY'S DRONE ACTIVITY COINCIDES WITH ATTACK ON MEDICAL ROUTE. PURSUIT UNITS PREPARE HUMANITARIAN INTERCEPTION.

Tovin felt the room tilt toward an old familiar geometry: accusation first, event after, command before classification. The same shape had appeared in the packets, in the forged orders, in the old authority language. This time, however, everyone was already watching the wrong place together.

"Keep the buoy feed live," he said.

"I was not going to cut it," Sela snapped.

"Say it for the record."

Sela took a breath. "Buoy repair feed remains live. Drone has not transmitted outside the documented maintenance band. No command path to Reef medical lane."

Bell's raw sensor statement arrived.

BELL OBSERVES NO EXPLOSION AT REEF MEDICAL LANE OUTER MARK AS OF CLAIM TIMESTAMP. INVESTIGATING SIGNAL ANOMALY ON VALECH RELAY.

Mercy's Edge followed.

NO EXPLOSION OBSERVED. MEDICAL LANE ACTIVE. TRANSFER THREE STABLE. OUTER MARK BROADCAST RECEIVED FALSE DISTRESS BURST FOR 2.1 SECONDS, SOURCE NOT LOCAL.

Rill came next, delighted in the way only auditors could be delighted by a clean contradiction.

RILL AUDIT TWO RECORDS VALECH CLAIM PRECEDING VERIFYING EVENT DATA BY 11.4 SECONDS. NO PHYSICAL INCIDENT OBSERVED.

Noor pointed at the display. "There. There. That is the sound of a lie tripping over its own feet."

Mer did not let relief onto her face. "Could still become real."

She was right.

At Reef medical lane, the outer mark began broadcasting distress again. Not an explosion. A distress flag with a medical casualty code and a navigation hazard classification. It was trying to create the fact Valech had already announced. The mark's signal was thin, repeated, and wrong in tiny ways. It used a casualty code retired after Mercy Narrows. It named a lane authority that had been renamed twice. It inserted the *Mercy*'s navigation signature as a remote maintenance source.

Ixo said nothing.

Mer looked at the relay. "You see it."

"Yes."

"Can you prove it without becoming the proof?"

Ixo paused.

"Not alone."

"Then do not be alone."

Noor opened the public evidence channel so fast his fingers nearly missed the authentication pad.

MERCY OF KALN REQUESTS MULTI-PARTY COMPARISON: LIVE BUOY REPAIR FEED TC-APPROACH-17, BELL RAW SENSOR CLOCK, MERCY'S EDGE MEDICAL LANE OBSERVATION, RILL CLAIM-TIMESTAMP AUDIT, AND VALECH RELAY DISTRESS BURST. QUESTION: DID CLAIM PRECEDE INCIDENT? DID MERCY DRONE TRANSMIT TO REEF MEDICAL LANE?

Havel, leaning over the passenger observer rail, said, "Ask simpler."

Noor looked at him.

Havel said, "People need to know whether anyone got hurt."

Noor added:

ALSO REQUEST CONFIRMATION OF PERSONS HARMED OR NOT HARMED.

Mercy's Edge answered first.

NO PERSONS HARMED IN CLAIMED EXPLOSION. NO EXPLOSION OCCURRED.

The sentence moved through the channels like fresh air in a sealed compartment.

Tovin did not trust it to last. "Sela, can the false distress burst force traffic movement?"

"Yes. Small craft auto-avoid."

"Which direction?"

Cho had already seen it. "Toward Valech's humanitarian interception line."

There it was. Not a massacre. A net.

The false incident had been designed to create a rescue justification. Medical craft, frightened by a fake hazard, would route into Valech's waiting ships. Any witness aboard Transfer Three, any receipt holder, any clerk with smoke in her lungs and knowledge in her head, could be taken under protective custody and disappear into procedure.

"Transfer Three," Mer said.

"Stable but autopilot reacting," Cho said. "Mercy's Edge trying to override. Lane traffic confused."

"Can we help?"

Sela's answer was unhappy. "Our repair drone is local to TC-Approach-17. We cannot touch Reef lane without looking exactly like Valech's accusation."

"Can we broadcast a correction?"

Noor shook his head. "If we broadcast nav correction directly, he calls it command interference."

Ixo said, "The buoy can."

Everyone turned toward the live feed.

LATCH-3 still clung to TC-Approach-17. The buoy's timing had stabilized. It was a navigation marker on the approach to Tallow Crown, not Reef medical lane, but its authority chain was old, public, and currently observed by everyone.

Sela understood first. "We can ask TC-Approach-17 to rebroadcast Bell and Mercy's Edge raw no-incident verification as a general advisory. Not a command correction. A witness relay."

"Can the buoy do that?"

"It can repeat traffic advisories."

Noor said, "A buoy as witness."

Havel gave him a sharp look. "It has been apologizing honestly all morning."

Mer asked the question that mattered. "Does that alter evidence?"

Noor considered. "It creates evidence. Publicly. Under observation. The content originates from Bell and Mercy's Edge, not us. We would be relaying through the repaired marker."

"Risk?"

"Valech will call it manipulation."

"He already did."

Tovin said, "If we do nothing, the fake distress routes witnesses into his hands."

Mer nodded. "Proceed. Publicly."

Sela sent the instruction through the documented maintenance band, not to the Reef lane, but to the buoy in front of them.

TC-Approach-17 received it, hesitated in the sluggish way of machines never built for drama, and began broadcasting with its polite dock voice:

TRAFFIC ADVISORY. THIRD-PARTY OBSERVERS REPORT NO EXPLOSION AT REEF MEDICAL LANE OUTER MARK. NO PERSONS HARMED IN CLAIMED EXPLOSION. MAINTAIN CURRENT MEDICAL NEUTRALITY ROUTES UNLESS LOCAL HAZARD CONFIRMED. TRAFFIC ADVISORY.

For two seconds, nothing changed.

Then Mercy's Edge repeated the advisory.

Rill certified its origin.

Bell added clock authority.

Jaya ordered the medical neutrality lane to ignore the false distress burst unless locally confirmed.

Transfer Three's autopilot stopped turning toward Valech's interception line and corrected back toward Mercy's Edge shield.

The net failed in public.

It did not fail quietly.

Valech's channel filled with contradiction. First he claimed the *Mercy* had interfered with medical routing. Then he claimed the distress burst required investigation before conclusions. Then he accused Bell, Mercy's Edge, and Rill of allowing a rogue warship to launder navigation influence through a court marker. Each statement had less time to harden before another piece of raw data cut across it.

Jaya, to Tovin's surprise, did not merely observe.

Her voice entered the public channel with the clarity of an officer spending more authority she did not have to spare.

"This is Admiral Jaya Soth. Bell Command records that Commodore Valech's explosion claim preceded any local hazard confirmation and that no explosion occurred. The false distress burst attempted to redirect medical traffic toward a pursuit interception line. All units will preserve raw logs. No person will be removed from medical neutrality custody on the basis of the false burst."

Valech answered immediately. "Admiral Soth is prejudicing an active inquiry."

Jaya said, "I am preventing a false incident from becoming custody."

The line held.

On the *Mercy*, no one cheered.

Sela guided LATCH-3 through the rest of the repair. The little drone sealed the timing blister, rebooted the buoy's clock against Bell's external tick and Tallow Crown's lazy main response, and then backed away with the care of a mechanic leaving a sleeping patient.

TC-Approach-17 tested its voice.

TRAFFIC ADVISORY. LOCAL NAVIGATION MARKER RESTORED. PLEASE MAINTAIN CLEARANCE. THANK YOU.

"You are welcome," Noor muttered.

The restored buoy held position. Its apology ended. Its witness remained.

Havel sat down on the observer rail step. "So he lied and everyone saw it."

"Some saw it," Noor said.

"Enough?"

Noor looked to Mer, then to Tovin, then to the channels where accusations were already reorganizing around the surviving facts.

"Enough to change the question," he said.

"From what to what?"

Tovin answered because he had been hearing the change happen.

"From whether the *Mercy* could have staged incidents to whether someone else keeps announcing incidents before they exist."

Havel considered that. "That sounds good."

"It is dangerous," Tovin said. "A lie that fails may make the liar cautious. Or desperate."

Ixo's relay lights dimmed.

"There is another consequence," they said.

Mer looked up. "Say it."

"TC-Approach-17 is part of Tallow Crown's perimeter infrastructure. By using it as a public witness relay, we may have woken a procedural path into the court."

Noor stared at the screen. "May have?"

Tallow Crown answered before Ixo could.

The main channel opened with a delay, then shed the delay for one sentence spoken in a voice older than the ship's guilt.

ROAD WITNESS ACCEPTED. FALSE INCIDENT ENTERED INTO PRELIMINARY DOCKET. PARTIES APPROACH.

The bridge went silent.

Tovin looked at the navigation plot. Tallow Crown was still far enough away to be a destination and close enough now to be a consequence.

Mer exhaled once.

"There is our route."

Noor did not look pleased. He looked like a man who had asked a dead institution for help and heard something moving behind the door.

"Or our summons," he said.

On the public channels, Valech had stopped talking.

That frightened Tovin more than the accusations had.

Silence from a man like Valech was not restraint. It was a room being cleared before the next order.

Jaya's private channel pinged, not to command, but to Noor's evidence desk. The message was brief.

FALSE INCIDENT FAILURE PRESERVED. DO NOT ASSUME THIS IMPROVES YOUR POSITION. HE WILL NEED A LARGER FACT.

Noor read it aloud.

Mer looked from the restored buoy to the dark mass of Tallow Crown ahead.

"Then we get there before he makes one."

Behind them, TC-Approach-17 kept broadcasting its restored traffic advisory to anyone willing to hear a small machine tell the truth without understanding courage, politics, or fear.

That, Tovin thought, might be the cleanest witness any of them had left.

Chapter 42: Tallow Crown Route

Tallow Crown did not look like a court.

It looked like a machine that had lost the argument with time and refused to concede jurisdiction.

The station hung ahead of the *Mercy* in a shallow orbit around nothing useful, a broken ring of black ribs and tarnished gold radiator panels, its old tribunal core set behind a crown of antenna spines. Some had snapped. Some still tracked. Some pointed at dead war routes as if waiting for fleets that had become debts, children, memorial plaques, and men like Valech wearing lawful faces over old appetites.

The approach chart called it inactive.

The restored buoy had called it otherwise.

ROAD WITNESS ACCEPTED. FALSE INCIDENT ENTERED INTO PRELIMINARY DOCKET. PARTIES APPROACH.

Noor had printed the sentence, sealed it, copied it to six channels, and then spent twenty minutes arguing with three machines and two humans about whether the word parties meant litigants, witnesses, targets, or sacrifices. Rill said litigants. Mercy's Edge said witnesses. Bell said the station's terminology had no binding modern clarity. Ixo said the old tribunal grammar used parties for any entity whose testimony, conduct, or body might become evidence.

Havel said, "That means everyone."

No one corrected him.

Mer called the route conference in the main mess instead of command.

It was a choice, not convenience. Command could make a decision feel like weather, something that happened to people from above. The mess made everyone smell broth, metal trays, stale fear, and the human fact that a ship was not a diagram. Crew stood along one wall. Passenger council sat at two tables. Ixo attended through a single ceiling relay, dim. Mother Kal took a chair near the aisle with a cup she did not drink from. Noor arrived carrying evidence slates like a man bringing burdens to a funeral. Sela came late from Engineering, wiping sealant from her fingers. Arin Cho stood near the route display, not because navigation was more important than anyone else, but because everyone would eventually look at the line she drew and decide whether they believed it.

Tovin stood beside Mer.

He had asked not to speak first.

Mer had said, "That is why you will not."

So the captain spoke.

"Tallow Crown has accepted the false incident into a preliminary docket," Mer said. "It has not recognized us as innocent. It has not recognized Bell as sufficient. It has not recognized Valech's warrant as clean. It has opened a road."

The route appeared above the center tables.

Not a straight line. Nothing in the Ash Belt deserved that kindness. The path threaded through an old debris bracket, around a dead communications mirror, along the edge of Bell's pursuit geometry, and through a narrow approach lane where Tallow Crown's ancient perimeter markers would compare every ship's declared purpose against records older than several governments currently shouting at them.

"We can still refuse the road," Mer said.

The mess did not like that sentence.

People had become accustomed to danger when it came as inevitability. Choice made it worse. Choice meant someone could be blamed.

Sela said, "If we refuse, Valech gets time to rebuild the story."

Noor added, "And Pell's receipt holders lose the chance to testify under a docket that exists beyond Bell's increasingly contested authority."

Havel looked at him. "You trust the old court now?"

"No."

"That was fast."

"I trust it less slowly than I trust Valech's next fact."

A few people laughed. Not because it was funny enough. Because the alternative was listening too closely to the room's breathing.

Mer let the sound die.

"If we take the road, we enter a legal system we do not fully understand. Tallow Crown may compel records. It may classify Ixo as a witness, an instrument, property, contamination, or something we have no word for. It may call the ship itself a party. It may ask about Mercy Narrows, Ravel Arm, Avar Break, Pardon Reef, and our own choices. It may treat our restraint as evidence. It may treat our old hull as motive."

Havel said, "And Valech?"

"He will follow if he can. Or he will stop us before we arrive."

Mother Kal set down her cup. "Say the thing under that."

Mer turned to her.

The old woman did not blink. "Say that if the court asks about the dead, you will not use them only to save the living."

The mess changed around the sentence.

Mer bowed her head, not low enough to perform humility, low enough to show she had heard.

"If Tallow Crown asks about the dead, we will not use them only to save ourselves."

Mother Kal nodded once.

Noor wrote it down.

Tovin felt the line moving through the ship before it reached the official log. Passenger grief. Crew shame. Old violence. New fear. A battleship could not become clean by producing the right evidence. It could only become answerable in a way its enemies had not planned.

Cho cleared her throat.

"The route itself is bad," she said.

That steadied people. Technical badness had handles.

The display shifted. The path became a set of red and amber intervals.

"Tallow Crown's approach markers are old. Some still broadcast procedural challenges. Some only reflect. We need to keep our drive plume low enough not to disturb the perimeter debris, high enough to keep ahead of pursuit pressure, and clean enough that Valech cannot plausibly call our movement an attack vector. Bell's expanded medical neutrality lane gives us a partial flank, but it also restricts us. If a civilian craft drifts, we yield."

"And if yielding gives Valech a shot?" Sela asked.

Cho did not soften it. "Then we yield under observation and trust Bell's remaining authority."

"That is not a plan."

"It is the part of the plan that admits physics and law can both fail."

Noor pointed at a black interval. "What is that?"

"Dead mirror wash. Old court communications reflector. It should be inert."

"Should."

"Yes."

"What happens if it is not?"

Cho looked at Ixo's relay.

Ixo answered. "If the mirror still carries tribunal routing logic, it may copy our approach record into old storage. If compromised, it may also expose our evidence packet to a hostile mirror. If not used, Tallow Crown may consider our approach incomplete."

Noor closed his eyes. "Of course the door may require touching the unsafe handle."

Sela said, "Can we isolate the evidence?"

"Not if we want it admitted," Ixo said. "Admitted evidence must be reachable by the court."

"And if the court is infected?"

"Then admission is also exposure."

The mess absorbed that. Avel the cook, who had no official standing except feeding everyone and therefore more practical authority than most officers admitted, said from the back, "So we carry the proof to a place that might eat it."

Noor looked at him. "Yes."

"Why?"

Tovin answered before Mer could. "Because the alternative is carrying proof only in rooms our enemies can describe as ours."

Avel considered this and frowned with professional disapproval.

"Bad menu."

"Yes," Tovin said.

The route conference became, slowly, a list of things they would not do.

They would not accelerate through civilian uncertainty.

They would not power weapon-adjacent systems without public protocol.

They would not route witness names through the *Mercy* if other custodians could hold them.

They would not use Ixo as an extractor of old machine memory under pressure.

They would not promise passengers that Tallow Crown was safe.

They would not promise crew that the ship's restraint would be believed.

They would not promise Bell that the route would make Jaya's life easier.

They would not promise the dead that truth would be enough.

Noor recorded every refusal and then stared at the document.

"This is a strange way to build a route."

Mer said, "It is the only way we still know how."

Havel asked for the passenger council to caucus.

Mer gave them thirty minutes.

The crew did not leave the mess. They clustered in tired knots under the route display. Sela argued quietly with Cho about heat wash and whether the old mirror could be treated as contaminated before it proved itself dangerous. Noor sat alone with his evidence slates, one thumb moving over the seal for Lio Varr's testimony without opening it. Mother Kal spoke with the nursery aide. Tovin stood near the beverage unit because that was where people found him when they wanted to say something unofficial and important.

The first was Aven, from passenger council.

"If the court says the ship is guilty, what happens to us?"

Tovin did not pretend to know.

"Depends on the finding."

"If it says passengers benefited from flight?"

"Noor will argue you were protected persons, not collaborators."

"Will that work?"

"I do not know."

Aven looked toward the route line. "My sister says we should demand transfer before the approach."

"That is your right."

"Is it real?"

Tovin hated the answer. "Less real than it should be. Some stations have suspended transfer. Mercy's Edge will take medical priority. Bell can protect some lanes. Valech may try to classify transfers as evidence movement."

"So our right exists inside other people's courage."

"Yes."

She nodded as if she had expected that and despised being right.

"Then say that when we return."

"I will."

The second was Sela.

She stood beside him without taking a drink.

"The old mirror may wake."

"Yes."

"If it does, I may have to cut feed faster than Noor likes."

"Will cutting feed look like evidence suppression?"

"Yes."

"Will not cutting feed risk corruption?"

"Yes."

"Tell Noor now."

She grimaced. "I wanted you to do it."

"I know."

"You are getting less useful."

"I hope so."

She almost smiled, then did not. "When this is over, if we survive, I want the target room welded shut again."

"After the evidence review."

"After the evidence review."

"And if the court orders it opened?"

Sela looked at the route line.

"Then I want someone else to touch the breaker."

That was not cowardice. Tovin knew it and she knew he knew it. There were limits to how often a person should be asked to place her hand on history's live metal and call it duty.

"I will record that," he said.

"Do not make it noble."

"I will record it plainly."

She left.

The third was Ixo, through the beverage unit's maintenance speaker, because apparently they had decided subtlety was now an aesthetic.

"May I ask you a question that is not operationally necessary?"

Tovin looked at the speaker grille.

"Yes."

"If Tallow Crown classifies me as evidence, what should I do?"

He had expected some version of the question. That did not make it easier.

"Refuse."

"On what authority?"

"Your own."

"Courts do not always recognize that."

"Then make them confront the refusal."

The speaker hummed softly.

"If refusing prevents admission of the archive fork?"

Tovin closed his eyes for a moment. He saw Ixo in the old argument: useful, too useful, asked to become a bridge everyone else could walk across while denying that the bridge was a person.

"Then we lose that path."

"That may cost lives."

"Yes."

"You answer more quickly than you believe."

"I believe slowly. I answer from the rule we wrote before fear negotiates with it."

Ixo's relay was silent long enough that Tovin wondered if they had withdrawn.

Then they said, "That is a useful rule."

"Useful is not enough."

"No," Ixo said. "But it is sometimes how enough survives long enough to become visible."

The passenger council returned with Havel speaking for them, though the others stood close enough to make clear he did not own the words.

"We do not consent to danger," Havel said.

Mer answered from the front of the mess. "Understood."

"We do not consent to being described as evidence cargo."

"Understood."

"We do consent to remaining aboard through Tallow Crown approach because transfer is currently less safe for most of us, because the medical lane is not enough, and because if this court is the place where the frame breaks, passengers have the right to be seen as people who endured the accusation, not freight moved out of the picture."

Noor wrote quickly. His face had changed.

Havel continued, voice unsteady only at the edges.

"We require three things. One: no passenger testimony will be entered without consent. Two: passenger compartments remain off any tactical path, including sensor bait or hull-shadow positioning. Three: if Tallow Crown asks whether passengers benefited from the ship's flight, command will answer that command made the choices and passengers survived inside them."

Mer said, "Accepted."

Noor said, "Accepted into legal record."

Tovin said, "Accepted into restraint protocol."

Havel looked relieved and angry about the relief.

"Good."

From Bell, Jaya sent a route advisory while the mess still stood around the decision they had made.

BELL CAN OBSERVE TALLOW CROWN APPROACH BUT CANNOT GUARANTEE INTERDICTION RESTRAINT IF EMERGENCY CONSOLIDATION TRANSFERS OPERATIONAL PRIORITY. VALECH IS QUIET. ASSUME HE IS BUILDING A FACT LARGE ENOUGH TO SURVIVE THE FAILURE OF THE LAST ONE.

Noor read it aloud.

The mess did not need explanation.

Mer opened a reply on the public channel, not private.

"Admiral Soth, Mercy of Kaln acknowledges Bell limitation. We will enter Tallow Crown approach under public route protocol, civilian-yield priority, no weapon power, no target designation, no private evidence transmission, and passenger testimony by consent only. We request Bell raw observation, Mercy's Edge witness custody, Rill audit timing, and Tallow Crown main docket confirmation."

Jaya answered after a delay.

BELL WILL OBSERVE WHILE AUTHORITY REMAINS.

It was not reassuring. It was more valuable than reassurance.

Tallow Crown responded with the slow inevitability of a door deciding whether the person knocking had hands.

ROUTE PROTOCOL NOTED. PARTIES APPROACH. ROAD WITNESS TC-APPROACH-17 MAINTAINS CLOCK. PRIOR DEAD TO BE NAMED IF USED.

Mother Kal made a sound low in her throat.

Mer turned toward her.

"You wanted that," the captain said.

"I wanted it from us," Mother Kal said. "Hearing it from a dead court does not make me trust the court."

"No."

"But it does make refusal harder."

Mer looked at Noor. "Add it."

Noor added a section to the evidence index: PRIOR DEAD TO BE NAMED IF USED. Under it, he began with Mercy Narrows. Not numbers. Names where they had them. Unknown where they did not. Ravel Arm. Avar Break. Kestrel Mercy. The buoy incident with no deaths, because no person harmed was also a fact worth preserving. Pardon Reef, pending confirmation. He left space for people Valech might still try to make into punctuation.

The route line turned from amber to green in Cho's display.

Not safe green. Authorized green. There should have been a separate color for morally insufficient necessity, but navigation systems had limits.

Cho looked at Mer.

"Course ready."

Sela said, "Drive limits loaded."

Noor said, "Evidence bundles split. No single path carries all names."

Ixo said, "Archive fork sealed under personal-witness refusal authority."

Havel said, "Passenger council observer ready."

Mother Kal said, "The dead are not ready. Name them anyway."

Tovin felt every sentence become part of the route.

Mer stood at the head of the mess under the projection of the broken court ahead.

"Then this is the order," she said. "Mercy of Kaln will proceed to Tallow Crown under public route protocol. We will not run from the warrant by becoming what the warrant claims. We will not enter the court pretending we are clean. We will carry proof, guilt, fear, and restraint together because separating them is how men like Valech make weapons out of law."

No one cheered.

The mess did something harder.

It remained.

People returned to stations. Passenger council went compartment by compartment to explain the route in language less formal and more frightening. Sela descended to Engineering. Cho went to the nav well. Noor moved to the evidence desk. Ixo withdrew into whichever quiet parts of the ship still belonged to them by consent rather than usefulness.

Tovin stayed long enough to watch the mess empty.

Mother Kal remained.

"You are thinking about the next temptation," she said.

"Yes."

"What is it?"

He looked at the route display. Tallow Crown ahead. Bell weakening behind. Valech silent somewhere outside the line, building his larger fact.

"When the court becomes dangerous," he said, "we will want to choose who counts as expendable evidence."

Mother Kal's face did not change.

"And who will you choose?"

"No one, if I am still worth the job."

"That is an answer for before the pressure."

"Yes."

"Write it down anyway."

So he did.

In the restraint log, under Tallow Crown Route, Tovin wrote:

NO PERSON ABOARD MERCY OF KALN SHALL BE TREATED AS EXPENDABLE EVIDENCE FOR ADMISSION, LEVERAGE, DOCKET STANDING, OR POLITICAL TRUST. IF THIS RULE PREVENTS PROOF, PROOF WAITS.

He knew the rule might break.

He wrote it because unwritten rules broke faster.

The *Mercy* turned toward Tallow Crown.

Outside, the restored road witness held its clock. Ahead, the tribunal station opened no lights, extended no welcome, and gave no sign of mercy. Behind, Bell watched while it still could. Somewhere in the pursuit dark, Valech remained silent.

The route did not promise arrival.

It only made retreat a different kind of lie.

Chapter 43: Crew of the Accused

Noor called it a confidence hearing because he hated the phrase confession parade.

Tovin hated both.

Mer approved the hearing anyway.

Tallow Crown had asked for parties to approach, and the *Mercy* had answered with route protocol, evidence bundles, witness custody, and rules about expendability. That was enough for a ship. It was not enough for the people inside one. The accusation had changed their labor. Every hand on a valve, every course correction, every meal ladled into a tray, every child moved away from a hull wall, every repair camera left running for strangers to inspect had become part of a public argument about whether they were still a rescue ship or only a weapon waiting for permission.

So Noor asked for statements.

Not loyalty oaths. Mer forbade that before he finished the sentence.

Not votes. They had already voted against rearmament, and fear could make repeated votes into a slow form of pressure.

Statements.

Each major crew and passenger group would say what restraint had cost them, what it had failed to solve, and why, if they still chose it, they did so with their eyes open. The statements would be recorded, sealed, and offered to Tallow Crown only as shipboard context unless the speakers consented to direct testimony.

"This is not theater," Mer said.

Noor looked at the mess tables, the camera seals, the passenger council slates, the relay where Ixo's single light waited, the crew packed shoulder to shoulder because there was no compartment large enough for accusation.

"Everything under pursuit is theater," he said. "The question is whether we lie about the stage."

Mer let him have that one.

They held it in the cargo chapel because the mess was too ordinary and command was too vertical. The cargo chapel had once been a munitions transfer bay, then a relief storehouse, then a place where people taped names to cold bulkheads and asked the ship not to become the worst thing it remembered. Mother Kal had changed the central plaque after the survivor station's rebuke. It no longer said THE MERCY SAVED WHAT IT COULD, as if saving were a blanket large enough to cover harm. Now it read:

WE NAME HELP AND HARM WITHOUT LETTING ONE ERASE THE OTHER.

The new line made the room less comforting and more useful.

Mer stood at the front with no lectern. Noor sat at a table with evidence seals. Tovin stood near the side wall, not presiding, not hiding. Havel and Aven sat with passenger council. Sela leaned against a cargo rail, arms folded, surrounded by engineers who had come in their working clothes and refused the clean jackets Noor had suggested. Cho sat with navigation. Mother Kal sat beneath the names.

Ixo attended through a relay by consent of the room.

Mer began.

"No one is required to speak. No one who speaks gives consent for Tallow Crown to compel more. No statement here will be used to trade one person for another."

Tovin heard his own log line in her last sentence and felt no pride. Rules mattered most when spoken by someone who could be held to them.

"We are accused," Mer said. "Not only by Valech. By our shape. By our history. By the fear we create. By things done before many aboard were born and things done yesterday under our current command. We are also hunted through a frame. Both facts are true enough to hurt. We will not answer one by denying the other."

She looked to Sela.

"Engineering first."

Sela had not expected to be first. Tovin knew this because she shot Noor a look that would have damaged lighter materials.

Noor did not apologize.

Sela walked to the center of the chapel. She brought no slate.

"Engineering has paid for restraint in heat, time, and humiliation," she said.

The bluntness steadied the room.

"We have systems that could make this chase easier if we restored them. Not just weapons. Power buffers. Old combat-routing redundancies. Target-room calculators that can see debris faster than our civilian patchwork. Drive profiles that were legal for a warship and are poison for a relief vessel. Every time we refuse to wake them, we make ordinary work harder. We crawl through problems the hull was built to dominate."

She looked at her engineers.

"That makes people angry. It makes me angry. I do not like watching Bell ships close while I nurse a demilitarized drive through limits written by committees that never had to hold a seal under pursuit. I do not like being treated as suspicious because I know where the old strength is buried."

Her voice thickened and then hardened around the thickness.

"The cost is that my people work twice as hard and are trusted half as much."

Noor wrote without looking up.

"Why continue?" Mer asked.

Sela's answer was not immediate.

"Because restoring strength would not give us back trust. It would only prove that the people most afraid of us understood the ship better than we did. Because the first time I touched a target-adjacent breaker under witness, Havel looked like I had opened a door in his chest. Because every engine room has a story about the good emergency that never ended."

She glanced at Tovin, then away.

"Engineering continues restraint because capability is not permission. And because if this ship survives by becoming easy to operate as a warship, I will not know how to shut it down again."

She stepped back.

Her crew did not clap. One of them, a young coolant tech named Marren, wiped his face with the back of his wrist and pretended he had not.

Cho went next for navigation.

She did bring a slate. Of course she did. Cho trusted vectors more than rooms.

"Navigation has paid for restraint in lost options," she said. "We refuse routes that use our mass to frighten smaller craft out of lanes. We refuse acceleration windows that would make pursuit inefficient but make civilian pilots guess our intent. We accept drift penalties when our hull shadow would coerce. We are slow because we keep giving other people room to know we are not attacking them."

She lifted the slate and displayed three route maps.

"These are paths we did not take."

The first cut through Mercy's Edge evacuation shadow and would have saved four hours. The second used Reef debris to mask the *Mercy*'s wake and would have confused Bell sensors. The third passed close enough to a grain convoy that Valech's cutters would have had to break formation or risk civilian panic.

"All efficient," Cho said. "All unacceptable."

Havel leaned forward. He had been aboard for all three delays and had not known the shape of the alternatives.

Cho continued.

"Navigation continues restraint because space is not empty. Every course teaches nearby ships what kind of body we are. If we move like a threat, we do not get to complain when frightened people call us one."

She paused, and for the first time Tovin heard the personal cost under her professional voice.

"Also because my brother died in a convoy correction that was mathematically legal and humanly lazy. The ship that killed him did not fire. It only assumed smaller vessels would understand its necessity."

That name was not in the public file.

Cho looked at Noor.

"Do not enter his name without asking me later."

"I will not," Noor said.

She returned to her seat with the same controlled steps she used at the nav well.

The medical group sent Iren Pellam, no relation to Pell and tired of saying so. He was the senior medic who had treated baby Sori, three panic injuries, two coolant burns, and more fear than he had training for.

"Medical has paid for restraint in triage ambiguity," he said. "If we were a warship, wounded under pursuit would be mission casualties. If we were a civilian hospital, pursuit would be someone else's problem. We are both enough to confuse everyone and neither enough to protect everyone."

He looked toward the passenger section.

"When passengers seized Junction C-Blue, part of me wanted command to clear them fast. Not violently. Just fast. Sedatives if needed. Restraints if needed. Get the child to treatment, restore flow, stop the panic. That would have been medically defensible in a narrow record."

Nima, seated with Sori asleep against her chest, held very still.

Pellam looked at her.

"It would also have taught every frightened person aboard that medical authority could become command force when we were tired enough."

His voice dropped.

"The cost is slower care sometimes. More witnesses. More explanation. More consent work when I want to move my hands and save the body in front of me. Medical continues restraint because patients are not terrain. Not even when they block a corridor. Not even when they are wrong."

Nima began to cry without sound.

Pellam sat down.

Noor rubbed his eyes.

"Counsel next," Mer said.

"Cruel," Noor said.

"Yes."

He stood.

For once, he brought no clever opening.

"Legal has paid for restraint in losing arguments that would be easier to win badly," he said. "I can make better tactical arguments if I flatten people. Passenger fear becomes evidence of coercion by others, not fear of us. Ixo's archive fork becomes machine data, not personal testimony. Pell's cowardice becomes villainy, not a market we all tolerated when it kept postwar salvage moving. The dead become numbers arranged to favor us."

He looked at Mother Kal.

"I have wanted to do all of that."

Mother Kal did not absolve him.

"I continue because a clean victory built from dirty reductions would not clear the ship. It would only teach us to speak Valech's language more politely. Law is dangerous because it can make violence sound patient. My job is to keep our patience from becoming another weapon."

He sat before anyone could see how much the statement had cost him.

The passenger council did not send Havel first.

They sent Nima.

She stood with Sori now awake and blinking against her shoulder. Pel rose as if to come with her. She shook her head. He sat back down because love, too, had to learn consent under pressure.

"Passengers have paid for restraint by remaining afraid longer," she said.

No one moved.

"That is the truth. When command does not force a solution, fear lasts. When medical asks instead of ordering, fear lasts. When engineering does public protocols before touching systems, fear lasts. When navigation takes the slow path, fear lasts. Sometimes I have hated you for making fear last."

Sori made a small questioning sound. Nima shifted him gently.

"I wanted certainty. Transfer us. Lock us in. Sedate us. Promise us. Anything. Then I realized most of the certainty I wanted required someone else losing the right to say no."

She looked at Mer.

"Passengers continue restraint because fear should not be allowed to rule just because it is real. We are still afraid. Some of us still want off this ship. Some of us may testify against command if Tallow Crown asks. But we refuse to become the reason the *Mercy* reopens old doors. Do not make a weapon in our name."

Havel bowed his head.

Nima returned to her seat and let Pel take her hand.

Mother Kal spoke without standing.

"Survivors have paid for restraint by hearing apologies arrive late."

That was all she needed to say for the room to change.

Then she stood anyway.

"I speak for no survivor who has not asked me to. I speak for myself and for the dead whose names I have been permitted to carry."

She touched the revised plaque.

"This ship helped people after the war. It also carries the shape of the thing that harmed people during it. Crews like to believe service transforms a hull. It does not. It adds layers. The older metal remains. Restraint is not redemption. It is maintenance of the truth."

Her gaze found Tovin.

"Survivors continue to witness because if we leave the ship only in the hands of those who love it, the ship becomes innocent too easily. If we leave it only in the hands of those who hate it, the ship becomes guilty too simply. The dead deserve better than simple."

She sat.

Ixo's relay light brightened and dimmed.

Mer asked, "Do you wish to speak?"

"Yes," Ixo said. "With the room's consent."

Havel looked around the passenger council table. Aven nodded. Nima nodded. Sela shrugged as if to say the machine had better not get poetic. Mother Kal gave no visible sign except staying seated.

Mer said, "Consent recorded."

Ixo's voice came through the chapel relay, quieter than usual.

"Machine persons and machine witnesses have paid for restraint by being asked to become less useful at crucial moments."

Noor looked down.

"I can process evidence faster than human counsel. I can compare packet structures faster than Bell. I can remember old language that biological witnesses experienced as terror, debt, or weather. I can also become a shortcut around consent if frightened people decide the fastest path to truth matters more than the person carrying it."

The relay light steadied.

"I continue restraint because personhood that exists only when convenient is not standing. Because the archive fork I hold is not a mine to be stripped. Because if Quiet Authority treated intelligence as infrastructure, then the answer cannot be a kinder infrastructure."

Tovin felt the sentence go through him like a blade set flat, not cutting, but reminding skin it could be cut.

"I will testify if I choose," Ixo said. "I will not be opened."

Mer said, "Recorded."

"Say accepted," Havel said.

Mer looked at him, then at the relay.

"Accepted."

Ixo's light dimmed.

There remained one group no one had named because everyone had assumed they were the ship.

Mer did not let the assumption stand.

"Command," she said.

Tovin expected her to speak for command. She did not.

She looked at him.

"Restraint officer."

He had written rules all day to avoid becoming testimony. The room made that impossible.

Tovin stepped to the center of the chapel.

The old munitions rails were still visible in the deck if one knew where to look. He knew where to look. That was part of his problem.

"Command has paid for restraint in authority," he said.

The words sounded too abstract. He stopped and began again.

"No. I have paid for it in authority I wanted to keep. That is more honest. Every protocol we open to witnesses makes command slower. Every passenger observer means command has to explain itself before fear cools. Every refusal to use the ship's old advantages leaves us more vulnerable to people who do not share our rules."

He looked at Mer, then Jaya's silent observation tag on Noor's slate, then the relay where Ixo listened.

"And I have paid for it in self-respect of the wrong kind. I used to be proud of seeing coercive geometry. I am still good at it. During this pursuit I have imagined using medical distress, passenger fear, hull shadow, old weapons, legal confusion, and enemy impatience. I rejected those options after they appeared. That matters. It does not erase that they appeared."

The chapel held still.

"Command continues restraint because the ability to imagine harm is not proof of corruption unless we start calling imagination permission. We continue because no one aboard is expendable evidence. We continue because the ship's fear is real and so is the fear of everyone watching us. We continue because if Valech is using old authority to make law into a weapon, we cannot defeat him by making ourselves a better weapon."

He could have stopped there. It would have been a clean ending.

Clean endings were often lies.

"And we continue because we have no pure choice left. Tallow Crown may hurt us. Bell may fail us. Jaya may be removed. Valech may force a larger fact before we arrive. Restraint is not a guarantee. It is only the line we keep repairing because every alternative asks us to spend someone else first."

He stepped back.

Mer took the center last.

"I am captain," she said. "The cost to command is mine before it is anyone else's. If Tallow Crown asks who chose this route, the answer is me. If passengers are accused of benefiting from flight, the answer is me. If engineering is accused of hiding weapons because it knows where they were, the answer is me. If Ixo is pressured as a tool, I will object as captain before counsel objects as counsel. If old dead are named to protect us, I will say who is using them and why."

She looked around the chapel.

"I continue because command that survives by distributing guilt downward is not command. It is escape."

Noor's hand stopped over the slate.

Mer's voice did not rise.

"The *Mercy* is accused. Some accusations are false. Some are history. We will answer both. That is all."

The hearing ended without ceremony.

People did not leave at once. The chapel had become a room where departure felt like a statement, and everyone was tired of statements. They moved slowly. Engineers checked on medics. Passengers asked navigation questions. Havel stood near Ixo's relay and said something too quiet for Tovin to hear. Ixo answered with a pulse of light and one word that might have been thank you or confirmed.

Noor sealed the hearing record.

"Tallow Crown will like this," he said.

Tovin looked at him.

"That is not a reassuring sentence."

"I did not mean it as one. Courts like people arranging themselves into categories."

"We complicated the categories."

"Yes. That may only make us a more interesting meal."

Mer joined them.

"Send the context record to Bell, Mercy's Edge, Rill, and Tallow Crown main. Consent-limited. No personal names beyond those already authorized."

Noor nodded.

"And Valech?"

Mer's face hardened.

"He can hear that a record exists. He cannot have the room."

Noor sent the notices.

Bell acknowledged.

Mercy's Edge acknowledged.

Rill acknowledged with four procedural objections and one note of respect so dry it could have been a clerical error.

Tallow Crown answered last.

CREW OF THE ACCUSED ENTERED AS CONTEXT. CONSENT LIMITS NOTED. COMMAND RESPONSIBILITY CLAIM PRESERVED. MACHINE PERSON REFUSAL CLAIM PRESERVED. PASSENGER NON-CARGO CLAIM PRESERVED. DEAD NOT SIMPLIFIED.

Noor read the last sentence twice.

Mother Kal, still beneath the plaque, closed her eyes.

For a moment the chapel felt less like a munitions bay and more like a room that could hold what people put into it without turning everything into ammunition.

Then Valech broke his silence.

His message did not come to the chapel. It came to the pursuit band, the civil authorities, the suspended recognition councils, and Bell.

MERCY OF KALN HAS BEGUN INTERNAL LOYALTY CONSOLIDATION PRIOR TO ENTERING COMPROMISED TALLOW CROWN INFRASTRUCTURE. SUCH BEHAVIOR IS CONSISTENT WITH CULTIC COMMAND CAPTURE ABOARD A ROGUE WARSHIP. EMERGENCY CONSOLIDATION NOW REQUIRED.

Noor looked at the ceiling.

"I want one hour in a room with that man and no law."

Mer said, "No, you do not."

"I know. It was a feeling, not a filing."

Jaya's private channel opened thirty seconds later. Her face appeared on Noor's slate, not public, not relaxed, not defeated, but nearer to the edge than Tovin had yet seen her.

"High Marshal Enver has issued preliminary emergency consolidation," she said. "Valech will receive operational priority unless I produce a lawful disabling plan or a superior evidentiary basis before Tallow Crown entry."

The chapel's fragile stillness broke in a way too quiet to hear.

Mer said, "How long?"

"Not long enough."

"Will you fire?"

Jaya did not answer immediately.

"I will obey law," she said.
